### SA-1 — Policy and Procedures (Control)

This requirement is about making sure the organisation has clear, written rules for how it buys and sets up new systems and services, and that the right people know about them and follow them. Without these rules, contracts and projects can lose direction, important legal and regulatory responsibilities may be missed, and security and privacy weaknesses can be introduced because different teams use different ways of working.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must create an organisation-wide policy for acquiring systems and services, along with supporting procedures. These must explain why the policy exists, what it covers, who is responsible for what, how senior leadership supports it, how different teams coordinate, and how compliance will be checked. The policy and procedures must also be consistent with all relevant laws, directives, regulations, and internal standards.

A named senior owner—either the Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) or a delegated Security Policy Manager—must be responsible for producing, documenting, and sharing the policy and procedures with security and privacy leadership, contracting officers, and programme managers. The procedures must also be shared with contracting and delivery staff and system security officers.

The policy and procedures must be reviewed at least every year. They must also be updated after audit findings, security incidents or breaches, major changes to system design, or changes in laws or directives. For the procedures, updates are also required after changes to acquisition processes, significant changes to vendors or contracting arrangements, or new or revised requirements.

##### CISO-owned acquisition security & privacy policy and procedures suite

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create and maintain an organisation-level (or mission/business-process-level) **system and services purchasing policy** and supporting **system and services purchasing procedures**. These must cover the SA requirements that the organisation has put in place for its systems and services.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-1 requires that the purchasing policy and procedures exist (not just repeating the SA requirements), match the organisation’s risk management approach, and are kept up to date. It also requires security and privacy teams to work together, to share the documents with specific roles, and to update them when defined events happen.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Policy set (organisation-level):** Create one main document (or a small set of linked documents) such as “System and Services Acquisition Security & Privacy Policy”. Include:
   - purpose and scope (organisation-level and/or mission/business-process-level)
   - how it links to risk management
   - expectations for how security and privacy teams will coordinate
   - a compliance approach that links relevant laws, directives, regulations, and standards to purchasing activities
2. **Procedures (how-to, by role):** Create “Acquisition Security & Privacy Procedures” that explain how the policy is followed in day-to-day purchasing across the full acquisition process. At minimum, write procedures for:
   - contracting officers
   - acquisition programme staff
   - system security officers  
   Use role-based workflows that clearly state required inputs, outputs, and the proof (evidence) that the steps were completed.
3. **Named owner:** Appoint an official owner to manage the policy and procedures (for example, the Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) or a delegated Security Policy Manager). Define responsibilities for drafting, review, approval, publishing, sharing, and regular updates.
4. **Share with proof:** Share the **policy** with security and privacy programme leadership, contracting officers, and programme managers. Share the **procedures** with contracting officers, acquisition programme staff, and system security officers. Use a controlled document repository and record acknowledgement and/or attendance.
5. **Review/update schedule and triggers:** Put in place a documented update cycle:
   - Review and update the policy **at least every year**.
   - Update the policy after events such as: audit findings, security incidents or breaches, major system/operational technology (OT) architecture changes, and changes to laws or Department of Defense (DoD) directives.
   - Review and update the procedures **at least every year**.
   - Update the procedures after events such as: new or changed SA-family requirements, changes to purchasing workflows, major vendor/contracting changes, and regulatory updates.
6. **Governance and change control:** Set up a small governance group (security/privacy plus procurement plus programme delivery, and legal/compliance as needed) to review proposed changes, record decisions, and ensure updates can be traced back to the defined triggers.

###### WHO
- **CISO or delegated Security Policy Manager** (policy/procedure owner)
- **Security and privacy programme leadership** (collaboration and review)
- **Contracting officers** and **acquisition programme staff** (people who use the procedures)
- **System security officers** (people who use the procedures and provide technical review)
- **Legal/compliance** (as needed for law/directive/regulatory changes)

###### Acceptance criteria
- An organisation-level purchasing policy exists, is owned by the CISO (or delegated Security Policy Manager), and has been shared with the defined policy recipients.
- Purchasing procedures exist, are written for the defined roles, and are shared with proof.
- The policy and procedures include a documented annual review process and are updated within the defined triggers after at least one applicable event (or there is a documented reason if no trigger occurred since the last publication).
- Evidence exists for: publication/version history, document-sharing records, and review/approval meeting notes or change-control tickets.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Draft the organisation-wide policy for acquiring systems and services, covering security and privacy. Include its purpose, scope, how it links to risk management, and how it stays consistent with legal and regulatory requirements.
- [ ] Assign the Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) (or the delegated Security Policy Manager) as the official owner, and document their responsibilities for drafting, approving, distributing, and reviewing the policy.
- [ ] Create role-based acquisition security and privacy procedures for contracting officers, acquisition programme staff, and system security officers. Include the required inputs and outputs, and the evidence records that must be kept.
- [ ] Publish both documents in a controlled document library and record who received them and who acknowledged them, for the policy and procedure recipients named in the plan.
- [ ] Set up a documented review and update schedule: review the policy and procedures every year.
- [ ] Set up and put into practice update triggers for the policy (audit findings, incidents or breaches, major changes to the architecture, and changes to legal or Department of Defense (DoD) directives) and for procedures (new or revised Security Assessment (SA) family requirements, changes to the acquisition process, significant changes involving vendors or contracting, and regulatory updates).
- [ ] Set up a governance meeting forum and a change-approval process to authorise updates and keep audit-ready records (meeting minutes, version history, and change request tickets).


---

### SA-2 — Allocation of Resources (Control)

This requirement is about making sure your organisation plans and pays for protection for every system and service from the start, rather than treating security and privacy as an afterthought. If you do not identify security and privacy needs early and fund them properly, you risk not protecting sensitive information and personal data enough, not meeting your privacy obligations, and leaving weaknesses that could be exploited or cause harm. This can also happen through weaknesses in suppliers and other third parties.

To meet this expectation, during mission and business planning you must identify and record the high-level information security and privacy requirements for each in-scope system or service. Then, through your capital planning and investment approval process, you must decide, record, and provide the resources needed to meet those requirements across the whole lifecycle. This includes buying the system, keeping it running, and managing supply-chain risks. Finally, you must record information security and privacy funding as separate, clearly identifiable line items in your programme and budgeting documents.

##### Security & privacy requirements-to-budget traceability for each system

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a repeatable, auditable process that connects (1) high-level information security requirements and (2) high-level privacy requirements identified during mission/business planning for each in-scope system/service to (3) the full set of lifecycle resources needed to meet them (buying, running, and managing supply-chain risk). Then record those resources as **separate/discrete** budget line items for **information security** and **privacy** in programme and budgeting documents.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control SA-2 requires that funding for information security and privacy includes resources for system and service acquisition, running (sustainment), and supply-chain risk activities across the whole system development life cycle. This funding must be based on requirements identified during mission/business planning, then included in capital planning and investment control. Security and privacy funding must be recorded as clearly identifiable line items.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain an authoritative system/service register** (for example, a configuration management database (CMDB) or workload inventory) with an owner, data categories, and lifecycle stage.
2. **Standardise mission/business planning templates** to capture high-level **information security** requirements and high-level **privacy** requirements for each system/service (including links to the evidence from the original risk assessment, regulatory/contract obligations, and data classification).
3. **Create a control-to-cost mapping library** that converts each requirement type into costed lifecycle resource categories, covering:
   - acquisition (for example, secure design/build activities, buying tools, and onboarding vendors with assurance checks)
   - sustainment (for example, operating monitoring/logging, managing vulnerabilities, patching, being ready to respond to incidents, and testing backups/disaster recovery)
   - supply-chain risk (for example, third-party security assurance, software bill of materials (SBOM) and assurance activities, vendor risk assessments, and secure integration testing)
4. **Produce a requirements-to-resources traceability pack** for each capital bid/investment review showing: requirement → mapped resource categories → lifecycle phase (acquisition/sustainment/supply-chain) → cost centre.
5. **Require discrete budget line items** in budgeting documents, so the investment submission has two clearly separated sections/rows: **Information Security** and **Privacy**, each filled using the traceability pack.
6. **Embed the traceability pack into the capital planning and investment control process**, so investment approval checks require: (a) security requirements, (b) privacy requirements, (c) mapped lifecycle resources, and (d) the two discrete budget line items.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security & Privacy Governance Lead**: owns the requirements templates, the mapping library, and the approval check criteria.
- **Enterprise Architecture / Portfolio Management**: maintains the system/service register and ensures the traceability pack is produced for each investment.
- **Finance / Capital Planning Office**: ensures budgeting documents accept and retain discrete security and privacy line items.
- **System/Service Owners**: provide requirement inputs during mission/business planning and confirm the mapped resources are correct.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each in-scope system/service with a capital bid, there is an auditable traceability pack showing security requirements and privacy requirements mapped to costed lifecycle resources (acquisition, sustainment, supply-chain).
- The investment submission/programming and budgeting document includes **separate/discrete** budget line items for **Information Security** and **Privacy**, populated from the traceability pack.
- The capital/investment approval process blocks approval if security/privacy requirement inputs are missing or if discrete budget line items are missing.
- The planning templates include evidence links to the original risk/regulatory/privacy obligations and data classification sources.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create or update an official system and service register, including the responsible owner and the current stage of its lifecycle.
- [ ] Publish mission and business planning templates that capture high-level information security and privacy requirements, with links to supporting evidence.
- [ ] Develop and maintain a library that links each control to its cost, covering activities related to acquiring, sustaining, and managing supply-chain risk.
- [ ] Implement a traceability pack generator that links each requirement to the costed lifecycle resources used for each investment bid.
- [ ] Update the capital planning and investment submission form to require two separate budget line items: **Information Security** and **Privacy**.
- [ ] Add approval gate checks to stop approvals when the required traceability pack evidence or individual line items are missing.


---

### SA-3 — System Development Life Cycle (Control)

This expectation is about making sure every system and service is built and updated in a controlled way, with clear checkpoints from the start to protect people’s data and privacy. Without this, organisations may release products with avoidable weaknesses, handle personal information incorrectly, or miss and fail to manage risks early—resulting in expensive rework, regulatory action, reputational damage, and harm to customers or staff.

The organisation must use its tailored system development approach, aligned with its risk management approach, and include security and privacy “gates” at the right points. This ensures these issues are considered during planning, building, testing, and release. It must also clearly define, document, and assign who is responsible for security and who is responsible for privacy across the entire development process, and name the specific people in those roles. Finally, it must build its security risk management and privacy risk management processes into the development activities, including the checkpoints.

##### Tailored SDLC with security/privacy engineering gates and evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an organisation-specific **system development life cycle (SDLC)** that includes **security and privacy review checkpoints** at key points in the work (for example: requirements, design, build, testing/verification, release/deployment, operations/change, and retirement). Each checkpoint must have clear **entry and exit requirements**, the **security/privacy documents and outputs** needed to pass, and **risk-based go/no-go decisions**. Keep audit records up to date continuously by using the organisation’s delivery tools.

###### WHY (control requirement)
**SA-3** requires an **SDLC process** that supports how systems are developed, implemented, and operated, and it must bring **security and privacy** into the process early. It also requires:
- **information security** and **privacy** to be considered in the relevant SDLC activities and checkpoints;
- **suitably qualified staff** (security/privacy architects and engineers, plus senior security/privacy roles) to be involved in the SDLC process;
- **security and privacy architecture** to be included in the organisation’s **enterprise architecture** and aligned with the organisation’s **risk management strategy**;
- **information security risk management** and **privacy risk management** to be built into SDLC activities (including checkpoints);
- consistent use across multiple organisations (for example, suppliers, developers, and integrators).

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the tailored SDLC and checkpoint model** in the SDLC standard (including the stage names, where the checkpoints occur, and what changes trigger returning to a checkpoint). Use the organisation-defined parameter: **“DoD RMF tailored SDLC with security and privacy engineering gates”** (sa-03_odp).
2. **For each checkpoint, set entry and exit requirements and required documents/outputs** that show security and privacy work has been completed. At minimum, each checkpoint must require evidence that:
   - security requirements and design considerations were created or updated;
   - privacy considerations were identified and turned into controls (for example, privacy data mapping/controls where applicable);
   - testing/verification plans cover the security/privacy requirements;
   - release/deployment readiness includes references to risk decisions.
3. **Use risk information in checkpoint decisions** by requiring that checkpoint exit requirements refer to the latest organisation risk decisions (for example, accepted risks, mitigations, and sign-off of remaining risk) produced through the organisation’s **information security risk management** and **privacy risk management** processes.
4. **Automate checkpoint checks in the continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline** to reduce manual effort and improve auditability. Examples of automated checks (as pipeline steps) include:
   - generating a software bill of materials (SBOM) and enforcing vulnerability thresholds;
   - scanning for secrets and preventing credential leakage;
   - checking infrastructure-as-code and configuration baseline policies;
   - capturing required build/test evidence (for example, unit/integration test results, and security test results where available).
5. **Require checkpoint approvals using role-based workflow ownership**: checkpoint approvals must be carried out by authorised security/privacy roles using workflow tools (for example, ticketing plus CI/CD approval steps) with identity-based authorisation. Ensure the SDLC standard clearly maps checkpoint responsibilities to security and privacy roles (and, where your governance requires it, to named individuals).
6. **Record and keep evidence for audit** by linking each checkpoint’s required documents/outputs and automated scan/test results to the system/service record (for example, the repository, evidence store, and change/release records). Keep evidence in line with the organisation’s retention requirements.
7. **Apply the same checkpoint model to supplier/developer/integrator work** by requiring external parties to provide the same checkpoint documents/evidence (through contractual requirements and, where feasible, technical integration into your pipeline).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **SDLC owner / Application Security & Privacy governance lead**: maintains the tailored SDLC and checkpoint definitions.
- **Security architect/engineer and privacy architect/engineer**: define the checkpoint documents, requirements, and review expectations.
- **Information security risk manager and privacy risk manager**: provide the risk decision outputs referenced by checkpoint exit requirements.
- **Engineering managers / DevOps leads**: implement CI/CD checkpoint automation and evidence capture.
- **Procurement / supplier management**: ensures supplier obligations align to the checkpoint documents and evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The organisation has an approved **tailored SDLC** document/process that defines checkpoint points, entry/exit requirements, and required security/privacy documents/outputs.
- CI/CD (or an equivalent delivery pipeline) enforces **mandatory security/privacy checkpoint checks** and prevents promotion when checkpoint exit requirements are not met.
- Checkpoint exit requirements reference the latest **information security risk management** and **privacy risk management** decisions.
- Evidence is produced and kept for each release/change, showing that the required checkpoint documents/outputs and automated checks have been completed.
- The checkpoint model is applied consistently to at least one representative **IT application** delivery stream and one representative **supplier/integrator** delivery stream (or a documented reason if this is not applicable).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the customised software development life cycle (SDLC) stages and the security and privacy checkpoints that align with the organisation’s risk management strategy.
- [ ] Create entry and exit criteria for the gate at each lifecycle milestone, and define the required security and privacy evidence checklists.
- [ ] Add information security and privacy risk decision checks to the criteria for exiting each project stage and to the approval workflow.
- [ ] Put in place continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) controls to automatically run security and privacy checks before code changes are released. This includes checking software dependencies and software bill of materials (SBOM), scanning for secrets, verifying configuration rules, and collecting required test evidence.
- [ ] Set clear responsibility and approval authority for security and privacy roles, linking each role to the specific “gate” or decision point it controls (and record the named individuals required).
- [ ] Set up a way to collect and keep evidence that connects gate artefacts, scan and test results, and approvals to each system or service change record.
- [ ] Update supplier, developer, and integrator onboarding and contractual requirements to provide the same gate deliverables and supporting evidence.


---

#### SA-3.1 — Manage Preproduction Environment (Enhancement)

This requirement is about protecting not only your live systems, but also your development, testing and integration workspaces. If these pre-production environments are not managed tightly, attackers or accidental changes could go unnoticed. Harmful code could be tested and then mistakenly carried forward, and risky third-party software or supplier components could be added before anyone realises.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must first identify all pre-production environments, then apply protection measures that match the seriousness of the impact if something goes wrong. This must be done consistently from early development through testing and integration.

It must also set out, in documented development processes, how security and supplier-related risks are considered at each stage. This should be supported by plans and strategies, programme protection planning procedures, and the results of a criticality analysis.

Finally, it must clearly assign named responsibilities for both security and development work, keep the supporting records, and demonstrate that protections are followed. Any exceptions must be formally approved and time-limited.

##### Risk-tiered preproduction environment baselines with automated drift checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create an official, complete list of all pre-production environments (development, test, and integration, including temporary build/test “sandboxes”) and apply **risk-based security baseline settings** to each environment throughout the software development life cycle (SDLC). Use automated checks and CI/CD (continuous integration/continuous delivery) approval steps to enforce these settings, so that any changes that “drift” from the baseline or are made without authorisation are detected and fixed.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-3(1)** requires protecting system pre-production environments **in line with their risk** throughout the **system development life cycle**, including development, test, and integration environments. This approach ensures protections are applied consistently from early development through later integration and testing, with evidence that is suitable for audit.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain a pre-production environment register**: record each environment’s name/ID, purpose (development/test/integration), owning team, network connection boundaries, what data it may handle, and its stage in the lifecycle. Include temporary environments created by CI/CD (for example, per-branch or per–pull request sandboxes) and ensure they follow the same baseline rules.
2. **Map criticality to each environment**: use the organisation’s criticality analysis results to assign each environment to a **risk tier** (for example, Tier 1/2/3). Base this on how critical the system component/service is and how sensitive the data is in that environment.
3. **Set “golden baseline” requirements for each tier**, at minimum covering:
   - **Identity and access**: least-privilege access roles, multi-factor authentication (MFA) for people using interactive access, and controlled processes for privileged access.
   - **Secure configuration**: hardened machine images/templates, network separation rules, and disabling or limiting local administrator access where practical.
   - **Vulnerability and patch position**: patching expectations by tier, plus a process for handling exceptions.
   - **Logging and monitoring**: centralised logging to the enterprise security information and event management (SIEM) system, retention aligned to policy, and alert thresholds.
   - **Change control**: only allow deployments from approved build outputs; require signed build outputs where practical.
4. **Enforce baselines using “policy as code” and CI/CD gates**:
   - Use automated configuration checks for infrastructure and applications (for example, infrastructure as code (IaC) policy checks and cloud security posture management (CSPM) and configuration compliance rules) that stop builds/deployments if baseline requirements are not met.
   - Add SDLC stage gates so that moving from development → test → integration cannot happen unless the compliance checks pass for the target environment tier.
5. **Continuously detect and fix drift**:
   - Run scheduled and event-triggered compliance scans for each environment tier.
   - Automatically create remediation tasks/tickets when drift is found; require re-checking before the environment can be used for integration testing.
6. **Keep evidence for audit**:
   - Store configuration compliance reports/snapshots, CI/CD pipeline confirmations, access logs, and exception records (including time-limited approvals) in an auditable repository.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Development Manager**: ensures environments are recorded and used according to SDLC stage gates.
- **Security Engineering / Cloud Security (or equivalent)**: defines the tier-based baselines and implements policy-as-code and compliance checks.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Team**: implements hardened templates, logging/monitoring integrations, and remediation automation.
- **Information Security Governance / Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC)**: checks that evidence and exception handling meet organisational requirements.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A complete, up-to-date **pre-production environment register** exists and includes development/test/integration and temporary sandboxes.
- Each pre-production environment is mapped to a **risk tier** based on recorded criticality analysis.
- Tier-specific **golden baselines** are documented and implemented as automated, enforceable checks.
- CI/CD promotion between SDLC stages is blocked when baseline compliance fails (shown and documented in at least one development → test and one test → integration workflow).
- Drift detection produces audit-ready evidence (compliance reports and pipeline confirmations) and remediation is tracked to completion.
- If exceptions are used, they are time-limited and produce audit-ready approval evidence.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date list of pre-production environments, covering development, testing, integration, and temporary continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) sandboxes.
- [ ] Assign each pre-production environment to a risk tier based on the results of the recorded criticality analysis.
- [ ] Set tier-specific “gold standard” requirements for identity and access, secure system settings, patching and vulnerability risk, and logging and monitoring.
- [ ] Set up “policy-as-code” and continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) stage checks that stop releases from moving to the next step if the baseline compliance tests fail.
- [ ] Set up continuous compliance checks and automated fixes for any changes (drift) in each environment tier.
- [ ] Set up how long audit evidence is kept for compliance reports, pipeline attestations, and any time-limited exceptions.


---

#### SA-3.2 — Use of Live or Operational Data (Enhancement)

This requirement is about controlling how teams use real, live information when they build, test, or connect systems. Any use of live (operational) data must be formally approved, properly recorded, and strictly limited. Otherwise, sensitive customer or business information could be exposed, copied, misused, or accessed by the wrong people during testing—causing real harm even before the system goes live.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- approve in advance which pre-production environments are allowed to use live (operational) data for a specific system or service;
- document the purpose and scope of that approved use; and
- ensure the restriction is enforced so only the approved uses take place.

The organisation must also protect these pre-production environments to the same level of sensitivity and risk as the live data they contain. It must keep evidence that access is authorised, recorded (logged), and removed when no longer needed, with ongoing review to ensure approvals and protections stay up to date.

##### Live data permits and policy-as-code gates for pre-production

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a controlled “Live/Operational Data Permit” process and enforce it with automated rules, so that only datasets (or specific fields) that have been explicitly approved can be added to development, test, and integration environments, for an approved reason and for a defined time period.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-3(2) enhancement requires managing extra risks from using live/operational data before systems go into production. The guidance says organisations can reduce risk by using test or dummy data. Where live/operational data is used, risk assessment methods can be used to decide whether it is acceptable. Any approved use must therefore be explicitly authorised, narrowly limited, and protected, so pre-production does not become a weaker point of exposure—particularly if personally identifiable information (PII) is involved.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a Live Data Permit workflow** (request ticket plus approval) for each system or service and for each pre-production environment (development, test, and integration) where live/operational data is requested. The permit must record: the requesting team, system/component/service, the environment(s), the dataset(s) or field(s) covered, the purpose, the data classification and whether it includes PII, the linked risk assessment and acceptance decision, any compensating controls (if applicable), and an expiry date.
2. **Set a security baseline for each data classification** (encryption for data stored and data sent, logging and monitoring requirements, retention limits, network access restrictions, and the access model). Require that the pre-production baseline is **at least as strong as** production for the same data classification.
3. **Turn permits into automated policy controls**:
   - allowlists showing which datasets or fields can be loaded into which pre-production environment,
   - allowlists showing which integration and extract, transform, and load (ETL) pipelines or data transfer jobs can copy data,
   - identity and role restrictions so only approved identities can access the pre-production data stores.
4. **Stop ad-hoc live data movement** by limiting data transfer tools and credentials, so live-data loading can only happen through the approved pipelines that check for an active permit.
5. **Provide audit evidence and monitoring**: record permit checks, data transfer job runs, and the dataset/field identifiers loaded, and log all access to the pre-production live-data stores. Alert on any transfer attempt without a valid permit, or any access outside the approved identities or groups.
6. **Automate expiry and removal**: when a permit expires, revoke access and start deletion or anonymisation for the approved live-data scope. Keep audit logs for compliance.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **System/Service Owner**: submits permit requests with the scope and purpose.
- **Data Owner / Data Steward**: confirms the dataset/field scope and data classification.
- **Information Security (and Privacy where PII is involved)**: approves the permit, confirms the risk acceptance, and verifies that the security baseline matches production strength.
- **Platform/DevOps Engineering**: implements the automated policy controls, pipeline restrictions, and expiry automation.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / security information and event management (SIEM) Operations**: monitors alerts and ensures audit evidence is retained.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every pre-production environment where live/operational data is used, there is an active Live Data Permit with complete details (environment, dataset/field, purpose, expiry) and a linked risk acceptance decision.
- Automated controls prevent any live-data transfer or loading into pre-production unless a valid permit exists and the dataset/field scope matches the permit.
- Pre-production security baseline controls (encryption, logging/monitoring, access restrictions, retention) are demonstrably at least as strong as production for the same data classification.
- All live-data transfers and accesses in pre-production are logged with enough identifiers to support audit review.
- When a permit expires, access is revoked and the approved live-data scope is deleted or anonymised in line with the permit, with evidence retained.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a “Live/Operational Data Permit” template that records: the environment, which dataset and fields are covered, the purpose, the data classification and whether it includes personal data, any agreed risk acceptance, and the expiry date.
- [ ] Set up a controlled approval process that includes sign-off from Information Security and Privacy teams where it applies.
- [ ] Create “policy-as-code” allowlists that limit what is permitted to approved pre-production environments, approved datasets and fields, and approved data transfer pipelines.
- [ ] Restrict live data loading to pipelines that have been approved by the permit process by removing or limiting any one-off export/import credentials and any direct access to related tools or file locations.
- [ ] Set up security baselines for the pre-production environment (encryption, logging and monitoring, data retention, and network and access restrictions) to be at least as strong as those used in the production environment for the same data classification.
- [ ] Set up security information and event management (SIEM) audit logging for permit checks, transfer execution, and access to pre-production data. Create alerts for transfers made without a permit or for unauthorised access.
- [ ] Automate the expiry of permits so access is removed and the approved live-data area is deleted or anonymised, while keeping audit evidence records.


---

#### SA-3.3 — Technology Refresh (Enhancement)

This expectation is about keeping the organisation’s technology up to date on purpose, not by accident. You must create and follow a planned schedule to refresh the system’s technology throughout its whole life. This includes the hardware, software and any embedded software, as well as the supporting ways of working, the skills of the people who run it, the suppliers or service providers you rely on, and the locations where updates are carried out.

Without this, the organisation may end up using equipment or software that no longer receives support or updates. That can leave known weaknesses uncorrected, increase the risk of unreliable or incorrectly sourced parts, and make it harder to meet privacy and security responsibilities. The result can be service disruption, failures to meet compliance requirements, and avoidable errors.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must:
- set clear timing for refresh activities, from planning through operation and maintenance
- build the refresh planning into how systems are bought and built
- base refresh decisions on documented security and privacy risk assessments
- record the schedule and the evidence that refresh actions were completed as planned
- keep the relevant plans and responsibilities up to date

##### Risk-based technology refresh schedule with SDLC gate evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Create and put in place a technology refresh plan that runs across the whole system development life cycle. This must cover hardware, software, and any embedded firmware/software, as well as the supporting ways of working (processes and skills), suppliers and service providers, and facilities and environments.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The enhancement to NIST SP 800-53 **SA-3(3)** requires the organisation to **plan for and implement a technology refresh schedule** throughout the system development life cycle. The guidance explains that using outdated or soon-to-be-outdated technology increases security and privacy risk, for example: unsupported components, inability to meet security and privacy requirements, slow or non-working components, untrusted sources, and increased complexity.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Set up an authoritative technology inventory** for the application and everything it depends on (infrastructure, platform services, databases, messaging, identity integrations, monitoring and logging agents, continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) tools, and any embedded firmware/software). Include at least: component name, version/firmware, environment (development/testing/production/disaster recovery), owner, supplier/service provider, and **support status** (for example, vendor support end date and whether security updates are still available).
2. **Set refresh triggers and timing** based on documented risk levels, not just calendar dates. For example: “refresh 6–12 months before the vendor’s end of security updates”, “review immediately when security updates are no longer available”, and “replace when components can no longer meet the required security and privacy controls”.
3. **Carry out security and privacy risk assessments** to support refresh decisions, and record the reasons. Every planned refresh item must point to the relevant risk assessment outputs (for example, the impact of unsupported components on confidentiality, integrity, availability, and privacy obligations).
4. **Create the technology refresh schedule** as a controlled backlog that includes: the planned action (upgrade/replace/decommission), affected components, due date or trigger, required maintenance window, testing and validation expectations, and a link to the related risk assessment.
5. **Deliver the plan through the system development life cycle (SDLC) and change management**: use stage-gate checks so that (a) design and build includes an “obsolescence check”, and (b) production rollout includes a “refresh readiness” check. Make sure each refresh is linked to an approved change record, with a rollback plan and proof that it was completed (updated inventory/configuration, release notes, and test results).
6. **Include refresh planning for supporting capabilities**: ensure the schedule covers updates to operational processes (for example, patch and upgrade runbooks), the required staff skills (training and competence updates for new versions), and supplier/service provider responsibilities (for example, contract or service-level expectations for updates).
7. **Keep audit-ready evidence**: keep the schedule, the risk assessment references, and completion evidence for each refresh item, and keep the schedule up to date as components change.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / System Owner**: responsible for maintaining the refresh schedule and ensuring it is carried out.
- **Cybersecurity / Privacy Risk Owner**: responsible for the risk assessment inputs and approvals that drive refresh decisions.
- **IT/OT Infrastructure and Platform Engineering**: carries out upgrades and replacements and updates the inventory/configuration.
- **Procurement / Vendor Management**: ensures supplier/service provider update obligations are captured and tracked.
- **Change Manager / Release Manager**: ensures refresh actions are implemented through approved change records and stage gates.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A controlled, current **technology refresh schedule** exists for the application and its dependencies, covering the full life cycle (including operations and maintenance and end-of-life planning).
- The schedule includes **hardware/software/embedded firmware/software** where applicable, plus supporting processes/skills/suppliers/facilities.
- Each scheduled refresh item is linked to a **documented security and privacy risk assessment** and has defined triggers and timing.
- Refresh delivery is evidenced through **approved change records** and updated inventory/configuration showing completion.
- SDLC stage gates show that obsolescence and refresh readiness checks happen before production rollout.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an official list of all applications and their dependency components, including their version or firmware, the environment they run in, the responsible owner, the supplier, and whether vendor support is available.
- [ ] Define documented times when updates must be refreshed (for example, when security updates reach a set threshold) and specify the required maintenance windows.
- [ ] Create a security and privacy risk assessment template to support refresh decisions, and require a documented risk reference for each refresh item.
- [ ] Publish a controlled technology refresh plan that covers upgrades, replacements, and retirement (decommissioning) of systems throughout their full life cycle.
- [ ] Add checks for when components are becoming outdated and whether they are ready to be replaced into the software development life cycle (SDLC) approval steps, and require proof before any release to production.
- [ ] Carry out each refresh using approved change records, including a rollback plan, proof of testing, and updated inventory and configuration records.
- [ ] Keep audit-ready records that link each schedule item to its risk assessment results and proof of completion, and ensure the schedule is kept up to date.


---

### SA-4 — Acquisition Process (Control)

This requirement is about making sure that when you buy or commission a system or service, the contract clearly states what the supplier must do to protect your information and people’s privacy. Without this, a supplier could provide something that looks acceptable on paper but is weaker than expected, does not include the right protections, cannot show what was built and tested, or provides incomplete or poorly protected documentation. That would leave you unable to operate safely, investigate problems, or hold anyone accountable.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must use its SA-4 acquisition template to set out in the contract (or by reference to version-controlled documents): the security and privacy requirements; the required strength of the protective measures; the evidence the supplier must provide from development and testing; the specific safeguards the supplier must put in place; and the security and privacy documentation the supplier must produce and protect. The contract must also describe the supplier’s build and test environment and the intended operating environment, clearly assign responsibilities for security, privacy, and supply-chain risk, and set measurable acceptance criteria to confirm whether the work is truly acceptable.

##### SA-4 contract annex pack with security/privacy traceability

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
For every purchase of a system, system component, or system service, make sure the contract clearly includes the organisation’s SA-4 acquisition template requirements (using {{ insert: param, sa-04_odp.01 }}). This must cover: (1) security and privacy requirements (based on SA-2), (2) requirements for how strong the security protections must be, (3) assurance requirements and the evidence the supplier must provide from development and testing, (4) security and privacy documents the supplier must deliver (including how those documents must be protected), (5) the development and intended operating environments, (6) who is responsible for what (organisation versus developer/supplier, including support, maintenance, and updates), and (7) acceptance criteria that can be measured.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-4 requires the organisation to include the specified security and privacy requirements, descriptions, and criteria in the acquisition contract (or by reference) using the organisation-defined parameter {{ insert: param, sa-04_odp.01 }}. The guidance also requires that any security/privacy requirements derived from SA-2, the strength-of-protection targets, the assurance evidence, the documentation needed across the full lifecycle, and the acceptance criteria are specified in the same way as the organisation’s normal acquisition or procurement requirements.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. Create and keep up to date a version-controlled “SA-4 Annex Pack” that matches the organisation’s SA-4 acquisition template (the contract wording referenced by {{ insert: param, sa-04_odp.01 }}). Include sections for functional requirements, strength of protection, assurance/evidence, documentation deliverables, environment descriptions, responsibility allocation, and acceptance criteria.
2. For each procurement, hold a requirements workshop to translate the SA-2 high-level security and privacy requirements into specific, testable functional requirements and acceptance criteria for the exact system or service being bought.
3. Write the strength-of-protection clauses using targets that fit the specific capabilities being procured (for example, resistance to tampering or bypass and resistance to direct attack). Link these targets to the actual protections being provided (such as authentication, encryption, logging and monitoring, and secure configuration).
4. Set assurance requirements as clear supplier evidence deliverables (for example, development records and assessment or test reports). This should allow the organisation to be confident that the required functionality is in place and meets the required strength-of-protection targets.
5. Specify the documentation the supplier must provide across the lifecycle (user and administrator guidance, secure deployment and configuration guidance, operational runbooks, and privacy or data-handling documentation where relevant). Add explicit contractual requirements for protecting these documents (for example, access control, secure transfer, and limits on distribution).
6. Add contract sections describing the supplier’s development or build environment and the intended operating environment (information technology and/or hybrid, as applicable). Define how integration, handover, and updates or maintenance will be carried out and how this will be evidenced.
7. Make sure acceptance criteria are measurable and linked to evidence review and acceptance testing results (for example, whether documentation is complete, verification results, and sign-off conditions). This lets the organisation objectively decide whether the purchased item meets the contract requirements.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- Procurement/contracting lead: ensures the SA-4 Annex Pack ({{ insert: param, sa-04_odp.01 }}) is inserted into, or referenced by, every relevant contract.
- Security and privacy engineering lead (with architects): performs the SA-2 to SA-4 mapping of requirements and defines the strength-of-protection and assurance evidence requirements.
- Legal/commercial lead: checks that the contract wording is enforceable and manages clause insertion and references.
- Acceptance/test lead: defines measurable acceptance criteria and the steps for reviewing evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each acquisition contract, the SA-4 Annex Pack (or equivalent contract wording) is included and references {{ insert: param, sa-04_odp.01 }}.
- The contract includes: SA-2-derived security and privacy functional requirements with measurable acceptance criteria; strength-of-protection requirements; assurance/evidence deliverables; lifecycle documentation deliverables plus contractual requirements to protect those documents; descriptions of the supplier’s development environment and intended operating environment; and responsibility allocation, including support, maintenance, and update expectations.
- Evidence and acceptance criteria are traceable (requirements → evidence → acceptance test and sign-off) and are sufficient to support an audit-ready verification decision.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a version-controlled “SA-4 Annex Pack” that follows the organisation’s SA-4 acquisition template wording ({{ insert: param, sa-04_odp.01 }})
- [ ] Update the procurement process so that, for every in-scope purchase, the SA-4 Annex Pack is either inserted or referenced.
- [ ] Run a workshop, as specified in the procurement process, to derive security and privacy functional requirements and acceptance criteria from SA-2 through SA-4, so they can be tested.
- [ ] Set targets for how strong each included security mechanism must be, and define the expected level of resistance for those specific mechanisms in the acquired scope.
- [ ] Specify what supplier assurance evidence you will receive (development and assessment documents, plus test and verification reports) and link each item to the acceptance criteria.
- [ ] Add lifecycle documentation deliverables and contractual clauses that require those documentation assets to be protected during transfer, storage, and distribution.
- [ ] In the contract, define clear, measurable acceptance tests and the evidence review and sign-off steps, so acceptance decisions can be made objectively.


---

#### SA-4.1 — Functional Properties of Controls (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build or supply your system clearly explain, in plain language, how the security and privacy features will work for users. This matters because if you only receive vague promises or technical details, you may not understand what actually happens when users try to use the system. That can result in weaker protection, privacy problems, or unexpected findings during audits and day-to-day operations.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must require the developer or supplier to provide a written description of the security and privacy features that users can see or experience at the points where they interact with the system. The description should focus on what users can see or do and what results they should expect. It should deliberately avoid hidden internal workings and details about internal data.

This requirement must be included in your procurement process, so it appears in invitations to bid, procurement documents, and contracts. It must also be reflected in your system security and privacy planning documents.

##### Procurement deliverable for interface-visible security/privacy control behaviour

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Require every system/component/service developer or supplier to provide a written, interface-focused description of the functional behaviour of each specified security and privacy control they will implement.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-4(1)** enhancement requires: *“Require the developer of the system, system component, or system service to provide a description of the functional properties of the controls to be implemented.”* The guidance also states that these functional properties must describe **what can be seen at the control’s interfaces** (the security/privacy capability, and the functions or mechanisms visible to users or other systems) and must **exclude internal functionality and internal data structures**.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. Create a standard procurement deliverable template (for example, **“Interface Behaviour & Functional Properties Description”**) that suppliers must complete for each in-scope control.
2. In the template, require the supplier to describe—at a minimum—the behaviour that is visible through the interface for each control, including:
   - **Control capability** (what security/privacy capability is provided)
   - **Interfaces** (user interface, application programming interface (API), command line interface (CLI), single sign-on (SSO), or workflow points where the control is used)
   - **Observable outcomes** (what users, administrators, or other systems can see)
   - **Inputs and outputs** (what triggers the control and what responses are returned)
   - **Success and failure/denial behaviour** (including user-facing error messages, prompts, and outcomes)
   - **Role/permission differences** (how behaviour changes for employees, contractors, administrators, and service accounts)
   - **Privacy-visible outcomes** (for example, consent prompts, outcomes for access or erasure requests, and visibility of retention notices—only at the interface level)
   - **Exclusions**: explicitly prohibit including internal implementation details (for example, code paths, internal component diagrams, database schemas, and internal data structures).
3. Add this deliverable requirement into **tender documents and contracts** as a contractual obligation, including a delivery deadline (for example, during design review or before acceptance testing) and a requirement to update the document for material changes.
4. Add an acceptance gate: the organisation will not accept the supplier’s security/privacy implementation evidence unless the deliverable is complete, matches the contract’s control list, and reviewers can trace each described interface behaviour to the intended control.
5. Set up a review process with security/privacy subject-matter reviewers to confirm that the descriptions are **interface-visible**, **focused on capability**, and **do not reveal internal workings or internal data structures**.

###### WHO (responsible roles)
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: adds the deliverable clause to information technology (IT) / operational technology (OT) procurement documents.
- **Application Security and Privacy engineering (subject-matter experts (SMEs))**: review and approve the interface-visible functional behaviour descriptions.
- **Programme/Project manager**: ensures supplier submission deadlines and that updates are provided on schedule.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each in-scope security/privacy control listed in the contract, the supplier provides a completed interface-focused functional behaviour description.
- Each description clearly states the **security/privacy capability** and the **interface-visible behaviour** (inputs, outputs/observable outcomes, and success and denial/error behaviour).
- The document includes **no internal implementation details** or internal data structures (for example, no database schema, internal component connections, or code-level logic).
- The deliverable is traceable to the contract control list and is reviewed and approved by security/privacy SMEs.
- For material changes, the supplier updates the deliverable and the organisation re-checks the interface-visible behaviour.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a standard acquisition document template that requires interface-visible functional features, aligned to each specified security and privacy control.
- [ ] Add a contract clause requiring suppliers to provide the template, and to exclude internal implementation details and internal data structures.
- [ ] Update the request documents—request for proposal (RFP) or invitation to tender (ITT)—so they require the template to be submitted at a specific point in the project lifecycle and again whenever there are material changes.
- [ ] Set up a procurement approval checkpoint that prevents contracts being finalised or accepted unless the required deliverable is available and has been linked to the relevant control list.
- [ ] Create a security and privacy subject-matter expert (SME) review checklist to check what users can see through the interface, the outcomes when requests succeed or are blocked, and how results differ depending on the user’s role (role-based access control (RBAC)).
- [ ] Record approved deliverables in the system security and privacy planning evidence repository so they can be traced during audits.


---

#### SA-4.2 — Design and Implementation Information for Controls (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build or supply a system provide clear, complete paperwork describing how the security features are designed and assembled. This lets you check that they are correct and will work as intended. Without this, you might buy something that appears secure at first glance but has weaknesses, hidden gaps, or parts that do not fit together properly. You would then struggle to prove what was delivered and to test it properly before relying on it.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must include these documentation requirements in procurement from the start. Your contracts and related buying documents should require the supplier to provide both:

- a high-level design (how the security-relevant parts fit together and connect to other systems or users), and  
- a low-level design (how the individual components are built and how they connect internally),

with enough detail for your team to analyse, test, and verify.

Your organisation should also define in advance what “sufficient detail” means, ensure this is applied when awarding contracts, and keep records showing the requirement was included and that the information delivered can be traced to what will be checked.

##### Security Design Pack deliverables for SA-4(2) procurement

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
For every in-scope system, system component, or system service you buy, require the developer or supplier to provide **design and build information** about the organisation’s security controls. This must include **high-level design** and **detailed (low-level) design** at a **level of detail sufficient to support analysis, testing, and verification**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-4(2)** (enhancement) requires the developer to provide design and build information that includes **high-level design** and **detailed (low-level) design** at a **defined level of detail** sufficient for **analysis, testing, and verification**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. Create a standard procurement clause and a deliverables schedule (a “**Security Design Pack**”) that every relevant contract must include.
2. In the clause, explicitly require the supplier to provide:
   - **High-level design**: security-relevant subsystems and **the connections (interfaces) between subsystems** (architecture-level security-relevant functionality).
   - **Detailed (low-level) design**: security-relevant modules and **the connections (interfaces) between modules** (module-level security-relevant functionality).
   - **Build representation**, where applicable (for example, references to source code locations, build artefact identifiers, and/or hardware schematic references).
   - Supplier and product identification and integrity evidence, where available (for example, version or serial number, verification hash or signature, software libraries used, purchase or download date, and vendor or download source).
3. Define and attach an organisation standard for what “**sufficient detail**” means for acceptance (for example, the documentation must allow test cases to be derived and verification evidence to be identified for each security-relevant interface and module).
4. Add **contract acceptance criteria** that the organisation will use to reject incomplete packs before sign-off:
   - Each security-relevant control requirement must be linked to specific sections of the high-level and detailed (low-level) design.
   - Each security-relevant interface must be described at both levels (subsystem interface and module interface) with enough detail to support independent review and testing.
   - Build representation must be traceable to the delivered build artefacts or schematics.
5. Add a procurement “gate” so contracts cannot be finalised without the deliverables schedule, acceptance criteria, and the required level of detail.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: inserts the clause and deliverables schedule into contracts.
- **Information Security (GRC) lead**: defines the “sufficient detail” standard and acceptance criteria.
- **Solution/Architecture owner**: checks that the required high-level and detailed (low-level) design scope matches the system’s security-relevant architecture.
- **Technical assurance/testing lead**: confirms the acceptance criteria support test planning and verification.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Contract deliverables include both **high-level design** and **detailed (low-level) design** for security-relevant functionality.
- Documentation is provided at a **defined level of detail** that clearly supports **analysis, testing, and verification** (for example, traceability to testable interfaces or modules and identifiable verification evidence).
- For applicable build representations, the supplier provides traceable references to delivered artefacts (for example, repository or build artefact identifiers, or schematic references).
- The organisation can carry out independent review and link each security-relevant control to the corresponding design sections and verification evidence.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Draft a standard procurement clause that requires a “Security Design Pack” for security-relevant controls.
- [ ] Define and publish the organisation’s standard for what counts as “enough detail” to support analysis, testing, and verification.
- [ ] Specify that the delivery schedule must include mandatory high-level design information, including security-relevant subsystems and the interfaces between those subsystems.
- [ ] Specify that the delivery schedule must include mandatory low-level design details, including security-relevant components and how those components connect and exchange information (module interfaces).
- [ ] Add acceptance criteria that require traceability from each security-relevant control to the related design sections and the verification evidence.
- [ ] Where relevant, require references to the implementation being used (for example, source-code or build artefact identifiers, or schematic references).
- [ ] Set up procurement approval gates so contracts cannot be finalised unless the delivery schedule and acceptance criteria are provided.


---

#### SA-4.3 — Development Methods, Techniques, and Practices (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build or supply your system can clearly show how they plan, build, test, and approve it. This reduces the risk that hidden mistakes go unnoticed. Without this, problems in the design or code could remain undetected until after launch, creating weaknesses that attackers could exploit or privacy harms that are harder to fix later.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must require the developer to use a complete, documented development process. This process must include model-based planning so that every requirement can be traced to the work done and the tests that prove it, and it must keep controlled records of what was built and how it changed.

The developer must also follow the specific secure and privacy-focused engineering methods your organisation requires, including:
- thinking about threats and risks,
- security design reviews,
- linking security needs to the tests,
- privacy engineering, including data minimisation, privacy threat thinking, and privacy impact assessments.

They must follow secure software development practices, including:
- checking code,
- running tests where appropriate,
- peer code review.

They must verify the system through unit testing, integration testing, system testing, and security testing, with coverage measures.

Finally, they must carry out quality checks using clear acceptance rules, peer reviews, defect tracking, and formal release readiness gates, and they must provide evidence through acquisition documents and contracts.

##### Evidence-first SDLC with MBSE traceability and security/privacy artefacts

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Require the developer to show that the delivered system/component/service was built using a system development life cycle (SDLC) process that uses up-to-date, proven methods for:
- systems engineering,
- systems security engineering,
- privacy engineering,
- software development,
- testing/evaluation/verification/validation (including security testing), and
- quality control processes.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-4(3)** enhancement requires the developer to **show that an SDLC process was used**. This SDLC must include up-to-date, proven methods for software development, systems engineering, systems security and privacy engineering, and quality control. Providing this evidence reduces hidden mistakes and increases confidence in the trustworthiness of the acquired system.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Add an **Evidence-First Life Cycle Evidence Pack (LCEP)** requirement to procurement and delivery acceptance. The LCEP must be mapped to the organisation’s defined methods (for example: model-based systems engineering (MBSE) with traceability and configuration management; threat modelling and security architecture reviews; privacy threat modelling and privacy impact assessments; secure software development life cycle (SSDLC) with static/dynamic analysis and code review; verification/validation with coverage measures; quality control through acceptance criteria, peer reviews, defect tracking, and release readiness gates).

Concretely:
1. **Define the LCEP template** (IDs, versions, dates, approvers, and traceability links) and require the developer to complete it for the specific delivered scope.
2. **Require MBSE traceability exports** (or equivalent outputs from the model repository) showing: requirement ID → design/model element(s) → test case IDs → test results. Include evidence of configuration baselines and change history.
3. **Require security engineering evidence** linked to the same traceability chain: threat model outputs, security architecture review records, and security requirements traced to design and tests.
4. **Require privacy engineering evidence** (where privacy engineering methods are selected/required by the organisation): privacy threat modelling and privacy impact assessment outcomes, linked to requirements/design and relevant tests.
5. **Require SSDLC evidence**: static analysis results, dynamic/runtime testing results (where applicable), and peer code review sign-offs. Record each finding as a defect/issue with closure status and link it to the affected requirements/tests.
6. **Require verification and validation reporting with coverage measures** across unit, integration, system, and security testing, including how any coverage gaps were handled (for example: documented reasons and compensating controls).
7. **Require quality control process evidence**: documented acceptance criteria, peer reviews (beyond code review where applicable), defect tracking, and **release readiness gate** records that reference the acceptance criteria and closure of required defects.

Concretely:
1. **Define the LCEP template** (IDs, versions, dates, approvers, and traceability links) and require the developer to populate it for the specific delivered scope.
2. **Require MBSE traceability exports** (or equivalent model repository outputs) showing requirement ID → design/model element(s) → test case IDs → test results, including evidence of configuration baselines and change history.
3. **Require security engineering evidence** tied to the same traceability chain: threat model outputs, security architecture review records, and security requirements traced to design and tests.
4. **Require privacy engineering evidence** (where privacy engineering methods are selected/required by the organisation): privacy threat modelling and privacy impact assessment outcomes, linked to requirements/design and relevant tests.
5. **Require SSDLC evidence**: static analysis results, dynamic/runtime testing results (where applicable), and peer code review sign-offs; require each finding to be recorded as a defect/issue with closure status and linkage to affected requirements/tests.
6. **Require verification and validation reporting with coverage measures** across unit, integration, system, and security testing, including how coverage gaps were handled (for example: documented rationale and compensating controls).
7. **Require quality control process evidence**: documented acceptance criteria, peer reviews (beyond code review where applicable), defect tracking, and **release readiness gate** records that reference the acceptance criteria and closure of required defects.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: inserts LCEP requirements into contracts and acceptance criteria.
- **Security engineering lead**: checks that security/privacy evidence and traceability are complete and correctly linked.
- **Application/Systems engineering lead**: checks MBSE traceability, configuration baselines, and SDLC evidence completeness.
- **Quality/Release manager**: checks release readiness gate evidence and defect closure.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The developer provides an LCEP containing **audit-ready evidence** that the SDLC was actually used for the delivered scope (not just a description of the process).
- Traceability is shown end-to-end: **requirements → design/model → tests → results**, with versioned configuration baselines.
- Security engineering evidence exists and is linked: **threat modelling outputs, security architecture review records, and security requirements traced to tests**.
- Privacy engineering evidence exists where required: **privacy threat modelling and privacy impact assessment outcomes**, linked to requirements/design/tests.
- SSDLC evidence is present: **static analysis, dynamic/runtime testing (as applicable), and peer code review sign-offs**, with findings tracked to closure.
- Verification and validation includes **unit/integration/system/security testing** with **coverage measures**, and documented handling of coverage gaps.
- Quality control evidence includes **acceptance criteria, defect tracking, peer reviews, and release readiness gate records** that reference closure status.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an evidence pack template for the Evidence-First Life Cycle (LCEP), mapped to the organisation’s chosen methods: model-based systems engineering (MBSE) traceability, security and privacy engineering, secure software development life cycle (SSDLC), verification and validation (V&V), and quality control.
- [ ] Add contract clauses requiring the developer to provide audit-ready LCEP evidence and end-to-end traceability exports (from requirements to design/model to tests to results), using version-controlled baseline records.
- [ ] Require security engineering evidence (threat models, security architecture review records, and security requirements traceability) to be linked to the same traceability graph.
- [ ] Require privacy engineering evidence—privacy threat modelling and privacy impact assessments—to be produced and linked to the relevant requirements, design, and tests whenever privacy methods are chosen or required.
- [ ] Require evidence of secure software development lifecycle (SSDLC) activities, including static code analysis, dynamic or runtime testing where applicable, and documented peer code review sign-offs. Track each identified issue until it is fully resolved (closed).
- [ ] Require verification and validation (V&V) reporting that includes coverage metrics for unit, integration, system, and security testing, and documented plans for how any coverage gaps are handled.
- [ ] Set clear “release readiness” gate requirements, including the required acceptance criteria, defect tracking, peer reviews, and confirmation that all required items are closed. Make these requirements mandatory for delivery sign-off.


---

#### SA-4.5 — System, Component, and Service Configurations (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring systems are built and re-built every time using the same safe, pre-approved security settings. Without this, a new system, software update, or reinstallation could quietly include weak or risky default settings—for example, unnecessary features left switched on, open access points, or settings that make it easier for criminals to break in or cause harm.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system or service developer to deliver the platform using the organisation’s specified “hardened” security configuration baselines. This includes applying the United States Government Configuration Baseline (USGCB) and Department of Defense Security Technical Implementation Guides (DoD STIG) settings for the platform, turning off any unused services and ports, and applying hardened secure configuration options.

The organisation must also ensure that if the system is reinstalled or upgraded later, the same baselines are used as the default configuration, rather than being replaced with weaker default settings.

##### Baseline-as-a-deliverable for USGCB/DoD STIG secure configs

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Require the system/component/service developer to build the platform using the organisation’s defined **security configurations** (United States Government Configuration Baseline (USGCB) and Department of Defense Security Technical Implementation Guides (DoD STIGs) for the platform). This includes **secure, hardened configuration baselines** and turning off any **unused services and network ports**. The developer must also provide **reinstall/upgrade materials that match the baseline**, so the organisation can use the same baseline as the default.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-4(5)** enhancement requires the developer to apply the organisation’s specified security configurations for the system/component/service. This includes examples such as **USGCB/DoD STIGs**, limits on **functions, ports, protocols, and services**, and **hardened configuration settings** (for example, changing default passwords). The enhancement also expects the organisation to be able to ensure the same baseline is used as the **default configuration** during later reinstall/upgrade.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. Create an **authoritative “Baseline Pack”** for each platform (operating system, middleware, runtime, container base images, and network services). It must state: the exact **USGCB/DoD STIG versions**, the target configuration items, and a clear **unused services/ports allow/deny list**.
2. Build the baseline requirements into **procurement**: in requests for quotation (RFQs)/tenders and contracts/SOWs (statements of work), require the developer to (a) implement the Baseline Pack, (b) turn off unused services/ports according to the deny list, and (c) deliver **reinstall/upgrade materials that match the baseline** (for example, hardened virtual machine/container images, infrastructure-as-code modules, configuration management profiles, and deployment pipeline templates).
3. Require **evidence that is ready for audit** at handover: a compliance assessment report aligned to STIG/USGCB (or equivalent configuration assessment output), plus a checklist that links each baseline requirement to the evidence. This must include clear proof that each denied service/port is not running/listening.
4. Require the developer to provide **repeatable build and deployment methods** so the organisation can apply the baseline as the default during reinstall/upgrade (for example, “golden” images and/or infrastructure-as-code modules that the organisation’s deployment pipeline uses by default).
5. Store the Baseline Pack, the audit evidence, and the delivered materials in a controlled repository, and link them to the system security plan and change records to prevent unauthorised changes from causing “configuration drift”.

###### WHO
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: updates RFQs/contracts/SOWs to include baseline deliverables and evidence requirements.
- **System architect / platform lead**: maintains the Baseline Pack and sets scope and versioning.
- **Developer/vendor engineering lead**: implements the baseline configurations and produces the acceptance evidence and reinstall/upgrade materials.
- **Security engineering / compliance assessor**: checks the acceptance evidence against the Baseline Pack.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The developer delivers a platform implementation that matches the Baseline Pack, including **USGCB/DoD STIG hardened settings**.
- Evidence is provided showing **unused services/ports are disabled** (per the deny list) and that the hardened configuration items are set as specified.
- The developer delivers **reinstall/upgrade materials** (images/infrastructure-as-code/configuration profiles) that, when used, reproduce the same baseline as the **default** configuration.
- All evidence and materials are stored with traceability to the Baseline Pack and the system security plan/change record.

###### Actions Required for Compliance
