- [ ] For each platform, define an official “Baseline Pack” that matches the exact United States Government Configuration Baseline (USGCB) and Department of Defense Security Technical Implementation Guide (DoD STIG) versions, and include a deny list of unused services and ports.
- [ ] Update requests for quotation (RFQs), statements of work (SOWs), and contracts to require developers to implement the Baseline Pack and to deliver reinstall and upgrade materials that match the baseline requirements.
- [ ] Specify the evidence required to accept the work, including a compliance report aligned to the Security Technical Implementation Guide (STIG) and United States Government Configuration Baseline (USGCB), plus a mapping checklist. This should also include proof that denied services and ports are not listening.
- [ ] Require delivery of repeatable “golden” artefacts (secure machine images and/or infrastructure-as-code (IaC) and configuration profiles) that the organisation’s deployment pipeline uses as the default.
- [ ] Store the baseline pack, acceptance evidence, and delivered artefacts in a controlled repository, with traceability to the system security plan.
- [ ] Check the acceptance evidence at handover and refuse the delivery if any required baseline item is missing or if any denied service or port is present.


---

#### SA-4.6 — Use of Information Assurance Products (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the tools used to protect sensitive, classified information are the correct, pre-approved ones, and that they have been properly checked before they are used. If an organisation uses the wrong protection tools, or uses them in the wrong situation (for example, when the communication network is not classified at a lower level than the information), the protection may not work and classified data could be exposed or handled incorrectly.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use only standard, off-the-shelf protection products that, together, form an approved solution for this specific “lower-to-higher classification” communication scenario. Before deploying that solution, the organisation must confirm that each product in it has been assessed or tested by the relevant authority, or through approved procedures.

The organisation must also keep an up-to-date list of what is in use, ensure that purchasing and contracts only select products that match the approved solution and have the required proof of assessment, record the details in its system planning documents, and assign clear responsibility for checking approvals and keeping records.

##### Enforce NSA-approved IA product eligibility via controlled catalogue

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an official, auditable register that links every deployed information assurance (IA) / IA-enabled commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) or government off-the-shelf (GOTS) product (including the exact supplier and version) to the specific National Security Agency (NSA)-approved key-management and cryptography coverage for the relevant lower-to-higher classification communication situation. Then make this register mandatory in deployment and change processes, so only products and versions that are explicitly listed and have been assessed and approved can be released.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The enhancement to security requirement SA-4(6) says that commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) IA or IA-enabled products used to protect classified information using cryptography may need to use NSA-approved key management. Therefore, the organisation must make sure the IA products it uses are NSA-approved for the relevant situation, and that each product and version is assessed or validated by the appropriate authority (or using NSA-approved processes) before it is deployed.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an official IA product and solution register** (the single source of truth) containing: product name, supplier, exact version/release, configuration profile, and the **specific lower-to-higher classification communication situation** it covers.
2. **Attach assessment and validation proof for each exact version**: record the assessment/validation reference identifier, what it covers (scope), the valid dates, and the approval or process basis that allows it to be used.
3. **Link to asset records and deployment pipelines**: automatically bring in the inventory of deployed software and firmware (software bill of materials, package versions, container image digests, and appliance firmware versions) and standardise identifiers so they match the register entries.
4. **Add “policy rules” checks in CI/CD and release/change management**: stop deployment or release if any IA / IA-enabled component is not listed in the register for the required situation, or if the exact version does not have linked assessment and validation proof.
5. **Treat changes as a re-assessment event**: when versions or configurations change (including key-management settings, cryptography library updates, or reconfiguration of IA components), automatically repeat the eligibility checks and require approval to proceed if the coverage or proof no longer matches.
6. **Produce audit-ready outputs**: create a report for each system release showing the IA components, how they map to the register, and the linked assessment and validation proof references.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Engineering Lead / IA Product Governance Owner**: maintains the official register and the proof mappings.
- **DevSecOps / Release Manager**: sets up and runs the deployment eligibility checks.
- **Configuration Management / CMDB Owner**: ensures the inventory and version information feeding the checks is accurate.
- **Authorising Official / Security Accreditation Authority (or delegated authority)**: approves exceptions only where NSA-approved processes allow.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every IA / IA-enabled cryptographic product deployed for the lower-to-higher classification communication situation, the system release report shows: (a) an explicit NSA-approved solution mapping for that situation, and (b) linked assessment and validation proof for the **exact** product version.
- Deployment pipelines block releases when an IA component/version is missing from the register or when proof is not linked.
- Change events that alter IA component versions or configurations trigger re-assessment and produce updated audit evidence.
- The register and proof references are version-controlled and can be reviewed by auditors.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the communication scenarios where information must be protected using NSA-approved key management, from lower to higher classification levels.
- [ ] Build and maintain an authoritative information architecture (IA) product and solution catalogue, including the exact vendor and version details and the mapping to specific scenarios.
- [ ] Link each catalogue entry to the evidence used for evaluation and validation, and record the validity dates and the approval or procedure basis.
- [ ] Link the inventory already deployed on devices (software bill of materials (SBOM), software inventory, and firmware versions) to the catalogue using standardised identifiers.
- [ ] Set up “policy-as-code” release checkpoints that stop deployments if the information assurance (IA) components or versions are not covered in the catalogue or there is no supporting evidence.
- [ ] Set up change-management workflows so that any update to an IA component’s version or configuration must be re-checked (re-validated) before it is released.
- [ ] For each release, produce audit evidence reports that link the deployed intelligent automation (IA) components to the National Security Agency (NSA)-approved coverage and evaluation records.


---

#### SA-4.7 — NIAP-approved Protection Profiles  (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the security tools and security-enabled technology you buy are genuinely trustworthy before you rely on them. Without this check, a company could unknowingly install products with weak or unproven protections, which could leave customer data, business systems, or financial processes exposed to theft, disruption, or fraud.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set clear purchasing rules so that any commercially provided security product is only chosen and used if it has been independently assessed and approved against the relevant National Information Assurance Partnership (NIAP) approved Protection Profile for its type of technology, where such an approved profile exists. If no NIAP-approved Protection Profile exists for that type of technology, then the organisation must only use the product if any built-in encryption used to enforce its security rules comes from a cryptographic module that is validated as Federal Information Processing Standards (FIPS)-approved or approved by the United States National Security Agency (NSA).

The organisation must build these checks into the procurement process, include the evidence needed in supplier requests and contracts, keep records of what was approved and the reasons for approval, maintain an up-to-date list of products in use, and assign named staff to verify compliance and ensure the correct evidence is kept.

##### Procurement assurance gate for NIAP PP or FIPS/NSA crypto validation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set up an auditable “procurement assurance gate” so that security-enabled commercial products are only bought and deployed when either:
(a) there is a National Information Assurance Partnership (NIAP)-approved protection profile for the relevant product technology type, and the supplier provides proof that the product was evaluated against that exact protection profile; or  
(b) when no NIAP protection profile exists, the product’s built-in encryption (used to enforce its security rules) is implemented using cryptographic modules that have been validated as Federal Information Processing Standards (FIPS)-approved, or approved by the United States National Security Agency (NSA).

###### WHY (control requirement)
Enhancement SA-4(7) requires that security-enabled technology supplied by commercial sources is only selected and used if it has been independently assessed against the relevant NIAP-approved protection profile for its technology type (where such a profile exists). Where no approved NIAP protection profile exists, the organisation must ensure that any built-in encryption enforcing the product’s security rules is based on FIPS-validated or NSA-approved cryptographic modules.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a Product Assurance decision rule** within procurement governance:
   - Identify the product’s **technology type**.
   - If an **NIAP-approved protection profile exists** for that technology type, require **supplier evidence** showing evaluation against that exact protection profile.
   - If **no NIAP-approved protection profile exists**, require **supplier evidence** that the product’s encryption enforcing its security rules uses **FIPS-validated or NSA-approved cryptographic modules**.
2. **Maintain a technology-type mapping catalogue** (technology type → applicable NIAP protection profile, if any). For unclear classifications, send the decision to a named Security Requirements Owner.
3. **Build the decision rule into the purchasing process** (for example, the enterprise resource planning (ERP) or procurement system workflow):
   - Add mandatory fields for technology type and evidence type.
   - Require suppliers to upload structured evidence in response to requests for information or quotations (for example, NIAP evaluation/validation details tied to the specific protection profile; or FIPS/NSA cryptographic module validation references).
   - **Stop purchase order (PO) creation** if evidence is missing, does not match the technology type, or cannot be verified.
4. **Link evidence to an official product/inventory record** and keep it for audit:
   - Store evidence in a controlled repository.
   - Record the product name, version, technology type, applicable NIAP protection profile (or a clear note that none exists), and the cryptographic module validation reference (FIPS/NSA) where applicable.
5. **Assign named roles and enforce ownership of the workflow**:
   - Security Requirements Owner (technology-type classification and rule application)
   - Evidence Verifier (checks and validates supplier evidence)
   - Procurement Compliance Approver (confirms the gate is completed before purchase)

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Requirements Owner**: classifies the technology type and decides whether an NIAP protection profile exists.
- **Evidence Verifier (Cybersecurity/Assurance)**: reviews and validates NIAP/FIPS/NSA evidence.
- **Procurement Compliance Approver**: authorises PO release only after the gate is completed.
- **Procurement Operations/Information Technology (IT)**: implements the workflow integration and captures evidence in the purchasing system.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every security-enabled commercial product purchased after implementation, the procurement workflow must record either:
  - verifiable evidence of NIAP evaluation against the applicable NIAP-approved protection profile for the product’s technology type, **or**
  - verifiable evidence that the product’s encryption enforcing its security policy uses FIPS-validated or NSA-approved cryptographic modules when no NIAP protection profile exists.
- PO creation is blocked when required evidence is missing or does not match.
- Evidence is kept and traceably linked to the official product/inventory record (including version and the specific NIAP protection profile or cryptographic module validation reference).
- Named roles are assigned and workflow ownership is enforced (no unauthorised bypass of the gate).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the decision rule for choosing between the NIAP Protection Profile and FIPS (Federal Information Processing Standards) or NSA (National Security Agency) cryptographic module evidence.
- [ ] Create and maintain a catalogue that maps each technology type to the National Information Assurance Partnership (NIAP) Protection Profile, with an escalation route for cases where the classification is unclear.
- [ ] Set up the procurement process so that users must upload supporting evidence, and so that purchase order (PO) creation is blocked if the evidence is missing or does not match.
- [ ] Set up an evidence repository, and link each approved product and version to its official inventory record, including the specific National Information Assurance Partnership (NIAP) profile or the Federal Information Processing Standards (FIPS) / National Security Agency (NSA) module reference.
- [ ] Assign named responsibilities (Security Requirements Owner, Evidence Verifier, Procurement Compliance Approver) and enforce the approval workflow so it cannot be skipped or bypassed.
- [ ] Run a trial on a defined set of product categories that include security features, and confirm that audit records can be traced end to end.


---

#### SA-4.8 — Continuous Monitoring Plan for Controls (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that when a supplier builds a system or service, they also explain how they will keep its protective measures working properly over time. Without this, changes to the system, new threats, or gradual weaknesses could cause the protections to stop working, leaving the organisation exposed to unauthorised access, data loss, or service disruption.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the developer to provide a continuous monitoring plan that fits with the organisation’s own approach to ongoing checks. The plan should clearly set out what will be monitored and how often, how the developer will decide whether the protections are still effective as things change, and what actions they will take if any protection fails or becomes ineffective, including how issues will be managed and escalated back to the organisation.

##### Developer continuous monitoring plan deliverable mapped to programme

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Require every system/component/service developer (including third-party suppliers) to produce an auditable **Continuous Monitoring Plan for Control Effectiveness** that is **consistent with the organisation’s continuous monitoring programme**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-4(8)** requires the developer to produce a plan for **continuously checking whether controls are still working** that is **consistent with** the organisation’s continuous monitoring programme. The plan must be detailed enough for the organisation to use as part of its ongoing monitoring.

###### HOW (specific approach)
Make this a **mandatory procurement and delivery requirement** using a standard plan template and clear checks on what must be included.

1. **Add a contractual deliverable** to procurement documents (SOW/contract/SLA):
   - Deliverable name: “Developer Continuous Monitoring Plan for Control Effectiveness”.
   - Submission timing: before acceptance/onboarding; updates at every material change (for example, a major release, a configuration change, or an updated threat model) and at least **annually**.

2. **Require plan sections** that allow the organisation to incorporate the plan into its programme, including:
   - **Control coverage mapping**: show how each monitored control (or group of controls) maps to the organisation’s continuous monitoring programme identifiers and the expected monitoring approach.
   - **Monitoring activity catalogue**: list the types of checks the developer will carry out to assess controls (for example, configuration compliance checks, vulnerability/patch verification, log/telemetry integrity checks, and access-control effectiveness checks).
   - **Frequency matrix**: how often each activity will be performed (and any event-based triggers).
   - **Effectiveness criteria**: how the developer decides whether a control remains effective over time.
   - **Failure/remediation actions**: what will happen if controls fail or stop being effective, including responsibilities, timeframes, and how issues are escalated and reported back to the organisation.
   - **Evidence artefacts**: what proof will be produced (for example, monitoring reports, compliance outputs, exception logs) and how it will be shared (format, timing, and method of access).

3. **Ensure consistency with the organisation programme** by requiring a **mapping and alignment statement**:
   - The developer must explain how their monitoring timing, types of checks, and reporting/escalation approach align with (and do not conflict with) the organisation’s continuous monitoring programme.

4. **Make auditability practical**:
   - During onboarding, create a checklist that links each plan item to the organisation’s monitoring processes (for example, ticketing/escalation routes and how often evidence is received).
   - Require suppliers to provide evidence artefacts on the same schedule as the plan’s monitoring frequency.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Procurement / Contracting lead**: updates templates and ensures the deliverable is mandatory.
- **Security governance lead (GRC)**: maintains the plan template, mapping requirements, and review criteria.
- **System/application owner or security architect**: checks the mapping from controls to the programme and the escalation routes.
- **Supplier/developer security lead**: produces and maintains the plan and evidence artefacts.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A signed/approved **Continuous Monitoring Plan** exists for the system/component/service.
- The plan includes **control coverage mapping** to the organisation’s continuous monitoring programme and an explicit **alignment statement**.
- The plan specifies **monitoring activity types**, **frequency (including triggers)**, and **effectiveness criteria**.
- The plan defines **failure/remediation actions** with **responsibilities, timeframes, and escalation/reporting** to the organisation.
- The plan identifies **evidence artefacts** and how they will be provided so the organisation can incorporate them into its continuous monitoring activities.
- The plan is updated at least **annually** and whenever there are **material changes**, as required by the contract.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update procurement templates to require developers to provide a “Continuous Monitoring Plan for Control Effectiveness” deliverable.
- [ ] Create and publish a standard plan template that requires: a link between each control and the relevant programme, the types of monitoring activities, a schedule showing how often each activity is carried out, and clear criteria for judging whether the monitoring is effective.
- [ ] Add contract clauses requiring the plan to be updated when there is a significant change, and at least once every year.
- [ ] Create and apply a review checklist to confirm that work matches the organisation’s continuous monitoring programme and that the correct escalation routes are followed.
- [ ] Require suppliers to provide evidence documents (evidence artefacts) and to state how often and by what method they will share that evidence with the organisation.
- [ ] Create an onboarding integration checklist that connects supplier evidence to our internal monitoring activities and to our ticketing and escalation processes.


---

#### SA-4.9 — Functions, Ports, Protocols, and Services in Use (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building or supplying a system clearly explain what it is meant to do and how it connects to other services. This helps the organisation know exactly what it is buying and running. Without clear explanations, the system could include hidden or unnecessary features, connect to the wrong communication entry points, use the wrong agreed data-sharing rules, or provide capabilities the business never intended—leading to avoidable risk of misuse, disruption, or compliance problems.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the developer to document, in plain language, the system’s intended functions, the network entry points (ports), the communication rules (protocols), and the services or capabilities intended for organisational use. This should also include any external services the system relies on.

The organisation should also check that what is described in the system documentation and procurement documents matches what was agreed in contracts, requirements, and service agreements, with no contradictions unless the organisation has explicitly approved any differences.

##### Developer “Intended Use” pack for functions, ports, protocols, services

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT must be done
Require the system/component/service developer to identify and document the **functions**, **communication ports**, **communication methods (protocols)**, and **services** intended for organisational use, including any **external services** the system relies on.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-4(9)** (Enhancement) requires: *“Require the developer … to identify the functions, ports, protocols, and services intended for organizational use.”* The guidance stresses doing this **early in the software development life cycle (SDLC)** (requirements/design) to avoid risky or unnecessary connections and to prevent expensive changes later.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Create a standard, organisation-controlled **“Intended Use” pack** that every developer must complete at the **requirements definition/design stage** (and update at each release). The pack must include a **mapping matrix** that links each intended function to the service(s) that provide it, the **protocol(s)** used, and the **port(s)/endpoints** that are exposed or used.

**Minimum pack contents (must be auditable and unambiguous):**
1. **Functions (intended for organisational use):** plain descriptions of what the component/service does.
2. **Services (intended for organisational use):** named capabilities offered or used (for example: web application service, application programming interface (API) service, messaging integration, supplier remote support channel).
3. **Protocols:** explicit protocol names (for example: HTTPS, SFTP, LDAP, OPC UA, MQTT) mapped to the relevant function/service.
4. **Ports / endpoints:** port numbers and endpoint identifiers (for example: fully qualified domain name (FQDN) plus port, IP range if applicable, service name in a container/orchestration context) mapped to the relevant protocol/function/service.
5. **External services:** identify any third-party/external services used for organisational purposes and list the intended interfaces (functions/services/protocols/ports).
6. **Environment scope:** state which environment(s) the intended connectivity applies to (for example: production versus test) and whether connectivity differs by environment.
7. **Change linkage:** version the pack and link it to the change record/release so the organisation can track what was intended for each release.

**Governance and verification approach:**
- Make completion of the pack a **procurement and delivery acceptance requirement** (for example: included in statement of work (SOW)/contract deliverables and release gates).
- Add **automated completeness checks** in the delivery pipeline (for example: no blank mappings; every protocol maps to a function/service; every service lists its ports/endpoints; external services are explicitly identified).
- Where feasible, do a **consistency review** between the pack and available supporting materials (for example: service manifests, application programming interface (API) gateway configuration, firewall/security group rules) to reduce mismatch over time, while keeping the SA-4(9) evidence focused on the developer’s intended-use identification.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application/Service Owner:** ensures the pack is required in procurement and release gates.
- **Developer/System Integrator:** produces and maintains the “Intended Use” pack during requirements/design and updates it for releases.
- **Security Architect / Security Engineering:** reviews the pack for completeness, alignment with risk, and correctness of the mappings.
- **Procurement/Commercial Lead:** includes the deliverable requirements in contracts/SOWs.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each system/component/service release, the organisation holds a versioned **“Intended Use” pack** completed by the developer.
- The pack includes a **complete mapping matrix** covering **functions → services → protocols → ports/endpoints**, with no protocols/services left unmapped.
- Any **external/third-party services** used for organisational purposes are explicitly identified with their intended interfaces.
- The pack is linked to the relevant **change record/release** and is available for audit.
- Automated checks confirm structural completeness (for example: mapping relationships are consistent), and the security review confirms the documentation is clear, unambiguous, and matches the intended organisational use.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an organisation-controlled “Intended Use” template with sections for **Functions**, **Services**, **Protocols**, **Ports/Endpoints**, and a **required mapping matrix**.
- [ ] Update the procurement documents and statement of work (SOW), and the release acceptance criteria, to require the developer to submit the versioned “Intended Use” pack at the requirements and design stage and for every release.
- [ ] Set up automated checks to confirm the information is complete and correctly linked—for example, every protocol is connected to a function or service, and every service lists its ports and endpoints.
- [ ] Set up a security architecture review process to confirm that all system mappings are clear and that any external or third-party services are clearly identified.
- [ ] Store the completed packs in a controlled repository and link each pack version to the relevant change record or release so it can be traced for audit purposes.


---

#### SA-4.10 — Use of Approved PIV Products (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that any identity-checking technology the organisation uses to verify staff and contractors is only purchased and installed from an officially approved list. It helps prevent the use of untested or unsuitable products that could weaken identity checks, making it easier for unauthorised people to gain access, impersonate employees, or disrupt services.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use a clear purchasing and contracting approach that requires products related to personal identity verification (PIV) to be chosen only from the approved list. It must include this rule in requests for quotations, purchasing documents, contracts, and service agreements. Before any product is put into live use, the organisation should confirm that the exact product matches the approved list, keep a record of the evidence, and ensure the system’s security documentation states this rule and names who is responsible for enforcing it.

##### Approved PIV product gate for procurement and deployment

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an organisation-wide “approved PIV product gate” so that any information technology product used to provide PIV capability (for example, PIV readers, middleware, authentication and issuance components, and PIV-related software) can only be bought and put into service if it is explicitly listed on the organisation’s FIPS 201-approved products list.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 SA-4(10) (enhancement) requires using only IT products from the FIPS 201-approved products list for the PIV capability implemented in organisational systems. This helps prevent unapproved or untested products from weakening identity checks used for multi-factor authentication.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Create and keep the approved list up to date** as the single source of truth for PIV-capable IT products. At a minimum, include: supplier (vendor), exact product name, model, and version/firmware where applicable, plus the list version/date and a record of changes.
2. **Use a standard product identifier format** consistently across buying, change activity, and acceptance (for example: supplier + exact product name + model + version/firmware).
3. **Apply a pre-deployment approval gate** in the buying and change process:
   - Require requests for PIV-related items to include the standard product identifiers.
   - Check the submitted identifiers against the current approved list.
   - Stop ordering and deployment if there is no exact match.
   - Send items that do not match to an exception process that requires named approvers and documented reasons.
4. **Include this requirement in buying documents**:
   - Update security requirements and acquisition policy/procedures so that tender wording, purchase documents, contracts, and service level agreements require suppliers to provide only PIV products from the approved list, and to provide product identifiers for acceptance.
5. **Automatically produce audit-ready proof at approval time**:
   - Record the approved-list version used, whether it was an exact match or not, the approver’s identity, and the related buying/change reference.
6. **Check what is actually in use**:
   - Run periodic checks of deployed PIV-related inventory (software versions and hardware model/firmware where available) against the approved list to spot any differences caused by manual or emergency installations.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Information Security / IAM Security Owner**: owns the approved list and defines the identifier format.
- **Procurement / Contracting Lead**: updates procurement templates, tender wording, and contract clauses.
- **Change Management / Release Manager**: ensures the gate is built into change and release processes.
- **System/Application Owners**: provide inventory details and ensure only gated items are deployed.
- **IT Asset Management**: supports reconciliation and collects evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- All PIV-related purchasing requests include the standard product identifiers and are checked against the approved list.
- No PIV-related product is ordered or deployed without an approved-list match (or a formally approved exception with recorded justification).
- For every approved item, there is evidence showing: the approved-list version, match result, approver identity, and the link to the buying/change records.
- Periodic reconciliation reports show no unapproved PIV-related products in live environments (or any exceptions are documented and approved).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up and manage the official list of Federal Information Processing Standards (FIPS) 201-approved products that can be used for all information technology components that support Personal Identity Verification (PIV) capability.
- [ ] Define a standard “product ID” format (supplier name + exact product name + model + version/firmware where relevant) and share it with the procurement and change-management teams.
- [ ] Add an approved-list check to the procurement and change process to stop requests for non-matching PIV products from moving forward.
- [ ] Update the wording in requests for proposals, purchasing documents, contracts, and service level agreements (SLAs) so that suppliers must provide only approved-list personal identity verification (PIV) products and the product identifiers for acceptance.
- [ ] Set up an exception process for items that are not approved. Each exception must have a named approver and a documented reason. Record every exception against the relevant change or procurement ticket.
- [ ] Set up automatic evidence collection for every approval, including the approved list version, the match result, who approved it, and the linked ticket.
- [ ] Carry out regular checks to compare the deployed personal identity verification (PIV) inventory against the approved list, and fix any differences or record why they exist.


---

#### SA-4.11 — System of Records (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that when the organisation hires an external supplier to run a system that holds people’s personal records on its behalf, the supplier follows the same legal rules as the organisation for how those records are used, who is allowed to see them, how access is tracked, and how the records are protected. Without this, personal information could be used for the wrong purposes, seen by the wrong people, not properly recorded, or left insufficiently protected—creating real risks of privacy breaches and loss of trust.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must include these specific Privacy Act requirements in the contract. This includes the required privacy notice, the permitted routine uses, the rules for access and the expectations for recording access, and the safeguarding measures. The organisation must also put in place a clear way to check that the supplier is actually meeting the contract requirements, and require the supplier to provide evidence showing how it has built these requirements into day-to-day operations.

##### Contract clause pack for Privacy Act system-of-records operation

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
When the organisation hires a contractor to run a system of records on its behalf, the contract must clearly state the organisation’s **Privacy Act requirements** for that system of records. This includes the system-of-records notice, what “routine uses” are allowed, how access and accounting must be handled, and the safeguarding requirements.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) **SA-4(11)** enhancement requires: *“Include {{ insert: param, sa-04.11_odp }} in the acquisition contract for the operation of a system of records on behalf of an organization to accomplish an organizational mission or function.”* The guidance also requires that, in line with the organisation’s authority, the organisation ensures the **Privacy Act** requirements are applied to the system of records operated by the contractor.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. Create a reusable **Privacy Act system-of-records clause pack** that can be inserted into procurement contracts. At minimum, it must cover the four areas covered by **sa-04.11_odp**: **applying the system-of-records notice**, **enforcing permitted routine uses**, **access and accounting**, and **safeguarding**.
2. For each system of records, keep a **requirements traceability matrix** that links each clause pack section to: (a) the exact system-of-records notice identifier/version, (b) clear, measurable contractor duties (for example, expectations for access logging and retention, how often audits/reports are produced, and required safeguarding controls), and (c) the evidence the organisation must receive.
3. During procurement, require contracting teams to insert the clause pack into the solicitation/contract and to name the **exact** system-of-records notice identifier/version and the traceability matrix as part of the contract (as a contract exhibit). Avoid vague wording such as “as applicable”.
4. Add contract acceptance criteria that require the contractor to show—before go-live and during day-to-day operation—that the notice, routine uses restrictions, access/accounting, and safeguarding requirements are being followed in practice, not only recorded on paper.
5. Set up a contract assurance schedule (for example, risk-based quarterly reviews). The organisation should request and review the evidence artefacts listed in the traceability matrix, including samples of access-accounting outputs and proof of safeguarding controls.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Contracting Officer / Procurement Lead**: ensures the clause pack and contract exhibits are included in the solicitation and final contract.
- **Privacy Officer / Data Protection Lead**: provides the correct system-of-records notice identifier/version and approves the clause pack content.
- **Information Security Lead**: checks that access/accounting and safeguarding duties are measurable and can be audited.
- **Contract Manager / Vendor Assurance Lead**: reviews evidence and tracks compliance against the contract exhibits.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Every contractor-operated system of records contract includes the **sa-04.11_odp** Privacy Act requirements through the clause pack and references the correct system-of-records notice identifier/version.
- The contract sets out clear duties for **routine uses restrictions**, **access/accounting**, and **safeguarding**, including the required evidence artefacts and the review/assurance schedule.
- Before go-live (and at least during the first operational review cycle), the contractor provides evidence showing the requirements are applied in practice (for example, access-accounting outputs and safeguarding control evidence). The organisation records acceptance against the traceability matrix.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a Privacy Act system-of-records clause pack that covers: how notice is given, the permitted routine uses, how people can access and how records are accounted for, and how information is safeguarded.
- [ ] Create and keep up to date a per-system traceability matrix that links each clause-pack section to the specific system-of-records notice identifier and version, and to the supporting evidence documents.
- [ ] Update procurement templates so that requests for proposals and contracts explicitly include the sa-04.11_odp requirements and reference the traceability matrix as an exhibit.
- [ ] Define contract acceptance criteria that require go-live and regular evidence showing the requirements are being followed in day-to-day operations.
- [ ] Set up a risk-based schedule to regularly review contractors’ proof of compliance, including samples of access and account records, evidence of safeguarding controls, and evidence that routine-use enforcement is being followed.


---

#### SA-4.12 — Data Ownership (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that when the organisation uses another company to handle its information, the information remains clearly the organisation’s responsibility and is fully dealt with when the work ends. Without this, a contractor could keep copies of important business records, customer or employee details, or other sensitive information after the contract finishes. That could create legal, financial, and reputational risk if the data is misused or accidentally exposed.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must include clear instructions in every relevant contract about who owns the data and what must happen when the work ends. The contract must state that the contractor must remove all organisation-owned data from its systems and either return it or securely dispose of it exactly as the contract requires. This must be completed within 30 days of the contract ending, or within 30 days of the organisation deciding the data is no longer needed—whichever comes first. This applies to personal information about identifiable people as well.

The organisation must also check that the contractor can provide proof that the data was removed and returned or disposed of on time.

##### Contractual data removal/return within 30 days with evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Update procurement and contracting so that **every contractor** who handles or processes **organisation-owned data** must, in their contract, **remove that data from their systems** and **either return it or securely dispose of it** within the **time period stated in the contract**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-4(12) (Enhancement) requires that contractors who run systems that contain organisation-owned data have **policies and procedures** to **remove the data** from their systems and/or **return it** within a **time period defined by the contract**. The organisation sets this as **within 30 days of contract termination or when the data is no longer required**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a standard contract schedule** titled “Organisation Data Ownership & Disposition (SA-4(12))” and require it for all relevant purchases (including managed services, software-as-a-service (SaaS)/platform-as-a-service (PaaS), and operational technology (OT)-adjacent vendor support where the contractor’s systems hold organisation data).
2. In the schedule, set out:
   - **Data ownership**: the organisation owns all organisation-owned data, including derived copies, exports, backups, logs, and staging materials held by the contractor.
   - **30-day dual-trigger timeframe**: the contractor must complete data removal/return/disposal **within 30 days of (a) contract termination or (b) when the data is no longer required, whichever happens first**.
   - **Required actions**: the contractor must **remove the data from their systems** and **either return it or securely dispose of it**, as stated in the contract.
   - **Evidence requirements**: the contractor must provide verifiable proof (for example, a signed completion statement, return receipt, or disposal certificate) and list the systems/environments covered.
   - **Backups/disaster recovery scope**: require the same data removal/return/disposal to include contractor-held backups and disaster recovery storage where the contractor controls them.
3. **Add procurement approval checkpoints** so contracts cannot be signed unless the SA-4(12) schedule is included, and the **30-day dual-trigger** and **evidence requirements** are clearly stated with no ambiguity.
4. **Operationalise the “data no longer required” trigger** by requiring the organisation to send a written disposition notice (for example, a ticket or email) that records the trigger date; the contractor must confirm receipt and the disposal/return deadline.
5. **Enforce at contract close-out**: contract management must request the required evidence within the deadline window and record acceptance (evidence received, systems listed, and dates matching the 30-day requirement).

###### WHO
- **Procurement / Contracting Lead**: maintains the standard contract schedule and the approval checkpoint.
- **Information Security / Data Protection Officer**: reviews the clause wording for scope (including personal data (PII) where applicable) and whether the evidence is sufficient.
- **Contract Manager / Vendor Manager**: issues the “data no longer required” notice, tracks deadlines, and checks evidence at close-out.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For a sample of active and recently ended contractor engagements, the contract includes the SA-4(12) schedule with:
  - clear statement of organisation data ownership,
  - an obligation to remove data from contractor systems and return/dispose it,
  - the **30-day dual-trigger** timeframe (termination or data no longer required, whichever comes first), and
  - clear, verifiable evidence requirements.
- For at least one ended engagement, evidence provided by the contractor is received and shows that disposition happened within the required timeframe and covers the contractor-controlled systems/environments listed in the contract.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Draft and get approval for a standard “Organisation Data Ownership & Disposition (SA-4(12))” contract schedule, including the 30-day period that starts when either trigger occurs.
- [ ] Update the procurement approval checkpoints so that a contract cannot be signed unless the SA-4(12) schedule is attached and the required evidence is included.
- [ ] Define and publish the organisation’s process for issuing a “data no longer required” notice, including how the start date for the process is recorded.
- [ ] Train contract managers to issue the final decision notices, track the 30-day deadline, and request supporting evidence from the contractor at the end of the contract.
- [ ] Create an evidence acceptance checklist (return receipt, disposal certificate, and completion statement) and record that the evidence was accepted by the contract deadline.


---

### SA-5 — System Documentation (Control)

This expectation is about making sure there are clear, up-to-date instructions for how a system is set up, run, and used safely. These instructions should explain what users and administrators must do, and what risks to watch out for. Without them, people may configure or operate the system incorrectly, security and privacy protections may be used the wrong way or not kept up to date, and known weaknesses in powerful administrative functions may be overlooked—leading to avoidable incidents and privacy harm.

The organisation must create or obtain administrator and user documentation for every system, component, and service. This must cover secure configuration, installation, and day-to-day operation (including how to restart safely), how to use and maintain security and privacy features, and the known security weaknesses linked to privileged configuration and use. If the documentation cannot be found, the organisation must record who was contacted, when, and what response was received, then recreate the missing material and record the request details. Finally, it must share the documentation with the system owner, the system security officer, and system administrators.

##### Maintain complete admin/user system docs with recreate workflow

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create and keep up to date complete **administrator** and **user** documentation for every system, component, and service. This must include secure instructions for configuration, installation, and day-to-day operation (including how to securely start again and resume securely after a break in service), guidance on how to use and maintain security and privacy features effectively, and a record of known vulnerabilities linked to privileged or administrative configuration and use. If documentation is not available or does not exist, record the documented attempts to obtain it (dates, who was contacted, and results) and then recreate the missing documentation.

###### WHY (control requirement)
This requirement (SA-5) means that any system documentation created or obtained by the developer must help organisational staff understand how the controls are implemented and how they operate. It also requires the organisation to consider how to check that documentation is high quality and complete. If documentation cannot be obtained, the organisation must record the attempts to request it and recreate the essential documentation. Documentation must be protected according to the system’s security category or classification.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Set documentation scope and responsibility**: For each system, component, or service, create a documentation record in the controlled repository and assign the **system owner**, **system security officer**, and **system administrators** as the people who should receive it.
2. **Use standard templates with required sections**: Use administrator and user documentation templates that, at minimum, cover secure configuration, secure installation, secure operation (including “initial start” and “resume secure operation after lapse”), how to use and maintain security and privacy features, and a section that clearly covers known vulnerabilities or risks linked to privileged or administrative functions and privileged configuration/use.
3. **Keep documentation current through scheduled reviews**: Add a “last reviewed” field and trigger a review when there are material changes (for example, updates to the configuration baseline, changes to the privilege model, changes to security or privacy controls, or new software releases). Keep evidence that the documentation was updated to match what is currently in place.
4. **Automate checks that documentation is complete**: Add automated checks that confirm the required sections exist, are not blank, and correctly reference the right system identifiers and security/privacy features. Do not allow documentation to be published or released if required content is missing.
5. **Use a documented “obtain or recreate” process**: If vendor or developer documentation is unavailable or does not exist, log the request attempts with **dates, contacts, and results**. Then create a plan to recreate it, produce the missing content using configuration baselines, operational runbooks, and security advisories, and record that the recreation was completed and by whom.
6. **Protect documentation based on classification**: Store and share documentation in a controlled repository with access controls that match the system’s security category or classification. Give extra protection to documentation that contains information relevant to vulnerabilities.

###### WHO
- **System owner**: responsible for ensuring the documentation is complete for the system and approving updates.
- **System security officer**: reviews guidance on security and privacy features and the content about vulnerabilities linked to privileged functions.
- **System administrators**: keep operational details accurate (including secure start and secure resume after a lapse) and ensure runbooks match the documentation.
- **Documentation/engineering lead (or platform team)**: maintains the templates, the automated pipeline checks, and the obtain-or-recreate process.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each system/component/service, there is an **administrator** and **user** documentation set stored in the controlled repository.
- Administrator documentation includes secure configuration, secure installation, and secure operation, including **secure start** and **secure resume after lapse**.
- Documentation includes guidance on effective **security and privacy feature use and maintenance**, and a section describing **known vulnerabilities/risks** linked to privileged/administrative configuration and use.
- Where vendor/developer documentation was unavailable or nonexistent, the organisation keeps an auditable record of **request attempts** (dates, contacts, results) and evidence that the missing documentation was **recreated**.
- Access controls for documentation reflect the system’s security category/classification, with increased protection for documentation that is relevant to vulnerabilities.
- Automated completeness checks pass, and there is “last reviewed” evidence for each documentation set.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create administrator and user documentation templates that include the required sections for secure setup, secure installation, and secure day-to-day operation (including how to securely restart and resume after a lapse).
- [ ] For each system, component, or service, assign named roles responsible for approving and distributing the documentation: the system owner, the system security officer, and the system administrators.
- [ ] Set up an “get it or recreate it” process that records each request attempt, including the date, the contact involved, and the outcome, when the required documentation is missing or does not exist.
- [ ] Recreate any missing documentation using the latest configuration baseline and current operating runbooks, and record who recreated it and the date it was completed.
- [ ] Set a regular schedule for reviewing documentation whenever there are significant security, privacy, or privileged-function changes, and keep a record showing that the updates were made.
- [ ] Store and share documentation in a controlled repository, using access permissions that match the system’s security level or classification, with extra protection for content that relates to known or potential vulnerabilities.


---

### SA-8 — Security and Privacy Engineering Principles (Control)

This requirement is about building and changing systems in a way that protects people’s privacy and reduces the risk of harm. It means planning for safety from the start and checking it at every stage. Without this, organisations may accidentally collect more personal information than they need, use it for the wrong purposes, or create weaknesses that attackers could exploit—leading to service disruption, financial loss, or privacy problems for individuals.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set clear security and privacy engineering principles and apply them when specifying, designing, building, deploying, and changing systems, including upgrades where feasible. It must use multiple layers of protection, consider likely threats before building, set clear limits on what the system can and cannot access, use safe default settings, and apply safeguards when buying or changing third-party components.

The organisation must also build privacy in from the beginning by collecting only what is necessary (data minimisation), using data only for the stated purpose (purpose limits), providing appropriate consent or notice, carrying out privacy impact assessments, and handling personal data securely. Developers must be trained to follow these principles and apply them to every change. The organisation must keep evidence in documented plans, assessments, and design records.

##### SDLC guardrails for security & privacy-by-design artefacts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set organisation-wide security and privacy engineering principles for systems, and make sure they are followed during **specification, design, development, implementation, and modification** (including upgrades/modifications where feasible). Keep audit-ready records showing the principles were applied, including **threat modelling** and **privacy-by-design** activities.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8** requires applying the organisation-defined security and privacy engineering principles when specifying, designing, developing, implementing, and modifying the system and its components (see **param: sa-8_prm_1**). The guidance also expects measures such as layered protections, a secure software development life cycle (SDLC), clear separation of system boundaries, secure-by-default settings, developer training, and threat modelling.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Set up a repeatable SDLC process that is enforced through tools, so teams must complete and link the required security/privacy engineering records for every change:
1. **Principles written into templates**: Turn **sa-08_odp.01** (layered protections, secure SDLC, threat modelling, boundary separation, secure-by-default, supply-chain risk controls) and **sa-08_odp.02** (privacy-by-design, data minimisation, limiting use to the stated purpose, consent/notice where applicable, privacy impact assessments, secure handling of personal data) into engineering templates and checklists that every project must use.
2. **Threat modelling check**: For any new feature, new data flow, or any modification that changes security risk, require a threat model record (use case, threat actors, attack methods/patterns, and compensating controls) and link it to the design and backlog items.
3. **Privacy-by-design check**: For any processing of personal data, or any change to purpose, retention, or access, require a privacy impact assessment (PIA) or an equivalent privacy risk assessment. This must include decisions on data minimisation and limiting use to the stated purpose, and link the outcomes to design controls (for example, access controls, encryption, retention, and consent/notice where applicable).
4. **Boundary and secure-by-default checks**: Require architecture/design documentation that clearly defines logical and physical security boundaries, and sets secure-by-default configuration baselines for the component (for example, default authentication and authorisation settings, encryption requirements, and logging/redaction rules for personal data).
5. **Supply-chain risk controls**: For changes to third-party libraries, containers, or similar items, require capturing provenance and a software bill of materials (SBOM), and review dependency risks in line with the organisation’s supply-chain risk controls. Keep evidence.
6. **Developer training evidence**: Make sure developers involved in changes complete role-based training on the written principles, and record and reference the training completion in the change package.
7. **Change package evidence**: For each change, create one “security & privacy engineering evidence pack” (links to design records, threat model link, PIA link where applicable, boundary/secure-default statements, supply-chain review evidence, and training evidence). Store it in the system’s configuration management/release repository.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Engineering Lead**: owns the templates for the principles, and the threat modelling and privacy-by-design check criteria.
- **Privacy Officer / Data Protection Lead**: confirms when PIAs are required and validates privacy-by-design outcomes.
- **Engineering Manager / DevOps Lead**: implements the SDLC process checks in the continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) and change management toolchain.
- **Developers**: complete the required records for each change.
- **Configuration Management / Release Manager**: ensures evidence packs are kept and version-controlled.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For a representative sample of recent changes (including at least one modification involving new/changed data flows and one involving personal data), the change package includes: (a) the linked threat model record where required, (b) the linked PIA/privacy risk assessment where personal data/purpose/retention/access changed, (c) documented security boundary separation and secure-by-default configuration statements, (d) supply-chain risk evidence for third-party component changes, and (e) developer training evidence.
- The SDLC process blocks merge and deployment when required records are missing (except for documented exceptions where the risk has been accepted).
- Evidence packs are kept and can be traced to the specific system component version/release.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Turn the organisation’s security and privacy engineering principles into practical engineering templates and checklists that every change team must use
- [ ] Set up software development lifecycle (SDLC) approval checkpoints so that, for any new or changed data flows and any security-relevant features, teams must record a threat model before work can proceed.
- [ ] Set up software development lifecycle (SDLC) approval checkpoints so that privacy impact assessments (PIAs) are required whenever the purpose, type (category), retention period, or access to personal data changes.
- [ ] Create and enforce architecture and design rules that clearly separate logical and physical boundaries, and set secure-by-default configuration baselines.
- [ ] Include evidence of supply-chain risk in the change request for updates to third-party components, such as a software bill of materials (SBOM) and provenance information, plus a review of dependency risks.
- [ ] Record when developers complete required training, and make sure this training is referenced in every change’s security and privacy engineering evidence pack.
- [ ] Set up evidence pack retention in configuration management so that all supporting records can be traced back to the specific component versions and releases.


---

#### SA-8.1 — Clear Abstractions (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure systems have simple, clearly defined ways for different parts to work together, so everyone understands exactly what each part does with information. Without this, people may accidentally use the system in the wrong way, internal details could unintentionally affect how other parts behave, and unclear or duplicated ways of doing the same task could lead to mistakes that expose data or harm privacy.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must create and use written procedures that explain how to apply clear, well-defined interfaces and functions when specifying, designing, building, implementing, and changing systems and services. It must record the design decisions, include this approach in security and privacy requirements, architecture, and plans, and keep evidence showing that the procedures were followed.

Named roles must be assigned to set these requirements, apply the procedures, ensure developers follow them, and oversee that they are applied correctly. The interfaces must be necessary, sufficient, consistent, easy to understand, precise, and straightforward to review and test.

##### Contract-driven interfaces with information hiding and contract tests

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT must be done
Design and implement component and service interfaces using clear, well-defined rules for what they do. Use consistent ways of handling data, and hide internal details so other parts of the system cannot depend on how things are built internally (representation independence). Provide an auditable set of interface agreements (contracts) and automated checks (conformance tests) so interfaces are easy to review, inspect, and test, and so callers cannot rely on internal workings.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(1) Clear Abstractions** requires applying the security principle of clear abstractions. The guidance highlights:
- **Simple, well-defined interfaces and functions** with **precise definitions of what they do**
- Consistent, easy-to-understand data handling and how data is managed
- Easier analysis, review, and testing
- Avoiding **redundant or unused interfaces**
- Avoiding **semantic overloading** (using the same interface or parameters to mean different things)
- **Information hiding / representation-independent programming**, so internal details are not visible to other components that call the interface

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain a contract catalogue** (authoritative list) that records every interface that can be called from outside, such as REST/GraphQL endpoints, remote procedure call (RPC) methods, message topics, software development kit (SDK) calls, and internal module application programming interfaces (APIs). Each entry must point to the relevant contract document and name an owner.
2. **Define interface contracts using standard schema and interface description tools** (for example, OpenAPI/AsyncAPI, protocol buffers (protobuf) and remote procedure call (gRPC) interface description language (IDL), GraphQL schema, and internal IDLs). Contracts must include:
   - Input and output formats (schemas) and data types
   - A clear error model (error codes, whether requests can be retried, and rules for whether actions can be safely repeated)
   - Security meaning at the interface boundary (for example, what authorisation claims or roles are required)
   - Behaviour rules and state changes where relevant
3. **Implement information hiding** by ensuring callers use only stable data objects and agreed views (data transfer objects (DTOs) and view models) and agreed, standard representations:
   - Do not expose persistence entities, internal database identifiers, internal state fields, or storage-specific structures
   - Ensure internal changes (such as schema refactoring) do not change the meaning of the published contract
4. **Remove redundant or semantically overloaded interfaces**:
   - Find duplicate interfaces that provide the same capability and deprecate one using a controlled migration plan
   - Enforce clear parameter naming and schema validation so parameters do not carry unclear or overloaded meanings
5. **Add automated contract and conformance testing**:
   - Generate contract tests from the interface definitions
   - Run these tests in continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) for every build and whenever contracts change
   - Include negative tests for invalid inputs and for unauthorised access attempts to confirm secure behaviour at the interface boundary
6. **Record design decisions and evidence**:
   - Store contract documents and test results as part of the system’s design and security records (for example, security architecture and design records) so auditors can trace interface definitions to the implemented behaviour.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application/Software Architect**: owns the contract catalogue structure, interface standards, and information-hiding design patterns.
- **Software Engineering Leads**: ensure developers implement interfaces according to the contracts and remove redundant or semantically overloaded interfaces.
- **Security Engineering / Application Security (AppSec)**: reviews security meaning in contracts and approves contract test coverage for secure behaviour.
- **Quality Assurance (QA) / Test Engineering**: ensures contract tests are built into CI/CD and kept up to date.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Every externally callable interface has an associated, versioned contract document in the contract catalogue.
- Interface contracts specify precise functional behaviour, including schemas, error behaviour, rules for safe repetition and retrying, and security meaning.
- Callers cannot see internal representations (no persistence entities, internal identifiers, or internal state fields are exposed through published interfaces).
- Redundant or unused interfaces are identified and either removed or formally deprecated with migration evidence.
- Contract and conformance tests exist for each interface, run automatically in CI/CD, and their results are retained as evidence.
- Changes to internal implementations do not change the published contract meaning, as validated by contract tests.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a catalogue of component and service interface contracts, with version numbers, and assign an owner for each one.
- [ ] Define clear interface agreements using standard formats—such as OpenAPI/AsyncAPI, Protocol Buffers with gRPC, GraphQL schema, or internal interface definitions—so that error handling and security rules are specified.
- [ ] Refactor interface implementations so they only expose the standard data transfer objects (DTOs) and view models, and so no internal persistence or internal state details are unintentionally exposed.
- [ ] Find duplicate interfaces or interfaces that have been given overlapping meanings, and deprecate or remove them using a controlled migration plan.
- [ ] Create automated tests to check that software matches its agreed interfaces, generate these tests from the interface descriptions, and include them in the continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline.
- [ ] Store contract artefacts and contract testing evidence in the system design and security documentation set so they can be traced during audits.


---

#### SA-8.2 — Least Common Mechanism (Enhancement)

This expectation is about reducing how much different applications and services depend on the same underlying workings, so they do not accidentally affect each other. If multiple parts of a system use the same access method or share the same information status, a mistake or malicious change in one part can spread and harm other parts that rely on that shared information. This can lead to wrong decisions, disrupted services, or the exposure of sensitive information.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must first identify which components share information across the mission applications and services. It must then design and build them so the amount of shared mechanisms is kept to the minimum necessary, avoiding shared access routes and shared data wherever possible. Any sharing that remains must be carefully controlled so one application cannot unintentionally affect or compromise another.

The organisation must document the procedures used during planning, design, development, implementation, and changes. It must keep evidence in design and architecture records, define security and privacy requirements, assign named responsibilities, and keep the supporting documents up to date throughout the system’s lifecycle.

##### Minimise shared state across mission services via bounded contexts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Implement the **least common mechanism** approach for the organisation-defined **systems or system components that share information (state) across Department of Defence mission applications and services** (SA-8(2) parameter **sa-08.02_odp**). Do this by redesigning and enforcing **shared mechanisms that are as small as possible and tightly controlled** (interfaces/access routes/shared data/state) between those components.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-8(2) requires applying the **least common mechanism** security design principle: reduce the amount of shared functionality that is used by more than one user, and ensure shared mechanisms (especially shared variables/state) are designed so they do not unintentionally weaken security. This lowers the risk that corruption of shared information by one component spreads to others, and it supports simpler design while reducing the chance of hidden ways to store or pass information.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify shared-information components**: create a traceable map showing which mission applications/services **read and/or update the same data/status** (for example: shared database tables, shared caches, shared in-memory state, common message topics with shared changeable content, shared feature-flag state, shared identity attributes used as state).
2. **Define clear ownership boundaries**: for each shared-information area, name a single **system of record** (the producer) and explicitly prevent other components from writing into that producer’s internal state.
3. **Replace direct shared-data access with clearer integration**: where possible, redesign so each consumer keeps its own state (for example, read views/projections) and receives updates through **event-driven integration** rather than directly writing shared variables/data.
4. **Minimise shared mechanisms**: make each integration use narrowly defined, version-controlled agreements (schemas) and avoid sharing the same storage layer across components (for example, separate schemas/databases per bounded context where feasible).
5. **Control any remaining shared mechanism at the boundary**: apply authorisation and integrity checks when receiving events or API requests (for example: workload/service identity, mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS), strict schema validation, reject unknown or invalid messages, and use protections against repeated delivery such as idempotency/replay protection) so one component cannot unintentionally corrupt another.
6. **Add “blast-radius” protections**: add validation, idempotency keys, rate limiting, and circuit breakers so malformed or excessive updates do not spread and cause widespread state corruption.
7. **Document and provide evidence**: record the shared-information map, ownership decisions, interface/contract specifications, and the design rationale in architecture and security design documentation. Ensure change records keep the least-common-mechanism intent throughout the lifecycle.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Solution Architect / System Architect**: leads the shared-information mapping, bounded-context design, and ownership boundary decisions.
- **Application/Platform Engineering Lead**: carries out refactoring, event/API contract changes, and boundary controls.
- **Security Engineering / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead**: ensures service-to-service identity, authorisation enforcement, and integrity controls are implemented correctly.
- **Configuration/Change Manager**: ensures design evidence and change records are kept for audit purposes.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented, reviewable **shared-information map** exists for all components identified under **sa-08.02_odp**, showing which components share which data/status and how.
- For each shared-information area, there is a clearly defined **system of record**, and there is documented (and technically enforced) prohibition on unauthorised writes by other components.
- Shared mechanisms are demonstrably **minimised**: direct shared database/table writes and shared changeable in-memory state across components are eliminated or reduced to a justified, controlled set.
- Any remaining shared integrations enforce **contract validation** (schema/version), **authorisation** (service identity), and **integrity protections** (for example, replay/idempotency) at the ingestion boundary.
- Evidence is available in architecture/security design records and change documentation showing the least-common-mechanism design intent and how it is maintained through changes.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a shared-data inventory map showing which sa-08.02_odp components read and write the same data or status, and through which mechanisms this happens.
- [ ] Assign one system of record (the organisation responsible for the data) for each shared-data domain, and document which parties are not allowed to make changes (write) unless they are the owner.
- [ ] Refactor the integrations to stop writing directly to shared variables or data. Instead, use separate bounded areas of responsibility and update information through event-driven messages, with each consumer keeping its own state.
- [ ] Use versioned, schema-checked event and application programming interface (API) agreements, and reject any invalid or unknown data at the point where information is first received (the ingestion boundary).
- [ ] Require that services authorise each other for shared integrations (workload identity) and use protection to ensure messages are genuine and not reused (mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS), replay protection, and the ability to safely repeat requests).
- [ ] Add blast-radius controls—such as rate limiting, circuit breakers, and input validation—to stop one component’s failure from spreading and corrupting other components.
- [ ] Record evidence of the architecture and security design (including the shared-state map, who owns what, contract specifications, and the reasons behind the choices). Make sure the change control process protects and preserves the design.


---

#### SA-8.3 — Modularity and Layering (Enhancement)

This expectation is about building security using a well-organised set of building blocks, then arranging those blocks into clear layers. This makes it easier to see what depends on what. Without a modular, layered design, weaknesses can spread, changes can accidentally remove or weaken protections, and it becomes harder to ensure only the right people can access Confidential or Secret information. It also becomes harder to make sure the system reliably records what happened and keeps data protected.

The organisation must design and implement all on-premises information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) systems that handle Confidential and Secret data. It must use separate, clearly defined security components for: proving identity, deciding permissions, recording security-relevant events, and protecting data. These components must be placed into a layered structure with clear boundaries, such as network zones, application tiers, and host layers.

The organisation must set out these principles in its procurement (acquisition) and change procedures, assign named responsibilities to the right people, ensure developers and information security staff apply the approach throughout the system’s life, and keep design documents and plans showing the modules, layer responsibilities, dependencies, and isolation boundaries. The organisation must also keep evidence that changes update the relevant design records.

##### Modular layered security design for IT/OT Confidential/Secret systems

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Design and implement the organisation’s modular and layered security approach across all on-premises information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) systems that handle Confidential and Secret data. Use separate, clearly defined security building blocks (for example: authentication, authorisation, logging, and data protection) arranged into clear layers (for example: network security zones, application layers, and host security layers).

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-8(3)** enhancement requires: “Implement the security design principles of **modularity and layering** in {{ insert: param, sa-8.3_prm_1 }}.” The guidance also requires security-informed breakdown into modules based on **trust, trustworthiness, privilege, and security policy**, and layering that clearly shows **dependencies and unwanted complexity**.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Define modular security building blocks** for the systems in scope (per **sa-08.03_odp.01**):
   - Authentication building block: sets up identity and sessions, with defined trust boundaries.
