   - Authorisation building block: makes access decisions and enforces them, using least-privilege.
   - Logging building block: creates security events, standardises them, protects their integrity, and forwards them.
   - Data protection building block: encrypts data while it is being sent and while it is stored, and manages encryption keys with clear separation of duties.
   For each building block, document: its purpose, what it takes in and produces, the owning role/team, the privileges it needs, and its explicit dependencies.
2. **Define a layered security structure** (per **sa-08.03_odp.02**):
   - Network security zones (for example: user access zone, application zone, management zone, and OT control zone where applicable).
   - Application layers (for example: presentation/edge, application logic, data access, and administration functions).
   - Host security layers (for example: operating system hardening layer, application runtime layer, and privileged administration tools layer).
   Assign each modular building block to one or more layers with clear boundaries and “allowed-to-call” relationships.
3. **Enforce separation boundaries in practice (not just in diagrams)**:
   - Separate security-critical processes/services with distinct address spaces where feasible, and align privilege separation with hardware-supported privilege areas.
   - Use network segmentation controls to restrict traffic between layers and zones to only what is required.
   - Ensure authorisation decisions are centralised or consistently enforced at defined enforcement points, to avoid repeated or conflicting access rules.
   - Ensure logging and audit processes are isolated from the application runtime, so that if an application component is compromised, audit trails cannot be easily disabled or altered.
4. **Apply the design across the system lifecycle**:
   - Require that acquisition, design, development, implementation, and change activities reference the modular/layered security design records.
   - For each change, update the relevant design records (building block interfaces, layer responsibilities, dependencies, and separation boundaries) and keep evidence that the updates were made.
5. **Create audit-ready design evidence**:
   - Keep a current “modular and layered security design” document (or set of documents) for each system. It must map building blocks to layers, list dependencies, and identify separation mechanisms.
   - Keep evidence linked to changes showing that updates to the system also update the design records.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: accountable for the system’s modular/layered security design and ensuring it stays up to date.
- **Security Architect / Enterprise Security Team**: defines building block contracts, the layering model, and minimum expectations for separation and enforcement.
- **Solution/Platform Architect**: implements the layered architecture and integration patterns.
- **Developers / Engineering Teams**: implement building block interfaces and ensure components do not bypass the defined enforcement points.
- **Infrastructure/OT Engineering (as applicable)**: implements zone separation, hardening of host layers, and privilege separation.
- **Information Security / Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC)**: checks that design records and change evidence are complete for audit.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented modular security design exists for each in-scope system, showing authentication, authorisation, logging, and data protection building blocks with defined interfaces, responsibilities, and dependencies.
- A documented layered security design exists showing network zones, application layers, and host layers, with an explicit mapping of building blocks to layers.
- Technical controls exist to enforce separation boundaries (for example: network segmentation rules, privilege separation, and an isolated logging/audit pipeline) consistent with the documented boundaries.
- Change management evidence shows that modifications update the modular/layered design records (interfaces, dependencies, and separation boundaries) and that the updated records are retained.
- Authorisation and logging are implemented at the defined enforcement points/building blocks so that access control and audit coverage cannot be bypassed through normal application behaviour.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create separate, reusable security component definitions for each system covering authentication, authorisation, logging, and data protection. For each component, document its interfaces, inputs and outputs, responsible owner, required privileges, and dependencies.
- [ ] Define the layered security approach for each in-scope system (network zones, application layers, and host layers), and assign each security module to the specific responsibilities of its layer.
- [ ] Put in place and record clear technical separation boundaries, including network segmentation, separating privileges and (where possible) separating computing memory, and using a dedicated, isolated system for audit and logging. Ensure these match the design records.
- [ ] Update the acquisition, design, and change procedures to require modular, layered design references. Ensure that when changes are made, they also update the module interfaces, each layer’s responsibilities, the dependencies between layers, and the boundaries that keep layers isolated from each other.
- [ ] Maintain audit-ready records that show the current mapping of each software module to its system layer, and document updates that are linked to changes, for all in-scope on-premises Confidential and Secret information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) systems.


---

#### SA-8.4 — Partially Ordered Dependencies (Enhancement)

This requirement is about designing systems so that each part depends only on the correct lower-level parts, in a clear, layered structure, rather than creating tangled “self-feeding” loops. Without this, making changes can become risky and unpredictable, faults can spread more easily, and it becomes harder to test, understand, and fix problems—especially when multiple teams or suppliers are involved.

The organisation must design and implement the application services, middleware components, and underlying platform services so that security-related dependencies always move in one direction—from higher layers to lower layers. Each layer should be as self-contained as possible. If any circular dependency loops exist, they must be kept within a single layer and not cross between layers.

The organisation must document this approach in its design and security architecture, include it in its security and privacy requirements and its security plan, apply it through procedures during specification, design, development, implementation, and modification, assign named responsibilities, and be able to demonstrate through design records and change records that dependency direction and circularity checks were carried out.

##### Enforce layered dependency direction with CI/CD cycle checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Set up a partially ordered dependency structure across the organisation-defined layers for **application layer services, middleware components, and underlying platform services**. Dependencies must **run from higher layers to lower layers**, and any **circular dependencies** must be limited to happen **within a single layer only** (not between layers).

###### WHY (control requirement)
The NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(4)** enhancement requires applying the security design principle of **partially ordered dependencies**. The guidance says system layers should be arranged so that inter-layer dependencies follow a clear order (higher depends on lower). It also says circular dependencies should be limited to **within layers**, making them easier to manage. This also supports testing and analysis.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the layer model and dependency rules** for the in-scope components: application services, middleware components, and platform services (as per `sa-08.04_odp`). Publish a dependency contract that clearly states:
   - **allowed inter-layer dependency directions** (for example: application → middleware; middleware → platform)
   - **not allowed reverse directions**.
2. **Create an allowlist of permitted dependency links** at the interface level (for example: approved application programming interfaces (APIs) or software development kits (SDKs), message topics, database access patterns, and shared libraries). Treat any dependency not on the allowlist as a potential breach.
3. **Automate dependency discovery and checks** in continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD):
   - **During build**: generate dependency diagrams from the build system (for example: Gradle/Maven/.NET project references, container/service descriptions, and package dependency listings).
   - **During release (optional but recommended)**: check how services interact using runtime call tracking and telemetry where available.
4. **Add CI/CD checks (gates)** that stop builds/releases when:
   - a **reverse-direction** dependency is introduced across layers
   - a **cycle across layers** is detected (a dependency chain that returns to the starting layer while passing through different layers).
5. **Use a controlled exception (waiver) process** only when deviation is unavoidable:
   - Require architecture and security review approval
   - Provide a documented reason, risk assessment, compensating controls, and an expiry date
   - Link waivers to change records so audit evidence shows exceptions are managed, not uncontrolled.
6. **Record and keep evidence of the control** in design and change documents:
   - Include the dependency contract in the security architecture/design documentation, and reference it from security/privacy requirements and the security plan
   - Keep CI/CD gate outputs and the waiver register as audit-ready evidence.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Solution/Enterprise Architect**: defines the layer model and dependency contract.
- **Application/Middleware Engineers**: make code changes to comply with the contract.
- **DevOps/Build and Release Engineers**: implement CI/CD dependency checks and capture evidence.
- **Information Security / Security Architecture Review Board**: approves and records waivers and compensating controls.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented dependency contract exists for the defined layers (**application → middleware → platform**) and is referenced in security architecture/security plan documents.
- CI/CD produces evidence (logs/reports) showing builds/releases fail when reverse-direction dependencies or cross-layer cycles are introduced.
- For any exception, a waiver exists with approval, reason, compensating controls, and expiry, and is linked to the relevant change record.
- A dependency report can be produced showing current dependency direction and confirming that any circularity is confined within a single layer.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the layer model for application services, middleware components, and platform services, and publish a dependency contract.
- [ ] Create a list of allowed connections between layers (inter-layer dependency edges), and explicitly block any connections that go in the reverse direction.
- [ ] Add automated dependency mapping to the build process for each component or layer.
- [ ] Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) checks to block cross-layer reverse dependencies and to detect and report cross-layer cycles.
- [ ] Set up a waiver process that requires architecture and security approval, a risk assessment, agreed compensating controls, and an expiry date, and link it to the relevant change records.
- [ ] Update the security architecture and security plan to reference the dependency contract, and store continuous integration (CI) gate results and waiver evidence so they are available for audit.


---

#### SA-8.5 — Efficiently Mediated Access (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure access decisions are checked in the right place, in the simplest effective way, so only the right people and systems can reach the resources they are allowed to use. Without this, organisations can end up with repeated or overly complicated checks that slow systems down, or with gaps where access is not properly controlled—raising the risk of unauthorised access to data, services, devices, or infrastructure.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must identify all on-premises points where access decisions are made (including host or virtualisation policy controls, network firewalls, application programming interface (API) gateways, and identity and access services) and define how “efficiently mediated access” will be applied. This means using the least complex enforcement method that still meets stakeholder needs and any stated constraints, and designing it so it does not create avoidable performance problems.

The organisation must build this into system security requirements, architecture, and plans; ensure it is followed during specification, design, development, implementation, and changes; keep clear records of the reasons for decisions and the performance considerations; and require named responsible people to oversee it during procurement and delivery.

##### Policy-as-code enforcement point selection with performance budgets

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Put in place efficient, controlled access to the organisation’s on-premises systems by making sure access decisions are applied at the lowest level of control that still meets stakeholder needs and stated limitations. Also ensure that adding this control does not create unnecessary performance slowdowns.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST Special Publication 800-53, SA-8(5) enhancement requires that the security design principle of **efficiently mediated access** is implemented for **{{ insert: param, sa-08.05_odp }}**. The guidance requires that access to resources (for example, services, network ports, data, and infrastructure) is controlled using the **simplest practical enforcement method** that still meets stakeholder requirements and constraints, while avoiding performance bottlenecks.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an enforcement point catalogue** for each on-premises system component in scope (for example, hypervisor/host policy engines, network firewalls, application programming interface (API) gateways, and identity and access management (IAM) services). For each type of resource (ports/services, data access, device/infrastructure access), record where access control can be applied.
2. **Define a decision matrix** that chooses the simplest suitable enforcement point(s) for each type of access decision, for example:
   - Identity and role-based authorisation decisions: use IAM (and IAM-integrated enforcement) where possible.
   - Request-level authorisation for APIs/microservices: use API gateway enforcement where possible.
   - Coarse reachability or port filtering: use network firewall enforcement where possible.
   - Local boundary enforcement for low-level resources (where applicable): use host/hypervisor enforcement where possible.
   The matrix must clearly record stakeholder requirements, constraints, and the reasons for the chosen enforcement point(s).
3. **Turn the selection into policy-as-code** so that each access decision includes:
   - the required enforcement point(s),
   - the reasons linked to stakeholder requirements/constraints,
   - and a **performance budget** (for example, the maximum allowed added 95th percentile latency and the expected impact on available processing capacity) to prevent avoidable bottlenecks.
4. **Enforce via continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) gates**:
   - Stop changes that add unnecessary or overly complex access control for the same purpose, unless an approved exception exists.
   - Stop releases that do not include required performance budget documentation and validation.
5. **Validate during operation using telemetry**:
   - Add measurement to the enforcement points (IAM, API gateway, firewalls, and host agents where used) to track access-control latency, error rates, and the outcomes of access decisions.
   - Add automated checks (detection-as-code) to identify “bypass” patterns (for example, successful access paths that do not pass through the expected enforcement point(s), or that do not produce the expected authorisation information).
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence for each system change**:
   - Store the enforcement point selection rationale, constraints, performance budget, CI/CD gate results, and runtime validation outputs alongside the system security architecture/design records.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architect / IAM and API Security Lead**: owns the enforcement point catalogue and decision matrix.
- **Application/Platform Engineering Lead**: implements policy-as-code and CI/CD gates.
- **DevSecOps (development, security, and operations) / Security Engineering**: maintains telemetry, detection-as-code, and evidence generation.
- **Change Manager / System Owner**: ensures any exceptions (where needed) are formally approved and tracked.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each on-premises system component in scope, there is a documented enforcement point catalogue and a decision matrix mapping access decision types to the simplest enforcement point(s) that meet stakeholder requirements/constraints.
- Policy-as-code is implemented so that access decisions cannot be satisfied without passing through the selected enforcement point(s) (no unauthorised bypass routes).
- CI/CD gates prevent unnecessary duplicate access control and require performance budget documentation and validation.
- Runtime telemetry shows that access-control latency and resource impact stay within the defined performance budgets during normal operation.
- Evidence packages exist for recent releases showing the rationale, constraints, performance considerations, CI/CD gate outcomes, and runtime validation results.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a list of enforcement points for on-premises components (such as hypervisor/host policy engines, network firewalls, API gateways, and identity and access management services) and link each type of resource to where traffic mediation should happen.
- [ ] Define and get approval for a decision matrix that, for each type of access decision, chooses the simplest enforcement method that meets stakeholder requirements. Include the requirements, any constraints, and the reasons for the choice.
- [ ] Set up “policy-as-code” so every access decision is tied to the chosen enforcement point(s), and so the system records a mediation performance budget.
- [ ] Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) policy gates to prevent unnecessary repeat mediation and to require evidence that each release meets the agreed performance budget.
- [ ] Set up enforcement points with monitoring data (telemetry) to measure how long mediation takes and how often errors occur, and to confirm that runtime policy checks are being followed correctly.
- [ ] For each change, create audit-ready evidence packs that include the reasons for enforcement points, any constraints, proof that performance was validated, and the results from continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) approval gates.


---

#### SA-8.6 — Minimized Sharing (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure different parts of a system do not use the same underlying information or workspace unless it is genuinely necessary. If they do, one user, service, or function could accidentally or deliberately view, change, or misuse another part’s information, or take advantage of hidden effects that reveal details that should be separate. This increases the risk of unauthorised access, disclosure, misuse, or tampering.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design, build, operate, and change each information system so that user-related resources are not shared by default between different system components. Any sharing must only be allowed when there is a clear, documented need, with an explicit request and formal authorisation.

The organisation must also avoid unnecessary shared “common” mechanisms, such as widely used global data. Where sharing is required, it must be carefully engineered to preserve separation and prevent hidden leaks.

The organisation must document these decisions and apply the same approach throughout specification, change, and modification. It must include this in security requirements, design records, architecture, and plans, assign named responsibilities, define the supporting processes and mechanisms, and keep evidence in the relevant records.

##### Isolation-by-default with explicit, authorised sharing contracts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Design and build the application so that **user-domain resources** (as defined for this system) are **not shared between different users, processes, and functions** unless sharing is **clearly requested and clearly authorised**. If sharing is required, it must be designed to keep separation intact (for example, by using re-use safely or virtualised handling) and to prevent hidden information leaks.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication 800-53 **SA-8(6)** enhancement requires applying the security design principle of **minimised sharing** for **{{ insert: param, sa-08.06_odp }}**. This means: by default, do not share; only share when it is truly necessary; and carefully design any shared features (especially shared data) so they do not allow unauthorised access, disclosure, misuse, alteration, or hidden storage/timing channels.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define user-domain resources** for this system (for example, tenant-scoped data objects, per-workflow state, in-memory/session state, and service capabilities) and document the boundaries between users, processes, and functions.
2. **Use an isolation-by-default design**: make sure components use separate execution environments and separate data areas by default (for example, separate namespaces/accounts/tenants, strict role-based access control (RBAC), and no shared storage locations between components).
3. **Remove hidden or automatic sharing routes**: remove or restrict shared global data, shared temporary folders, broadly accessible caches, and shared database structures that do not enforce strict separation (for example, enforce tenant/workflow row-level separation where sharing cannot be avoided).
4. **Create explicit sharing agreements** only for the few cases where sharing is unavoidable: use narrowly scoped interfaces (application programming interfaces (APIs), queues, events) with strict input/output formats, handle requests in a way that does not rely on shared context, and perform authorisation checks at the point where sharing begins.
5. **Keep separation even when sharing is required**: ensure shared mechanisms are safe to re-use (reentrant) or virtualised (for example, use per-request/per-tenant context objects; avoid mutable global state; bind correlation identifiers to the caller’s identity) to prevent mixing of context and hidden leaks.
6. **Enforce “no sharing by default” through policy-as-code** in deployment and configuration (for example, automated checks that fail builds if components are given broad cross-component access or configured to use shared resources without an approved sharing agreement).
7. **Test separation automatically**: add negative tests that try to access resources across components and confirm both (a) access is denied, and (b) there are no observable side effects that would indicate hidden storage or timing leaks for high-risk flows.
8. **Keep audit evidence**: for each sharing agreement, store the documented request, the approval/authorisation record, the design reason (why sharing is necessary), and the test evidence showing separation is preserved.

###### WHO
- **Solution/Architecture Owner**: defines user-domain resource boundaries and the isolation-by-default design.
- **Application Security Engineer**: designs sharing agreements, authorisation boundary checks, and safe re-use/virtualised patterns.
- **Platform/DevOps Engineer**: implements policy-as-code enforcement and deployment safeguards.
- **Developers**: implement the agreement-based interfaces and remove hidden sharing routes.
- **Quality Assurance (QA)/Security Testing Lead**: implements and runs separation tests and records evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For the defined **user-domain resources**, components **cannot access** each other’s resources by default (verified by automated negative tests).
- Any sharing happens only through **explicit sharing agreements** that require **explicit request + explicit authorisation**, and can be traced to approval evidence.
- Where shared mechanisms are used, they are **reentrant/virtualised** so a caller’s context cannot mix across components/tenants (verified by tests for cross-context separation).
- Evidence exists for: resource boundary definitions, sharing agreement approvals, policy-as-code enforcement outputs, and separation test results.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document the system-specific set of user-related resources, and the component boundaries that must not share those resources.
- [ ] Refactor the architecture and configuration to ensure isolation by default—run components in separate execution environments and separate data areas, and remove any shared storage locations used across components.
- [ ] Identify and remove hidden or common ways that information can be shared (for example, shared global data or shared temporary/cache folders), or limit this sharing by using strong separation controls.
- [ ] Create clear sharing agreements for interfaces (application programming interfaces (APIs), message queues, and events), with permission checks at the boundaries and tightly limited data formats (schemas).
- [ ] Implement reentrancy and virtualisation patterns for any shared components that are required, to prevent different users’ or tasks’ information from being mixed up and to stop unauthorised information leakage.
- [ ] Add policy-as-code safeguards that prevent deployments from introducing unauthorised sharing between components.
- [ ] Develop and run automated separation tests to confirm that one component cannot access another, and that there are no noticeable or measurable side effects.


---

#### SA-8.7 — Reduced Complexity (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping the security parts of your on-site systems as simple and as small as possible, so they are easier to understand and harder to get wrong. If security designs become too complicated, people are more likely to make mistakes, important security protections can be missed, and weaknesses can be introduced when building, deploying, or changing the system—especially in mixed environments where different types of equipment and software must work together.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must apply the reduced-complexity approach to all on-premises mixed information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) systems and components that perform security functions. It must also create clear written procedures showing how this simplicity is considered during planning, design, development, implementation, and any later changes.

The organisation must assign named responsibilities, use practical checks such as review steps and approval gates to confirm simplicity before changes go live, and keep design and planning records showing how the approach was applied and carried into the deployed configuration. This includes recording any time-limited periods where complexity may temporarily increase during transitions.

##### Reduced-complexity security design gates for IT/OT security functions

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Apply a “reduced complexity” engineering approach to all on-premises mixed information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) system parts that perform security functions. Do this by setting clear limits for simplicity and checking them at key design and architecture review points throughout the lifecycle, with evidence that can be audited.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The requirement in NIST Special Publication 800-53, SA-8(7) enhancement, says: “Implement the security design principle of reduced complexity in {{ insert: param, sa-08.07_odp }}.” The guidance explains that security designs should be “as simple and small as possible” to make them easier to understand and analyse, and to reduce the chance of security weaknesses. It also notes that complexity may temporarily increase during transition periods.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the scope**: create and maintain an organisation-owned inventory of “systems or system components that implement security functions” for on-premises mixed IT/OT environments (this matches the SA-8(7) scope).
2. **Turn reduced complexity into clear, checkable limits**: publish a small set of measurable design limits, such as:
   - reduce the number of security components
   - reduce the number of trust boundaries
   - avoid duplicated security rules or policy logic
   - minimise bespoke (custom) integrations
   - use standardised enterprise security services where possible
   - reduce the number of conditional branches or exceptions in security decision logic
3. **Build these limits into lifecycle documents**: update security architecture documents and change-pack templates to include a required **Complexity Delta** section, stating what was removed or simplified, what interfaces or data paths were reduced, and what remains. Also add a clear **Transition Exception** section when complexity must temporarily increase (limited in time, with a plan to simplify).
4. **Require approval through design review gates**: add a mandatory “Reduced Complexity” checklist to the engineering design review process / change advisory board (CAB) workflow for any change that affects security functions. Approval must confirm the design is “as small and simple as possible” and that unused or duplicated features are removed or have a documented justification.
5. **Capture and keep evidence**: store the approved architecture diagrams, Complexity Delta explanations, and configuration and policy summaries that match the current deployed baseline. Where available, attach automated validation results (for example, policy-as-code linting and testing results for security rule sets).
6. **Assign named responsibilities**: assign specific roles (for example, security architect or engineering owner, and information security approver) to complete the Complexity Delta, carry out the review, and approve any exceptions.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architect / Engineering Lead**: defines and applies the reduced-complexity limits; completes the Complexity Delta in design and change packs.
- **Information Security Approver (or Security Governance Lead)**: checks that the design is as simple and small as possible and approves and records any time-limited transition exceptions.
- **Change Manager / CAB Chair**: ensures the reduced-complexity gate is mandatory for relevant security-function changes.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each on-premises mixed IT/OT security-function component in scope, there is an approved reduced-complexity design record (architecture plus Complexity Delta) that covers the current deployed state.
- Every security-function change includes a completed Complexity Delta and passes the reduced-complexity design review gate before release.
- Any transition exception (temporary increase in complexity) is limited in time, documented with a plan to simplify, and approved by the information security approver.
- Evidence is retained and can be traced back to the change and approval record and the deployed configuration baseline.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date list (inventory) of on-premises mixed information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) systems and components that carry out security functions, within the scope of SA-8(7).
- [ ] Publish simpler engineering constraints and clear, measurable rules for security design (for example, reduce the number of security components, reduce trust boundaries, and avoid duplicating policy logic).
- [ ] Update the security architecture and change-request templates so they require a “Complexity Delta” explanation and, where relevant, a time-limited “Transition Exception” section.
- [ ] For any change to a security function, make sure the engineering design review and change advisory board (CAB) process includes mandatory, simplified checklist items.
- [ ] Set up named roles for completing Complexity Delta, carrying out the review, and approving any exceptions, and record each approval in the change management system.
- [ ] Set up evidence retention that links the approved, simpler documents (such as diagrams, written explanations, and policy or configuration summaries) to the configuration baseline that is actually deployed.


---

#### SA-8.8 — Secure Evolvability (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure a system’s security protections keep working and can be improved safely as the system changes—for example during upgrades, maintenance, reconfiguration, or when new capabilities are added. Without this, a business-driven change could accidentally weaken security, disrupt how security rules are applied, or create new weaknesses by changing how parts of the system connect. That could leave the organisation exposed in ways that were not tested or planned for.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must create and follow clear, written procedures for planning and carrying out security-safe changes across the whole system life cycle—from requirements and design, through development, implementation, and later updates. It must document how security will remain easy to maintain and update, set out security and privacy requirements that explain how changes will be handled, and keep a security plan and system design that show how security protections will be preserved.

Named individuals must be responsible for setting the requirements, applying the approach during building and change activities, and supporting checks and verification. Their planning must be based on likely future mission direction, expected changes in threats, and maintenance needs—not on ad hoc decisions.

##### Secure evolvability change gates for mission systems and policy enforcement

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a repeatable, evidence-based “secure evolvability” change process for the organisation-defined **systems or system components** (mission systems, interfaces, and **security policy enforcement components**). This ensures security protections stay in place and can be updated safely when the system’s structure, interfaces, connections, functions, or configuration change.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(8)** (Enhancement) requires applying the security design principle of **secure evolvability** to the specified systems/components. The guidance focuses on planning for change (upgrades, ongoing support, reconfiguration), avoiding one-off changes that accidentally weaken security, and ensuring any new or unexpected behaviour remains secure through a controlled approach.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Create and run a “gated” release pipeline and supporting engineering procedures. Apply them to every relevant change (upgrades, ongoing support, reconfiguration, interface updates, and security policy enforcement updates):

1. **Define secure evolvability procedures** covering the full lifecycle phases relevant to the system: specify/design, develop, implement, and modify. Procedures must require: (a) a security impact analysis for the planned type of change, (b) updates to security design records, and (c) evidence to prove security checks before and after the change.
2. **Carry out structured change impact analysis before any build/release**: assess how the change affects security protections across the system’s architecture, interfaces, connections, and security policy enforcement behaviour. Planning must be based on expected mission or business direction, expected changes in the threat environment, and expected maintenance/support needs—not on ad hoc assumptions.
3. **Use gated releases in the CI/CD process**:
   - Gate A: architecture/interface impact assessment completed and approved.
   - Gate B: security policy/configuration update plan completed (including how enforcement components will behave after the change).
   - Gate C: pre-deployment validation (automated and manual checks) showing security protections are preserved.
   - Gate D: post-deployment security regression checks and monitoring confirmation.
4. **Implement security policy enforcement as testable, version-controlled configuration where possible** (for example, “policy-as-code” or “infrastructure-as-code” modules for access control, network segmentation, conditional access, and security configuration). Ensure changes can be traced back to requirements and produce evidence that can be audited.
5. **Add interface and security regression testing for each change type**:
   - Interface contract tests (data format/API compatibility) to prevent unintended bypasses of authorisation/authentication or broken trust boundaries.
   - Security regression tests for authentication/authorisation, encryption in transit, logging/telemetry, and least-privilege boundaries.
6. **Update security design records as part of the release process**: update the system security plan and security architecture/design records to reflect the updated security protections and enforcement behaviour (or clearly document any deferral, including the accountable owner and an expiry date).
7. **Define rollback/recovery expectations in the procedure and release plan**: ensure there is a defined rollback route to a known-good security configuration, and confirm that rollback is validated as part of the release evidence.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: responsible for ensuring the secure evolvability procedure is applied to the mission system and its interfaces.
- **Information Security / Security Architecture Lead**: approves the security impact analysis and updates to security design records.
- **Engineering Lead / DevOps Lead**: builds the gated release pipeline, automated tests, and evidence collection.
- **Change Manager / Release Manager**: ensures changes follow the defined gates and that evidence is kept for audit.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Every change within the defined scope (mission system, interfaces, or security policy enforcement components) is processed through the secure evolvability procedure and the gated release workflow.
- For each release, there is documented security impact analysis, updated security design records (security plan/architecture), and retained evidence of security regression validation before and after the change.
- Interface compatibility and security enforcement behaviour are verified through automated regression tests (and manual checks where automation is not practical).
- A validated rollback path exists for changes that affect security, and rollback expectations are included in the release plan.
- Approval and verification roles are named and applied consistently (no one-off or ad hoc approvals).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish secure procedures for how mission systems, interfaces, and security policy enforcement components are specified, designed, developed, implemented, and changed, so they can evolve safely over time.
- [ ] Set up a controlled continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) release process that requires a security impact assessment, a plan to update security policies and configurations, and named approvals before any deployment.
- [ ] Implement policy and configuration enforcement changes as version-controlled, testable configuration packages (for example, policy-as-code and infrastructure-as-code modules), with clear traceability back to the original requirements.
- [ ] Add automated tests for interface contracts and security regression to confirm, after every change, that authentication and authorisation still work correctly, data is still encrypted while in transit, logging and telemetry still capture the required information, and least-privilege access boundaries are still enforced.
- [ ] Update the system security plan and the security architecture/design records as part of the release evidence pack (or, if you defer the documents, record an expiry date and the accountable owner).
- [ ] For security changes that could affect security, define and test a rollback and recovery plan that returns systems to a known-good security configuration, and keep records showing the rollback evidence.


---

#### SA-8.9 — Trusted Components (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that every building block used in your security systems is genuinely trustworthy for the job it does, and that this trust is not weakened when parts are combined. Without this, a weak or poorly verified component could undermine key protections such as verifying who someone is, protecting data, recording events, applying updates, or controlling access to networks—leaving the organisation exposed even if everything else is well designed.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- identify all systems and components that provide or depend on security functions;
- decide what level of trust each one must have based on how critical it is; and
- use a consistent method to measure both the required trust and the component’s actual trustworthiness.

The organisation must also:
- document how trust is passed between components;
- treat combined systems conservatively by assuming the overall trustworthiness is no higher than the least trustworthy part, unless there is clear, evidence-based justification; and
- ensure these checks are built into procurement, design, development, implementation, and any changes, with decisions and supporting evidence recorded in the relevant project and planning documents.

##### Trusted component assurance levels with dependency-aware composition

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a measurable “trustworthiness” rating for every system and component that either provides security functions or relies on them. When components are combined into larger (compound) components, make sure the overall trustworthiness is not accidentally lowered. Use a conservative-by-default approach, with exceptions only when there is clear, evidence-based justification.

###### WHY (control requirement)
This requirement (NIST SP 800-53 SA-8(9) enhancement) asks for the security design principle of **trusted components**. In practice, a component must be trusted to at least the level needed for the security functions it supports. Use a **common rating scale** for both:
- the trust level that is required, and
- the trust level that is actually achieved.

For compound components, overall trustworthiness must be assumed to be that of the **least trustworthy** subcomponent, unless there is a clear security engineering reason supported by credible evidence.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Scope trusted components**: Create a complete inventory of all systems and components that either provide or rely on security functions (for example: identity, cryptography, logging/auditing, patching/updates, network security) for the application across all environments (production, test, development, disaster recovery).
2. **Create a trustworthiness scale**: Define one organisation-approved trust scale (for example, AL1 to AL4) with clear criteria that can be checked and evidenced (for example: signed software artefacts, software bill of materials (SBOM) availability, secure update mechanism, responsiveness in vulnerability management, cryptographic module validation/attestation, secure boot/attestation where applicable, independent assessment results).
3. **Map security functions to required trust**: For each component type and security dependency chain, set the **required trust level** based on how much other components depend on its security functions (for example: identity provider (IdP) and multi-factor authentication (MFA) and cryptography integrations need higher assurance than telemetry-only collectors).
4. **Model trust dependencies and chains**: Record how components trust each other (including compound components) in architecture/design documents and component records. Clearly state which components depend on which security functions.
5. **Enforce conservative composition**: For compound components/subsystems, calculate overall trustworthiness as the **minimum** trustworthiness of the subcomponents by default.
6. **Allow exceptions only with evidence-backed rationale**: If engineering wants to claim a higher overall trustworthiness than the least trustworthy subcomponent, require a documented security engineering rationale. This must state the trust objectives and include relevant credible evidence, and it must go through an approval process.
7. **Make it part of change/release decisions**: Build the trust mapping and evidence requirements into the application’s release and change process. Component upgrades or new component introductions must not be approved unless the component’s evidenced trustworthiness meets the required trust level for its dependency role.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: defines the trust scale, trust mapping, dependency chains, and exception rationales.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Manager**: ensures the inventory and dependency documentation are kept up to date, and that release gates are followed.
- **Procurement/Vendor Management** (where applicable): provides evidence (attestations, assessment reports, SBOMs, signed artefact details).
- **Change Advisory Board (CAB) / Release Manager**: enforces approval gates for component changes.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A complete, up-to-date inventory exists for the application covering all components that provide or rely on security functions.
- A single trustworthiness scale is defined with clear, evidence-based criteria.
- For each component, the required trust level is documented based on its security dependency role, and the component’s actual trustworthiness is evidenced.
- For compound components, overall trustworthiness is calculated as the least trustworthy subcomponent by default.
- Any exception to the conservative rule includes an approved security engineering rationale with credible evidence.
- Release/change records show that component introductions/upgrades were blocked or required remediation when evidence did not meet the required trust levels.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date list of every system and component that either provides security functions for the application or relies on security functions, across all environments.
- [ ] Define an organisation-approved trustworthiness scale with clear, verifiable criteria, and share it with the engineering and procurement teams.
- [ ] Create a mapping from each security function to the level of trust it requires, and apply the correct trust level to every component based on how that component depends on security.
- [ ] Document the “trust dependency chains” (including combined or nested components) in architecture and design documents, and in component inventory files (manifests).
- [ ] Use a “most cautious wins” approach for combined parts: the overall trustworthiness is limited by the least trustworthy part.
- [ ] Set up an exception process that requires a security engineering justification and credible supporting evidence when proposing that a component is more trustworthy than the least trustworthy part it depends on.
- [ ] Add trust-level and evidence checks to the application’s change and release approval process, so component upgrades cannot be approved unless the required trust level is met.


---

#### SA-8.10 — Hierarchical Trust (Enhancement)

This requirement is about setting up trust in a clear, one-way chain across your system. The most basic parts must be trusted first, and no part should end up depending on itself through a loop. Without this, a weak or compromised component could be used to “back up” the safety of stronger parts, making it harder to be confident that the whole system is secure as it is put together from different elements.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must design, build, and maintain a documented hierarchy of trust for the system’s software components and the underlying hardware or firmware trust anchors. This hierarchy must have no circular dependencies, and you must be able to explain which way the system relies on each layer, starting from the bottom and moving upwards.

You must apply this approach in your procurement (acquisition) and change processes. It must be used during specification, design, development, implementation, and any modification. You must also keep evidence of this in your records for design, security and privacy requirements, architecture, and your security plan.

If you intentionally place a more trusted component in a lower layer that still relies on a less trusted one, you must document and obtain approval for the justification, so the overall trust position is not weakened.

##### Hierarchical trust dependency matrix with circular-trust checks

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Define and document a hierarchy of trust that covers the system’s hardware and firmware trust “starting points” (trust anchors) and all software parts that affect security decisions. Also document the direction of trust relationships clearly, from the lowest layer up.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-8(10) requires implementing the security design principle of **hierarchical trust** for the organisation-defined scope (**{{ insert: param, sa-08.10_odp }}**: the information system, its software components, and its underlying hardware and firmware trust anchors). The guidance also requires removing **circular trust dependencies** so that any reasoning or evidence used to support security assurance remains valid when combining different components that have different trust levels.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a trust scope and component inventory** covering: hardware and firmware trust anchors (for example, secure boot, measured boot and trusted platform module (TPM) attestation verification points), boot chain elements (for example, bootloader and firmware update verification), operating system and runtime, middleware, and application components that make security-critical trust decisions.
2. **Build a “trust dependency matrix”** mapping each component to the *specific lower-layer trust mechanism* it relies on (for example, which trust anchor validates which artefacts or measurements; which component uses which trust decision).
3. **Set a one-way trust ordering rule**: security checks and security-critical authorisation decisions must ultimately trace back to lower-layer trust anchors. The dependency relationships must be representable as a partial order (meaning there is no circular trust).
4. **Check for circular trust loops** in the dependency graph created from the matrix (both direct and indirect links). Identify any cycle where a more trusted lower-layer component depends on a less trusted higher-layer component in a way that effectively makes them equivalent for trust purposes.
5. **Manage intentional exceptions (if any)**: if a highly trusted lower-layer component depends on a less trusted higher-layer component (as allowed by the guidance), document and obtain formal approval for the justification, showing that the overall trust position is not weakened.
6. **Publish the trust hierarchy as assurance evidence** by linking the matrix and loop-check results into the system’s security architecture and the system security plan records. Tie this evidence to configuration baselines (for example, secure boot and attestation verification settings, and rules for verifying signed artefacts).
7. **Enforce during change**: add a checklist gate so that any change affecting trust anchors, signing and verification, the boot chain, attestation verification, or security-critical trust decisions triggers an update to the matrix and a re-run of the loop check.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **System Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the hierarchical trust model, the matrix, and the loop-check method.
- **Platform / Infrastructure Engineering Lead**: provides accurate details of the boot chain and trust anchor implementation.
- **Application / Software Engineering Leads**: identify security-critical trust decisions and how they depend on lower-layer trust mechanisms.
- **Change Control Board (CCB) / Security Governance**: approves any documented exception where a highly trusted lower-layer component depends on a less trusted higher-layer component.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented **trust dependency matrix** exists for the defined scope (**sa-08.10_odp**) and clearly shows dependency direction from trust anchors upwards.
- A completed **circular-trust loop check** shows **no circular trust dependencies** (or any cycles are explicitly justified and approved in line with the guidance).
- The hierarchy (matrix, diagram, and loop-check results) is **referenced in the system security architecture and system security plan**, with traceable evidence.
- The change process includes a **mandatory review and update** of the matrix and loop check for any change that could affect trust anchors, signing and verification, the boot chain, attestation verification, or security-critical trust decisions.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the hierarchy of trusted elements, covering the information system, software components, and the hardware and firmware trust anchors.
- [ ] Create an inventory of the boot process components, trust anchors, operating system and runtime components, middleware, and security-critical application components.
- [ ] Create a trust dependency matrix that shows, for each component, the specific lower-layer trust mechanism it depends on.
- [ ] Run and record a check that follows a circular-trust loop across the derived trust dependency map.
- [ ] Document and obtain approval for any intentional exception where a highly trusted lower-level component relies on a less trusted higher-level component.
- [ ] Publish the hierarchy evidence (for example, the matrix, diagram, and loop-check results) in the system security architecture records and the system security plan records.
- [ ] Add a change-control step that requires updating the trust matrix and re-checking the loop validation for any change that affects trust anchors or trust verification.


---

#### SA-8.11 — Inverse Modification Threshold (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the most trusted parts of your security setup are also the hardest to interfere with. If a highly trusted component can be changed without strong safeguards, an attacker could quietly alter how encryption keys are handled or how access is granted. This could lead to unauthorised access, fraud, or the ability to get around protections.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out how it will apply the “more trust means stronger protection” approach to cryptographic modules, key management services, and identity and access management components. It must ensure that as the level of trust increases, so does the strength of protection against unauthorised changes.

This should be done using the component’s built-in safeguards where appropriate, along with additional protections provided by other parts of the security design and by the environment in which the component runs.

The organisation must document this across the full lifecycle in procurement and change procedures, record the design decisions in system documents, assign named responsibilities, and be able to demonstrate that higher-trust components receive stronger safeguards and stricter approval for changes—supported by evidence that these protections are actually in place and active.

##### Trust-tiered tamper resistance for crypto, KMS and IAM components

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Define and implement an **inverse modification threshold** approach for the organisation-defined in-scope component types—**cryptographic modules, key management services (key management services (KMS)), and identity and access management (identity and access management (IAM)) components**—so that the **level of protection against unauthorised changes increases in line with the component’s assigned trustworthiness**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-8(11) requires implementing the **security design principle of inverse modification threshold**: as trust in a component increases, **protection against unauthorised changes must also increase to the same degree**, using (a) the component’s **self-protection/innate trustworthiness** and/or (b) **protections provided by other parts of the security design**, including the **operating environment**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a trust-tier model** for each in-scope component type (cryptographic modules, KMS, IAM) that assigns a trust level based on factors such as the component’s role in authentication/authorisation, the sensitivity of key material, and the potential impact if it is compromised.
2. **Link each trust tier to specific requirements for resisting unauthorised changes**, with the requirements becoming stronger as trust increases. For example:
   - **Higher-trust tiers** must use stronger safeguards such as enforcing signed artefacts, integrity checks, restricted administrative interfaces, and stricter change approvals.
   - **Lower-trust tiers** may use baseline integrity checks and standard change controls.
3. **Put in place self-protection controls** where available for each component type (for example: signed updates/configuration, integrity checks, hardware-backed key storage and integrity evidence, and locked-down administrative access points).
4. **Put in place architectural and environmental protections** that become stronger as the trust tier increases (for example: more secure hosting, restricted network routes to administrative endpoints, separation of duties between those who approve and those who deploy, immutable or append-only audit logging, and controlled continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) pathways).
5. **Apply the trust-tier requirements in the delivery and operations process** so that higher-trust components cannot be changed or deployed unless the stronger tier requirements are met (for example: policy checks in CI/CD, configuration management guardrails, and approval-step gating).
6. **Record the design decisions and supporting evidence** across the full lifecycle: update system security architecture/design documentation and change/acquisition procedures to reference the trust-tier mapping and the required evidence outputs (for example: signed artefact verification results, integrity evidence records, and approval logs).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the trust-tier model and the mapping from tiers to controls.
- **Application/IAM/KMS/Platform Owners**: implement component-specific self-protection and operational safeguards.
- **DevOps/Platform Engineering**: implements CI/CD and configuration guardrails and produces the required evidence.
- **Information Security / Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC)**: checks the documentation, ensures governance across the lifecycle, and supports audit readiness.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The organisation has a **documented trust-tier model** covering **cryptographic modules, KMS, and IAM components**.
- For each trust tier, there is a **documented and implemented mapping** to stronger safeguards against unauthorised changes for higher trust.
- Evidence shows that **self-protection** and **architectural/environmental protections** are in place for higher-trust tiers (for example: signed artefact enforcement and integrity/evidence results; restricted administrative pathways; immutable audit records).
- Change and deployment processes **enforce** the trust-tier mapping (higher-trust components require stronger approvals/guardrails and cannot be deployed or changed outside the approved process).
- System design/security documentation and acquisition/change procedures **reference** the inverse modification threshold approach and the required evidence outputs.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define a trust-level model for cryptographic modules, key management systems (KMS), and identity and access management (IAM) components, including clear trust requirements and definitions for each trust level.
- [ ] Assign each trust level to specific, checkable requirements for modification resistance, with the requirements becoming stronger at higher trust levels.
- [ ] Set up self-protection for higher-trust levels, such as enforcing signed updates and configuration changes, checking that components have not been tampered with (integrity checks and attestation), and restricting administrative access to hardened, locked-down interfaces.
- [ ] Put in place security measures that grow as the trust level increases—for example, limit which network paths administrators can use, use more secure hosting, separate responsibilities between different roles, and keep audit records that cannot be changed once written.
- [ ] Apply the tier mapping during continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) and configuration management by using policy rules and requiring approval before changes can be released.
- [ ] Update the system security architecture and design documents, and the acquisition and change procedures, to record the tier mapping and the required evidence outputs.


---

#### SA-8.12 — Hierarchical Protection (Enhancement)

This requirement is about strengthening the most trusted parts of your system so they provide the main protection, rather than relying on every other part to defend itself.

In practice, if the most trusted components (such as the operating system kernel and other top-level system parts) do not clearly enforce boundaries, a harmful or poorly behaved application could gain access to things it should not, change critical settings, or weaken the whole environment—especially when users are not fully trustworthy.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must:
- clearly identify which components are the most trusted
- document who is protected by whom, and what protections are expected
- reflect this in your system design, security plans, and architecture records

You must also ensure your procurement and change processes require this approach at every stage—specification, design, build, deployment, and updates—with named individuals responsible for requirements and oversight.

Finally, you must keep evidence that changes were tested and verified, so less-trusted applications remain blocked by the trusted components, and so applications are not expected to provide the same level of protection as the kernel.

##### Kernel/most-trusted components enforce boundaries for less-trusted apps

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put stronger protection in layers by making sure the operating system kernel and other most-trusted system parts enforce security rules that protect weaker (less-trusted) application parts. Less-trusted applications must not be expected to provide the same level of protection as the kernel.

###### WHY (control requirement)
This requirement, based on NIST SP 800-53 SA-8(12) enhancement, asks you to apply the security design principle of **hierarchical protection** for **{{ insert: param, sa-08.12_odp }}**. The principle is that a component does not need to be protected against components that are more trustworthy. In the extreme case, the most trusted component protects itself against all other components.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the trust hierarchy** in the system security design: clearly label the **operating system kernel and other most-trusted system components** as “most trusted”, and label the enterprise business application components (web/application programming interface (API)/application services, agents, integrations) as “less trusted”.
2. **Record who is responsible for protection**: for each security boundary (for example, process privileges, file storage access, device access, communication between processes, controlling outgoing and incoming network traffic, and handling credentials), state that enforcement is done by the kernel/operating system security features (and other most-trusted components), not by application code.
3. **Build boundaries enforced by the kernel** using operating system controls that application behaviour cannot bypass (for example, running with least privilege, mandatory access controls, controlling system calls and device access, operating system sandboxing/containment, and operating system access control for files/registry/secrets). Make sure application code cannot increase its privileges to carry out actions that are not allowed.
4. **Set acceptance tests that prove “the kernel blocks it”**: define testable requirements such as “a less-trusted application running as a normal user cannot read protected secrets”, “cannot write to protected system locations”, “cannot access restricted device interfaces”, and “cannot carry out privilege escalation attempts”.
5. **Run boundary verification tests during release checks** (including realistic normal-user execution scenarios). Keep evidence showing that prohibited actions are blocked by the kernel/operating system security components, and that the application is not relied upon to restrict itself.
6. **Keep the hierarchy in place after changes**: when operating system images, kernel/host security components, or application deployment settings change, re-run the boundary verification tests and update the architecture and security documentation to reflect any changes to what is considered “trusted” and “less trusted”.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **System/Platform Architect**: defines and maintains the trust hierarchy and the statements about boundary responsibilities.
- **Security Engineering / Security Operations Centre (SOC) Engineering**: writes the boundary test cases and ensures the evidence is ready for audit.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineers**: implement operating system-level enforcement (sandboxing/mandatory access controls/least privilege) and build verification into continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) or release pipelines.
- **Application Owner**: confirms application components are treated as less trusted and does not introduce compensating requirements that effectively ask for “kernel-level” protection from the application.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The system security architecture clearly identifies the **operating system kernel and other most-trusted system components** as most trusted, and the application components as less trusted.
- For each defined boundary, the documentation states that enforcement is performed by the kernel/most-trusted components.
- Release validation tests show that prohibited actions from a less-trusted application context are **blocked by kernel/operating system security controls** (not by the application cooperating), and results/logs are kept as evidence.
- After changes to operating system/host security components or deployment configurations, boundary tests are re-run and documentation is updated to preserve the trust hierarchy.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and get approval for a “trust hierarchy” section in the system security design. Identify the operating system kernel and the other most trusted components as “most trusted”, and label the application components as “less trusted”.
- [ ] Define responsibility statements for each protection boundary, including who enforces it, what is blocked, and what evidence will be collected.
- [ ] Set up operating system (OS) controls to enforce least-privilege execution and use mandatory access controls and sandboxing, so the operating system’s security features manage and enforce the boundaries.
- [ ] Create release validation test cases that try forbidden actions from a less-trusted application and confirm the request is blocked at the operating system (OS) level.
- [ ] Add boundary verification tests to the release process, and keep the logs and supporting files as evidence for audit purposes.
- [ ] Re-run boundary checks and update the architecture and security documentation whenever the operating system kernel or host security components change, or when the deployment configuration changes.


---

#### SA-8.13 — Minimized Security Elements (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping the number of “highly trusted” parts of your system as small as possible, so fewer components need to be relied on to keep things secure. If an organisation includes unnecessary trusted services, it becomes harder to show that they are safe, easier for mistakes to slip in, and easier for attackers to take advantage of weaknesses through complicated links between those trusted parts.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design, build, operate, and update the information system and its trusted components (such as identity services, key handling, and access control services) using the principle of minimising trusted components. It must not add extra trusted components unless there is a clear need.

The organisation must also write and follow procedures that apply this approach during planning, design, development, deployment, and any changes. It must assign named individuals responsibility for these decisions, and keep current design and security planning documents that show which trusted components are used, why they are necessary, and that their interactions are kept simple.

##### Trusted component inventory with approval gate and interaction mapping

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create and keep a system-specific **trusted component inventory** that covers the information system and its **trusted components** (for example, identity, key management, and access control services). For every trusted component, require a **justification record**. Also enforce a **“no new trusted component without approval”** rule during design, development, release, and changes.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(13)** enhancement requires applying the security design principle of **minimised security elements** for the defined scope (**{{ insert: param, sa-08.13_odp }}**: the information system and its trusted components). The guidance says the system should include **as few trusted components as possible** to reduce the **cost and complexity of security analysis**. It also says security checks must analyse **how trusted components interact**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define “trusted component” for this system** in the architecture standard (for example, the main identity provider, the key custody/usage authority, the policy decision authority, and trust anchors such as certificate authorities (CAs) and hardware security modules (HSMs)). Make sure your definition matches what your organisation considers “trusted” for SA-8(13).
2. **Create a trusted component inventory** listing every component treated as trusted. Include: component name, owner, trust boundary, the security function that depends on trust, the assurance/verification approach, and **trust interaction links** (who relies on whom, and what is checked).
3. **Set up a justification gate**: for each trusted component, require a documented reason explaining why the security function cannot be achieved without that trust (and why options that would reduce the trusted scope are not used).
4. **Enforce a delivery pipeline gate**: add a workflow/continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI-CD) policy check that blocks merges/releases that introduce or expand a trusted component (or add a new service that becomes the main authority for identity, keys, or authorisation) unless the inventory and justification are updated and approved.
5. **Require evidence of simple interactions**: for each trusted component, require an interaction map and a short security verification note showing that trust links are limited and interactions are not unnecessarily complex.
6. **Keep the inventory up to date when things change**: when identity, key management, authorisation/policy enforcement, trust boundaries, or security-critical integrations change, update the inventory and justification and repeat the interaction mapping review.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **System/solution architect**: defines the trusted-component categories and maintains the inventory structure.
- **Information security (application security (AppSec)/enterprise security architecture)**: approves trusted-component justifications and evidence that interactions are simple.
- **Engineering leads / DevOps**: ensure the pipeline gate requirements are met for releases and changes.
- **Change manager / release manager**: ensures the gate is applied for production releases.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A current **trusted component inventory** exists for the system and includes all components treated as trusted within the scope of **sa-08.13_odp**.
- Every trusted component has an **approved justification** explaining why it is needed and why alternatives that would reduce trusted scope were not chosen.
- The delivery pipeline (or equivalent release governance) **prevents** introducing new or expanded trusted components without updating the inventory and obtaining approval.
- For each trusted component, there is documented **interaction mapping** showing that trust relationships are limited and that security verification of interactions is practical (not unnecessarily complex).
- For at least one recent representative change affecting identity, keys, or authorisation, evidence shows the inventory and justification were updated and reviewed before release.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish what a “trusted component” means for this system, in line with sa-08.13_odp.
- [ ] Create and maintain a trusted component inventory for the information system, including the responsible owners and the defined trust boundaries.
- [ ] Create a justification record for each trusted component. The record must explain why the component is necessary and why no alternative options are being used.
- [ ] Document the trust relationships for each trusted component, showing who depends on whom and what is checked or verified.
- [ ] Set up a release and design approval checkpoint that stops any new or expanded “trusted” components from being added unless the component list (inventory) and the reasons for the change are updated and approved.
- [ ] Update the inventory and re-approve the justifications for any change that affects identity, key management, authorisation or policy enforcement, or trust boundaries.


---

#### SA-8.14 — Least Privilege (Enhancement)

This requirement is about giving every part of your systems only the permissions it truly needs to do its job, and nothing more. Without this, an error, misuse, or security incident in one component could spread further than necessary, allowing unauthorised people to change settings, view sensitive records, or interfere with critical functions such as logging and key handling.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design, build, operate, and update all information systems and the parts that affect security. This includes identity and access controls, audit and logging, key management, and administrative interfaces. Permissions must be kept to the minimum needed.

This approach must be built into the organisation’s way of working throughout the entire lifecycle—from defining requirements and designing the system, through development, implementation, and later changes. It must also be clearly documented in the system’s security and privacy requirements, architecture, security plan, and related procurement and design records.

##### Least-privilege role engineering with interface-scoped authorisation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Design, implement, and continuously verify **least privilege** for **all security-relevant system components** (for example: identity and access control, audit logging, key and certificate management, and administrative interfaces) so each component and interface can be used **only with the minimum permissions needed** for its purpose.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(14)** enhancement requires applying the security principle of **least privilege** to the organisation-defined set of **systems or system components** (parameter: `sa-08.14_odp`). The requirement is that permissions are **enough to do the job, but not more**, and that access to interfaces and internal parts is broken down into the **finest practical level of detail**.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Create an authorisation agreement from interface to action** for each security-relevant interface (administration consoles, audit configuration endpoints, audit data access/view endpoints, key and certificate management application programming interfaces (APIs), and any privileged operational endpoints). Define which actions are allowed for each interface and each role (for example: an audit manager configures settings; an audit operator safely collects and stores audit data; an audit reviewer can only view).
2. **Set up roles and permissions to match the agreement** using role-based access control (RBAC) and attribute-based access control (ABAC) in the enterprise identity provider, ensuring that:
   - standard users cannot access administrative, audit, or key-management capabilities;
   - audit reviewer roles are strictly **read-only** for audit data;
   - key-management roles are limited to the required key lifecycle tasks (for example: generate, rotate, import, export where applicable) and explicitly exclude unrelated administrative actions.
3. **Apply least privilege at the application boundary** by implementing server-side permission checks for every security-relevant action (do not rely on hiding options in the user interface). Ensure each application programming interface (API) endpoint, controller, and service method maps to the authorisation agreement and **denies by default**.
