4. **Apply least privilege inside the application design** by structuring modules so they only work with the specific elements they need, using the most limited access possible (for example: read-only access for reviewer functions; write access only within the narrow module that performs audit data ingestion and storage; key material access confined to the key-management module).
5. **Add automated permission regression tests and detect changes**:
   - tests that confirm prohibited actions fail for each role and interface;
   - periodic checks that the actual permissions (identity provider groups and roles, and application authorisation rules) still match the documented minimums.
6. **Document the least-privilege design** in the system security architecture/design records and the system security plan, including the interface-to-action agreement, role definitions, and proof that enforcement is working (test results and review outputs).

###### WHO
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineer**: owns the interface-to-action authorisation agreement and the internal module least-privilege design.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: implements server-side authorisation and module boundaries.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead**: implements RBAC/ABAC roles and permissions, and any just-in-time (JIT) or approval process for privileged actions.
- **Security Assurance / Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC)**: ensures documentation and evidence are kept for audits.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every security-relevant interface, only the defined subset of roles can perform each action; all other attempts are denied (confirmed by automated tests).
- Audit reviewer functions are strictly view-only (no configuration, no ingestion, and no key or administration actions).
- Key-management actions are limited to the key-management module and granted only to the minimum required roles.
- Application authorisation is enforced on the server for each action (no reliance on client-side controls).
- Evidence is available: the interface-to-action agreement, role and permission mappings, automated regression test results, and records of periodic permission drift reviews.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up an agreement that clearly defines who is allowed to perform actions for each security-relevant interface (administration, audit settings, access to audit data, and key and certificate management).
- [ ] Set up narrowly defined user roles and permissions in the enterprise identity provider (IdP) so they match the contract exactly, including read-only audit reviewer access and restricted key-management roles.
- [ ] Set up authorisation checks on the server for every security-related action, using a “deny by default” approach, and link each action to its contract.
- [ ] Restructure the application modules so each module contains only what it needs to do its job, and use the minimum required permissions (read or write) for its responsibilities.
- [ ] Create and run automated permission tests for each job role to confirm that blocked actions are prevented and allowed actions work correctly.
- [ ] Set up regular checks to confirm that users’ actual access permissions match the documented minimum access needed for their roles (least privilege).
- [ ] Publish and keep evidence in the system security architecture and design records, and in the system security plan, showing how responsibilities and connections between components work, and linking them to the test results.


---

#### SA-8.15 — Predicate Permission (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that the most sensitive access and the most damaging administrative changes cannot be approved or carried out by a single person working alone. The risk it addresses is that one mistake, deception, or misuse of authority could allow access to Secret and Classified (Secret) information, or make a high-impact change that is difficult to reverse.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design and operate its privileged access request process and approval checks for Secret and Classified (Secret) data access and high-impact administrative actions so that more than one authorised person must agree before anything can proceed. This agreement must happen either at the same time or through a defined step-by-step sequence, where each step depends on the previous one.

The organisation must apply this control across the full system lifecycle, from planning and design through building, deployment, and later changes. It must also keep clear records and documentation showing how approvals are enforced, including evidence that at least two separate authorised entities approved the same request before access was activated or the action was completed.

##### Enforce 2+ approver predicate permission for Secret access & high-impact admin

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement “predicate permission” for in-scope **systems or system components** by ensuring that **no single authorised person or account** can approve or complete access to **Secret/Classified (Secret)** data, or carry out **high-impact administrative actions**, without **two or more separate authorised approvals**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(15)** enhancement requires applying the security design principle of **predicate permission** within the organisation-defined scope (**sa-08.15_odp**: privileged access management workflows and approval gates for Secret and Classified (Secret) data access and high-impact administrative actions). The approach must reduce the risk that a single mistake, deception, or misuse of trust could lead to an outcome that cannot be undone and causes major harm.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Define the in-scope “predicates” and actions
- Create a clear list that maps:
  - **Secret/Classified (Secret) data access** predicates (for example: vault item categories, database structures such as schemas/tables, storage locations such as buckets/paths, and application programming interface (API) access scopes), and
  - **high-impact administrative actions** predicates (for example: turning audit logging on or off, changing privileged role assignments, rotating or breaking keys, changing access rules, turning monitoring off, and “break-glass” emergency access).
- For each predicate, define the required multi-approval model:
  - **Simultaneous**: approvals from **two different authorised entities** within a defined time window; and/or
  - **Sequential**: Approver A enables Approver B’s step, with the workflow designed so that no single approver can finish the action alone.

###### 2) Put the enforcement gate into the privileged access workflow (PAM)
- Send all in-scope requests through a central privileged access management (PAM) workflow (or an equivalent privileged access orchestration service).
- Configure the workflow so that:
  - The privileged access session/token, or the high-impact change, is **stopped** until the required approvals are in place.
  - The approver cannot approve their own request (require **separate identities** for each approval step).
  - The workflow design ensures **no single approver can approve more than one action** (approvals must be linked to the specific request and predicate).

###### 3) Link approvals to request integrity and stop weakening
- Make sure the approval decision is tied to the exact request details (for example: the target resource, action type, time window, and environment) and is recorded in a way that can be checked later.
- Keep approval-policy settings as version-controlled records, and separate duties for changes to the approval logic, so updates cannot weaken the predicate-permission requirement.

###### 4) Provide audit evidence suitable for assessment
- For every request, record:
  - request ID, predicate/action type, target resource, and environment,
  - each approver’s distinct identity, approval times, and
  - the execution time (or the reason for denial/cancellation).
- Send audit records to the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) and logging platform so independent checks can confirm that **at least two distinct authorised entities** approved before access is activated or completed.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **PAM / identity and access management (IAM) engineer**: implement workflow gating, identity linking, and audit logging.
- **Security architect / compliance owner**: define the predicates, approval models, and acceptance criteria.
- **Privileged access governance owner**: approve the in-scope mapping and ensure separation of duties for changes to approval-policy logic.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every in-scope Secret/Classified (Secret) access request and high-impact administrative action:
  - The system **must not** issue privileged access tokens/sessions or carry out the action until the required **multi-party predicate** is met.
  - Approvals must come from **two distinct authorised entities** (no single identity can complete the workflow).
  - The workflow must support the defined **simultaneous or sequential** approval model, with the required time window or step dependency enforced.
  - Audit evidence must show approver identities and times, and the execution/activation time.
- Any attempt to bypass the gate (for example: early execution, self-approval, or changing request details) must be blocked and logged.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define which access rules apply to in-scope Secret/Classified (Secret) information, and define the rules for high-impact administrative actions, mapped to privileged access management (PAM) request types.
- [ ] Set up privileged access management (PAM) approval rules so that changes require two out of two (2-of-2) or two out of three (2-of-3) separate authorised approvals, using either a shared time window and/or a required order of steps.
- [ ] Apply the permission check gate so that issuing privileged tokens or sessions and carrying out actions are blocked until the required approvals are in place.
- [ ] Set up identity binding so that a person cannot approve their own request, and so every approval is linked to the exact details of the request (the target, environment, action, and time window).
- [ ] Set up version-control approval policy settings and apply separation of duties for changes to the approval rules
- [ ] Enable and export audit records to security information and event management (SIEM) that capture the request ID, the identities of the approvers, the times approvals were made, and the times the actions were executed or activated, for evidence.


---

#### SA-8.16 — Self-reliant Trustworthiness (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure the systems that protect your business do not need to constantly “check in” with outside services to stay trustworthy. In practice, if a system’s security relies on a working connection to an external provider, an outage, interference, or tampering could cause the system to stop enforcing protections or to operate less securely.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- identify every system component that must be trustworthy for its security functions, and
- design and build those components so they are secure by default and can keep working safely on their own, using external connections only to add extra capability—not to maintain the basic level of trust.

The organisation must also define what happens if the system becomes isolated from external services, and ensure it can reconnect and resynchronise safely without weakening security.

Finally, these requirements must be built into how systems are specified, designed, developed, implemented, and changed, with clear records showing that the approach was considered and verified.

##### Design offline-capable security functions with safe resynchronisation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up self-reliant trustworthiness for all in-scope **systems or system components** by making them **trustworthy by default** and able to keep performing their security duties **on their own**. External systems (for example, identity, policy, time, logging, and monitoring) must only be used to **add capability**, not to provide the baseline trust level.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(16)** requires implementing the security design principle of **self-reliant trustworthiness** for the organisation-defined set of **{{ insert: param, sa-08.16_odp }}**. The guidance says systems should:
- rely less on other systems for their own trustworthiness,
- avoid “fail-open” behaviour when external connections become unreliable, and
- support **operate on their own first, then resynchronise safely**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **List in-scope trustworthy components**: For each system/component in scope, identify the security functions that must be trustworthy (for example, enforcing authorisation, validating keys/certificates, secure logging, enforcing malware/controls, and checking integrity).
2. **Set trust boundaries and local authoritative sources**: For each security function, define what is authoritative locally (for example, hardware-backed trust anchors, locally stored keys, signed policy bundles, and cached authorisation decisions protected with integrity checks).
3. **Classify external dependencies as supplementary vs baseline-required**: For each dependency, decide whether the security function can still work securely without it. Redesign any dependency that is baseline-required so the component can enforce security using locally pre-set or cryptographically verifiable information.
4. **Add isolation mode (no fail-open)**: For each security function, create a clear “dependency unavailable” mode. In this mode, the component must stay secure by default (typically **deny-by-default** or restricted operation aligned with the last-known-good policy/state, with integrity and expiry controls).
5. **Resynchronise safely when connectivity returns**: When connections come back, resynchronise using versioning and replay protection (for example, sequence numbers). Confirm the authenticity of updates (for example, signature verification) and apply conflict-handling rules that protect security (for example, reject conflicting policy updates and stay in restricted mode until reconciliation is complete).
6. **Create audit-ready design evidence**: Update architecture and security design documents to clearly cover: (a) trust boundaries, (b) dependency classification, (c) isolation mode behaviour, and (d) resynchronisation rules. Provide test evidence showing secure behaviour during simulated dependency outages.

###### WHO
- **Application Security Architect / Solution Architect**: responsible for trust-boundary design, dependency classification, and resynchronisation logic.
- **Engineering Lead / Software Engineers**: build offline-capable enforcement and reconciliation mechanisms.
- **Security Engineering / Assurance**: checks the design for “no fail-open” and validates the evidence.
- **Operations/Platform Team**: helps run dependency outage testing and confirms operational behaviour in production-like environments.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every in-scope system/component, the security functions that require trustworthiness can enforce security **without** connectivity to each external dependency classified as supplementary.
- During simulated external dependency outages, the system does **not** switch to fail-open behaviour; it operates in a defined isolation mode that matches security requirements (for example, deny-by-default or restricted last-known-good with integrity/expiry).
- When connectivity is restored, the system resynchronises using authenticated updates and replay/version controls, and reconciliation does not weaken security.
- Architecture/security design documentation and test results provide clear, auditable evidence of trust boundaries, isolation behaviour, and resynchronisation rules for the in-scope {{ insert: param, sa-08.16_odp }}.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a complete list of all in-scope systems and components ({{ insert: param, sa-08.16_odp }}) and match each one to the security functions that depend on it being trustworthy.
- [ ] Define the trust boundaries for each security function and set out the local, authoritative sources used to enforce the baseline requirements.
- [ ] For each external dependency, label it as either **supplementary** or **required as a baseline**. Then redesign any dependency that is **required as a baseline** so that it is **not necessary for trust**.
- [ ] Set up a clear “isolation mode” for each security function so it cannot fail open. For example, use a deny-by-default approach, or allow only a restricted “last known good” option that is protected by integrity checks and an expiry time.
- [ ] Set up authenticated resynchronisation that includes protection against replayed or out-of-date messages, and apply clear conflict-handling rules to ensure security safeguards are maintained.
- [ ] Update the architecture and security design documents to record the system’s trust boundaries, how it isolates components, and the rules for resynchronising data. Then run outage tests to produce evidence suitable for audit.


---

#### SA-8.17 — Secure Distributed Composition (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that when multiple parts of a distributed system are designed to apply the same security rules, the overall system enforces those rules just as effectively as each part does on its own. If this is not done, the way components work together can unintentionally weaken protection, creating opportunities for unauthorised access, incorrect handling of sensitive information, or inconsistent application of security rules.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly identify which distributed components are intended to enforce the same security policy. It must then use documented, repeatable procedures to apply this approach consistently across specification, design, development, implementation, and any changes.

The organisation must also produce and keep up to date design and security architecture documents, along with security and privacy requirements and specifications. These documents must show that the overall system has been analysed to confirm consistent, system-wide enforcement. Evidence of this analysis must be recorded in the system security plan and in supporting records.

##### Secure distributed composition via policy contract and composition test gates

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement secure distributed composition by (1) deciding which distributed components apply the same system security rules, (2) writing those shared rules in a form that can be repeated and checked by machines, and (3) using automated system build and integration test checks (“test gates”) to confirm that the combined system enforces the rules at least as well as each component does on its own.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-8(17) requires implementing the security design principle of **secure distributed composition**: when distributed components that apply the same system security rules are combined, the resulting system must enforce those rules **at least as well as** the individual components. The guidance also requires a thorough review of the combined system’s security design to ensure consistent rule enforcement, including how components communicate and how distributed data is kept consistent.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the shared-rules component set**: Create a “policy contract” that links the specific distributed components (for example: API gateway, authorisation service, microservices, data access layer, messaging consumers/producers) to the **same** security rule scope (for example: authorisation for a resource type, encryption-in-transit requirement, audit/logging requirement, input validation rules).
2. **Make the rules enforceable and consistent**: Implement the shared rules as version-controlled policy-as-code (or equivalent enterprise policy templates) and ensure enforcement cannot be avoided through internal calls between services (for example: use the identity provider/authorisation service as the consistent decision point; use “deny by default” for unauthorised service-to-service requests).
3. **Add composition test gates**: In continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD), require automated integration/composition tests that cover real interaction paths between the identified components, including negative and failure scenarios (for example: missing or invalid tokens, revoked roles, inconsistent claims, attempts to downgrade communication protocols, and stale or replicated data situations). Tests must confirm that the combined system does not allow access or processing that any single component would have blocked.
4. **Analyse distributed interactions and record results**: Carry out and document architecture-level analysis of how communication protocols and any distributed data consistency methods affect rule enforcement (for example: time windows for eventual consistency, retry behaviour, and message re-delivery). Keep the analysis outputs and test results as audit-ready records.
5. **Integrate into the lifecycle and provide oversight**: Set up a repeatable process that requires the policy contract and composition test gates to be applied during specification, design, development, implementation, and any changes for the defined component set. Assign named owners (security/privacy requirements owner and release approver) to ensure the gates are not skipped.

###### WHO
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the policy contract structure, the policy-as-code approach, and the composition test strategy.
- **Platform/DevOps Engineering**: implements the CI/CD composition test gates and ensures they run for relevant changes.
- **Application Owner / Release Manager**: ensures the process is followed during specification/design/development/implementation/change.
- **Information Security (oversight)**: reviews the architecture analysis and approves any exceptions.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented **policy contract** exists that clearly identifies the distributed components applying the same security rule scope.
- The shared rules are implemented as **version-controlled, repeatable** policy records (policy-as-code or equivalent enterprise templates), and enforcement is consistent for interactions between components/services.
- CI/CD composition test gates run for changes affecting the defined component set and provide evidence that the combined system enforces the rules **at least as effectively** as individual components (including negative and failure-mode tests).
- Architecture analysis records show that communication protocols and any distributed data consistency mechanisms do not create unexpected gaps in rule enforcement; evidence is retained for audit.
- The lifecycle process is in place and enforced (with named responsibilities), so that changes to the component set require updated policy records and passing composition test gates.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a policy agreement that lists the set of distributed components that implement the same security policy scope (sa-08.17_odp).
- [ ] Put the shared security rules into version-controlled “policy as code” (or equivalent enterprise policy templates), and make sure that calls between services cannot avoid these security checks.
- [ ] Define measurable “at least as effectively” requirements for the policy scope, for example: the allowed or denied outcomes, the logging and audit events that must be recorded, and the encryption requirements.
- [ ] Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) checks (“test gates”) that run integration tests across the identified components, including tests for expected failures and negative scenarios.
- [ ] Review the communication protocols and any methods used to keep distributed data consistent, and record your findings and test evidence for audit.
- [ ] Update the system lifecycle procedure so that any specification, design, development, implementation, or modification of the component set must pass the required composition test checkpoints and include approved updates to the policy contract.


---

#### SA-8.18 — Trusted Communications Channels (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that when your systems send **Confidential** or **Secret** (classified) information over network connections, the communication route is truly trustworthy from start to finish. If it is not, sensitive information could be intercepted, copied, or changed while it is travelling between systems, or the wrong systems could connect and receive information they should not have.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must first identify every system component that sends **Confidential** or **Secret** information over network links. It must then decide what level of protection is required for each type of data and for each pair of communicating components. For every such connection, access must be limited so that only authorised, appropriately trusted endpoints can use it. The information must also be protected while it is in transit, so it cannot be intercepted or tampered with.

The organisation must document this approach in its procurement and design documents, security planning and architecture, assign clear responsibilities to named roles, and keep records showing that the approach was applied during specification, development, implementation, and any changes.

##### mTLS trusted channels for Confidential/Secret data flows

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up trusted communication links for all system components that send **Confidential or Classified (Secret)** information across network connections by:
1) **Limiting** which endpoints can communicate (endpoint trust alignment), and
2) Using **end-to-end protections** to protect data while it is travelling (encryption + protection against tampering + authenticated sessions).

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(18)** enhancement requires applying the security design principle of **trusted communications channels**. The guidance says each communication link must be trustworthy to the level needed for the security functions it supports. This is done by **restricting access to the link** and using **end-to-end protections** to stop data being intercepted or changed.

The organisation-defined parameter **sa-08.18_odp** applies this to **all system components that transmit Confidential or Classified (Secret) data over network interconnections**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Identify in-scope trusted channels
- Create and maintain an authoritative inventory of network connections (service-to-service calls, API calls, message bus topics, database links, file transfers, remote administration channels) where the data payload is **Confidential** or **Secret**.
- For each data flow, record: source component, destination component, data classification, and the level of security dependency (for example, whether the destination relies on the link for security functions such as authentication/authorisation decisions, sharing key material, or actions that must be recorded for audit purposes).

###### 2) Set trusted-channel requirements based on sensitivity and dependency
- Define two channel tiers that match the data classification:
  - **Confidential**: require authenticated encryption in transit with strong Transport Layer Security (TLS) settings (for example, TLS 1.2 or later), certificate checks, and protection against tampering.
  - **Secret**: require stronger assurance (for example, TLS 1.3 where feasible), stricter certificate checks, shorter certificate lifetimes and more frequent certificate rotation, and stronger key and cipher rules.
- Assign each data flow to the required tier based on **sa-08.18_odp** (Confidential vs Secret) and the security dependency it supports.

###### 3) Enforce endpoint trust and restrict access to the channel
- Use **mutual authentication** at the transport level using **mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS)** (or an equivalent mutual-authenticated transport) for service-to-service communications.
- Use an enterprise workload identity approach (workload certificates or SPIFFE-like identities) integrated with the organisation’s identity and certificate governance.
- Apply allow-lists so only authorised endpoint identities can connect (for example, service mesh policies, Kubernetes NetworkPolicies, security groups, or ZTNA broker rules).

###### 4) Apply end-to-end protections for data in transit
- Configure clients and servers to require:
  - encryption while data is travelling,
  - protection against tampering,
  - authenticated sessions only (no unauthenticated fallback),
  - certificate validation (including SAN/hostname, Extended Key Usage (EKU), trusted issuer chain, and certificate revocation checks where feasible).
- Ensure protection is **end-to-end between the communicating components** (not just protected “hop by hop”), consistent with the guidance.

###### 5) Make the approach auditable and controlled for change
- Store the trusted-channel requirements and mappings (flow inventory plus tier requirements) in architecture and security design documents.
- Use policy-as-code (where supported) so deployments that try to create Confidential/Secret flows without the required mTLS tier are blocked during CI/CD (continuous integration and continuous delivery).
- Keep evidence: mTLS handshake logs/telemetry, certificate validation results, and records showing network policy enforcement.

###### WHO
- **Solution/Platform Architect**: defines trusted-channel tiers and maps data flows to requirements.
- **Security Engineering / Identity and Access Management (IAM) and Public Key Infrastructure (PKI) Team**: issues workload identities/certificates and defines certificate validation standards.
- **Network/Platform Engineering**: implements mTLS and network/service allow-lists.
- **Application Owners**: ensure services use the required mutual-authenticated transport for in-scope flows.
- **Compliance/Assurance**: checks evidence and coverage against the flow inventory.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For **100%** of in-scope flows identified under **sa-08.18_odp**, communications use mutual authentication (mTLS or equivalent) with encryption and tamper protection.
- No Confidential/Secret flow is allowed without the required channel tier (Confidential vs Secret) and endpoint allow-list enforcement.
- Evidence exists for each in-scope flow showing: which endpoint identities were used, that certificate validation succeeded, and that channel access restrictions were enforced.
- CI/CD (or an equivalent change control mechanism) prevents deployment or configuration that would introduce a Confidential/Secret channel without the required trusted-channel protections.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a complete list of every connection between networks where Confidential or Classified (Secret) information is sent, and label each data transfer with its source, destination, and sensitivity level.
- [ ] Define trusted-channel levels (Confidential versus Secret), specifying the required mutual authentication and the required strength of transport security.
- [ ] Provide workload identities and certificates for all in-scope endpoints, and standardise the rules for validating certificates.
- [ ] Set up services to use mutual transport authentication (mTLS, or an equivalent mutual-authenticated connection) and turn off any non-authenticated or weaker fallback connection options.
- [ ] Set up endpoint allow-lists and enforce network and service rules so that only authorised identities can set up each trusted connection.
- [ ] For each in-scope flow, enable and keep audit evidence, including: mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS) connection handshakes and telemetry, results of certificate checks, and logs showing policy enforcement.
- [ ] Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) “policy-as-code” checks to stop deployments that introduce or change Confidential or Secret data flows unless the required trusted-channel tier is in place.


---

#### SA-8.19 — Continuous Protection (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring the part of the system that enforces security rules is always switched on, reliable, and protected, so sensitive information is never left unprotected. Without this, there could be short or hidden periods where unauthorised people gain access, or where the system behaves unsafely during start-up, shutdown, failures, or recovery—including after a successful attack.

The organisation must ensure that every request that could affect security is checked by the security enforcement mechanism, that the mechanism itself cannot be changed by unauthorised people, and that the organisation can prove through analysis and testing that the mechanism is complete and works as intended.

The organisation must also keep the system in a secure state when errors occur, and recover safely back to normal operation, a reduced operation mode, or an alternative operating mode. When security settings are changed for different configurations, the organisation must clearly explain why the change is needed for operations, verify that it will not create an insecure state, and show that the change is applied cleanly—without leftover effects from the previous settings that could conflict.

##### Always-on reference monitor with fail-closed request gating

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement the organisation-defined **reference monitor and its enforcement components** (SA-8(19) ODP: *“the information system security reference monitor and its enforcement components”*) to provide **continuous protection**. This means they stay protected and enforce the security policy **without gaps** during **system start-up, normal operation, failures, interruptions, and shutdown**, and in both **full** and **degraded** operating modes.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-8(19) requires implementing the **continuous protection** security design principle for the reference monitor/enforcement components. This means:
- **No time when protection is missing** while data/requests are being created, stored, processed, communicated, or while the system is starting, running, failing, recovering, or shutting down.
- **Reference monitor checks**: every relevant request is reviewed; the reference monitor can protect itself from tampering; and correct and complete operation can be shown through analysis and testing.
- **Safe failure and recovery**: keep the system in a secure state during errors, faults, or successful attacks, and recover safely to normal, degraded, or alternative modes.
- **Configuration changes**: any policy changes must be traceable to operational need and **verifiable** to prevent insecure states.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Define the enforcement boundary and ensure all relevant requests pass through it
- Identify every request route that can affect security policy enforcement (for example: user interface/application programming interface (API)/administration endpoints, service-to-service calls, scheduled or asynchronous jobs, and message consumers).
- Ensure each route is handled by the reference monitor/enforcement components (for example: API gateway/authorisation service, policy engine, service mesh authorisation layer, or host-level enforcement), with **no bypass routes**.

###### 2) Use fail-closed start-up with readiness gates
- Add clear **service readiness gates** so the application (and any request listeners) cannot accept or process requests until the reference monitor/enforcement components are:
  - policy loaded,
  - integrity verified,
  - dependencies healthy, and
  - enforcement confirmed as active.
- Configure the runtime/orchestration to **deny by default** when enforcement is not available (fail-closed), rather than allowing requests through (fail-open).

###### 3) Keep enforcement active during shutdown and restart
- During shutdown/restart, keep enforcement active until the system can no longer process requests.
- Stop accepting new requests first, then drain/terminate in a controlled order, so there is no period where requests are handled without enforcement.

###### 4) Implement secure degraded-mode behaviour without losing protection
- For degraded configurations, define a **secure degraded mode** where enforcement stays active and continues to validate requests (possibly with reduced capability), but without creating unprotected periods.
- Ensure degraded mode still maintains a secure state and does not weaken authorisation checks.

###### 5) Provide evidence through analysis and testing (audit-ready)
- Produce test evidence showing:
  - unauthorised requests are blocked during start-up, normal operation, and restart windows,
  - enforcement remains active during induced failures (for example: dependency loss or policy service restart),
  - and no request route bypasses the reference monitor.
- Include negative tests for situations such as “policy not loaded” or “enforcement not ready”.

###### WHO
- **Application/Platform Architect**: defines the enforcement boundary and how requests are routed for mediation.
- **Security Engineering Lead**: defines the fail-closed and degraded-mode security behaviour.
- **Platform/DevOps Engineer**: implements readiness gates, shutdown ordering, and runtime/orchestration configuration.
- **QA/Security Testing**: runs negative and chaos-style tests and compiles evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- **Start-up (Boot)**: No security-relevant request is accepted or processed until the reference monitor/enforcement components confirm enforcement readiness and policy integrity is verified.
- **Normal operation (Runtime)**: All security-relevant request routes are mediated by the reference monitor/enforcement components (no bypass routes).
- **Restart/Shutdown**: There is no observable period where requests are processed without enforcement.
- **Failure/Degraded mode**: During induced failures and degraded configurations, the system keeps a secure state (fail-closed or secure degraded mode) and continues to validate requests.
- **Evidence**: Automated and manual test results (including negative tests) demonstrate continuous protection across start-up, normal operation, failure, interruption, and shutdown, with documented analysis showing that request mediation is complete.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and record all request routes that affect security, and confirm they are controlled by the system’s security decision and enforcement components.
- [ ] Set the system to “fail closed” during startup by keeping listeners and routes disabled until the policy has been loaded and enforcement is confirmed to be active.
- [ ] Add integrity checks for the reference monitor and enforcement components, and stop operation (fail closed) if the checks do not pass.
- [ ] Set up a controlled shutdown and restart sequence so the system stops accepting new requests before enforcement is turned off, preventing any period where requests could be handled without protection.
- [ ] Define and implement secure “degraded mode” behaviour that continues to check authorisation and does not weaken policy checks.
- [ ] Create and run tests that confirm failures during start-up, restart, and “enforcement unavailable” situations, to prove that unauthorised actions are blocked while the system is transitioning
- [ ] Collect audit-ready proof (timelines, enforcement status reporting, and test results) showing continuous protection across all stages of the system’s lifecycle.


---

#### SA-8.20 — Secure Metadata Management (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that information which describes other information—such as file labels, classifications, and access rules—is treated as important and protected on its own. If these details are not protected, or if they can be changed, people could accidentally or deliberately get around the rules that decide who can see what. This could result in unauthorised disclosure or sensitive information being leaked.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure that the system and all parts that handle this “describing information” correctly connect each label or classification to the right item, apply the organisation’s confidentiality and accuracy rules to the describing information itself, and check that it is reliable enough to support correct access decisions. This includes protecting describing information even when it is stored separately from the data it refers to, supporting describing information that points to other describing information, and, where the organisation uses multiple sensitivity levels, ensuring items that contain describing information are labelled appropriately.

The organisation must define these practices, document them, and assign responsibility for them across the system’s full lifecycle—from planning and design through building, deployment, and change management.

##### Cryptographically protected metadata trust boundary

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement secure metadata management by treating security metadata (for example, labels/classifications and access-control information) as “first class” security items. Keep metadata confidential and accurate so it cannot be changed to bypass access decisions. Make sure metadata is correctly linked to the data it applies to, including metadata that describes other metadata.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-8(20)** enhancement requires implementing the security design principle of **secure metadata management** for the organisation-defined scope (**sa-08.20_odp: systems or system components that provide metadata services, such as label/classification and access-control metadata handlers**). The guidance says metadata must be protected whenever the security policy requires full protection of information or “self-protection” by the security system. Systems cannot self-protect unless they protect the data they rely on to work correctly. In multi-level security (MLS) environments, items that contain metadata must be labelled, and the metadata must support correct access decisions.

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define a metadata “trust boundary” and allow only “metadata-only” updates
- Create a dedicated **Metadata Services** boundary (a logical service/module) responsible for creating, updating, and providing security metadata (labels/classifications and access-control metadata).
- Block direct metadata updates from application users/systems. Allow changes only through authorised processes (for example, an admin/approval workflow, a privileged service identity, and role checks).

###### 2) Link metadata to its target data using integrity protection
- For each metadata record, include unchangeable linking fields (for example, **resource/object identifier**, tenant, environment, and version) and the metadata values used for enforcement (for example, label/classification and access-control attributes).
- Protect the full metadata content (including the linking fields) using **cryptographic integrity** (for example, a digital signature or message authentication code (MAC)) created by the Metadata Services trusted identity.

###### 3) Check metadata integrity before enforcing access (fail closed)
- Any component that uses metadata to make authorisation decisions must verify the integrity protection **before** using the metadata values.
- If verification fails (missing/invalid signature/MAC, linking fields do not match the intended target, or the version is outdated), the system must **fail closed** (deny access or apply the lowest-privilege/most restrictive policy) and record an auditable security event.

###### 4) Protect metadata confidentiality
- Encrypt metadata while it is being sent (in transit) and when it is stored (at rest) for metadata stores and metadata application programming interfaces (APIs).
- Use least-privilege access controls for metadata stores so only Metadata Services and authorised enforcement components can read metadata.

###### 5) Support metadata that refers to other metadata (self-referential metadata)
- If metadata includes references to other metadata (for example, classification/impact levels of a filename that is itself metadata), ensure the integrity protection covers the reference identifiers. When enforcing, resolve and verify the referenced metadata using the same integrity approach.

###### 6) MLS alignment (if applicable in your environment)
- Ensure items that contain metadata are labelled according to the MLS scheme used by the organisation.
- Ensure subjects/objects have sensitivity levels, and that enforcement uses the verified metadata labels to control access.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Application Owner / System Owner**: accountable for designing the metadata trust boundary and the enforcement behaviour.
- **Security Architect**: defines the metadata structure (schema), linking fields, integrity approach, and MLS alignment.
- **Platform/Backend Engineers**: implement metadata services, signing/MAC verification, and fail-closed enforcement.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM)/Privileged Access Administrator**: sets up authorised workflows and service identities for metadata updates.
- **Security Operations**: ensures monitoring/alerting and collects evidence for metadata integrity failures.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Metadata updates happen only through the defined Metadata Services trust boundary and authorised workflows.
- Every metadata record includes an integrity protection mechanism that cryptographically covers both the metadata values and the linking fields for the target object.
- Enforcement components verify metadata integrity before using it; invalid metadata leads to fail-closed access decisions and generates audit evidence.
- Metadata at rest and in transit is encrypted; metadata stores are access-controlled using least privilege.
- For self-referential metadata, referenced metadata is resolved and verified using the same integrity mechanism.
- (If MLS is in scope) items containing metadata are labelled, and access mediation uses verified metadata labels consistent with the MLS model.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and list all systems that manage label or classification information and access-control metadata, and document where that metadata is created, stored, changed, and used.
- [ ] Define a metadata structure that includes fixed (unchangeable) target details and the metadata values that will be used to enforce the requirements.
- [ ] Set up a separate “metadata services” trust boundary that only allows metadata updates through approved workflows and using privileged service identities.
- [ ] Add cryptographic integrity checks (a digital signature or a message authentication code (MAC)) to every metadata record, covering both the metadata values and the fields that link the metadata to its intended target.
- [ ] Update all points where access decisions are made to check that the metadata is complete and has not been altered before using it, and to deny access if the check fails.
- [ ] Require encryption while data is being sent and while it is stored for metadata application programming interfaces (APIs) and metadata storage systems, and limit access to metadata data to the minimum permissions needed (least-privilege access).
- [ ] Record and keep audit evidence of any metadata integrity failures and any metadata update events, so they can be used for security monitoring and review.


---

#### SA-8.21 — Self-analysis (Enhancement)

This requirement is about building in self-checks so the system can review its own condition when it starts and while it is running, and only continue if everything is working as expected. Without these built-in checks, a harmful change, a malfunction, or a temporary fault could go undetected and then spread, causing the hypervisor and critical services to run in an unsafe or incorrect way.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure that the trusted boot firmware, the hypervisor, and the critical service components are designed and built to carry out self-checks at several points during operation. They must assess their own internal condition to a limited extent and verify that the higher-level components they rely on are intact and working correctly, creating a trusted start-up sequence.

The results of these checks must be used to stop or reduce the impact of errors so they do not spread, and to confirm that each component’s configuration matches the expected configuration. The organisation must document how this is applied across the full build and change lifecycle, assign named responsibilities, and keep evidence showing it was implemented and verified.

##### Stage-based self-analysis for trusted boot, hypervisor, and services

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement self-checks across the trusted boot firmware, the hypervisor, and the organisation’s critical service components. Each component should be able to check its own internal state and, to a limited extent, the integrity and/or functionality of the next higher-level component(s) at multiple points during start-up and execution. Use these results to stop externally caused errors, internal malfunctions, or temporary faults from spreading, and to identify configuration conflicts against the expected configuration.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication 800-53, Security Assessment and Authorisation (SA)-8(21) enhancement requires: “Implement the security design principle of self-analysis in {{ insert: param, sa-08.21_odp }}.” The guidance calls for hierarchical self-checking, including a trusted boot style “chain of trust” (the root verifies itself; lower-level components verify higher-level components), and using the results to prevent errors and detect configuration conflicts.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the self-check scope (per sa-08.21_odp)**: clearly list the trusted boot firmware, the hypervisor, and the organisation-defined “critical service components” that must participate in self-checking.
2. **Create an expected configuration baseline for each participating component**: include signed and verified firmware and hypervisor images, plus a release manifest for critical services (for example: image digests/hashes, configuration schema version, and the allowed set of dependencies).
3. **Implement hierarchical, stage-based self-checks**:
   - **Root/firmware stage**: carry out limited internal self-tests (for example, firmware integrity/ROM integrity) and verify the hypervisor using measured/verified boot; record the measurements/attestations.
   - **Hypervisor stage**: carry out limited internal self-tests (for example, basic CPU/memory feature sanity checks and hypervisor integrity) and verify each critical service virtual machine/container image and its configuration digest against the release manifest before starting it.
   - **Critical service stage**: carry out limited internal self-checks (for example, configuration schema validation, dependency availability checks, and basic functional health checks) before accepting workloads.
4. **Use fail-closed behaviour to prevent spread**: if any stage self-check fails (for example, integrity mismatch, configuration conflict, or self-test failure), the component must refuse to proceed to the next stage and enter a documented safe/recovery mode that limits impact (for example, stop booting into maintenance/recovery, or keep services offline).
5. **Use self-check results as auditable evidence and operational safeguards**: send stage identifiers and pass/fail outcomes (including the reason for any configuration digest match/mismatch) to a central logging and monitoring system; raise alerts for “chain broken” and “configuration conflict” events.

###### WHO
- **Platform/Infrastructure Security Architect**: defines the scope and baseline requirements.
- **Hypervisor/Systems Engineer**: configures measured/verified boot, implements hypervisor pre-start validation, and implements fail-closed logic.
- **Application/Service Owner**: implements critical service pre-flight configuration validation and limited self-tests.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / Monitoring Engineer**: integrates evidence into security information and event management (SIEM) and monitoring, and defines alerting.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The trusted boot firmware verifies the hypervisor and records measurable/attestable results.
- The hypervisor verifies the integrity and configuration digests of each critical service component before starting it.
- Each critical service detects configuration conflicts against the expected baseline (for example, schema/version/digest mismatch) and refuses to start/accept workloads when there is a mismatch.
- Any self-check failure triggers fail-closed behaviour that prevents propagation beyond the failing component (the documented safe/recovery mode is used).
- Evidence is produced for each stage (pass/fail plus reason codes) and is centrally logged with timestamps and stage identifiers, with alerting for chain-break/config-conflict events.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] List and document the components involved: trusted boot firmware, the hypervisor, and the critical service components.
- [ ] For each participating component, define and publish the expected “baseline” configuration, including signed images and the release manifest’s digest and schema version.
- [ ] Turn on measured and verified boot, and configure the firmware to check the hypervisor and record the proof (attestation) and measurements.
- [ ] Set up checks before the hypervisor starts to confirm that the critical service image and its configuration match the release record (release manifest).
- [ ] In each critical service, run limited self-checks and validate the configuration against an agreed schema, and record clear pass or fail results with specific reason codes.
- [ ] Set the system to “fail closed” so that if a stage fails, the next stage will not start, and a documented safe or recovery mode will be triggered.
- [ ] Add evidence of stage pass/fail results and configuration conflicts to the central logging and monitoring system, with alerts for broken process chains and configuration mismatches.


---

#### SA-8.22 — Accountability and Traceability (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that every important security action can be traced to the exact person or service that carried it out, and that the record shows the correct order of events. Without this, unauthorised activity is more likely to go unnoticed, blame can be wrongly assigned to the wrong person, and investigators may not be able to work out what happened after an incident.  

To meet this expectation, your on-premises audit logging and security monitoring setup must record security-relevant actions with clear “who did what for whom” details, link related events across different parts of the system, and keep timestamps consistent so the sequence can be reliably reconstructed. Just as importantly, the audit trail must be protected so only authorised staff can access it, and no one without permission can change, delete, or tamper with the records.

##### Accountability-ready audit events with immutable, ordered storage

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement the security principle of **accountability and traceability** by making sure the organisation’s on-premises audit logging system produces and stores security-relevant events that:
- clearly identify the **actor** (user or service) and the **on-behalf-of target** for actions involving a subject and an object,
- keep the **correct order** of actions using reliable time information,
- and protect the **audit trail** from unauthorised access and changes.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(22)** requires implementing accountability and traceability so security-relevant actions can be traced back to the entity on whose behalf the action was taken. It also requires a trustworthy audit setup that records the right details, supports correct event ordering, and protects the audit trail from unauthorised access or modification.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and enforce a standard audit event format** across the on-premises audit logging system and related security monitoring components (for example, log collection agents and audit generation services). The format must include, at minimum:
   - `actor` identity (human user or service account),
   - `actor.auth_method` (how the actor was authenticated),
   - `on_behalf_of.target` (the user/account/resource/system context the action is carried out for),
   - `action` and `object` identifiers,
   - `correlation_id` (to link related events across components),
   - `event_time` (time from the original event) and `ingest_time` (time the collector received it).
2. **Add identity details early and consistently** at the log collection or audit generation stage so events never include “unknown” actor or on-behalf-of information. Fill `on_behalf_of.target` using authoritative enterprise sources already available in the environment (for example, identity and access management logs and privileged delegation records). Where delegation applies, ensure delegated or administrative actions record both the delegator and the delegatee.
3. **Carry correlation identifiers through the full process**: make sure the correlation ID is created at the start of a security-relevant workflow (or derived in a consistent way from an existing session or transaction identifier) and is passed through log collection, security information and event management (SIEM) correlation, and audit trail storage.
4. **Ensure reliable event ordering using time synchronisation**: configure and run the on-premises time synchronisation service so all components that generate audit events, collectors, and the SIEM correlation engine use the same trusted time source(s). Check for time drift and raise alerts for time issues to prevent incorrect reconstruction of event sequences.
5. **Store the audit trail as append-only and tamper-evident**: save audit events in the on-premises audit trail storage using an append-only approach with strict access control (role-based access control (RBAC)) and integrity protection (for example, cryptographic signing or chaining per batch or per event). Limit administrative capabilities that could change or remove audit records.
6. **Secure the ways audit data can be accessed**: apply least privilege for SIEM users and services (separate roles for search, export, and administration). Ensure audit storage application programming interfaces (APIs) and agents use strong authentication and are protected against unauthorised viewing or export.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architect / Compliance Architect**: owns the standard audit event format and the acceptance criteria.
- **SIEM/Logging Engineering Lead**: implements enrichment, correlation ID propagation, and SIEM integration.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineer**: implements and maintains time synchronisation and secure audit storage configuration.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / Security Operations**: checks that audit trails support routine and investigative (forensic) analysis, and that access controls meet operational needs.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Every security-relevant event in the on-premises audit trail includes a non-empty, uniquely identifiable `actor` and `on_behalf_of.target`.
- Related events across log collection, SIEM correlation, and audit storage can be reconstructed using `correlation_id`.
- Event ordering can be reproduced: for a test workflow, the reconstructed sequence matches the real execution order, and time drift alerts trigger when thresholds are exceeded.
- Audit trail storage is append-only and tamper-evident: attempts to modify or delete records are blocked or result in verifiable integrity failures.
- Only authorised roles can view or export audit records; unauthorised access attempts are denied and recorded.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define a standard audit event format that includes: the person or system that carried out the action (actor identity), the intended recipient or target acting “on behalf of” (on-behalf-of target), a reference number to link related events (correlation_id), the time the event happened (event_time), and identifiers for the item involved and the action taken (object/action identifiers).
- [ ] Set up identity-first enrichment in the on-premises audit logging system to fill in who performed the action (the actor) and who they acted for (on-behalf-of) using trusted, authoritative sources.
- [ ] Pass the `correlation_id` through log collection, security information and event management (SIEM) correlation, and audit trail storage so that events can be linked end to end.
- [ ] Set up and check that all systems that produce audit records and the security information and event management (SIEM) correlation engine have the correct time, and put in place monitoring for time drift with alerts if it happens.
- [ ] Set up the audit trail storage so that records can only be added (append-only) and cannot be altered without detection (for example, using cryptographic signing or linked records), and limit administrative access to authorised interfaces only.
- [ ] Use least-privilege role-based access control (RBAC) for access to the security information and event management (SIEM) system and audit storage (create separate permissions for searching, exporting, and administration), and check that unauthorised access is blocked.


---

#### SA-8.23 — Secure Defaults (Enhancement)

This expectation is about ensuring that every system feature that protects people and information begins in a strict, safe state. It should block access by default and only allow actions when there is clear permission. Without this, a system could unintentionally allow access when it is first installed, after updates, or when start-up problems occur—letting unauthorised users enter, carry out privileged actions, or turn off important security recording.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- identify all system components that make access decisions, handle logins, manage permissions, and produce security logs;
- define and document a restrictive default configuration for each component, including what is blocked unless explicitly allowed;
- ensure the default “as shipped” settings are controlled and not weakened;
- ensure teams follow the documented procedures throughout the system’s lifecycle; and
- ensure the system either starts securely or refuses security-relevant actions if initial setup fails.

##### Secure-default baselines for authz/authn and security logging

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set secure default settings for every in-scope security feature in the application (including access control, authentication, authorisation, and security logging). By default, the system must run in a strict “deny unless explicitly authorised” mode, including during start-up and when failures occur.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(23)** requires applying the security design principle of **secure defaults** to the organisation-defined scope (**sa-08.23_odp: systems or system components that provide security functions, including access control, authentication, authorisation, and logging**). This means:
- The **default “out of the box” configuration** must be restrictive and cautious.
- Security controls must **block requests unless the request is correctly formed and matches the security policy**.
- If **start-up fails**, the system must either **carry out the requested work using secure defaults** or **not carry out the work**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a component map** covering the whole application. Identify every security feature and where it connects to other systems (for example: web/application programming interface (API) authentication middleware, admin consoles, identity and access management (IAM) / identity provider (IdP) integration, API gateways, service-to-service authentication, authorisation checks, audit/log pipelines, and any “fallback” modes).
2. **Define secure-default rules for each component** using a clear deny-by-default approach:
   - Default access is **deny** for unauthenticated/unauthorised users and for privileged actions.
   - Authorisation must be **explicit** (role-based access control (RBAC) / attribute-based access control (ABAC)), with no automatic “admin” permissions and no permissive fallback behaviour.
   - Security logging/auditing must be **enabled by default** for authentication and authorisation outcomes (including denials and actions that affect privileges).
3. **Use “fail closed” start-up behaviour** for security dependencies:
   - If the application cannot validate tokens, cannot obtain required policy/keys/metadata, or cannot reach required authorisation/logging dependencies, it must **block security-relevant actions** or **refuse to start/serve** (choose what best fits the application’s operating model, but ensure it aligns with secure-defaults).
   - Any reduced-functionality (“degraded”) mode must still be **deny unless explicitly authorised**.
4. **Document and enforce secure-default baselines** as auditable configuration records:
   - Use configuration-as-code (IaC) templates for authentication/authorisation and logging settings.
   - Ensure the **default “out of the box” settings** in each environment (production/test/development as applicable) match the secure baseline and are not weakened by environment-specific changes without approval.
5. **Add automated checks** to prove the defaults:
   - Automated tests and configuration checks must confirm that unauthorised requests are denied by default and that security events are logged by default.
   - Include tests for start-up failure scenarios (for example: IdP metadata not available, failure to fetch JSON web key set (JWKS)/policy, logging pipeline unavailable) to confirm fail-closed/secure-default behaviour.

###### WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / Security Architect**: defines secure-default rules and fail-closed requirements.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: implements the configuration and code changes.
- **Platform/DevOps Engineer**: sets up the baselines and enforces them through continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD).
- **Cybersecurity Operations / Security information and event management (SIEM) Engineer**: checks that audit/log pipeline defaults and routing/retention are correct.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every in-scope security feature component, the **default configuration denies** unauthorised access and privileged actions unless explicitly authorised by policy.
- Security controls **deny unless requests are correctly formed and consistent with the policy** (no permissive-by-default logic).
- During start-up failure of security dependencies, the application either **refuses the operation** or **continues using secure defaults** (fail-closed for security-relevant actions).
- Security logging for authentication/authorisation outcomes is **enabled by default** and confirmed through automated tests.
- Secure-default baselines are **documented and enforced as auditable configuration records** (with evidence produced by CI/CD checks and test results).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] List all in-scope security components for the application and all places where they connect to authentication, authorisation, and logging.
- [ ] Set “deny by default” secure default rules for every component, including privileged actions, with clear allow conditions that match the agreed policy.
- [ ] Set the system to fail closed during initial setup if security checks—such as token validation, policy enforcement, or security logging—are not available.
- [ ] Create a standard “secure by default” setup for authentication (authn), authorisation (authz), and security logging, and make sure it is followed by enforcing checks in your continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline configuration.
- [ ] Enable audit logging for authentication and authorisation by default, and send it to the security information and event management (SIEM) logging pipeline using verified default settings.
- [ ] Create and run automated tests to prove that unauthorised requests are blocked by default, and that situations where initial setup fails remain secure.


---

#### SA-8.24 — Secure Failure and Recovery (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure that when a security protection fails, the system does not accidentally allow access, and that when it recovers, it returns in a safe way. Without this, a failure could leave services partly working in a way that allows unauthorised people to gain access, or could cause recovery steps to undo protections and create new security weaknesses.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design, document, and implement how its information system and its security failure-handling and recovery processes behave at every stage of use, including start-up, normal operation, shutdown, and maintenance. The system must detect security-related failures within its defined limits and, by default, move into a safe “block access” state. It must handle interruptions without leaving permissions or data in an unsafe combination, and it must keep security protections in place even during any reconfiguration.

If recovery is needed, the system must restore service in a normal, reduced, or alternative secure mode without breaking policy. It must do this using safe switching, returning to a secure previous state (rollback), or shutting down securely when recovery is not possible. The organisation must keep clear records and have contingency procedures that are tested.

##### Fail-closed security enforcement with secure recovery cutover

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place the organisation’s required “secure failure” and “secure recovery” approach so that **any security control failure** or **any recovery action** cannot break security rules. The system must either:
- keep running in a **reduced/alternative secure mode**, or
- carry out a **secure shutdown** or **rollback**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-8(24) requires implementing the security design principle of **secure failure and recovery**: when something fails or when recovery actions are taken, the system must not end up violating security rules. The guidance also requires the system to detect real and upcoming failures (within agreed limits) during **startup (initialisation), normal use, shutdown, and maintenance**. It further requires that when the system is being changed or reconfigured, it must keep **continuous enforcement of security rules** during that process.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Define how each system component should behave during secure failure and recovery
- Use the organisation-defined scope in **sa-8.24_prm_1** to identify the exact components responsible for secure failure and secure recovery (for example: deny-by-default fail states, failover, rollback, secure shutdown).
- For each component, document what it should do during: **initialisation**, **normal operation**, **shutdown**, and **maintenance**.

###### 2) Set deny-by-default fail states at the security decision point
- Create a central security enforcement point (for example: an API gateway, reverse proxy, or service mesh policy enforcement point) that is configured to **deny by default** if security-critical dependencies are unhealthy or unavailable (for example: identity/token validation service cannot be reached, policy evaluation service is failing, key material cannot be retrieved).
- Make sure this enforcement point returns a safe result (for example: deny/unauthorised or “service unavailable” as appropriate) rather than “allow even though there is an error”.

###### 3) Keep security rules enforced during changes (failover/cutover/rollback)
- Treat failover, cutover, and rollback as **security-critical activities**.
- During reconfiguration, use a staged cutover approach so there is **no gap** where requests could bypass security checks.
- If the new enforcement path is not fully ready, keep the system in the deny-by-default (or otherwise secure) mode.

###### 4) Provide secure recovery options: reduced secure mode or secure shutdown/rollback
- If recovery is possible, bring the service back in a **normal, reduced, or alternative secure way**, while still enforcing security rules.
- If recovery is not possible, carry out a **secure shutdown** of the affected services or start a **rollback to a secure state** (for example: rollback to a known-good configuration/release) so security protections are not broken.

###### 5) Detect failures within agreed limits and keep evidence
- Implement health checks and failure detection for security-critical functions, and set the operational limits for detection.
- Record audit/log events that show: the detected failure condition, the component identifier, the chosen secure failure/recovery action (reduced/alternative/rollback/shutdown), and the date and time.

###### WHO
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: responsible for defining the secure failure/recovery behaviour for the **sa-8.24_prm_1** components.
- **Security Engineering / Platform Engineering**: implement the deny-by-default enforcement point, manage reconfiguration orchestration, and implement failure detection.
- **Operations / SRE / Infrastructure Team**: implement and run tested recovery procedures (failover/cutover/rollback/secure shutdown) and ensure evidence is kept.

###### Acceptance criteria
1. For every security-critical component in **sa-8.24_prm_1**, there is documented behaviour for initialisation, normal operation, shutdown, and maintenance, showing that failures and recovery actions do not violate security rules.
2. When security-critical dependencies fail, the system moves into a **deny-by-default** state (or an equivalent secure state) and does not grant access.
3. During failover/cutover/rollback, there is no visible period where requests bypass the security enforcement point; security rule enforcement remains continuous.
4. Recovery either resumes service in a secure reduced/alternative mode, or performs a secure shutdown/rollback when recovery is not possible.
5. Failure detection produces auditable events showing the failure condition and the secure failure/recovery action taken, including timestamps and component identifiers.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and record the specific ways that **sa-8.24_prm_1** handles security failures and recovers afterwards, covering start-up, normal use, shutdown, and maintenance.
- [ ] Set the security enforcement boundary to “deny by default” when security-critical dependencies (identity, policy, or key material) are unhealthy or cannot be reached.
- [ ] Set up health checks and failure alerts for security-critical functions, within the agreed operating limits.
- [ ] Treat failover, cutover, and rollback as security-critical activities, and carry out the cutover in stages to prevent any time window where security rules could be bypassed.
- [ ] Put in place recovery steps that either continue operation in a reduced but secure mode (or switch to an alternative secure mode), or safely shut down and roll back to a previously verified secure state.
- [ ] Create and keep audit and log records for any detected failures and for the secure failure and recovery action that was chosen, including the date and time (timestamps) and the identifiers for the affected components.


---

#### SA-8.25 — Economic Security (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure your security decisions for systems that handle Confidential and Secret information are sensible and good value for money—not too much and not too little. Without this approach, you could end up paying for stronger protection than you need, or you could spend too little on protection that would have helped prevent serious harm from a breach. You could also fail to collect enough credible evidence to show that your safeguards actually work.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use a documented cost–benefit approach when specifying, designing, building, implementing, and changing the in-scope systems and components. Security measures must be selected so that their cost is no more than the damage they are expected to prevent. The organisation must also balance the expected value of assurance activities (the confidence gained from credible evidence) against the effort and cost of obtaining that evidence.

The organisation must record the results and carry them forward into acquisition policies, design documents, security and privacy requirements, the overall security design, and the system security plan. Named individuals must be responsible for producing, applying, and reviewing the justification.

##### Economic security cost–benefit gates for Confidential/Secret components

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Set up an “economic security” cost–benefit decision process for the information system and any parts of it that handle **Confidential** and **Secret** information. The goal is to make sure security measures and assurance activities (checks and proof) are **no more expensive than the damage they would prevent if there were a breach**. It also ensures that the **value of assurance** is compared to the **cost and effort needed to gather credible evidence**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-8(25) Economic Security** requires applying the organisation’s economic security design principle within the organisation-defined scope ({{ insert: param, sa-08.25_odp }}). This applies to **systems or system components that handle Confidential and Secret data**. The guidance requires that the organisation:
- sizes security measures so their **strength and cost** are not higher than the **potential damage prevented**;
- compares the **benefits of assurance** against the **cost and effort** of obtaining **relevant and credible evidence**;
- documents the reasoning and carries it into security design documents (for example, security requirements/specifications, security architecture, and the system security plan), with named responsibilities.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Define what is in scope and the economic security documents needed
- Create an “economic security scope” tag in the architecture and configuration management database (CMDB) for systems/components that handle **Confidential/Secret** data.
