- Standardise the required documents for each design or change affecting in-scope components:
  - **Harm/Damage model** (which breach outcomes are plausible, and what damage is assumed);
  - **Control mechanism cost model** (the main drivers of implementation and operating cost);
  - **Mechanism selection rationale** (why the chosen security strength is sufficient and not excessive);
  - **Assurance/evidence plan** (what evidence will be collected, why it is credible, and what level of assurance it supports);
  - **Assurance cost/effort estimate** (time, tools, testing, and any third-party costs);
  - **Traceability matrix** linking the decision record to security requirements, security architecture elements, and the relevant parts of the system security plan.

###### 2) Use “policy-as-code” to enforce approval gates for design reviews
- Add mandatory completion of the economic security documents to the design/change approval workflow for in-scope components.
- Use policy-as-code checks to ensure:
  - all required information is provided (harm model, mechanism rationale, assurance plan, evidence cost/effort);
  - there is traceability from each security requirement and architecture element to the economic security decision record;
  - the decision record is referenced in the system security plan update for the change.
- Block approval if any required documents are missing or traceability is incomplete.

###### 3) Apply cost–benefit sizing to security measures and assurance
- For each proposed change to a security measure (for example, encryption strength, authentication assurance level, logging/monitoring depth, segmentation controls), require a short, auditable justification that:
  - compares the **expected cost** of the measure to the **expected damage prevented** (using the organisation’s risk assumptions);
  - avoids choosing controls that are stronger than needed.
- For each assurance activity (for example, penetration testing depth, how often evidence is collected, configuration checks), require a justification that:
  - explains why the evidence is **relevant and credible**;
  - states why the **assurance benefit** is worth the **evidence cost and effort**.

###### 4) Set review timing and update only what has changed
- Re-check (re-validate) the economic security decision record:
  - at least **annually**; and
  - whenever there is a **material change** (a new threat scenario, a major architecture change, a new regulatory requirement, or a significant change in control cost or feasibility).
- For changes, require a “delta economic security” update (update only what has changed) rather than rewriting everything from scratch.

###### WHO
- **Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the economic security document templates and review criteria.
- **Application Owner / Product Owner**: ensures the design/change submission includes the required economic security decision record.
- **Governance/Compliance Lead**: maintains the policy-as-code approval gates and ensures traceability is ready for audit.
- **Change Manager / Release Manager**: ensures the approval gates are applied consistently across environments (development/test/production/disaster recovery).

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every design or change affecting an in-scope Confidential/Secret component, the approval record must include:
  1) a harm/damage model and mechanism selection rationale;
  2) an assurance/evidence plan with evidence credibility rationale;
  3) an assurance cost/effort estimate;
  4) traceability to security requirements/architecture and the system security plan.
- Policy-as-code approval gates must prevent approval when required economic security documents or traceability links are missing.
- Economic security decision records must be revalidated at least annually and updated for material changes.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an in-scope tagging rule for systems and components that handle **Confidential** and **Secret** data in the architecture repository and configuration management database (CMDB).
- [ ] Create standard templates for economic security documents, including: a harm model, a mechanism cost model, an assurance and evidence plan, and a traceability matrix.
- [ ] Set up automated “policy-as-code” approval checkpoints that require economic security evidence and clear traceability before any design or change is approved.
- [ ] Include references to economic security decision records in updates to the system security plan for all in-scope changes.
- [ ] Set a regular review schedule (at least once a year, plus updates when there are significant changes) and require updated information on economic security whenever those changes occur.
- [ ] Train application and security architects to produce clear, auditable cost–benefit justifications and credible evidence plans


---

#### SA-8.26 — Performance Security (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure security protections are built in a way that does not unnecessarily slow the organisation’s systems down. Without this, stronger protections—such as secure access checks, protecting sensitive data, and keeping detailed records of activity—could make services slow or unreliable. That can cause delays, workarounds, or even failures during busy periods.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design, build, and maintain the system and the parts that affect security so that performance and security needs are clearly defined, prioritised, and balanced from the start. It must assess how much each security feature affects performance, especially the more demanding ones, and record why any compromises are acceptable.

Where possible, it should use the most efficient security options available, including simpler building blocks that add little overhead. The organisation must apply and record this “performance versus security” approach throughout specification, design, development, implementation, and changes, and keep the relevant approved documents and trade-off analysis up to date.

##### Performance-security design gate with measured trade-off evidence

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Set up a repeatable “performance-security” review gate for every security-relevant design change across the information system and its security-relevant parts (for example: access control, cryptographic services, logging, and key management). The gate must make sure security features are chosen and designed so they do not unnecessarily slow the system down. Where there is any compromise between performance and security, it must be assessed, explained, and checked as part of the review.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 SA-8(26) enhancement requires applying the security design principle of **performance security**: security features should be built to avoid unnecessary performance loss. Performance and security needs must be clearly stated and prioritised. Security services that require significant computing effort (especially cryptography) must be assessed and shown to have no significant effect, or an explicitly acceptable trade-off. Designers must follow the required constraints and record the trade-off.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define performance-security requirements for each security-relevant component**: use a standard template to capture (a) the security mechanism(s) to be used, (b) performance limits (latency, throughput, processor use, and memory) for normal and peak demand, (c) the rules for prioritising when performance and security requirements conflict, and (d) the acceptance thresholds for “no significant impact” or an “acceptable trade-off”.
2. **Carry out and record trade-off analysis for security overhead**: for each security-related change (for example: stronger authentication, changing cipher suites, changing key rotation frequency, adding or changing logging/telemetry), produce a trade-off record. Where possible, include measured results from test environments and load tests; otherwise include validated estimates, clearly stating assumptions, and a plan for monitoring.
3. **Choose the lowest-impact security options where possible**: the review gate must explicitly consider less resource-intensive alternatives (for example: efficient cryptographic options, hardware-assisted options, caching where safe, and low-overhead enforcement points), unless they are not available or do not meet the required strength or capability.
4. **Apply the gate in engineering workflows (policy-as-code)**: build the template completion and trade-off evidence requirements into continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) and infrastructure automation pipelines, so security-relevant changes cannot be merged or deployed without the required documents and approvals.
5. **Check performance impact using telemetry and regression tests**: set baseline key performance indicators before the change, then confirm after deployment that the overhead stays within the agreed limits. Use automated checks to detect performance regressions and link them to security events or configuration changes.
6. **Keep auditable records and link them to change evidence**: ensure the system security plan and architecture, and the performance-security trade-off records, are version-controlled, approved, and linked to the relevant change tickets and releases.

###### WHO
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the performance-security requirements template, the trade-off approach, and the approval criteria.
- **Performance Engineering / Site Reliability Engineering (SRE) / Platform Team**: performs load testing, sets baseline telemetry, and carries out regression validation.
- **Application Owner / Product Engineering Manager**: ensures changes follow the gate and provide the required evidence.
- **Change Management / Release Manager**: blocks deployment when gate evidence is missing.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every security-relevant design change, there is an approved performance-security requirements record and a trade-off analysis showing that overhead is either (a) demonstrated to have no significant impact against the agreed performance limits, or (b) an explicitly justified acceptable trade-off.
- Trade-off records include the component scope (access control/cryptography/logging/key management), performance limits, and the evidence type (measured/validated estimate), plus a monitoring and verification plan.
- CI/CD (or an equivalent process) prevents deployment when required performance-security documents are missing or incomplete.
- After deployment, telemetry confirms that performance overhead remains within the agreed limits for both normal and peak demand scenarios.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a template for performance and security requirements that covers security-relevant parts of the system, performance limits (budgets), how priorities are decided, and the criteria for passing acceptance tests.
- [ ] Define a trade-off analysis standard covering security overhead, including the types of evidence to use, the assumptions to make, and the monitoring and verification plan, including for cryptography.
- [ ] Set up automated policy checks in continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) to stop security-relevant deployments unless the deployment template and the documented trade-off evidence have been approved and linked to the change request ticket.
- [ ] Run performance baseline tests for computationally intensive security services (for example, authentication, cryptography, and key management) in the staging environment using load levels that match expected peak demand.
- [ ] Require a clear choice and justification for the minimum-overhead security measures to use, including consideration of less computationally intensive options where they are sufficient.
- [ ] Set up automated checks to detect regressions in telemetry after security configuration changes, and confirm that the post-deployment overhead stays within the agreed limits.
- [ ] Update and version the system security plan and architecture, and keep auditable records of the agreed trade-offs for every approved change.


---

#### SA-8.27 — Human Factored Security (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making the tools used by system administrators and operators to set up and manage security clear, sensible, and safe to use. If these screens and tools are confusing, slow, or unhelpful, people may make mistakes, misunderstand what a change will do, or become frustrated and turn off protections. That can leave systems more exposed than intended.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must define and document how it will apply user-friendly design to privileged access and security configuration screens and tools. It must also include clear, plain usability expectations in the system’s security requirements. The organisation must carry out usability analysis, record the plan and responsibilities, and involve the right teams so that information security reviews and approves the approach.

The organisation must ensure the interfaces provide meaningful feedback and clear warnings when changes affect security. They must enforce protections without being intrusive, help administrators understand the impact of their choices, and ensure that what users intend is what the system actually enforces both before start-up and during day-to-day operation. The organisation must provide evidence that these practices were considered and improved during design and change work.

##### Human-factored UX for PAM and security configuration consoles

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Apply human-factored security design to the organisation’s privileged access management (PAM) and security configuration screens used by system administrators and operators. Make sure the user interface is easy to understand, gives clear and relevant warnings or feedback for actions that affect security, and applies security rules in a way that does not unnecessarily slow down or hinder day-to-day work.

###### WHY (control requirement)
This requirement, based on National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 SA-8(27) enhancement, asks the organisation to apply **human-factored security** to the defined systems/components (parameter **sa-08.27_odp**: *PAM and security configuration interfaces for system administrators and operators*). The guidance requires:
- security features and supporting services to have an intuitive, user-friendly interface
- clear, meaningful feedback and warnings when users make insecure choices
- security enforcement that is not disruptive and does not reduce efficiency
- extra focus on administrator/operator screens so staff can understand the impact of their choices
- confidence that the user’s intent is correctly translated into the security enforcement (both before changes take effect and while the system is running)

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define and document human-factored security requirements for PAM/configuration
- Create a **Human-Factored Security User Experience (UX) Standard** for all PAM and security configuration screens and workflows.
- Include measurable usability and safety requirements (for example: labels written in plain language, consistent wording, required confirmation text, and a clear display of the current security state).

###### 2) Implement “intent-to-enforcement” interface safeguards
For every action that changes security policy in the PAM/configuration interface:
- Show a **security-impact summary** explaining what will change, what will be weakened or strengthened, and who or what is affected.
- Provide **before-and-after previews** (differences) for security-relevant settings.
- Add **contextual warnings** for insecure or high-risk choices (for example: expanding access scope, reducing assurance or multi-factor authentication (MFA) strength, turning off security checks, or increasing privileged session duration).
- Use **non-intrusive enforcement approaches**: show information step-by-step, use sensible defaults, automate routine tasks, and request additional authentication only when the risk increases significantly.

###### 3) Provide clear feedback and confirm the effective security state
- After an action is applied, show **meaningful success feedback** that states the exact security-relevant setting that changed.
- Confirm the **effective enforcement state** (for example: “Policy enforced as: <X>”) and show any required next steps (approval pending, re-authentication required, or restart/service impact).

###### 4) Test with usability analysis and improve
- Run usability analysis sessions with representative system administrators and operators using the real PAM/configuration workflows.
- Record findings, prioritise improvements, and update the UX standard and interface implementation accordingly.

###### 5) Governance and accountability for administrator/operator interface changes
- Assign accountable roles (for example: Security Engineering plus the identity and access management (IAM)/PAM product owner plus an Information Security reviewer) for updating the UX standard and approving changes that affect security-impacting workflows.
- Keep audit-ready evidence: the UX standard, design rationale, usability analysis report, and change records that link interface behaviour to security enforcement outcomes.

###### WHO
- **Product Owner / IAM-PAM Console Owner**: owns adoption of the UX standard and the backlog of improvements.
- **Security Engineering / Information Security**: defines categories of security impact, sets warning criteria, and approves the UX standard and changes.
- **UX Designer / Human Factors Lead**: designs and validates the interface patterns.
- **System Administrators / Privileged Operators (representatives)**: take part in usability analysis and validate that “intent-to-enforcement” is correct.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For all security-policy-changing actions in the PAM/configuration interface, the interface must provide: (1) an explicit security-impact summary, (2) contextual warnings for insecure/high-risk choices, and (3) confirmation of the effective security state after enforcement.
- Usability analysis with representative administrator/operator users must show that participants can correctly predict the enforcement outcome for common and high-risk actions (measured against task success and error-rate thresholds defined in the UX standard).
- Evidence must exist showing how interface actions map to enforcement behaviour (including any required follow-up steps), and records must be kept of usability findings and any fixes made.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a human-centred security user experience (UX) standard for privileged access management (PAM) and security configuration screens. This should cover warnings, confirmation prompts, and feedback showing the current effective security state.
- [ ] For every workflow in the PAM (privileged access management) and configuration user interface that changes a security policy, provide a plain-language summary of the security impact and a clear before-and-after preview.
- [ ] Add clear, specific warnings for insecure or high-risk choices, and only require step-up authentication when the risk increases significantly.
- [ ] After enforcement, confirm that the change has taken effect, and complete any required follow-up actions (approval pending, re-authentication, or restarting services and any related service impact).
- [ ] Run usability testing sessions with representative system administrators and operators, record the results, agreed fixes, and the approval evidence (sign-off).
- [ ] Set up an approval process for user experience (UX) changes that affect security controls, including review by Security Engineering and Information Security and keeping complete change records that are ready for audit.


---

#### SA-8.28 — Acceptable Security (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure your customer-facing website and mobile apps protect people’s privacy in a way that feels right to users and works smoothly, not confusingly. If privacy protections are unclear, too slow, or difficult to control, users may stop using the service, use it in unintended ways, or take risky workarounds. This can result in personal information being shared or handled incorrectly.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly set out what users should reasonably expect for both privacy and performance, based on its privacy policy and the app’s intended design. It must then build these expectations into the app’s requirements, design, overall system planning, day-to-day development, and any changes.  

Users must be able to easily restrict privacy-related actions, and privacy messages must be clear and match what the system actually does. The organisation must document how it will achieve this, assess privacy impacts and privacy risks, keep the records, and ensure the same user experience works properly on both the website and mobile apps.

##### Privacy & performance expectations contract for web/mobile

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement the security design principle of **acceptable security** for the user-facing web portal and mobile applications that handle **Confidential** and **Secret** data. This means setting out what users should reasonably expect for **privacy and performance**, turning those expectations into **clear, testable requirements**, and enforcing them using **user-facing controls** and **measurable performance limits**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-8(28)** requires implementing the security design principle of **acceptable security** for **systems or system components** (here: the user-facing web portal and mobile applications handling Confidential and Secret data). The guidance says the privacy and performance provided by the system must be **in line with what users expect**, and that users must be able to **limit their actions to protect privacy**. If the system does not meet users’ privacy and performance expectations, users may avoid it or use it in ways that are inefficient or insecure.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define acceptable security expectations** (privacy + performance) in a versioned “Privacy & Performance Contract” aligned to the organisation’s privacy policy and the system’s intended design. Include:
   - Privacy expectations: what data is collected and processed, what users can opt out of, what privacy controls exist, how long user preferences last, and what happens when users limit their actions.
   - Performance expectations: measurable targets for key user journeys involving Confidential/Secret data (for example, page/application response time percentiles, error-rate limits, and timeouts/retry behaviour that prevents insecure workarounds).
2. **Turn expectations into enforceable requirements** with clear acceptance criteria for both **web and mobile**, including:
   - User interface and experience rules: privacy notices and control labels must be clear, not misleading, and consistent across platforms.
   - Privacy control behaviour: controls must be available where relevant, must actually restrict the underlying processing, and must keep working as intended (for example, across sessions/logins) according to the contract.
   - Backend enforcement: authorisation and data-handling logic must follow the privacy restrictions (not just show them to users).
3. **Build the contract into the system’s lifecycle**:
   - Ensure each contract requirement can be traced back to the relevant part of the privacy policy, and to the system’s privacy and security design documents and change records.
   - Include the contract in procurement and supplier/vendor requirements where third-party components affect privacy and performance.
4. **Enforce through automated checks**:
   - Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) checks for privacy control presence and correctness (for example, coverage of consent/notification text, control persistence, and negative tests showing that restricted actions do not trigger prohibited processing).
   - Add performance budget tests for the defined journeys (for example, load testing and automated monitoring checks) and stop releases when budgets are exceeded.
5. **Confirm with evidence of user expectations**:
   - Run usability and privacy understanding tests (for example, task-based tests) to confirm users can understand and use privacy controls to protect privacy without resorting to insecure workarounds.
   - Keep the results as auditable evidence linked to the contract requirements.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Product Owner / Service Owner**: owns the Privacy & Performance Contract and ensures it reflects user expectations.
- **Privacy Officer / Data Protection Lead**: checks alignment with the organisation’s privacy policy.
- **Security Architect**: ensures backend enforcement and traceability to the security design.
- **UX Researcher / UI Lead**: designs privacy controls that users can understand and runs understanding tests.
- **Engineering Lead / DevOps**: implements CI/CD enforcement and performance budget release gates.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A versioned **Privacy & Performance Contract** exists for the web portal and mobile apps handling Confidential/Secret data, and it can be traced to the organisation’s privacy policy and the system’s design documents.
- Privacy controls are **available to users**, **easy to understand**, and **actually restrict** underlying processing consistently on both web and mobile.
- Privacy preferences/controls work according to the contract (including persistence rules) and are verified by automated tests.
- Performance budgets for key Confidential/Secret user journeys are in place and releases are blocked when budgets are exceeded.
- Usability and privacy understanding evidence shows users can limit their actions to protect privacy without confusing or unsafe workarounds.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a version-controlled privacy and performance agreement for the web portal and mobile applications that handle confidential or secret information, aligned with the organisation’s privacy policy.
- [ ] Set clear, measurable privacy and performance targets for the main user journeys, and document the platform-specific requirements for both web and mobile.
- [ ] Put privacy restrictions into the back-end so they genuinely block processing (not just what users see on the screen), and make sure the control behaviour works the same way across all platforms.
- [ ] Add automated continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) tests to check that privacy notices and controls are present, correct, and remain in place. Include negative tests to confirm restricted actions are blocked.
- [ ] Set performance limits (for example, acceptable response time, error rate, and timeouts) for the agreed customer journeys, and stop the build if any limit is exceeded.
- [ ] Carry out usability and privacy understanding testing to confirm that users can understand and use the privacy controls to protect their privacy, without needing unsafe workarounds.
- [ ] Create a clear audit trail showing how each contract requirement maps to the relevant privacy policy sections, design documents, and change records.


---

#### SA-8.29 — Repeatable and Documented Procedures (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure your on-premises systems are built, configured, and changed in a way that can be repeated and is fully documented. That way, the same system can be recreated correctly later. Without this, you risk making changes that are inconsistent or incomplete, having unclear security settings, and being unable to clearly show what was done and why—particularly when staff leave, systems are rebuilt, or an incident needs investigation.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must:
- identify which on-premises components are in scope, and which security-relevant configuration items are covered; and
- create clear, step-by-step procedures for producing security requirements, and for designing, developing, implementing, and modifying those components and configuration items across the entire lifecycle.

These procedures must be detailed enough for someone else to rebuild the component, and the outcomes must match the records you keep.

You must also keep documentation up to date and aligned, including (as applicable) system design, security and privacy requirements, security architecture, the system security plan, and other relevant records. Finally, ensure your acquisition policy includes these procedures, and that it names roles responsible for setting requirements, carrying out lifecycle activities, and providing security oversight.

##### Repeatable security builds using versioned procedures and evidence

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up repeatable, documented processes for the on-premises information system components and the security-relevant configuration items for them (as defined by **sa-08.29_odp**). This ensures the same component can be rebuilt later in a consistent way, with evidence that can be checked.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The NIST **SA-8(29)** improvement requires using the security principle of **repeatable and documented procedures** for the **systems or system components** defined in **sa-08.29_odp**. The guidance expects documented methods that allow a component to be **fully and correctly rebuilt** later. It also expects supporting materials (for example, documentation and test results) to be **repeatable** and **consistent** for inspection.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define what is in scope**: create and maintain an inventory that links each in-scope on-premises component to its **security-relevant configuration items** (for example, operating system security hardening baseline, authentication integration settings, TLS/cipher settings, firewall rule sets, logging and retention settings, privileged access settings, how secrets are handled, and backup/recovery settings).
2. **Create “procedure packs” for each stage of the lifecycle** (documented, version-controlled, and linked to the in-scope items) covering at least:
   - security requirements specification (how measurable security requirements are worked out),
   - security design (how design decisions map to configuration parts),
   - secure development (how changes are produced and reviewed, including dependency pinning and build repeatability where applicable),
   - secure implementation (deployment steps, rollback, and verification steps),
   - secure modification (change process, approvals, and what evidence must be captured).
3. **Make rebuilding predictable** for each in-scope component by using declarative, version-controlled build and deployment materials (for example, configuration management and/or infrastructure-as-code equivalents) so the same inputs produce the same outputs.
4. **Produce consistent evidence automatically** during each build, rebuild, and change:
   - configuration exports (for example, rendered templates and effective configuration snapshots),
   - automated test results (unit, integration, and security checks where applicable),
   - drift and compliance reports comparing intended versus actual configuration.
   Store the evidence with unchangeable references to the procedure pack version and the build/deployment version.
5. **Link system and security documents to the procedure versions**: ensure the system design, security architecture, security plan, and related records reference the procedure pack versions and the evidence produced for releases and rebuilds.
6. **Include procedure packs in procurement and assign named responsibilities**: update procurement policy and requirements so suppliers and internal teams must use the same procedure packs when delivering and changing in-scope components. Assign roles for requirements, lifecycle activities, and security oversight.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application/Platform Engineering Lead**: owns the procedure packs and the rebuilding automation.
- **Information Security (AppSec/ISec) Lead**: checks that the procedures cover security requirements and design, and that the evidence expectations are met.
- **Configuration Management/Release Manager**: ensures version control, evidence retention, and links to system documentation.
- **Procurement/Architecture Governance**: ensures procurement policy includes the procedure requirements.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The organisation can choose any in-scope component and **rebuild it** using the documented procedures and versioned build/deployment materials, producing the same security-relevant configuration (within agreed tolerances) and the same type of test evidence.
- Each in-scope component has a **documented, version-controlled procedure pack** covering requirements, design, development, implementation, and modification.
- For every release, rebuild, or change, the system produces **consistent, checkable evidence** (configuration snapshot/export plus test results plus drift/compliance report) that can be traced back to the procedure pack version.
- System and security documentation (for example, system design, security and privacy requirements, security plan) references the procedure pack versions used for the component’s current state.
- Procurement policy and responsibilities are updated so delivery and modification of in-scope components follow the same repeatable procedure approach.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep up to date an inventory mapping for **SA-08.29** that lists the in-scope components and maps them to security-relevant configuration items.
- [ ] Maintain author version-controlled procedure packs covering requirements, design, secure software development, secure implementation, and secure changes (modifications)
- [ ] Create build and deployment files for each in-scope component in a consistent, repeatable way, and store them in a controlled repository.
- [ ] Automate the creation and storage of configuration snapshots/exports, test results, and drift and compliance reports for each build or rebuild.
- [ ] Link each release or rebuild record to the exact procedure pack version and the build output version in your release evidence store.
- [ ] Update the system design and security plan documents so they reference the procedure pack versions used for the current component state.
- [ ] Update the acquisition policy and assign named responsibilities to ensure staff follow the procedure packs for both delivery and changes.


---

#### SA-8.30 — Procedural Rigor (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure your systems are designed and changed in a disciplined, well-documented way, so the final result is correct and matches the plan. Without this, teams could unintentionally add features that were never intended, use outdated or misleading design information, or make changes based on assumptions about how something works. This can create weaknesses that are difficult to spot and fix later.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out clear, documented procedures that cover the full process—from planning and design, through building and putting the system into service, to later changes—for the most important parts, such as sign-in, who can access what, protection of data, recording key events, and network protections. These procedures must include checks and approvals, require design documents to be produced and kept accurate so they match what is actually built, and ensure changes are made using the current design information rather than working it out after the fact.

The required level of detail must match how much the system needs to be trusted. Systems that require higher trust must be developed and evidenced using more rigorous work.

##### Procedural-rigor SDLC gates with authoritative design as source of truth

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put strong, repeatable procedures in place for the information system and the parts that affect security (for example, authentication, access control, encryption, logging, and network security functions). Define these procedures with enough coverage, detail, and clarity, and enforce them using change control supported by clear evidence.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST Special Publication (SP) 800-53 Security Assessment and Authorisation (SA)-8(30) enhancement requires applying the security design principle of **procedural rigour**—meaning the strictness of the system’s life cycle processes must match how much the system is expected to be trusted. This is achieved by:
- Stopping new or unintended functionality from being introduced through checks and balances.
- Ensuring security engineering outputs (such as specifications and design documents) stay **authoritative**, so the system can be understood **as it was built**.
- Making changes easier and safer by relying on **detailed, up-to-date design documentation** rather than trying to work out the design after the fact.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define scope and procedural depth**: Maintain an organisation register of security-relevant components that are in scope (from the SA-08.30_odp parameter). Define procedural-rigour levels (for example, low/medium/high trustworthiness) and link each level to the required review depth, types of evidence, and number of approvals.
2. **Create life cycle procedure templates**: For each stage of the life cycle (design/specification, build/configuration, implementation/deployment, and change/modification), publish standard procedure templates. These must state the required inputs, outputs, and evidence (for example: security requirements and design artefacts, architecture/security diagrams, threat model outputs where applicable, test plan and test results, deployment evidence, and “as-built” configuration exports).
3. **Enforce checks and balances through gated change control**: Use continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) and release gates so security-relevant changes cannot be merged or deployed unless:
   - The required sections of security design documentation are present and current.
   - Each change can be traced to the relevant security requirements and design artefacts (using requirement/design identifiers).
   - Verification evidence exists (for example: automated policy/configuration compliance checks and linked test evidence).
   - Approvals are separated by role (for example: security engineering review and information security approval for higher trustworthiness levels).
4. **Make design documentation authoritative and keep it current**: Require that every security-relevant change updates the authoritative design record (for example: architecture/security diagrams, configuration/security baselines, and descriptions of interface/security behaviour) within the same change package, or uses a clearly approved exception.
5. **Confirm “as built” matches the authoritative design**: For each deployment, capture and store “as-built” evidence (for example: infrastructure as code (IaC) plan/apply outputs, configuration exports, security baseline snapshots, and the current state of firewall/web application firewall (WAF)/transport layer security (TLS)/logging configuration). Then run automated checks to confirm it matches the authoritative design documentation.
6. **Apply rigour that matches trustworthiness**: Use the procedural-rigour level selected for the change’s trustworthiness level to decide the required number and type of reviews, and how strict the evidence requirements must be—so higher trustworthiness gets higher procedural rigour.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Product Owner**: ensures changes are classified and packaged with the required artefacts.
- **Security Engineering Lead**: defines the required security design outputs and review criteria.
- **Information Security (Application Security (AppSec)) / Security Governance**: approves higher-trust changes and any exceptions.
- **Release Manager / DevOps Lead**: implements CI/CD, release gating, and evidence capture.
- **Configuration Management Lead**: maintains authoritative design records and “as-built” evidence repositories.

###### Acceptance criteria
- There is a documented, organisation-approved procedural-rigour process covering the life cycle stages for in-scope security-relevant components.
- CI/CD or release gating prevents deployment of security-relevant changes unless required security design artefacts, traceability, approvals, and verification evidence are in place.
- For a sample of recent security-relevant changes, the authoritative design documentation is updated and matches the captured “as-built” configuration/evidence.
- Trustworthiness levels clearly change the required procedural depth (for example, higher levels require additional approvals/evidence) and are applied consistently.
- Any exceptions are explicitly recorded, approved, time-limited, and supported by compensating controls and evidence.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date list of the security-relevant components that are in scope for SA-08.30_odp, including authentication, access control, encryption, logging, and network security.
- [ ] Define procedural-rigor levels linked to trustworthiness levels, including required checks, types of supporting evidence, and the roles authorised to approve them
- [ ] Publish lifecycle procedure templates that set out the required security design deliverables and the proof needed to verify them for design, building/configuration, deployment, and changes (modifications).
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipelines, or release gates, that stop security-relevant deployments unless all of the following are in place: complete documentation, clear traceability, approvals from separate authorised roles, and verification evidence.
- [ ] Require that every security-relevant change updates the official design records and includes evidence of the final “as-built” configuration and security baseline in the same change package (or records an approved exception).
- [ ] Automate checks that compare the current “as built” configuration against the approved design (for example, by exporting configurations and running compliance checks against policies/configuration standards), and store the results for audit purposes.


---

#### SA-8.31 — Secure System Modification (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that when your organisation changes or upgrades any systems that are important for security, the change does not quietly weaken protection. Without this, a routine update, patch, or settings change could unintentionally break safeguards, increase the risk of unauthorised access, or stop key protections from working as intended—leaving the organisation exposed in ways that are difficult to spot until it is too late.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must apply the same careful, disciplined approach used when the system was first designed and built to every later change to all parts that affect security. Before any change is put into live use, it must be analysed for its security impact and checked against the organisation’s agreed level of acceptable risk, with the results recorded. Changes must be managed through formal change records and configuration tracking, with clear approvals and supporting documentation, and the system’s configuration records must be updated to match what was actually deployed.

##### Secure change gate with pre-deployment security analysis evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put a mandatory “secure system modification” checkpoint in place for all security-relevant changes to the defined systems and components (information technology (IT) and, where applicable, operational technology (OT)-adjacent components). Every change must be security-analysed before it is deployed or used in operations. The results must be recorded, approvals must be captured, and the configuration and design baselines must be updated so they match what is actually deployed.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-8(31)** enhancement requires applying the security design principle of **secure system modification**. This means changes must keep the system secure against the organisation’s security requirements and the agreed risk tolerance of relevant stakeholders. Because upgrades and changes can turn a secure system into an insecure one, the organisation must apply the same level of care as during initial development. This includes **careful security analysis before implementation and deployment**, with evidence and configuration and change control.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define what counts as “security-relevant” changes** for the application’s multi-tier architecture. Examples include identity and authentication changes, authorisation and roles, logging and telemetry, encryption and key management, network and security policy changes, dependency or library upgrades, database or storage changes, application programming interface (API) or messaging changes, changes to privileged or administrative functions, and security service integrations.
2. **Create a secure modification process** that requires, for every security-relevant change, a pre-deployment **Security Impact Assessment (SIA)**. The SIA must clearly explain how the change affects the system’s ability to remain secure against the organisation’s security requirements and the agreed stakeholder risk tolerance. The SIA must include: the affected security requirements, identified security impacts, the risk outcome (accept, mitigate, or rollback), and a clear rollback or contingency plan.
3. **Apply a change workflow checkpoint** in the organisation’s information technology service management (ITSM) and release tools. Deployment to test or production (or use in operations) must be blocked unless the change record includes: a completed SIA reference, the required approvals (security and system or application owner roles), and recorded evidence that the analysis was completed **before** deployment.
4. **Link configuration management to the change**. Require the change record to link to the relevant configuration items (for example, infrastructure as code (IaC) modules, container images, application build outputs, database schema migration scripts, and network or security policy objects). Also ensure the **configuration baseline and configuration management database (CMDB) entries** are updated to match the deployed “as-built” state.
5. **Add automated checks where possible** (without replacing the required analysis). After deployment, run checks to confirm that key security control settings still meet requirements. Examples include configuration drift checks against secure baselines, confirming that logging and telemetry endpoints are active, confirming encryption settings, and validating that authorisation policies and roles are unchanged or updated correctly. Record the results back into the change record.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / System Owner**: ensures changes follow the secure modification process and that an SIA is completed for security-relevant changes.
- **Information Security / Cybersecurity**: reviews and approves the SIA outcomes against security requirements and risk tolerance.
- **Change Manager / Release Manager (ITSM)**: enforces the workflow checkpoint and ensures deployment cannot proceed without the required evidence.
- **Configuration Manager / Platform Engineering**: ensures configuration baselines and CMDB or configuration records are updated to match the deployed artefacts.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every security-relevant change, the change record includes a completed SIA **before** deployment or operational use, with a documented risk outcome and rollback or contingency plan.
- Deployment to production or other operational environments is blocked (technically and through process) when required SIA evidence or approvals are missing.
- Configuration baseline, CMDB, and configuration records are updated to match the deployed “as-built” state for each approved change.
- Where automated validation is implemented, the results are captured in the change record and show that key security settings remain compliant or are updated correctly in line with the approved SIA.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document which systems and components, and which types of changes, are considered security-relevant for this application.
- [ ] Create a secure change process that requires, before deployment, a Security Impact Assessment mapped to the security requirements and the risk tolerance of relevant stakeholders.
- [ ] Set up the IT service management (ITSM) and release process so that deployments are blocked unless the change record includes completed security information and approvals (SIA) evidence and the required security and system approvals.
- [ ] Update the configuration management requirements so that every approved change updates the configuration management database (CMDB) and the configuration baselines to match the actual “as-built” system that has been deployed.
- [ ] After deployment, run automated security checks (for example, confirm the system has not drifted from its secure baseline, confirm logging and monitoring data are enabled, and confirm encryption and access rules are in place) and record the results in the change record.
- [ ] Train application, platform, and security reviewers on the secure change procedure and what evidence and approvals are required.


---

#### SA-8.32 — Sufficient Documentation (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build, run, and change your on-premises systems have clear, up-to-date instructions for the security features that protect access, record activity, and safeguard sensitive data. Without this, staff may set up security incorrectly or misunderstand how it is supposed to work. This can result in unauthorised access, missing records of important events, or data being left unprotected.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must provide and keep a well-organised set of security documents and role-based training covering:
- identity (who can access)
- logging (what gets recorded)
- access control (who can do what)
- encryption services (how data is protected)

This must also include plain-language explanations of what could go wrong if settings are used incorrectly or set up wrongly. The documents and training must be kept current as the system is specified, designed, developed, implemented, and changed. There must be clear responsibility for updates, version-controlled records, and evidence that relevant roles can find the latest guidance and correctly explain the risks.

##### Role-based security docs and training for identity, logging, access, encryption

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Set up and keep **enough documentation and supporting training** for people who specify, design, develop, implement, run, and change the on-premises information system and the security-relevant parts of it—specifically **identity, logging, access control, and encryption services**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-8(32)** enhancement requires applying the security principle of **having sufficient documentation** for the defined systems and components. The guidance says staff must have **clear, adequate information** so they help system security rather than weaken it. It also says the documentation should be **supported by training** to reduce mistakes and incorrect use or misconfiguration.

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Create one single, authoritative “Security Sufficient Documentation” set (version-controlled)
- Keep one repository (for example, an internal wiki or document platform) with **structured templates** for each security-relevant component:
  - **Identity services** (how users are authenticated, multi-factor authentication expectations, how service accounts are handled, account lifecycle, how privileged access is handled)
  - **Logging and monitoring** (what events are logged, where logs are sent, how long logs are kept, expectations for log integrity, and alerting triggers)
  - **Access control** (the role model, least-privilege rules, where authorisation decisions are made, separation of duties, privileged workflows)
  - **Encryption services** (what is encrypted, who is responsible for keys, key rotation expectations, how certificates and keys are handled, and what happens when encryption fails)
- For each component, include:
  - **Purpose and design intent** (what the control is meant to achieve)
  - **Configuration and use instructions** (how to implement and how to operate)
  - **Checks to confirm it is correct** (how to verify the configuration)
  - **Common wrong uses and misconfigurations, with plain-language risks** (what could go wrong and why it matters)
  - **Owner and last reviewed date**

###### 2) Link documentation to the system’s single source of truth for configuration
- For each component, link the relevant documentation sections to the authoritative configuration records (for example, identity provider (IdP) configuration objects, security information and event management (SIEM) or agent policy definitions, identity and access management (IAM) or authorisation policy definitions, and key management service (KMS) key and certificate configuration records).
- Make sure documentation clearly states the **environment scope** (production, test, disaster recovery) and any differences between environments.

###### 3) Keep documentation up to date through change control
- Update the documentation set whenever a change request affects identity, logging, access control, or encryption.
- Require a **documentation update plus a “risk delta” note** (what new wrong use could become possible, or what risk is reduced) as part of the change approval process.
- Ensure documentation and configuration records stay consistent after releases.

###### 4) Provide training matched to each role and the documentation
- Provide training packs mapped to roles (at minimum):
  - **Administrators and engineers** (how to configure and check identity, logging, access control, and encryption)
  - **Change managers and release operators** (what must not be broken, and how to confirm security-relevant outcomes)
- Training must explicitly cover **“Do” and “Do not” guidance** for at least two security-relevant features per component (for example: multi-factor authentication enforcement and privileged access workflow; log integrity and retention plus correct SIEM routing; least-privilege role assignment plus privileged approval; encryption key rotation expectations plus certificate and key handling).
- Keep evidence of completion (who completed it, when, and the version of the training material).

###### 5) Make documentation easy to find and suitable for audit
- Ensure staff can quickly locate the latest approved guidance for the relevant component and environment.
- Keep an audit trail showing document version, approval and owner, and how training versions map to the documentation.

###### WHO
- **Security Architecture Lead / Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) office**: owns the standard for sufficient documentation and the templates.
- **System Owner / Application Security Owner**: ensures the documentation set covers the on-premises system and its security-relevant components.
- **Identity and IAM Lead, Logging and SIEM Lead, Encryption and KMS Lead**: provide component-specific content and verification steps.
- **Change Manager / Release Manager**: ensures documentation update requirements are enforced in the change process.
- **Learning Management System (LMS) / Training Coordinator**: publishes role-based training and keeps completion evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A version-controlled documentation set exists and covers **identity, logging, access control, and encryption services** for the on-premises system components defined in **sa-08.32_odp**.
- Each component’s documentation includes **clear configuration and use instructions** and **plain-language risks** for common wrong uses and misconfigurations.
- Documentation is linked to the authoritative configuration records and is **kept current** through the change process (no security-relevant change is released without the required documentation update and approval evidence).
- Role-based training is provided for the relevant roles, aligned to the documentation, and completion evidence is retained with references to the training and document versions.
- Audit evidence shows that staff can locate the latest approved guidance and understand their security-relevant responsibilities (documentation version plus training completion records).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set the official documentation templates for identity, logging, access control, and encryption services, including the risks of misuse or incorrect configuration.
- [ ] Create the first version-controlled set of documentation for the on-premises system components listed in **sa-08.32_odp**, and assign named owners and review dates.
- [ ] Link each documentation section to the system’s configuration “source of truth” records for the relevant component and environment.
- [ ] Update the change control process so that any changes to identity, logging, access, or encryption settings must include updated documentation and a note explaining the change in risk (a “risk delta” note).
- [ ] Create and deliver role-based training materials that match the documentation. For each component, include clear “do” and “do not” guidance for at least two security-relevant features.
- [ ] Set up a way to record proof that training has been completed, and link each record to the correct document version. Also check that staff can find and access the most recent approved guidance.


---

#### SA-8.33 — Minimization (Enhancement)

This requirement is about collecting and using people’s personal information only when it is genuinely needed, sharing only the minimum required, and keeping it for no longer than necessary. Without this, organisations may unintentionally collect too much information, use it for the wrong reasons, share it with the wrong parties, or keep it indefinitely. This increases the risk of privacy harm, complaints, regulatory action, and expensive clean-up after mistakes.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must have written privacy minimisation procedures that clearly state what personal information may be collected, used, shared, and kept only when it is directly needed for authorised purposes, and what to do when it is not needed. It must build these minimisation rules into system design and settings, document how they are applied, assess privacy impacts and risks, assign clear responsibilities to relevant staff and system developers, enforce the rules through practical controls, and review minimisation when systems or services are bought or changed, keeping evidence in the organisation’s records.

##### Privacy minimisation procedures and enforcement in app data flows

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place documented privacy minimisation procedures and apply them across the application’s full data life cycle (collection, use, sharing, and retention). This means personally identifiable information (PII) is only handled when it is directly relevant and needed for an authorised purpose, and it is kept only for as long as necessary.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 SA-8(33) enhancement requires: “Implement the privacy principle of minimization using {{ insert: param, sa-08.33_odp }}.” The guidance says minimisation means:
- only processing PII that is directly relevant and necessary to achieve an authorised purpose, and
- only keeping PII for as long as necessary,
supported by processes that align with applicable laws and policies.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define minimisation procedures (authorised-purpose driven):** Create a set of procedures that links each authorised purpose to:
   (a) which PII data items are allowed,
   (b) which processing activities are allowed,
   (c) which sharing is allowed and who can receive it, and
   (d) the retention period, including what to do when it ends (delete or archive).
   This must explicitly cover collection, use, sharing, and retention.
2. **Create a PII field inventory and purpose catalogue:** Keep an authoritative list of all PII data items the application uses (including in forms, application programming interfaces (APIs), message content, logs, exports, and database tables/columns). Link each item to an authorised purpose and explain why it is necessary.
3. **Apply minimisation in system settings and code paths:**
   - Set up user interface (UI) and API checks to **collect only the required fields** for each purpose (block optional PII by default).
   - Use **field-level allowlists** for sharing outside the application (for example, only send the minimum PII needed to downstream services).
   - Apply **retention controls** at the storage level (for example, database time-to-live (TTL) or partitioning, object life cycle policies, and approved deletion jobs) so they match the retention periods in the procedures.
   - Ensure logs and monitoring data do not capture unnecessary PII (mask or redact where possible), and ensure any kept logs follow the same minimisation retention periods.
4. **Build minimisation into change management:** Require that any change to data structures, forms, API contracts, data processing pipelines, sharing connections, or retention settings references the minimisation procedure version and the authorised purpose. Keep proof of the change (for example, ticket references, updated purpose/field mapping, and updated retention configuration).
5. **Check using privacy impact assessment evidence:** For each authorised purpose and for major changes, carry out or update a privacy impact assessment (or equivalent privacy risk assessment). This must explicitly check minimisation (why each PII field is needed, what is shared, and how long it is kept) and record the results.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Data Protection Officer / Privacy Lead:** owns the minimisation procedures and the mapping from purpose to PII.
- **Application Owner / Product Owner:** ensures authorised purposes are defined correctly and implemented.
- **Security Architect / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead:** ensures access and sharing routes support sharing only what is necessary.
- **Engineering Lead / Platform Team:** implements the technical enforcement (settings, code, and retention mechanisms).
- **Change Manager / Governance Lead:** ensures change records include the required minimisation evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented minimisation procedure exists that explicitly covers **collection, use, sharing, and retention** of PII and links each rule to an **authorised purpose**.
- An auditable PII field inventory exists mapping each PII item to an authorised purpose and a necessity explanation.
- The application enforces minimisation through demonstrable controls (for example, field-level allowlists/validation for collection and outbound sharing; storage retention controls aligned to the documented retention periods; masking/redaction for logs where PII is not needed).
- Change records for relevant data-flow and retention changes reference the minimisation procedure version and include evidence of updated purpose/field mapping and updated retention configuration.
- Privacy impact assessment evidence exists showing minimisation was assessed for each purpose and major change, including retention duration and sharing scope.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Draft privacy minimisation procedures that set out the authorised reasons for collecting personal information and the specific personal information (PII) that is allowed for collection, use, sharing, and retention.
- [ ] Create and keep an auditable record of all personal data (PII) fields, showing which authorised purposes each one supports and the reason it is necessary.
- [ ] Set up application input checks and agreed application programming interface (API) rules so that, for each purpose, the system collects only the required personal information (PII) fields.
- [ ] Set up field-by-field allowlists for outbound sharing integrations, and make sure logs and telemetry mask or remove any unnecessary personal data (PII).
- [ ] Set up and record storage retention controls—such as time-to-live (TTL) settings, file lifecycle rules, and deletion jobs—so they match the agreed data minimisation retention periods.
- [ ] Update the change-management templates so that every change to data flows or data retention references the data minimisation procedure version and the supporting evidence.
- [ ] Carry out or update privacy impact assessments for each purpose or major change, and record the results of data minimisation checks—whether it is necessary, who it is shared with, and how long it is kept.


---

### SA-9 — External System Services (Control)

This requirement is about making sure that when you use outside companies to run services for you, they protect your information and your customers’ privacy to the same standard you would expect if you were running the services internally. Without this, an external provider could allow the wrong people to access systems, not respond quickly to problems, leave security weaknesses unfixed, set up services incorrectly, not keep proper records of activity, or handle data in ways that break privacy commitments—putting your organisation at risk even though you do not manage their day-to-day work.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out in contracts that every provider must use strong protections, including access controls, handling incidents, fixing weaknesses, secure configurations, recording activity, encryption, complying with privacy requirements, and ongoing monitoring. The organisation must also clearly document who is responsible for overseeing these services and what access roles users and staff have. It must then continuously check that providers are meeting their obligations by using quarterly independent reports, annual independent assessments, regular reviews of service-level agreements, ongoing monitoring of provider alerts and logs, and tracked follow-up actions until any issues are fully resolved.

##### External provider security addendum with evidence rights & SLA remedies

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create and require a standard contract addendum called **“External System Services Security & Privacy Addendum”** for all external service providers. It must require them to put in place specific security and privacy controls, provide proof of compliance on a set schedule, support continuous monitoring by sharing monitoring data, and meet measurable service-level agreement (SLA) results. It must also include remedies and required actions if they do not comply.

###### WHY (control requirement)
**SA-9** requires that when the organisation uses external system services, it builds and maintains confidence through a clear chain of trust. This is done by recording why the organisation trusts each external relationship and by ensuring providers are contractually required to implement the needed controls and measurable outcomes, including remedies and required actions for non-compliance. The responsibility for managing risk remains with the authorising officials.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. Create a **“Provider Security & Privacy Requirements”** catalogue that maps to the organisation’s required controls for external services (access control, incident response, vulnerability management, secure configuration, audit logging, encryption, privacy compliance, continuous monitoring).
2. Create a contract addendum template that:
   - Clearly lists the required security and privacy controls.
   - Defines service boundaries and expectations for how data is handled (including privacy obligations).
   - Defines end-user security roles and responsibilities, and provider responsibilities.
   - Requires evidence delivery: **quarterly SOC 2 (Type II)** and/or **ISO 27001** reports, plus **annual independent assessments**.
   - Requires support for continuous monitoring: the provider must supply security monitoring data (for example, logs compatible with **security information and event management (SIEM)** and/or alert webhooks/application programming interfaces (APIs)) so the organisation can monitor whether the provider’s controls are being met.
   - Defines measurable SLA outcomes (for example: how quickly patches and vulnerability fixes are completed by severity, encryption requirements, audit log retention, how quickly alerts are issued and notifications are sent, and incident response timeframes).
   - Sets out remedies and required actions for non-compliance (for example: fix timelines, escalation steps, service credits, and rights to support transition/hand-over if the service ends).
3. Include oversight and monitoring responsibilities in the addendum (for example, **RACI** for authorising official oversight, security team review, and escalation of operational incidents).
4. Put in place a contract approval process so that no external services contract is approved unless it includes the addendum, evidence rights, SLA remedies, and telemetry/data-sharing requirements.
5. Keep an auditable **“trust relationship record set”** for each provider, including: the signed contract/addendum versions, the applicable version of the requirements catalogue, received evidence (quarterly/annual), SLA review results, details of continuous monitoring data access, and remediation tracking with proof of closure.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Authorising Official** (responsible for risk management and oversight of external services)
- **Procurement/Legal** (contracting and enforceability)
- **Information Security/Privacy Officer** (mapping requirements, defining evidence review criteria, and defining SLA outcome measures)
- **Service Owner/Vendor Manager** (day-to-day contact, conducting SLA reviews, and coordinating remediation)

###### Acceptance criteria
- **100%** of external system service contracts include the required security and privacy addendum template (or an approved exception with documented risk acceptance).
- For each provider, quarterly **SOC 2/ISO 27001** evidence and annual independent assessment evidence are received and checked against the defined requirements catalogue.
- SLA clauses include measurable outcomes and clear remedies/required actions for non-compliance.
- Continuous monitoring data sharing is contractually required and operationally available (for example, **SIEM** log ingestion and/or an alert notification mechanism).
- A complete, audit-ready trust relationship record set exists for each provider, including remediation tracking with proof of closure.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a “Provider Security and Privacy Requirements” catalogue that links each required external service control to the relevant requirement (access control, incident response, vulnerability management, secure configuration, audit logging, encryption, privacy compliance, and continuous monitoring).
- [ ] Publish a standard “External System Services Security and Privacy Addendum” template that includes: evidence access rights, the responsibilities of end users and service providers, and the service-level agreement (SLA) remedies and response requirements.
- [ ] Add quarterly evidence delivery requirements for the Service Organisation Controls 2 (SOC 2) and International Organization for Standardization 27001 (ISO 27001) into the addendum, and include the requirement for an independent assessment each year.
- [ ] Add contractual requirements for continuous monitoring data (for example, logs compatible with security information and event management (SIEM) and/or alert notification mechanisms) that are sufficient to support the provider’s control compliance monitoring.
- [ ] Set measurable service level agreement (SLA) targets, split by severity, for patching and fixing vulnerabilities, responding to incidents, encryption, audit logging, and how quickly alerts and notifications are sent.
- [ ] Set up a contract approval checkpoint so that no external services contract can be authorised unless the required addendum is included and any agreed deviations are documented.
- [ ] Set up and keep a complete, auditable record of the trust relationship for each provider. This should include evidence reviews, the results of service level agreement (SLA) reviews, and tracking of any fixes, with proof of closure.


---

#### SA-9.1 — Risk Assessments and Organizational Approvals (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the organisation checks the risks carefully before it pays a third party to run or provide security services, and only proceeds when the right senior decision-makers have approved it. Without this, the organisation could end up outsourcing security work to a provider that creates new problems for the business, such as weaknesses that attackers could exploit, mishandling personal information, disruption to important services, or hidden reliance on other companies in the provider’s supply chain.

To meet this expectation, before buying or outsourcing dedicated security services (for example, running security tools, monitoring and responding to incidents, or providing key security services), the organisation must complete a written risk assessment. This assessment must cover: impact on the business or mission, security risk, privacy risk, system risk, and supply-chain risk. The organisation must then obtain approval from the Chief Information Security Officer and the Contracting Officer Representative before the contract is awarded or the outsourcing starts, and keep clear records showing that both approvals and the risk assessment were completed on time.

##### Procurement gates for outsourced security services risk & approvals

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Before the organisation awards or commits to any **dedicated information security services** (for example, managed security device operations, key management services, and incident monitoring, analysis, and response), the organisation must:
1) complete a **written organisational risk assessment** covering **mission/business**, **security**, **privacy**, **systems**, and **supply chain** risks; and
2) obtain **documented approval** from the defined approving roles **before** the award or outsourcing begins.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-9(1)** enhancement requires that risk assessments for information security services include the relevant risk types (including supply chain) and that the organisation obtains **organisational approvals** from the specified people before it proceeds with buying or outsourcing dedicated security services.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Set up an auditable procurement process with two enforced checkpoints and an evidence pack:
1. **Define scope in procurement terms**: configure the procurement system to flag any planned purchase or outsourcing that counts as “dedicated information security services” (for example, security operations centre monitoring, analysis, and response; managed firewall operations; key management operations).
2. **Use a structured risk assessment template**: require a written risk assessment report with mandatory sections that explicitly cover:
   - **Mission/business impact**
   - **Security risk**
   - **Privacy risk**
   - **System risk** (including impacts to the organisation’s systems and environments)
   - **Supply-chain risk** (including the provider’s subcontractors and dependencies)  
   Record the service start date and the contract award or commitment date.
3. **Enforce timing through workflow checks**: add workflow rules that stop contract signing or purchase order release unless the risk assessment **is completed and dated before** the award or outsourcing start.
4. **Enforce two approvals**: require recorded approvals from the organisation-defined roles:
   - **Chief Information Security Officer (CISO)**
   - **Contracting Officer Representative (COR)**  
   The workflow must block the award or commitment until both approvals are in place, with timestamps and identities recorded.
5. **Create and keep an evidence pack**: automatically compile and store an auditable record linking:
   - the risk assessment report (with dates and scope)
   - the contract or purchase order document
   - the approval records (CISO and COR)
   - the service start date and contract award or commitment date  
   Store this in the acquisition file repository for audit.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Chief Information Security Officer (CISO)**: approves the organisational risk assessment for security, privacy, and system impacts.
- **Contracting Officer Representative (COR)**: approves the acquisition or outsourcing decision from a contracting perspective.
- **Procurement operations / contract management**: sets up and runs the procurement workflow checkpoints.
- **Information security risk owner / security governance team**: completes the risk assessment report.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every procurement of dedicated information security services, the acquisition file must include:
  - a **written** organisational risk assessment report that explicitly covers **mission/business, security, privacy, system, and supply-chain** risks; and
  - **documented approvals** from **both** the **CISO** and **COR**, with timestamps; and
  - evidence that the risk assessment date is **earlier than** the contract award or commitment and the outsourcing or service start.
- The procurement workflow must prevent contract signing or purchase order release if either the risk assessment document is missing or undated, or if one or both approvals are missing.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up the procurement process to identify and flag purchases or outsourcing of dedicated information security services.
- [ ] Create a written risk assessment template with required sections covering mission or business risks, security risks, privacy risks, system risks, and supply-chain risks.
- [ ] Put in place checks within the workflow to stop any contract award or purchase order release unless the risk assessment is attached and dated before the award or outsourcing start date.
- [ ] Require two separate documented approvals before any signature or commitment: one from the Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) and one from the Contracting Officer Representative (COR).
