- [ ] Automate the creation of an evidence pack that can be audited, linking the risk assessment, the contract or purchase order, the identities of the people who approved it, and the relevant dates.
- [ ] Save the evidence pack in the acquisition file repository and check that it can be retrieved when needed for an audit.


---

#### SA-9.2 — Identification of Functions, Ports, Protocols, and Services (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure every external provider of managed services clearly explains how their service connects and works. This includes what the service does, which numbered access points it uses, what communication rules it follows, and any supporting services it depends on. This matters because if these details are unclear or missing, the organisation may accidentally allow the wrong connections, find it difficult to block unsafe traffic, or be unable to explain and manage what is actually being used—leading to avoidable risk of disruption, data loss, or unauthorised access.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must ask providers of cloud and on-premises managed services—including virtual private networks (VPN), domain name system (DNS), email, web hosting, application programming interface (API) gateways, and third-party remote access—to provide a written, service-specific list before using the service or at the start of use. The organisation must then record and keep this information in its procurement and oversight documents, ensure it is included in contracts and service agreements and in the buying documents, and check that it matches the organisation’s security requirements.

##### Contractual Technical Appendix for vendor service ports/protocols

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
For every external system service provider (for example: cloud/on-prem managed services, virtual private network (VPN), domain name system (DNS), email, web hosting, application programming interface (API) gateways, and third-party remote access), require a **service-specific written Technical Appendix**. This appendix must clearly state the **functions**, **network ports**, **communication methods (protocols)**, and **other required services** needed to deliver that specific service.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-9(2)** enhancement requires the organisation to require providers of the defined **external system services** to identify the **functions, ports, protocols, and other services required** for use of those services (parameter: **sa-09.02_odp**).

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Update procurement and contract templates** for all in-scope external managed services to include a mandatory schedule titled (or equivalent to) **“Technical Appendix – Service Connectivity & Dependencies”**.
2. Define a **standard appendix format** that, at minimum, includes per service:
   - **Functions** (what the provider will do for the service)
   - **Ports** (explicit port numbers and network endpoints; include both the data connection and the management connection where applicable)
   - **Protocols** (explicit protocol names/variants, for example HTTPS/Transport Layer Security (TLS), DNS over User Datagram Protocol (UDP)/Transmission Control Protocol (TCP))
   - **Other required services or components** (for example identity and authentication integration, logging and monitoring endpoints, update or management services)
   - **Scope mapping** (each listed item must be linked to the specific service being delivered, not stated in general terms)
3. Add a **contract approval workflow checkpoint** (for example in a contract lifecycle management (CLM) tool) that blocks approval unless the appendix is present, includes explicit entries for **ports** and **protocols**, and is provided **before or at the start of service use**.
4. Require **version control and change notification**: any change to functions, ports, protocols, or required services must trigger an updated appendix and be recorded in the service oversight record.
5. Store the appendix in a **controlled repository**, linked to the supplier and service record, and to the relevant system or service oversight documents (so the information can be traced during audits).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement and Commercial Contract Manager**: ensures templates and workflow checkpoints are used.
- **Information Security / Security Architecture**: defines the appendix format and checks it is complete.
- **Service Owner / Information Technology (IT) Operations**: confirms the appendix is captured before the service is onboarded and maintains the link to the service oversight records.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each in-scope external managed service in use, there is a **service-specific Technical Appendix** that explicitly lists **functions, ports, protocols, and other required services**.
- The appendix is **available before or at the start of service use** and is kept in the organisation’s service oversight and procurement records.
- Contract approval cannot be completed in the workflow unless the appendix passes minimum completeness checks (ports and protocols are present and mapped to the specific service).
- When the provider changes connectivity or dependencies, an updated appendix is produced, versioned, and includes an auditable record of changes.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update procurement and contract templates to include a mandatory schedule called **“Technical Appendix – Service Connectivity and Dependencies”** for all in-scope external managed services.
- [ ] Define and publish an internal appendix template that requires clear, explicit entries for each service’s: functions, ports (port numbers and endpoints), protocols (names and variants), and any other required services or components—mapped to that specific service.
- [ ] Set up a contract approval “gate” in the workflow that stops approval unless the appendix is included, and ensure the required network ports and protocols are provided before or at the start of the service.
- [ ] Require providers to include management-plane and data-plane connectivity details (where applicable) in the appendix, rather than using generic descriptions.
- [ ] Store the completed appendix in a controlled, version-managed repository. Link it to the relevant vendor or service record and to the related service oversight documents.
- [ ] Add a contractual change-notification clause requiring an updated appendix to be submitted whenever functions, ports, protocols, or required services change.


---

#### SA-9.3 — Establish and Maintain Trust Relationship with Providers (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure you can rely on external suppliers, not just on what they say, but on written commitments you can check. Without this, a supplier could fail to protect your systems and data, conceal weaknesses, ignore the security settings you agreed, or delay telling you about a security incident—leaving you exposed to financial loss, service disruption, regulatory issues, and harm to people’s personal information.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must, before and while using each external service provider, set out and record the security and trust requirements in contracts or service agreements. This includes providing evidence that the required security standards are met (CMMC-L2/L3 evidence), allowing audit rights, supporting ongoing security monitoring, following the agreed secure configuration settings, and reporting any security incident within 24 hours.

Where relevant, the contracts must also cover how regulated information is handled and how vulnerabilities are managed, as well as privacy obligations such as collecting and using only the minimum necessary data, having a lawful basis for processing, notifying breaches within 72 hours, and limiting access to personal data to only what is needed.

The organisation must keep the relevant records showing these trust arrangements are in place and continue to be followed.

##### Provider trust clause pack with evidence gates and incident timelines

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up and keep enforceable trust arrangements with external service providers by building the organisation’s security and privacy trust expectations into contracts and service-level agreements (SLAs), and by requiring proof-based acceptance and ongoing assurance.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-9(3)** requires the organisation to **set up, document, and maintain trust arrangements** with external service providers. This must be based on the organisation’s defined **security expectations, characteristics, factors, or conditions** (parameter: **sa-9.3_prm_1**) and must reflect how confident the organisation is that the provider’s risk is at an acceptable level. The guidance also stresses that trust is complex and must be supported by **contract terms** and **evidence that the approach works**, including support for incident response and plans for upgrades or replacing outdated components.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Use a standardised **“Trust Clause Pack”** and a proof-based **trust gate** during procurement and vendor management:
1. Create a clause pack that clearly covers the organisation’s trust expectations in **sa-9.3_prm_1**, including: **CMMC-L2/L3 evidence**, **audit rights**, **support for continuous monitoring**, **secure configuration baseline requirements**, and **24-hour incident reporting**.
2. Add an annex defining the **minimum incident-notification trigger**, the **communication channel(s)**, and the **minimum information** to be reported within 24 hours, aligned to the contract.
3. Add an annex defining the **secure configuration baseline** requirement (for example, a version-controlled baseline standard and what changes must be notified). Require the provider to prove compliance (for example, provide baseline assessment outputs) on a defined schedule.
4. Add an annex defining **continuous monitoring support** (what monitoring outputs are provided, how often, and how the organisation receives proof) so trust is maintained during ongoing service delivery.
5. Add an annex defining **audit rights** (including rights for authorised representatives, access to evidence, and verification that fixes work). Require the provider to supply evidence showing effectiveness.
6. Apply an **acquisition trust gate**: do not approve or allow “go-live” until the provider submits an **evidence pack** mapped to the clause pack and it has been reviewed for being up to date (for example, evidence “freshness” rules defined by the organisation).
7. Apply **ongoing maintenance**: require periodic updates of evidence and change notifications linked to the provider’s monitoring outputs and configuration baseline changes. Record all trust arrangement documents (contracts/annexes, evidence packs, review outcomes, and incident-notification records).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement / Contracting lead**: ensures the clause pack is inserted into contracts and that the contract can be enforced.
- **Supply-chain risk / vendor management lead**: runs the trust gate process and maintains the provider trust register.
- **Information Security (and Privacy, where applicable)**: checks that evidence matches the requirements, that incident-notification requirements are clear, and that baseline and monitoring arrangements are sufficient.
- **Legal/compliance**: ensures contract wording is enforceable and consistent with regulatory obligations.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each external provider in scope, there is a signed contract/SLAs that includes the Trust Clause Pack annexes covering **CMMC-L2/L3 evidence**, **audit rights**, **continuous monitoring support**, **secure configuration baselines**, and **24-hour incident reporting**.
- For each provider, there is a documented evidence pack mapped to the clause pack requirements, and the provider cannot be approved for go-live without evidence review sign-off.
- Ongoing trust maintenance records exist (evidence updates, monitoring outputs received, baseline compliance evidence, and documented review outcomes).
- Incident response records show that the provider’s notification process and 24-hour reporting expectations are both defined in the contract and can be tested in practice (for example, through a tabletop exercise or documented testing/validation where feasible).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Draft and get approval for a standard Trust Clause Pack that directly covers **sa-9.3_prm_1**, including: **CMMC Level 2 and Level 3 evidence**, **audit rights**, **support for continuous monitoring**, **secure configuration baseline settings**, and **24-hour incident reporting**.
- [ ] Create template packs for collecting provider evidence and guidance on how to match that evidence to each clause pack annex, including rules for how up-to-date the evidence must be.
- [ ] Add an “acquisition trust” checkpoint to the vendor onboarding process. Before the vendor can go live, require security approval to confirm that all required evidence is complete, up to date, and matches the contract requirements.
- [ ] Set out and document what starts an incident notification, which communication channels must be used, and the minimum information that must be included for reporting within 24 hours. Include these requirements in the contract annex.
- [ ] Set up a regular schedule to update evidence and notify changes so that continuous monitoring can keep the baseline compliance secure, and record the results in the provider trust register.
- [ ] Carry out a contractual enforceability review with Legal and Compliance to make sure audit rights and requirements to provide evidence are clearly stated and practical to follow.


---

#### SA-9.4 — Consistent Interests of Consumers and Providers (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure that, when you use external firms to manage your on-site technology and operational systems, their people and working practices genuinely align with your organisation’s interests. Otherwise, a provider could supply unsuitable staff, conceal who really owns the business, use subcontractors you have not approved, or act in ways that put your organisation at risk—even if the contract documents look correct.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must first clearly identify which external providers and which types of their staff are covered. It must then carry out background checks on the selected individuals before they start work on your premises, and keep evidence that these checks were completed. Before awarding or continuing contracts, it must review records of ownership and any subcontractors. It should only use providers with a proven record of successful work for you (or, where this is not possible, document an approved exception and use an alternative way to provide assurance). The organisation must also record the reasons why each provider is considered trustworthy.

Finally, it must plan and carry out regular, periodic, unannounced visits to the providers’ sites, and keep records of what was checked and any issues found.

##### Provider trust onboarding with evidence-led access gating

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an evidence-based process for bringing external service providers on board and controlling their access. The process should confirm that the providers’ interests match the organisation’s interests by: (1) requiring background checks for selected provider staff, (2) checking ownership and subcontractor records, (3) using only trusted providers with a documented basis for trust (or an approved exception with extra safeguards), and (4) ensuring contracts allow routine, periodic, unannounced visits to provider facilities.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 Security and Assessment (SA)-9(4) enhancement requires the organisation to “verify that the interests of {{sa-09.04_odp.01}} are consistent with and reflect organisational interests” using these actions: background checks for selected provider staff, reviewing ownership records, using only trustworthy service providers (for example, proven prior trust relationships), and carrying out routine periodic unannounced visits to provider facilities.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define scope and selection criteria**: Keep an authoritative catalogue of external service providers that manage on-premises information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) systems and supporting services. Include risk tiers and identify which provider staff categories are “selected” (for example, people with privileged access, staff with on-site data-centre/OT access, and identity/administration support).
2. **Implement an onboarding workflow with required evidence fields** (in a governance, risk, and compliance (GRC) or workflow tool):
   - **Background check evidence** (provider staff identity, check type, completion date, and expiry/renewal schedule).
   - **Ownership and subcontractor records** (extract from the ownership register or equivalent, subcontractor list, and what subcontractors are allowed to do).
   - **Trust basis record** (evidence of a prior successful relationship, performance or renewal approvals, reference checks, or a documented approved exception with extra safeguards).
3. **Enforce access control gates** (policy-as-code / workflow controls):
   - Prevent or automatically revoke “on-premises access approval” until background check evidence and the trust basis are recorded and still valid.
   - Prevent privileged access being granted until the person is in the approved selected-staff list and their evidence is current.
4. **Link to identity and access management**: When onboarding is approved, grant time-limited access through enterprise identity provider (IdP) groups and roles (for example, Microsoft Entra ID or Okta) and apply conditional access for privileged actions. Ensure access time windows are linked to the onboarding record.
5. **Build unannounced visit rights into purchasing**: Update contract/service level agreement (SLA) templates and onboarding packs to include the organisation’s right to conduct routine periodic unannounced facility visits, the provider’s cooperation duties, and a way to record visit outcomes and any issues.
6. **Maintain audit-ready evidence records**: Store tamper-evident (or immutable) records that link the provider, contract, staff member, access time window, screening evidence, ownership/subcontractor review results, and the trust basis.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Third-Party Risk Manager / Vendor Governance Lead**: owns the provider catalogue, trust criteria, and evidence requirements.
- **Procurement / Contract Manager**: updates contract/SLA templates to include visit rights and cooperation requirements.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead / Security Engineering**: implements access control gating with the identity provider and privileged access controls.
- **GRC / Compliance Analyst**: maintains the evidence records and supports audit readiness.

###### Acceptance criteria
- There is an onboarding workflow that requires and records: (a) background checks for selected staff, (b) review of ownership and subcontractor records, and (c) a documented trust basis (or an approved exception with extra safeguards).
- On-premises access approval is technically blocked until the required evidence is present and valid; privileged access granting is also gated in the same way.
- Contract/SLA templates include rights and obligations for routine periodic unannounced facility visits.
- The evidence records show traceability from the provider and staff member to the contract, access time window, and the screening/trust/ownership review records.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and publish an approved external provider catalogue for on-premises information technology (IT), operational technology (OT), and supporting services, grouped by risk level.
- [ ] Define which provider personnel categories must undergo background checks and have privileged access approved before they can access systems.
- [ ] Set up a governance, risk and compliance (GRC) and workflow onboarding process that requires supporting evidence for background checks, identifying the responsible party and any subcontractors, and recording the basis for trust.
- [ ] Use “policy as code” to prevent on-premises and privileged access approvals until the required evidence is available and up to date.
- [ ] Update the contract and service level agreement (SLA) templates to include rights for routine, periodic, unannounced facility visits, and to require the provider to cooperate with these visits.
- [ ] Link onboarding approval records to the organisation’s identity provider to automatically set up time-limited access that is recorded against the evidence ledger.
- [ ] Set up an audit-ready, tamper-evident record (evidence ledger) that links each provider, the people involved, the access time windows, and the results of each verification.


---

#### SA-9.5 — Processing, Storage, and Service Location (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure your information, and the services that handle it, are only used in approved locations. This helps ensure it is never processed, stored, or delivered from the wrong country. If you allow providers to use other locations, you may lose control over which laws apply, make it harder to investigate and respond to incidents, and increase the risk that sensitive or regulated information is handled in ways you are not authorised to support.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must restrict where information is processed, stored, and where system services run to the United Kingdom and the United States (including facilities controlled by the United States Department of Defense (DoD)). It must only use specific hosting and processing locations that are explicitly listed in its system plan and that have been checked and approved by Legal and Compliance for the relevant export and certification obligations.

The organisation must include these location limits in its procurement processes, tender documents, service agreements, and contracts with external providers. It must keep evidence showing Legal and Compliance have validated each approved location, and it must regularly confirm ongoing compliance whenever providers or service arrangements change.

##### SSP-approved location register with legal/compliance sign-off gates

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an auditable, system-specific **Approved Processing/Storage/Service Locations Register** and ensure that **only locations listed in the system security plan (SSP)** are used for information processing, data storage, and delivering system services. Every listed location must be **checked and approved by Legal/Compliance** against the organisation’s stated requirements (for example: **International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR)**/**Export Administration Regulations (EAR)** and **Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification level 2/level 3 (CMMC-L2/CMMC-L3)**).

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-9(5)** enhancement requires: **“Restrict the location of information processing, information and data storage, or system services to [defined locations] based on [defined requirements].”** Here, the restricted locations are **the United Kingdom (UK) and the United States (US) (including Department of Defense (DoD)-controlled facilities)**. The restriction is based only on **approved hosting and processing locations** that are **listed in the SSP** and **validated by Legal/Compliance** for **ITAR/EAR and CMMC-L2/CMMC-L3**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and keep up to date a register** (for each system and for each service type, such as web/application programming interface (API) layer, data layer, backup/disaster recovery (DR), logging/security information and event management (SIEM) forwarding, and any supplier-managed support portals). The register must list the **exact permitted processing/storage/service locations** (for example: cloud regions, data-centre identifiers, tenancy/contracted footprints, and any DoD-controlled facility identifiers).
2. For every register entry, record **evidence of Legal/Compliance validation** that the location is approved for the organisation’s requirements (**ITAR/EAR and CMMC-L2/CMMC-L3**). Include **scope, effective dates, and the identity of the approver**.
3. **Put the register into the SSP** by referencing it, and make sure the SSP does not rely on vague statements (for example, “UK/US only”) unless those statements are mapped to the specific locations listed in the register.
4. Apply **procurement and change-management controls** so that no new contract/service level agreement (SLA), subcontractor, or service configuration can be approved unless the supplier’s proposed regions/facilities match an **Approved Location Register ID**.
5. Add **operational checks**: confirm that every live environment/service instance is tagged with the **Approved Location Register ID** (using configuration management database (CMDB) or service inventory metadata). Also ensure runtime settings (for example, cloud region selection, backup/replication targets, and log export destinations) cannot be changed to non-approved locations without raising a change request.
6. Define **change triggers** that require re-validation and an SSP update when suppliers change regions, add new subcontractors, change service plans, or change data flows that affect where processing/storage/service delivery happens.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: responsible for aligning the SSP and ensuring services are deployed only to approved locations.
- **Information Security / Governance, Risk & Compliance (GRC)**: owns the structure of the Approved Locations Register, the evidence requirements, and SSP updates.
- **Legal/Compliance**: carries out and signs the location validations for **ITAR/EAR and CMMC-L2/CMMC-L3**.
- **Procurement / Vendor Management**: ensures procurement documents and SLAs reference the Approved Location Register and prevent unapproved locations.
- **Cloud/Infrastructure Engineering (or operational technology (OT) supplier management where relevant)**: ensures technical configurations and service deployments are tagged and restricted to approved locations.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The SSP includes a **restricted locations section** that maps to an **Approved Locations Register** listing **only the UK and US (including DoD-controlled facilities)**.
- Each location in the register has **documented Legal/Compliance validation evidence** for **ITAR/EAR and CMMC-L2/CMMC-L3**, including effective dates and scope.
- Procurement and change records show that **no service instance or contract** is approved unless it maps to an Approved Location Register entry.
- CMDB/service inventory and deployment configurations show that **processing, storage, and service delivery** are tagged to approved location IDs, and that non-approved locations are blocked or require an exception process.
- When provider/service arrangements change, the organisation completes **re-validation and an SSP update** before the change is accepted into production.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the system-specific register of approved processing, storage, and service locations, using clear location identifiers for the United Kingdom and the United States (including facilities controlled by the United States Department of Defense).
- [ ] Get legal and compliance approval for every entry in the register that covers International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR), Export Administration Regulations (EAR), and the Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification level 2 (CMMC-L2) and level 3 (CMMC-L3). Keep the approval evidence together with the scope and the effective dates.
- [ ] Update the system security plan (SSP) to reference the register and list the approved locations for processing, storage, and service delivery.
- [ ] Update procurement templates, tender documents, and service level agreements (SLAs) to require suppliers to map locations to the Approved Location Register IDs, and to prohibit any locations that are not approved.
- [ ] Set up a change-management approval step so that any new or changed services cannot be approved unless every processing, storage, and service endpoint is linked to an approved location ID.
- [ ] Set up change triggers for any changes to the provider region, subcontractor, or service plan that require re-checking (re-validation) and updating the service security plan (SSP) before they can be used in live production.


---

#### SA-9.6 — Organization-controlled Cryptographic Keys (Enhancement)

This expectation is about keeping the secret “scrambling codes” (encryption keys) used to protect your information under your organisation’s control, even if you use an external supplier or outside service to store or move your data. If the supplier’s staff can access or use these keys, they could potentially remove the protection and read your confidential information. That could result in financial loss, legal issues, and damage to trust.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly identify which external systems handle your encrypted data. It must then set things up so that only the organisation can encrypt and decrypt the data. This can be done either by encrypting before sending and decrypting after receiving within the organisation, or by using an external component that still cannot reveal or use the keys.

The organisation must document how it will do this, include the requirement in its purchasing rules, procedures, contracts, requests for proposals, and service agreements, and assign named responsibilities for managing the keys and managing suppliers. It must also be able to provide evidence—through testing—that supplier staff cannot decrypt the data.

##### Exclusive key control via organisation-side encryption/decryption

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an encryption approach so that any data that is **stored or sent through an external system** is treated as **ciphertext only** by that external system. The **organisation** must do all **encryption and decryption**, using **cryptographic keys controlled by the organisation**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-9(6)** requires the organisation to **keep exclusive control of cryptographic keys** for encrypted data that is stored or sent through an external system. The guidance allows either:
- encrypt and decrypt **within the organisation** as data is sent and received, or
- use an external component that still allows **exclusive organisational access** to the encryption keys.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **List the external systems in scope** that will store or transmit the organisation’s encrypted data (for example: Software as a Service (SaaS) storage, managed file transfer, hosted ticketing attachments, cloud object storage, external application programming interfaces (APIs)).
2. **Choose the organisation-side encryption approach** for each in-scope data flow:
   - **Client-side or gateway-side encryption**: encrypt before data leaves; decrypt only after data enters.
   - Make sure the external system only ever receives/stores **ciphertext**.
3. **Use keys controlled by the organisation**:
   - Create and store keys in an organisation-managed key management system (KMS) / hardware security module (HSM) (or equivalent) under organisational control.
   - Configure the system so **decryption can only be requested from within the organisation boundary** (for example: only organisation service accounts can request decryption; the external provider cannot get key material or trigger decryption).
4. **Enforce exclusivity technically**:
   - Disable any “provider support decryption” capability (no plaintext export, no key escrow to the provider, no provider-managed decryption services).
   - Limit decryption permissions to tightly defined workload identities, and require strong authentication for any human access routes.
5. **Contractual and operational controls**:
   - Add procurement and contract/service level agreement (SLA) requirements stating that provider staff must not access keys or plaintext and must treat ciphertext as unreadable.
   - Require proof that the provider cannot decrypt (for example: configuration confirmations and/or controlled test results).
6. **Provide audit-ready evidence**:
   - Record the chosen approach in system security documentation (for example: security plan/design documents) and keep a record mapping each external system to the encryption/decryption approach.
   - Run and record verification tests showing that provider staff cannot decrypt (for example: try decryption using only provider-accessible routes; confirm it fails, and confirm that only organisation-controlled decryption endpoints work).

###### WHO
- **Application Owner / Data Owner**: confirms which external systems and data flows are in scope.
- **Security Architect / Cryptography Architect**: designs the encryption/decryption approach and the key exclusivity controls.
- **Platform/Cloud Engineering**: sets up key management system (KMS)/hardware security module (HSM) configuration, encryption/decryption services, and identity restrictions.
- **Procurement/Vendor Management**: updates contracts/SLAs and ensures provider obligations can be enforced.
- **Security Assurance / Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC)**: checks the evidence, maintains compliance records, and coordinates testing.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each identified in-scope external system, **only ciphertext** is stored/sent; the external system never receives plaintext.
- Decryption is possible **only** using organisation-controlled keys and organisation-controlled decryption endpoints/identities; provider-accessible accounts cannot decrypt.
- Contracts/SLAs include clear requirements that provider staff cannot access keys or perform decryption.
- Evidence is available showing verification results (configuration review plus testing) that provider staff cannot decrypt the organisation’s data.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep up to date an official list of external systems that store or send encrypted organisation data.
- [ ] Use encryption on the client side or at the gateway so external systems only receive and store encrypted data (ciphertext).
- [ ] Set up organisation-managed key management service (KMS) and hardware security module (HSM) keys, and limit decryption to service identities controlled by the organisation.
- [ ] Disable or block any provider-accessible decryption, key-escrow, or “support decryption” routes, and document this control.
- [ ] Update procurement documents, contracts, and service level agreements (SLAs) to require that the provider’s staff cannot access encryption keys or plaintext data.
- [ ] Run and record verification tests to prove that paths accessible to the provider cannot decrypt, while paths accessible to the organisation can decrypt successfully.


---

#### SA-9.7 — Organization-controlled Integrity Checking (Enhancement)

This requirement is about being able to confirm that information stored with an outside supplier stays complete and unchanged. Without this, data could be accidentally damaged or deliberately altered, and you might not find out until it is too late. This could result in incorrect decisions, financial loss, or regulatory issues.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure its systems can check the integrity (that is, whether the data is still complete and unaltered) while the information remains inside the supplier’s environment. This should use built-in checking methods that do not require moving or exporting the data.

The organisation must also have clear, documented procedures for how these checks are carried out. It must name specific people responsible for integrity checking and for making sure purchasing decisions include these needs. These requirements must be included in contracts, service agreements, and procurement documents so suppliers are required to cooperate.

Finally, the organisation’s plans for system security and supplier risk must cover how integrity checking will be carried out for information stored externally.

##### External storage integrity verification via provider attestations

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an organisation-controlled way to confirm that information has not been altered while it is stored inside an external provider’s system. Use provider-supported cryptographic integrity checks (for example, signed confirmations or challenge/response digest checks) **without moving the data out of the provider’s system**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST Special Publication 800-53 **SA-9(7)** (enhancement) requires the organisation to **check the integrity of information while it is stored in the external system**. The guidance notes that external storage can reduce visibility, and that integrity checks should be possible **without transferring data out of the external system**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the integrity approach** for each external dataset or object type (for example, an object-level hash, a chunk-level Merkle root, or a dataset-level signed list/manifest). Also define the **expected verification points** (for example, when data is written, on a scheduled basis, or when data is accessed or during an incident).
2. **Get provider support** by stating in procurement and contracts that the provider must support an **integrity verification method without exporting data**, such as:
   - a verification interface (application programming interface) that returns a signed confirmation of the current integrity information, or
   - a challenge/response process where the provider calculates the digests/roots internally and returns only the verification result/confirmation.
3. **Build an integrity-check service** in the organisation environment that:
   - stores the expected integrity information (for example, manifests/digests) in an organisation-controlled store,
   - logs in to the provider using enterprise identity (workload identity for automated processes; multi-factor authentication (MFA) for operators),
   - calls the provider’s verification interface/workflow,
   - checks the provider’s signatures/confirmations and compares the returned integrity information with the expected values.
4. **Run checks and keep evidence**:
   - perform checks according to the agreed schedule,
   - record verification requests, provider responses, signature/confirmation validation results, and evidence for how mismatches are handled in the security information and event management (SIEM) and logging platform,
   - raise alerts for mismatches, missing confirmations, or verification failures.
5. **Define and document procedures** for how integrity checks are requested, verified, recorded as evidence, and how mismatches are handled (for example, quarantine/recovery actions, escalation, and linking to incidents).
6. **Assign named responsibilities** (for example, the data owner/application owner for choosing datasets and defining expected integrity information, and the security/operations owner for running checks and reviewing evidence). Ensure these responsibilities are reflected in the system security plan and supplier management documentation.

###### WHO
- **Application/Data Owner**: decides which external datasets need integrity checking and the expected integrity approach/verification schedule.
- **Security Engineering / Security Operations**: implements the integrity-check service, validates signatures/confirmations, sets up alerting, and captures evidence.
- **Procurement/Vendor Management**: ensures contracts and service level agreements (SLAs) include provider cooperation for integrity verification without exporting data.
- **Incident Response Lead**: owns the mismatch/incident handling procedure and escalation.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each selected external dataset/object type, the organisation can request an integrity verification while the data remains in the provider environment and receive a cryptographically verifiable result (for example, a signed confirmation or a validated digest/manifest) **without exporting the data**.
- The integrity-check service validates the provider’s confirmations/signatures and produces audit-ready evidence (logs/records) for both successful and failed checks.
- Mismatches (or an inability to verify) trigger alerts and follow the documented escalation/incident handling procedure.
- Contracts and SLAs explicitly require the provider to support the integrity verification method used by the organisation.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the integrity checks for each external dataset (hashes, Merkle root, and signed manifest) and set how often the checks are repeated (verification cadence).
- [ ] Update procurement and service level agreements (SLAs) to require the provider to prove their integrity, without exporting any data (for example, using an application programming interface (API) or a signed attestation and challenge-response).
- [ ] Set up an organisation integrity-check service that logs in to the provider and verifies signed proof documents (attestations) and digital fingerprints (digests).
- [ ] Store the expected integrity information in a repository that is controlled by your organisation and access-restricted, and protect it using suitable integrity safeguards.
- [ ] Set up automated scheduled checks and alerts for any mismatches in the security information and event management (SIEM) or monitoring system.
- [ ] Document and train procedures for running integrity checks, keeping supporting evidence, and escalating any mismatches or incidents
- [ ] Assign named responsibilities for choosing datasets, running the verification checks, and reviewing the evidence


---

#### SA-9.8 — Processing and Storage Location — U.S. Jurisdiction (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping your organisation’s information processing and data storage physically within the United States, so it only happens in facilities located within the United States’ legal jurisdiction. The real-world risk it helps prevent is that sensitive business information could be handled or stored in other countries, where you may have less control, fewer protections, and fewer effective legal options if something goes wrong—such as unauthorised access or disruptive security incidents that could harm people, operations, and other organisations.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly set a “United States only” location rule and build it into how it buys and manages systems and services, including services provided by third parties. It must identify which information and services are covered, set clear requirements for external providers, and ensure these rules are included in procurement documents, contracts, and service agreements.

The organisation must also document how it decides which legal jurisdiction limits apply, and name specific people who are responsible for ensuring the rule is followed—from purchasing through day-to-day use.

##### US-only jurisdiction procurement gate with contract clauses

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place a mandatory “United States only” rule for where all information is processed and where data is stored (including third-party and managed services). Enforce it through a procurement and contracting approval step that cannot be bypassed.

###### WHY (control requirement)
This requirement, based on National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53, Security Assessment and Authorization (SA)-9(8) enhancement, means information must be processed and stored only in locations within the legal boundaries of the United States. This approach ensures the rule is clearly defined, applied during purchasing, and written into contracts for both processing and storage.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the United States-only rule** in an organisation standard. It must clearly cover both **information processing** (for example: computing, indexing, caching, log collection, analytics, and handling support tickets) and **data storage** (for example: databases, object storage, backups, disaster recovery replication, and archives). Also state what is covered (the system and any system services it uses).
2. **Create a check for each purchase** to confirm whether the supplier will process and/or store in-scope information. This includes subcontractors and support functions.
3. **Build the rule into the procurement process** using policy-based automation (or an equivalent automated control) so that purchasing cannot move to contract award until the jurisdiction check is completed and the required proof and contract wording are included.
4. **Standardise contract and service level agreement (SLA) wording** requiring the provider to: (a) process and store in-scope information only in United States facilities; (b) stop cross-border replication of in-scope data; (c) require subcontractors to follow the same United States-only requirement; (d) notify you of breaches or incidents; and (e) support audit rights and/or provide proof of compliance.
5. **Collect provider proof** when the supplier starts and at set review times (for example: signed statements confirming data location, lists of subcontractors, and proof of the configuration/region used to show United States-only processing and storage).
6. **Assign clear responsibilities (RACI)** across Procurement (the approval gate), Information Security (owner of the United States-only rule and proof standards), and Third-Party and Supply Chain Risk Management (management of subcontractor compliance and ongoing monitoring). Keep a documented escalation route for any exceptions.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement Lead / Category Manager**: runs the procurement approval gate and ensures no contract is awarded without completion.
- **Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) / Information Security Manager**: owns the United States-only rule, the proof requirements, and the approval criteria.
- **Third-Party Risk Manager**: manages supplier and subcontractor compliance and the ongoing review schedule.
- **Legal/Commercial Contracts Officer**: adds the standard contract clauses and negotiates any exceptions.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented United States-only jurisdiction rule exists and clearly covers processing and storage for the system and its system services.
- The procurement workflow includes an approval gate that cannot be skipped for the jurisdiction check for all relevant purchases (including software as a service (SaaS) add-ons, managed services, hosting, and support).
- All new or renewed contracts and SLAs for in-scope services include the standard United States-only jurisdiction clauses and the required audit and proof-of-compliance requirements.
- For each in-scope provider, onboarding proof is collected and kept (for example: signed location/jurisdiction statement, subcontractor list, and proof that processing/storage is in United States regions).
- Named roles and escalation procedures are in place, and exception handling is documented and approved by Information Security.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Publish a United States–only jurisdiction standard that defines which information processing and data storage activities are included (in scope).
- [ ] Create a jurisdiction applicability check for each acquisition to determine whether the provider processes and/or stores in-scope information.
- [ ] Set up an unskippable procurement checkpoint that requires the jurisdiction review to be completed before any contract is awarded.
- [ ] Update the standard contract and service level agreement (SLA) templates to require processing and storage only in the United States, restrict the use of subcontractors, and include audit and attestation requirements.
- [ ] At onboarding and renewal, require and keep proof from the provider, including a signed statement of residency/jurisdiction, a list of subcontractors, and confirmation that services are provided from the United States region.
- [ ] Assign responsibility (RACI) for procurement approval checkpoints, information security sign-off, and third-party risk monitoring, including documented handling of exceptions.


---

### SA-10 — Developer Configuration Management (Control)

This expectation is about making sure every change to a system is properly controlled, recorded, and checked for safety and privacy effects, from when it is first built through everyday use. Without this, harmful weaknesses could be added without being noticed, fixes might be missed or only partly applied, and unauthorised or accidental changes could enter the live system. This could lead to data loss, disruption to services, or privacy breaches.

To meet this requirement, the system developer must keep a controlled, documented record of changes for the system’s key building materials. This includes the formal design, design documents, build and implementation records, source code, hardware diagrams, test set-ups, and the version currently running. Only changes that have been approved by the organisation may be made, and each approved change must be logged together with its potential security and privacy impacts.

The developer must also track any security weaknesses found, record how each one is addressed, and report the findings and results to the system security officer and the incident response lead.

##### Gated developer change control with approved artefact integrity

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set up a developer configuration management process that (1) protects the master copies and security-relevant materials from unauthorised changes or destruction, (2) records and approves changes across the development and operations life cycle, (3) keeps a security-relevant change history, including what version is currently running, and (4) tracks security weaknesses and how they are fixed, with reporting to the system security officer and the incident response lead.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-10 Developer Configuration Management** requires developers to keep strict control of configurations throughout the system development life cycle. This is to track **authorised changes** and prevent **unauthorised changes**, including protecting master copies of security-relevant material and keeping controlled records for configuration items such as the **formal model, design specifications, implementation documentation, source code, hardware schematics, test fixtures, and the current running object code**. The guidance also expects tracking of **security weaknesses and how they are resolved**, with outcomes reported to the **system security officer** and the **incident response lead**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define configuration items (CIs) and link them to repositories/artifact stores**: formal model, functional/high-level/low-level design specifications, implementation documentation, source code, hardware schematics (digital materials), test fixtures/test documentation, and the current running object code (for example, signed release packages/images).
2. **Create an auditable change process in the organisation’s ticketing system**: every security-relevant change must reference an **approval record** and include required fields for **affected CIs**, **proof of authorisation**, **security impact**, **privacy impact**, and **rollback/verification proof**.
3. **Allow only approved changes to progress in CI/CD**: stop merges/builds/releases unless the pipeline can confirm the change is linked to an approved change record (for example, by checking a change ID included in commit information and verified by the pipeline).
4. **Protect the integrity of master copies and release materials**: require signed commits/artifacts (where supported), store checksums, produce and keep software bill of materials (SBOMs) and build provenance for each release, and limit write access to master branches/artifact repositories to authorised maintainers only.
5. **Track security weaknesses through to what is deployed**: link vulnerability/security weakness tickets to the exact CI/CD change(s) and release(s) that fix them; require closure criteria that include verification results and deployment evidence, and keep the mapping from weakness → fix → version.
6. **Keep an “intended vs running” version list during operation**: automatically record which signed release/version is deployed in each environment (development/test/production/disaster recovery) and raise exceptions when the running version does not match the approved release record.
7. **Report to the system security officer and incident response lead**: produce reports based on events and on a regular schedule summarising (a) new security weaknesses received, (b) weakness fixes and the deployed versions, and (c) any pipeline exceptions or version drift findings.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Developer lead / Engineering manager**: owns the CI/CD approval gating process and the CI mapping.
- **Security engineering / AppSec**: defines the required security/privacy impact fields, what verification is expected, and the rules for linking each weakness to its fix.
- **System security officer**: receives reports and checks that approvals and evidence meet organisational expectations.
- **Incident response lead**: receives weakness fix and deployment evidence relevant to incident handling.

###### Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- For each security-relevant release, there is an auditable record showing: the approved change ID, affected configuration items, security impact and privacy impact fields, verification evidence, and the resulting **current running object code** version.
- CI/CD pipelines do not allow release of materials that are not linked to an approved change record.
- Master copies (source/design/test materials) and release materials are protected using access controls and integrity measures (for example, signed materials/checksums/provenance), with evidence kept.
- Security weakness tickets can be traced end-to-end to the specific approved change(s) and the deployed version(s) where the fix is present.
- During operation, the system keeps an inventory of running versions and flags any unauthorised drift/exceptions for review and reporting.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document the configuration items approved by the organisation, and link each one to the relevant repository or storage location for files (including the currently running compiled software).
- [ ] Set up a change process for all security-relevant changes that requires approval evidence, and includes mandatory fields for both security impact and privacy impact.
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) so that code cannot be merged, built, or released unless the pipeline confirms it is linked to an approved change record (change ID verification).
- [ ] Put integrity protections in place for master copies and release items (for example, signed files, checksums, and provenance information), and keep audit evidence for each release.
- [ ] Link vulnerability and security flaw tickets to the change process, so that each flaw is matched to the approved change(s) and the software version(s) that have been deployed with the fix.
- [ ] Automate keeping track of the software version running in each environment, and raise an alert if it differs from the approved release record.
- [ ] Set up automated reports to the system security officer and the incident response lead showing the status of security flaws, the fixes applied, the software versions currently in use, and any changes or exceptions that do not match the expected setup.


---

#### SA-10.1 — Software and Firmware Integrity Verification (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that every piece of software and firmware you receive from a supplier includes a way to prove it has not been secretly changed. Without this, unauthorised changes could be missed, which could cause systems to malfunction, leak information, or be taken over. It also helps prevent counterfeit components being passed off as genuine.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must require in procurement and supplier agreements that the developer enables integrity checking for all delivered software and firmware, including any later updates. The supplier must also provide the information and the checking method you need to verify what you received.

You should keep clear records showing that the supplier’s integrity checks were actually used for the specific versions you received and for the updates you installed. These records should also be linked to the approved change paperwork, so you can trace what was checked and when.

##### Require developer integrity verification artefacts for software/firmware

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Require the system developer (and any upstream supplier) to provide integrity checks for all delivered software and firmware parts, including every update. Also require them to provide the integrity-check information and tools needed to confirm that the delivered items are genuine and unchanged.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-10(1)** (Enhancement) requires the **developer** to provide integrity checks for **software and firmware parts** so the organisation can spot unauthorised changes (and, where possible, help detect counterfeit items). The guidance notes that integrity can be checked using **developer-provided one-way hashes** and that delivered items include **updates**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a standard “Integrity Verification Artefact Pack” for each release** for both software and firmware parts (as applicable). The pack must, at minimum, include developer-provided integrity information (for example, **SHA-256 hashes** or an equivalent) and/or a developer-supplied verification method (for example, **signed manifests** or signed update packages) that can be checked offline or through an approved verification process.
2. **Update procurement and delivery contract wording** (statement of work (SOW), service level agreements (SLAs), order forms) to require the developer to:
   - enable integrity checks for **every delivered software and firmware part**;
   - enable the same integrity checking capability for **every delivered update/patch**;
   - provide integrity information tied to the exact component versions/build identifiers (build IDs);
   - include verification instructions and any required public keys/certificates (where signature-based checking is used);
   - explain (where feasible) how the method helps detect replacement/substitution and counterfeiting.
3. **Set up an automated release intake/acceptance process** within the organisation’s release management:
   - confirm the artefact pack is provided for each release/update;
   - check the delivered files against the developer’s hashes or verify signed manifests/packages;
   - record the result (pass/fail), the exact versions/build IDs checked, and the checking method used.
4. **Link proof of integrity checking to change control**:
   - connect evidence that integrity checking was enabled/received and that integrity checking was carried out to the relevant change request, release record, and deployment approval.
5. **Apply consistently across multiple environments** (development/test/production/disaster recovery): ensure the same verification artefacts and checks are used when moving releases between environments.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: update contracts (SOWs) and manage supplier onboarding requirements.
- **Application/Platform release manager**: define the artefact pack format and run release intake.
- **Security engineering / DevSecOps**: build and maintain the verification tools/process and manage key/certificate handling.
- **Change manager**: ensure evidence is linked to the relevant change authorisation records.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each software/firmware release and each installed update, the developer provides an integrity verification artefact pack containing integrity information and/or a verification method tied to the exact versions/build IDs.
- The organisation’s release intake process successfully validates the delivered items using the developer-provided method (hash comparison and/or signature/manifest verification) and records the outcome.
- Evidence of integrity checking (artefact pack receipt plus verification result) can be traced to the specific change/release approval record for both initial deliveries and updates.
- Contract requirements clearly cover both **delivered components** and **delivered updates**, and explicitly require the developer to enable integrity verification.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an “Integrity Verification Artefact Pack” template for software and firmware releases and updates, including integrity information such as hashes and/or signed manifests/packages.
- [ ] Update procurement documents (statement of work (SOW), service level agreements (SLAs), and order terms) to require that every delivered component and every update includes integrity checks that developers have enabled.
- [ ] Require suppliers to provide integrity information linked to the exact component version and build identifier, and to include the required public keys or certificates whenever digital signatures are used.
- [ ] Implement a release intake acceptance checklist to confirm that the delivered files match the integrity information or verification method provided by the developer.
- [ ] Record the results of each verification (method used, software versions or build identifiers, whether it passed or failed, and the date and time). Then link those results to the matching change or release approval in the change management system.
- [ ] Run a trial with one supplier release to confirm that the package format and the checking process fit operational requirements before expanding to more suppliers.


---

#### SA-10.2 — Alternative Configuration Management Processes (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that when changes are proposed to important systems or services, they are reviewed and approved by the organisation before anything is put into use, even if there is no dedicated development team managing those changes. Without this, risky changes or updates that could harm privacy may be approved by mistake, leading to service disruption, exposure of sensitive information, or legal and reputational damage.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must put in place an alternative change-management process that uses suitable internal staff to review and approve every proposed change. This must include separate checks by people responsible for security and, where relevant, privacy. No change should be implemented until the required security and privacy impact assessments have been completed and recorded.

The organisation must also document how this will work in its change-management policy and plan. This should clearly set out roles and responsibilities and keep evidence of approvals and completed assessments for each change.

##### Three-gate alternate CM approvals for system/service changes

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Where there is no dedicated developer configuration management team, the organisation must use organisational staff to run an alternative configuration management process. Every proposed change to systems, system components, or system services must be reviewed and approved by organisational staff. Security and, where applicable, privacy impact assessments must be completed and reviewed before the change is carried out.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-10(2)** (Enhancement) requires an **alternative configuration management process** that uses **organisational staff** to **review and approve proposed changes** and to **complete security and privacy impact assessments before implementation**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Set up a “three-gate” change approval process and enforce it through your change or ticketing system:
1. **Define the alternative configuration management process** in the organisation’s configuration management policy and reference it in the configuration management plan. State clearly that it is used when there is **no dedicated developer configuration management team**.
2. **Set up mandatory approval gates** in the workflow:
   - **Configuration management (CM) gate**: organisational CM reviewer(s) check that the change is clearly described, linked to the affected configuration items, and matches the agreed baselines and standards.
   - **Security gate**: organisational security reviewer(s) check that a **security impact assessment** has been completed and reviewed.
   - **Privacy gate (conditional)**: organisational privacy reviewer(s) check that a **privacy impact assessment** has been completed and reviewed when personal data is involved.
3. **Require evidence for every change ticket before it can move to “implemented/released”**:
   - CM approval record
   - Security impact assessment document (or equivalent approved record)
   - Privacy impact assessment record where applicable
   - Proof of review and approval (including dates/times, who approved, and the decision)
4. **Control workflow progress** so the ticket cannot move to implementation unless all required gates and evidence are in place. Use workflow rules and, where available, automated checks in line with policy (for example: required attachments, identifiers for affected assets, and data classification tags that trigger a privacy review).
5. **Set up roles in practice** by naming organisational groups or roles for CM, security, and privacy reviewers, and make sure they are independent of the person raising the change.
6. **Use the process consistently** across relevant change types in your environment (for example: enterprise application configuration changes, infrastructure configuration changes, and updates to commercial off-the-shelf software or service configurations). Ensure the workflow is used for both manual changes and automated or continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD)-driven releases.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Configuration Manager / Configuration Management Lead**: owns the alternative configuration management process definition and the CM gate.
- **Information Security Officer / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the security impact assessment requirement and the security gate.
- **Data Protection Officer / Privacy Lead** (or equivalent): owns the privacy impact assessment requirement and the privacy gate (when personal data is in scope).
- **Change Manager / Service Owner**: ensures the workflow is used for all applicable changes.
- **Ticketing/Workflow Administrator**: sets up and enforces the rules in the change system.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For any applicable change, the workflow records **CM approval** and **security impact assessment review** **before** implementation.
- Where personal data is in scope, the workflow records **privacy impact assessment review** **before** implementation.
- The configuration management policy and plan document the **alternative configuration management process** used when there is no dedicated developer configuration management team.
- There is auditable evidence for a sample of changes (for example, the last 3 to 5 production changes): approvals, assessment documents, and timestamps show the correct order (approval and assessment completed before implementation).
- Workflow enforcement blocks movement to implementation when required evidence is missing.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update the configuration management policy and configuration management plan to define the SA-10(2) alternate configuration management (CM) process for situations where there is no dedicated developer configuration management (CM) team.
- [ ] Set up the change or ticket process so that **Configuration Management (CM)** and **Security** approvals are required, and **Privacy** approval is required only when applicable.
- [ ] Define the required ticket documents for each approval stage: the change management (CM) approval record, the security impact analysis, and the privacy impact analysis where personal data is in scope.
- [ ] Set up workflow controls so that items cannot be moved to “implemented/released” until all required approvals and supporting analyses have been added and reviewed.
- [ ] Assign independent review groups or roles for change management (CM), security, and privacy, and document what each group or role is responsible for.
- [ ] Run a pilot using the latest system or service and any changes to the configuration provided by the commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) product, and confirm that the audit evidence shows that all required approvals and assessments were completed before implementation.


---

#### SA-10.3 — Hardware Integrity Verification (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the hardware you buy and receive can be checked to confirm it has not been secretly altered before it reaches you. Without this, someone could replace parts or tamper with the device or its software, and you might only find out after it has caused disruption, data loss, or financial harm.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must require the supplier or developer to provide and enable a practical way to verify the hardware’s integrity using their own checking methods and tools. This can include hard-to-copy identifiers, verifiable serial numbers, and physical protections that show if tampering has occurred.

You must also ensure the same verification approach applies to any hardware and firmware updates delivered as part of the service. Finally, you should keep evidence that these checks were actually carried out for the items and updates you receive, and that the approach remains in place for later changes.

##### Developer-enabled hardware integrity checks for deliveries and updates

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Require the system developer (and any hardware supplier acting as part of the developer’s delivery) to put in place checks that confirm the integrity of hardware components. Also require that the same checks are applied to any delivered hardware and firmware updates.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-10(3)** (Enhancement) requires: **“Require the developer of the system, system component, or system service to enable integrity verification of hardware components.”** The guidance explains that integrity can be checked using **labels that are difficult to copy**, **serial numbers that the developer can verify**, and/or **anti-tamper technologies**. It also states that delivered hardware includes **hardware and firmware updates**, which must be covered by the same approach.

###### HOW (specific approach)
Set up an “Integrity Verification Enabled” delivery requirement that is written into the contract and carried out when hardware is received and commissioned.

1. **Create an Integrity Verification Approach (IVA) template** that the developer must complete for each hardware component family (and for each update type that includes hardware/firmware changes). The IVA must state:
   - The **integrity check(s)** the developer enables (for example: hard-to-copy label scheme, serial number format and how it is validated, anti-tamper indicators/technology).
   - The **steps the organisation can carry out** (including any tools, procedures, or validation contacts provided by the developer).
   - The expected **“authentic/original” values** (for example: rules for validating serial/label values, and cryptographic checks where relevant) and the **clear pass/fail criteria**.
   - How the checks cover **delivered hardware and delivered firmware updates** (for example: how the update’s authenticity is verified and linked to the component identity).

2. **Add procurement/contract clauses** requiring the developer to:
   - Enable the IVA checks for delivered hardware components.
   - Provide the organisation with the **instructions/tools** needed to carry out the checks.
   - Provide **coverage for any hardware/firmware updates** delivered as part of the service.
   - Provide an **evidence pack** for each delivery/installation/commissioning activity and for each update release.

3. **Use a standard receiving/commissioning acceptance process** for hardware and firmware updates:
   - Carry out the verification steps specified by the developer when the item arrives and/or is installed.
   - Record the outcome as **PASS/FAIL**, including the verified identifiers (for example: serial/label values) and any anti-tamper indicators.
   - If the developer provides verifiable serial numbers or tool-based checks, save the tool outputs and validation records.
   - For firmware/hardware updates, verify authenticity using the IVA method and link the evidence to the **asset record** and the **change/release identifier**.

4. **Keep audit-ready evidence**:
   - Store the IVA (approved version), the receiving/verification records, and the update verification records in the organisation’s configuration/change management repository.
   - Keep evidence for as long as required under the organisation’s records retention policy.

###### WHO
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: includes SA-10(3) IVA and evidence-pack requirements in contracts/Statements of Work (SOWs).
- **Security architect / compliance lead**: defines the IVA template, pass/fail expectations, and acceptance criteria.
- **Infrastructure/Operational Technology (OT)/engineering receiving team** (where applicable): performs the checks at delivery/commissioning and records the evidence.
- **Change/release manager**: ensures update evidence is captured and linked to the correct release/change record.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each delivered hardware component family, there is an approved **Integrity Verification Approach (IVA)** that specifies the enabled check(s) and the objective pass/fail criteria.
- For each delivery and each delivered hardware/firmware update, the organisation can carry out the developer-specified checks and produces an **audit-ready evidence record** showing PASS/FAIL and the verified identifiers/records.
- The evidence shows that the approach covers both **hardware components** and **delivered firmware updates** included with the service.
- No hardware/firmware update is accepted for production/operational use without recorded verification results that match the IVA.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an Integrity Verification Approach (IVA) template that requires: the verification mechanism(s), the verification steps, and clear objective pass/fail criteria for each hardware component family.
- [ ] Update the procurement and statement of work (SOW) contract clauses to require the developer to enable hardware integrity checks and to provide the organisation with the required tools and instructions.
- [ ] For each delivery and for each delivered hardware or firmware update, require an evidence pack that includes the verification results and the PASS/FAIL outcome, linked to the specific asset identifier and the release or change identifier.
- [ ] Create a receiving and commissioning acceptance checklist that runs the independent verification and records evidence that is ready for audit.
- [ ] Ensure that change and release processes require verification evidence based on in-vehicle assessment (IVA) for any delivered firmware or hardware updates before operational acceptance.
- [ ] Store internal verification approvals (IVA) and supporting verification evidence in the organisation’s configuration and change management repository, and keep them for the period required by the organisation’s retention policy.


---

#### SA-10.4 — Trusted Generation (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that when a developer creates a new version of the system’s key components, they can show exactly what has changed by automatically comparing the new output against the previously approved version. Without this kind of checking, harmful or unintended changes could be introduced during development. That could mean the system no longer works as intended, or that the protections it is meant to provide could be weakened.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system developer to use comparison tools to confirm that newly produced security-relevant hardware descriptions, source code, and object code match the earlier approved versions. In practice, the developer should be able to demonstrate that each new version of these items has been compared with what was approved before, so that any authorised updates continue to enforce the system’s security rules.

##### Trusted build gates using diffing of security-relevant artefacts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a developer “trusted generation” workflow that automatically checks newly created **security-relevant hardware descriptions, source code, and object code** against previously approved versions. New changes can only be promoted when the comparisons meet agreed criteria.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-10(4)** (Enhancement) requires developers to use **tools to compare newly generated versions** of **security-relevant hardware descriptions, source code, and object code** with **previous versions**. This helps ensure that approved changes do not accidentally weaken how security rules are enforced.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Identify security-relevant items** for the application’s delivery model (for example: infrastructure-as-code modules, device/firmware descriptors if relevant, build configuration files, security policy configuration bundles, and any compiled items that enforce security controls).
2. Keep an **approved baseline repository** for each release line. It must contain the previously approved versions of:
   - hardware descriptions (or equivalent items in your environment),
   - source code,
   - object code/build outputs (or reproducible equivalents).
3. In the CI/CD pipeline, add a **trusted generation stage** that:
   - creates the new items in a controlled build environment,
   - runs **automated comparison tools** for each item type (for example: text or syntax-aware comparisons for source and configuration; binary/object comparisons using hashes and/or binary comparison where appropriate),
