   - saves **comparison results and item identifiers** (commit IDs, build IDs, hashes, and signing/provenance information).
4. Apply **promotion gates**:
   - promotion is blocked unless the comparisons are either **no differences** or match an **authorised change record** (meaning the baseline update was explicitly approved through your existing change/release process),
   - object-code comparisons must be linked to **verifiable build provenance** (for example: deterministic build settings where possible, fixed (pinned) toolchains/dependencies, and/or signed artefacts).
5. Store proof in an **audit-ready, append-only** location (for example, immutable log storage) so auditors can see what was compared and what changed.

###### WHO
- **Application/DevOps Engineering Lead**: builds the pipeline steps and classifies the items to be checked.
- **Build/Release Manager**: ensures the baseline is managed correctly and that authorised change records are linked to promotions.
- **Information Security / Compliance**: checks that the saved evidence meets SA-10(4) expectations and that the right security-relevant items are included.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each release, the pipeline produces an auditable record showing that **newly generated hardware descriptions, source code, and object code** were **compared** against the **previous approved versions** using automated tools.
- Promotions to staging/production happen only when comparison results meet the agreed gate criteria (no unauthorised differences).
- Evidence includes: the baseline reference, new item identifiers, comparison/diff outputs (or equivalent hash/provenance comparison evidence for object code), and build provenance metadata.
- Security-relevant items are clearly covered by the comparison workflow (a coverage list is maintained and reviewed).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and record which items (“artefacts”) in your delivery pipeline are security-relevant for this application, such as hardware descriptions, source code, and compiled (object) code.
- [ ] Create and keep an approved “baseline” repository for each release line, for every type of security-relevant artefact.
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline steps that create new deliverables (artefacts) and automatically compare them against the approved baseline versions.
- [ ] Set up promotion gates so a release is blocked unless the changes are either empty or exactly match a change record that has been explicitly authorised.
- [ ] For each build, capture and store audit evidence, including the baseline reference, the identifiers for new artefacts, the difference results and checksums, and the build history and signing details.
- [ ] Review and test the workflow using at least one authorised change and one simulated unauthorised change to confirm that the access gate rules are enforced.


---

#### SA-10.5 — Mapping Integrity for Version Control (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the organisation’s “master version” records for important parts of the system stay exactly the same between what the developer builds and what the organisation holds on site. In everyday terms, it prevents the organisation’s paperwork and the real system from drifting apart. If they do drift, the organisation may unknowingly use the wrong software, firmware, or hardware designs, which can cause outages, failed updates, or hidden weaknesses that are difficult to spot and fix.

To meet this expectation, the system developer must keep a documented link between the developer’s master build information and the organisation’s on-site master copy for the currently approved version. The developer must update this link whenever the current version changes, and must check item by item that the two master records match for the current version during initial development and later updates.

The developer must also produce and keep clear evidence of these checks. This evidence must show the version, what was compared, the result, and that all relevant items were covered—including design details and source code identifiers—so the organisation can demonstrate compliance during review.

##### Signed version manifest and automated mapping reconciliation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
The system developer must keep the link (mapping) accurate between (1) the developer’s master build information describing the current version of security-relevant hardware, software, and firmware, and (2) the organisation’s on-site master copy of the same information for the current approved version. This means keeping an accurate, version-specific, item-by-item mapping, and providing proof that the two master copies match for the current version.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-10(5)** requires the developer to keep the mapping accurate between the master build information and the on-site master copy for the current version, including during initial development and updates over the lifecycle. The guidance stresses that changes to security-relevant components during development and updates must not cause differences (“drift”) between the developer’s records and the operational (on-site) records.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Create a **signed “version manifest”** produced by the developer whenever the current version changes, and use an **automated reconciliation** process to compare what is in the manifest with the organisation’s on-site master copy.

1. **Define a standard identifier system** for security-relevant items so both sides can match them consistently (for example, hardware part numbers/serial ranges where applicable, drawing/design identifiers, software module identifiers with build identifiers, and firmware versions with cryptographic hashes).
2. **Create a version manifest** from the developer’s build process for each “current version” release or update. The manifest must include, at minimum, the full list of security-relevant items and their identifiers (including designs/drawings and source-code identifiers/build identifiers) that make up the current version.
3. **Digitally sign the manifest** (using a signing key controlled by the developer) and store it in a repository with access controls. This protects the mapping record itself from unauthorised changes.
4. **Maintain the organisation’s on-site master copy** (for example, the configuration management database (CMDB)/asset inventory plus application/endpoint inventory and configuration records) using the same standard identifiers and version labels.
5. **Run an automated, item-by-item reconciliation** whenever a new version becomes “current” and after each approved update window. The reconciliation must:
   - Check that the manifest signature is valid.
   - Compare the manifest items to the on-site master copy items for the same current version.
   - Produce a comparison report showing matched items and any differences (missing, extra, or mismatched identifiers).
6. **Keep evidence of integrity checks** for the current version, including: the current version identifier, the manifest signature check result, the reconciliation date and time, the list of items checked, and the pass/fail outcome (including any differences so they can be investigated).
7. **Only approve a release/current version when the mapping matches**. Systems must not be treated as meeting SA-10(5) unless the reconciliation results show the mapping matches for the current version.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Developer / DevOps lead**: defines the identifier system, creates and signs the version manifests, and ensures the build process includes everything required.
- **Configuration management / CMDB owner (organisation)**: maintains the on-site master copy using the standard identifiers.
- **Security/compliance engineer**: checks that the evidence requirements are met and ensures the release gate aligns with governance.
- **Release manager**: applies the approval gate based on the reconciliation results.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each designated **current version**, a **signed version manifest** exists and is produced from the developer’s master build information.
- The organisation’s on-site master copy includes matching records using the same standard identifiers and version label.
- Automated reconciliation performs an **item-by-item comparison** between the manifest and the on-site master copy for the current version and produces an auditable report.
- Integrity check evidence is kept for each current version and includes: version ID, items checked, signature check status, reconciliation date and time, and pass/fail result.
- Approval for the release/current version is blocked when reconciliation shows mismatches for the current version.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define a single, standard set of identifiers for all security-relevant hardware, software, firmware, designs and drawings, and source code and build records.
- [ ] Add a build-pipeline step that creates a complete version manifest for every current-version release or update.
- [ ] Add a cryptographic signature to the manifest creation process, and store the signed manifests in a repository that is protected by access controls.
- [ ] Update the organisation’s on-site master records (configuration management database (CMDB) and inventory/configuration records) so they use the same standard identifiers and the current version labels.
- [ ] Set up an automated reconciliation job that checks the validity of manifest signatures and compares each item one by one against the on-site master copy.
- [ ] Set up release and current-version approval checks so that approval is only granted after a reconciliation check has been completed for the current version.
- [ ] Keep the reconciliation and signature-check evidence from the current version, including the list of items checked and the pass or fail result.


---

#### SA-10.6 — Trusted Distribution (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that any important update provided by a supplier is exactly the same as the approved version it was based on, with no hidden changes. Without this, a malicious or accidental change could be introduced into hardware, software, or firmware updates. That could cause systems to malfunction, expose sensitive information, or allow attackers to gain access through a “trusted” update.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the supplier to use documented, repeatable steps for trusted delivery. These steps must be based on clearly identified approved “master” copies. The supplier must check each update before sending it to confirm it matches the master copy exactly. The supplier must also keep evidence showing these checks were completed, and must only deliver the update after the verification succeeds.

The organisation must also:
- define which updates are considered security-relevant in its purchasing documents,
- set clear responsibilities in contracts and agreements,
- review and monitor the supplier’s update handling over time, and
- keep records that link each delivered update to the approved master copy and the verification results.

##### Trusted update distribution with master-copy traceability evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a developer-run, auditable “trusted distribution” process for all **security-relevant** hardware, software, and firmware updates delivered to the organisation. Each update must be **exactly the same as the developer’s approved master copies**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-10(6)** (Enhancement) requires the developer to use procedures that ensure security-relevant updates sent to the organisation are **exactly the same as the master copies**. This helps prevent tampering or accidental changes during delivery.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define what counts as “security-relevant updates”** in procurement and governance documents (for example: firmware/BIOS, signed installers, agent updates, container images, operating system/application patches, and any update that could affect the organisation’s security posture).
2. **Require the developer to keep approved master copies** using configuration management (including version/build identifiers, references to unchangeable files, and controlled change management).
3. **Require an exact-match check before release**, written into the contract, where the developer confirms each update matches the relevant master copy (for example: hash/signature checks and/or confirming the delivered file is identical). The developer must only release after this check succeeds.
4. **Use controlled delivery channels and release packaging** so updates cannot be changed between the check and delivery (for example: approved repositories/content delivery network endpoints, signed files, and controlled outputs from the build/release process).
5. **Require evidence for every delivery** that links the delivered update to the master copy and the verification result. At minimum, this must include: master copy ID/version, the expected verification value(s) (hash/signature), a release record reference, and confirmation of delivery.
6. **Set up an organisation process to receive and check evidence**: store the developer’s evidence in a controlled repository and, where possible, independently verify on receipt by comparing the delivered file’s hash/signature with the expected values provided by the developer.
7. **Keep checking and enforcing ongoing compliance** through supplier governance (service level agreement/key performance indicator checks, periodic evidence reviews, and audit rights) to ensure the trusted distribution process remains effective over time.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement / Vendor Management**: define update requirements in procurement documents, include contract/service level agreement clauses, and manage supplier governance.
- **Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC) / Security Assurance**: set evidence requirements, run the evidence intake process, and carry out periodic reviews.
- **Application/Infrastructure Release Engineering (or equivalent)**: ensure receipt-side verification steps are carried out consistently.
- **Supplier/Developer**: maintain master copies, perform exact-match verification, and provide evidence for each delivery.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each security-relevant update delivered, the organisation receives evidence that includes a **master copy identifier/version** and a **verification outcome** confirming the delivered update matches the master copy.
- The developer’s contract/service level agreement includes enforceable requirements for: **master copy maintenance**, **pre-release exact-match verification**, **controlled distribution**, and **per-delivery evidence**.
- The receipt-side process either (a) independently verifies the delivered file against the expected hash/signature values, or (b) records why independent verification is not feasible and relies on contractual evidence with compensating assurance.
- Evidence is stored in a controlled repository and can be traced to the receiving asset/release record for audit purposes.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish a list of “security-relevant updates” to be included in procurement (including firmware/basic input/output system (BIOS), signed software installers, updates to installed agents, and container images).
- [ ] Update the tender documents and contract/service level agreement (SLA) templates to require the developer to keep the approved master versions under configuration management.
- [ ] Add contract clauses requiring that, before any release, each update is checked to ensure it exactly matches the corresponding master copy, and that it is released only after the check is successful.
- [ ] Use controlled distribution channels and secure packaging so that verified items cannot be changed between the time they are checked and when they are delivered.
- [ ] Set required evidence details for every delivery (master copy ID/version, expected hash or signature, release record reference, and delivery confirmation), and set a deadline for when the evidence must be delivered.
- [ ] Set up a central place to collect evidence, and link each delivery’s evidence to the relevant receiving asset or release record so that audits can trace where everything came from.
- [ ] Where technically feasible, verify on the receiving side by comparing the delivered file’s hash or signature with the expected values provided by the developer.


---

#### SA-10.7 — Security and Privacy Representatives (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that the right experts formally approve important system changes before they are released to live (production) systems. This is important because production changes can unintentionally weaken security or mishandle people’s personal information, and fixing these problems after the update is already in place can be difficult and costly.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must include the Information System Security Officer in the formal change review and approval process for every approved production change to Confidential and Secret systems, so security risks are checked before the change is implemented.

In addition, the organisation must include the System Privacy Officer in the same review and approval process for every approved production change that affects personal data flows or processing, so privacy impacts are considered before anything is released.

##### Gate CAB approvals with ISSO/SPO mandatory reviewers

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Update the organisation’s SA-10b-aligned IT change management and control process (change advisory board (CAB) workflow) so that the required **security representative** and **privacy representative** are **included** in the CAB workflow for the specified change types.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-10(7)** enhancement requires that **{{ insert: param, sa-10.7_prm_1 }}** be included in **{{ insert: param, sa-10.7_prm_2 }}**. Based on the organisation’s defined parameters, this means:
- **Security representative ({{ insert: param, sa-10.7_prm_1 }}):** the **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)**
- **Privacy representative ({{ insert: param, sa-10.7_prm_1 }}):** the **System Privacy Officer (SPO)**
- **Configuration change management and control process ({{ insert: param, sa-10.7_prm_2 }}):** the **IT change management and control process under SA-10b (CAB review and approval workflow)**

The guidance highlights that configuration changes can have unintended security and privacy impacts, so identifying these issues early in the process is essential.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Set up enforceable “stop-and-check” controls in the CAB tool (IT service management (ITSM) / change system) so CAB approval cannot be completed without the required representative(s), and so there is clear evidence for audits.

1. **Define the key change details** in the change request record:
   - System classification (must support **Confidential** and **Secret**)
   - Personal data impact indicator (must state whether the change **affects personal data flows or processing**)
   - Links to any required privacy/security impact documents (for example, a data protection impact assessment (DPIA) reference), if your process uses them.
2. **Set conditional routing and required approvals** in the SA-10b CAB workflow:
   - If system classification is **Confidential** or **Secret** → require **ISSO** as a mandatory reviewer/approver.
   - If personal data impact is “yes” (affects personal data flows or processing) → require **SPO** as a mandatory reviewer/approver.
   - If both conditions apply → require **both** ISSO and SPO.
3. **Prevent “no bypass” workflow actions**:
   - Do not allow the workflow to move to **CAB-approved / ready for production** unless the required approval(s) are present.
   - Disable or restrict any workflow action that would allow CAB completion without the required representative(s) (for example, remove “approve without required roles” options; add workflow validation rules).
4. **Record audit evidence automatically**:
   - Ensure the CAB record captures the approver identity and approval date/time for ISSO/SPO approvals (and the specific change ID/version).
   - Ensure this evidence is kept in the CAB system as part of the SA-10b change record.
5. **Make tagging accurate and consistent**:
   - Add a structured intake step (form fields) for system classification and personal-data impact, so requesters must choose from controlled options.
   - Require a short justification when personal-data impact is marked “yes” (to support later review and reduce incorrect tagging).
6. **Identify and fix exceptions**:
   - Run scheduled checks to flag any CAB-approved production change where the required ISSO/SPO approvals are missing or not recorded.
   - Send exceptions to the CAB process owner for correction and follow-up action.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **CAB process owner / Change manager**: owns workflow configuration and exception reporting.
- **ISSO**: provides mandatory security review/approval for qualifying changes.
- **SPO**: provides mandatory privacy review/approval for qualifying changes.
- **ITSM/automation engineer** (with governance approval): implements workflow stop-and-check controls, validation, and evidence capture.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For CAB-approved production changes to **Confidential/Secret** systems, the CAB workflow record shows **ISSO** approval/review with identity and date/time.
- For CAB-approved production changes that **affect personal data flows or processing**, the CAB workflow record shows **SPO** approval/review with identity and date/time.
- The CAB workflow cannot reach **CAB-approved / ready for production** without the required representative(s) for the applicable conditions.
- Automated audit evidence (approver identity and date/time) is present in the CAB record for every qualifying approval.
- Exception reporting shows zero instances (over a defined pilot period) of qualifying changes being approved without the required ISSO/SPO approvals.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update the change request form to record the system’s classification and whether personal data is affected, using approved options.
- [ ] Set up the SA-10b change process so that any production changes classified as **Confidential** or **Secret** require approval from the **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)**.
- [ ] Set up the SA-10b change workflow so that any production change that affects personal data flows or how personal data is processed requires approval from the SPO.
- [ ] Add workflow checks to prevent moving to “CAB-approved” or “ready for production” when the required approvals are not in place.
- [ ] Enable automatic recording of the approver’s identity and the date and time for the information system security officer (ISSO) and system programme officer (SPO) in the change advisory board (CAB) record.
- [ ] Set up scheduled reporting to check whether any changes that were approved by the change advisory board (CAB) are missing the required approvals from the information system security officer (ISSO) and the system programme officer (SPO). If any are missing, send them to the CAB process owner to fix the issue.


---

### SA-11 — Developer Testing and Evaluation (Control)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build your software keep checking it for security and privacy problems long after the design is finished—not just at the start. Without this, weaknesses could be introduced in new releases or after important changes, potentially leading to unauthorised access, data leaks, or harm to individuals’ personal information.

To meet this expectation, the developer must create and follow an ongoing plan for both security and privacy checks, and keep records showing the plan was carried out and what the results were. Every time code is released, and after any change that could affect security, they must run a full set of tests, including tests for individual parts, combined parts, and the whole system, as well as repeated checks to confirm nothing has broken. These repeated checks must happen at least monthly, even if there is no release.

The checks must cover all parts that affect security and how those parts work together. They must use a combination of code review and real, running tests, plus authorised attempts to find weaknesses. Any problems found must be recorded, tracked through a clear and verifiable process to fix them and then re-test, and corrected.

##### CI/CD security & privacy quality gates with evidence and remediation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an automated, repeatable developer testing and evaluation programme that runs at **all stages after design is complete** and, at minimum, **every time code is released** and **after any change that could affect security**. This includes **unit, integration, system, and regression** testing. The programme must use the required **depth** (both automated checks and live testing, plus targeted, authorised penetration tests) and the required **coverage** (all security-relevant parts of the system and the ways they connect). It must keep **proof that testing happened and what the results were**, and it must use a **traceable fix process**: **log the issue → fix it → retest/confirm → close it with evidence**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) **SA-11 Developer Testing and Evaluation** requires the developer to ensure that, at all stages after design is complete, testing and evaluation confirm that required controls are:
- implemented correctly,
- working as intended,
- enforcing security and privacy rules, and
- meeting security and privacy requirements.

It also recognises that connections and changes can unintentionally weaken controls that were previously implemented. The guidance expects clearly defined activities, appropriate rigour and frequency, documented outputs, and evidence that the process was carried out properly—along with fixing and correcting any flaws found.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain version-controlled Security & Privacy Assessment Plans** (kept in the repository or a controlled document system) that specify:
   - **Testing/evaluation types**: **static analysis, dynamic analysis, targeted penetration tests** (depth), plus **unit, integration, system, regression** (which stages are covered).
   - **Frequency**: **at each code release and after any security-relevant change**, with **regression at least monthly**.
   - **Coverage rules**: include **all security-relevant components and interfaces** (define these in an attack-surface/security architecture register).
   - **Evidence outputs**: pipeline run identifiers, test reports, scan outputs, penetration test summaries, and links back to the relevant release and change records.
2. **Define “security-relevant components and interfaces”** by maintaining an auditable register (for example: authentication journeys, administrative application programming interfaces (APIs), data import/export, key management endpoints, message topics/queues, webhooks, and privilege escalation routes). Link each register item to the automated tests that cover it.
3. **Set up CI/CD quality gates** that:
   - Run **unit → integration → system → regression** testing on **every code release**.
   - Run the full test suite (or an approved subset for the impacted areas) on **every security-relevant change**, plus **targeted penetration tests** for the impacted interfaces.
   - Run **monthly regression** on a schedule even if no release happens.
4. **Apply the required testing depth in the pipeline**:
   - **Static analysis** (for example: security testing of code, dependency and software bill of materials (SBOM) checks, and checks for exposed secrets).
   - **Dynamic analysis** (for example: security testing of running behaviour and security-focused integration tests in temporary test environments).
   - **Targeted penetration tests** (authorised, limited-scope tests against the defined interfaces, carried out in a controlled test environment).
5. **Use a verifiable fix-and-confirm process**:
   - Turn findings into tracked issues that record severity, the affected component/interface, and who is responsible for fixing it.
   - Require **retest/verification** before closing any issue.
   - Do not allow promotion/release if findings are not fixed, unless your organisation permits an explicitly documented and evidenced risk acceptance.
6. **Keep audit-ready evidence**:
   - Store the outputs from each run (reports, scan logs, penetration test summaries) and link them to the **release identifier** and **change record**.
   - Protect evidence according to the system’s security classification (for example, access-controlled storage and retention in line with organisational policy).

###### WHO
- **Application/Software Engineering Lead**: owns the Security & Privacy Assessment Plan and the quality gate design.
- **DevSecOps Engineer**: implements the CI/CD triggers, tool integration, and evidence capture.
- **Security Testing Lead / Application Security (AppSec)**: defines the penetration test scope and confirms the required depth and coverage are met.
- **Change Manager / Release Manager**: ensures releases are only approved when evidence and remediation outcomes are in place.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For **every code release** and **every security-relevant change**, the pipeline runs **unit, integration, system, and regression** testing.
- **Regression runs at least monthly**, even when there are no releases.
- The pipeline includes **static and dynamic analysis** and **targeted penetration tests** for the defined security-relevant components/interfaces.
- Evidence is retained and traceable to the specific **release/change** (run identifiers, reports, and summaries).
- Findings follow a **trackable remediation workflow**, including **retest/verification** before closure, and releases are blocked unless remediation is complete or there is an approved, evidenced exception.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a version-controlled security and privacy assessment plan that sets out the types of tests to be carried out, how deep they go, what areas they cover, how often they are repeated, and what proof (evidence) must be produced.
- [ ] Keep an auditable record of security-relevant components and connections, and link each one to the automated tests that cover it.
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline triggers so that, every time code is released, the unit, integration, system, and regression tests run, and so that they also run after any change that affects security.
- [ ] Run monthly regression tests in the pipeline, even if no release is made.
- [ ] Add static analysis, dynamic analysis, and targeted penetration tests (with a defined scope) to the pipeline quality checks.
- [ ] Set up a tracked process to fix security flaws, where each fix must be retested and verified before the issue is formally closed.
- [ ] Store and protect evidence for each pipeline run, and link the results to release identifiers and change records.


---

#### SA-11.1 — Static Code Analysis (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that, before software is added to your systems, the people writing the code use an automated tool to check it for common security weaknesses and then record what the tool finds. Without this, simple coding errors or older, vulnerable code can be missed and may allow unauthorised access, cause data loss, or disrupt services—leading to costs and damage to your organisation’s reputation.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system, component, or service developer to run static code analysis on their source code to identify typical flaws, and to produce clear written results showing the scan findings. The developer must also provide those documented results as evidence that the analysis was completed, so the organisation can review them and track whether identified issues are addressed.

##### CI/CD static analysis with audit-ready reports and merge gates

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Add static code analysis (static application security testing (SAST) and dependency/vulnerability analysis) to the development pipeline so every code change is automatically scanned. Keep the results and records so they can be used as auditable evidence.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-11(1)** enhancement requires developers to **use static code analysis tools to find common weaknesses** and to **document the results of the analysis**. It also expects evidence such as trends in defect density for critical defect types, and proof that issues were reviewed and fixed (or that risk was accepted with a clear justification).

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Standardise a secure build pipeline template** for all application repositories (multi-tier enterprise applications, application programming interfaces (APIs), services, and any custom tooling). Make sure static analysis runs early—on every pull request and before merge/release.
2. **Run SAST and dependency analysis in the same pipeline stage:**
   - **SAST** to detect common insecure coding patterns and weaknesses.
   - **Dependency analysis** (software composition analysis (SCA)) to identify vulnerable or out-of-date libraries included in the build.
3. **Produce consistent, easy-to-review outputs** for each scan, including at minimum: repository, commit SHA, branch/pull request (PR) ID, build number, tool name and version, scan date/time, and a machine-readable report (for example, security automated results interchange format (SARIF)) plus a human-readable summary.
4. **Apply merge/release “gates” using policy-as-code** based on agreed thresholds for common flaw categories (for example, block new critical/high findings; require triage for medium; require explicit justification for accepted risk). Developers must either:
   - fix the findings (with a link to the fix), or
   - document suppression/acceptance with an expiry date and rationale.
5. **Require developer review and documentation:** for each finding that is not automatically fixed, require a documented outcome (fixed / accepted risk / suppressed) and link it to the pull request or issue tracker.
6. **Retain tamper-evident evidence:** store the generated reports and scan details in an access-controlled, tamper-evident evidence location linked to the build outputs and the release record.
7. **Track defect density and false-positive rates** for critical defect types, and periodically review high ignore/suppression rates to confirm the tool is effective (or adjust rules/quality profiles).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application engineering lead / DevOps engineer:** implement and maintain the pipeline template, tool configuration, and evidence retention.
- **Developers:** fix findings or provide a documented outcome (fix link / acceptance rationale / suppression with expiry).
- **Application security (AppSec) or security engineering:** set thresholds, review evidence, and tune analysis rules to reduce false positives.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every pull request and release candidate, the pipeline runs static analysis (SAST and dependency analysis) and produces a report with commit/PR/build metadata and tool versions.
- Merge/release is blocked when new critical/high findings are introduced, unless there is a documented and time-bound risk acceptance/suppression.
- Evidence of analysis is retained for each build/release in an immutable, access-controlled location and can be traced to the corresponding code change.
- Developers provide documented outcomes for findings (fixed / accepted risk / suppressed with expiry) and links to remediation actions.
- Periodic review shows progress on fixes and identifies excessive suppression/false positives for investigation.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Choose and standardise software security testing (SAST) and software composition analysis (SCA) tools for all application repositories.
- [ ] Add automated code checking (static analysis) to the continuous integration (CI) process so it runs on every pull request and every release candidate.
- [ ] Set up the tools to produce consistent, audit-ready reports that include the commit identifier (commit SHA), the pull request or build reference, and the version of each tool.
- [ ] Set up automated “policy-as-code” checks at the point of merging and releasing code to block releases that exceed critical defect limits or introduce high risk from vulnerable third-party dependencies.
- [ ] Require developers to record what they decided for each issue found, including one of the following: a link to the fix, the reason for accepting the risk, or a suppression with an expiry date.
- [ ] Store scan reports and related details in an unchangeable, access-controlled evidence repository, linked to the corresponding build outputs.
- [ ] Set up regular reviews of defect density and the rates at which defects are suppressed or incorrectly flagged as false positives for the most critical defect categories.


---

#### SA-11.2 — Threat Modeling and Vulnerability Analyses (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building a system think through, before and during testing, how the system could be attacked and what weaknesses might be present in both the overall design and the individual parts. Without this, new changes made during development can accidentally introduce flaws, and attackers could take advantage of exposed connections, weak third-party components, or poorly handled login and permission rules. This could lead to data loss, service disruption, or unsafe outcomes—particularly in environments where office information technology (IT) is connected with operational technology (OT).

To meet this requirement, the organisation must ensure the system developer carries out threat thinking and checks for vulnerabilities during development and again during testing and evaluation. They must base this work on the system’s impact level, any mixed IT/OT context, known or assumed threats, how data moves through the system, where responsibility and trust change between parties, and the level of risk the organisation is willing to accept.

The developer must:
- analyse every interface and data route that can be accessed from outside
- assess all components and any third-party dependencies
- use recognised, structured methods for identifying threats and standard severity ratings
- use both automated and manual testing approaches
- review the code and configuration

They must then provide clear written evidence of:
- the threats identified
- the weaknesses found
- the risk ratings
- the fixes planned
- final sign-off of any remaining risk by the Information System Security Officer.

##### Threat model & vuln analysis gates with STRIDE/CVSS evidence

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a repeatable engineering process that requires developers to (1) carry out threat modelling and vulnerability analysis during development and (2) do it again during later testing and evaluation. This must use the organisation’s defined context and methods, and produce audit-ready proof, including the planned mitigations and sign-off of any remaining (residual) risk by the Information System Security Officer (ISSO).

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-11(2)** (enhancement) requires developers to carry out threat modelling and vulnerability analysis during development and again during testing and evaluation. This ensures that changes to the design and implementation are taken into account, and that any new vulnerabilities introduced by those changes are reviewed and addressed. The organisation’s defined parameters also require specific context inputs, methods, the required level of coverage (breadth and depth), and clear criteria for accepting evidence.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain the required threat-modelling context** for each system, component, or service release. This includes impact level, notes about mixed information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT) environments, known and assumed threats, data flows, trust boundaries, and the acceptable risk levels.
2. **Use STRIDE-based threat modelling** at the system level, and link the results to **all externally reachable interfaces and data flows**. For each interface and data flow, record the likely attack paths or misuse scenarios and the **crossings of trust boundaries** (required breadth and depth).
3. **Carry out component-level vulnerability analysis** for **all components and third-party dependencies**, with sufficient depth to include review of code and configuration, authentication and session handling, and privilege boundaries.
4. **Run the required analysis methods at the correct points in the lifecycle**:
   - Development gate: **static application security testing (SAST)**, dependency scanning, and configuration review. Triage findings using **Common Vulnerability Scoring System (CVSS)**-based scoring.
   - Testing and evaluation gate: **dynamic application security testing (DAST)** and manual penetration testing for externally reachable interfaces. Confirm that fixes were applied and record the verification results.
5. **Produce evidence packs** that meet the organisation’s defined acceptance criteria:
   - Threat model evidence: complete, traceable to requirements, includes mitigations, and shows acceptance of residual risk.
   - Vulnerability evidence: test method used, affected versions, CVSS scores, remediation actions, and verification results.
   - **Residual risk sign-off by the ISSO** must be recorded and linked to the release.
6. **Apply lifecycle gates in the delivery pipeline** so delivery to testing and evaluation cannot proceed unless the required documents and evidence exist and are complete, including the ISSO residual risk sign-off for the release.

###### WHO
- **Developer / Engineering Lead**: produces the threat model and vulnerability analysis evidence, and ensures mitigations are implemented.
- **Security Engineer / Application Security (AppSec)**: checks that the work is complete against the required methods, breadth and depth, and required evidence fields; supports STRIDE modelling and helps define the scope for penetration testing.
- **ISSO (Information System Security Officer)**: reviews residual risk and provides sign-off.
- **Continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) Release Manager**: ensures pipeline gates enforce evidence completeness and correct linking to releases.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each release candidate, there is a **development** threat model and vulnerability analysis covering all externally reachable interfaces and data flows, and all components and third-party dependencies, including attack paths or misuse scenarios and trust-boundary crossings.
- There is a **testing and evaluation** update showing re-analysis results and confirmation that mitigations were verified, including DAST and manual penetration testing outcomes for externally reachable interfaces.
- Evidence includes: required context inputs, STRIDE outputs, CVSS-based vulnerability scoring, affected versions, remediation actions, verification results, and **ISSO residual risk sign-off**.
- Pipeline gates stop progress if evidence is missing or incomplete, or if ISSO sign-off is not present.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the information needed for threat modelling, including: impact level, notes about information technology (IT) and operational technology (OT), known and assumed threats, data flows, trust boundaries, and the acceptable risk levels.
- [ ] Create threat-modelling templates based on STRIDE that require, for every externally accessible interface, documented attack paths (how an attacker could proceed), abuse cases (how the system could be misused), and any crossings of trust boundaries (where control shifts between different levels of trust).
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) checks so that, during development, the system automatically runs static application security testing (SAST), scans for vulnerable dependencies, and reviews configuration settings. Use Common Vulnerability Scoring System (CVSS) scores to prioritise issues and keep a record of how each issue was triaged.
- [ ] Set up testing and assessment checkpoints to run dynamic application security testing (DAST). For externally accessible interfaces, require targeted manual penetration testing and record the affected software versions and the results of the checks.
- [ ] Set up evidence generation that creates audit-ready threat model and vulnerability analysis reports, with clear links back to the requirements and documented mitigations.
- [ ] Before promoting a release to testing and evaluation, record the ISSO (Information System Security Officer) residual risk sign-off and link it to the release artefacts.


---

#### SA-11.3 — Independent Verification of Assessment Plans and Evidence (Enhancement)

This requirement is about having an independent person double-check that the developer’s security and privacy testing plans were followed properly, and that the evidence collected from that testing is both accurate and complete. Without this independent check, mistakes or gaps could be missed, which could leave weaknesses that others could exploit, or privacy commitments that are not actually met. This could harm customers, breach legal responsibilities, and damage trust.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must appoint an independent security assessor who was not involved in creating the security or privacy assessment plans or the supporting evidence. The assessor must report to the Chief Information Security Officer and must have the right training and professional certifications for assessments. The organisation must then provide the assessor with enough information to carry out the verification. If any information is missing, the organisation must authorise the assessor to obtain what is needed to complete the checks for both security and privacy.

##### Independent IV&V gate for security/privacy assessment plans and evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an independent verification and validation (IV&V) check that confirms: (1) the developer security assessment plan was implemented correctly, and (2) the developer privacy assessment plan was implemented correctly. It must also confirm that the evidence produced is accurate and complete. The independent reviewer must be suitably qualified and must not have been involved in creating the plans or evidence. The reviewer must report the results to the chief information security officer (CISO).

###### WHY (control requirement)
Security and privacy control requirement SA-11(3) enhancement requires that independent reviewers with the right expertise, skills, training, qualifications, and experience verify that the developer security and privacy assessment plans were implemented correctly. This includes checking that the evidence collected is accurate and complete, and that the reviewer has enough information (or authority) to carry out the verification.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Appoint and qualify an independent reviewer**: choose a reviewer who was not involved in developing the security/privacy assessment plans or the supporting evidence, and who meets the organisation’s independence requirements (for example, reports to the CISO; has Department of Defense (DoD) / Common Criteria Security Requirements (CCSR) assessment training and relevant certifications). Keep records showing the reviewer’s qualifications.
2. **Create a standard evidence pack** for each assessment cycle/milestone (for example, before release and after fixes): include the security assessment plan version, privacy assessment plan version, scope, test execution records, tool outputs, findings, remediation status, and a traceability record showing coverage of all in-scope requirements.
3. **Provide controlled access to verification inputs**: store the evidence pack in a read-only evidence repository/workspace for the reviewer. Give time-limited access to any supporting systems and logs needed (for example, continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) run details, signed artefact lists, scan reports, and relevant privacy artefacts). If information is missing, ensure the reviewer can request it through an approved escalation/authority route.
4. **Run the IV&V check at defined points in the delivery lifecycle**: require an IV&V review before contract acceptance/release (and before production release for major changes). The reviewer verifies the work against the plan requirements and checks that the evidence is complete and accurate (including sampling, plus extra checks for high-risk areas such as authentication/authorisation, handling of secrets, logging, and privacy/personal identifiable information (PII) data flows).
5. **Produce an auditable verification report**: the reviewer records what was checked (plan/evidence versions, evidence hashes/identifiers), the verification method used, any gaps found, and a pass/fail (or conditional) outcome. Submit the report to the CISO and keep the records for audit.

###### WHO
- **Independent reviewer (IV&V agent)**: checks that the plans were implemented correctly and that the evidence is accurate and complete; produces the IV&V report.
- **CISO**: receives the IV&V results and ensures governance decisions are made.
- **Application/DevSecOps lead**: prepares the standard evidence pack and ensures it is available in the evidence repository.
- **Security governance/assurance function**: keeps records of the reviewer’s qualifications and manages the IV&V process.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The reviewer is clearly independent (not involved in developing the plans or evidence) and meets the organisation’s independence requirements (reports to the CISO; has DoD/CCSR assessment training and relevant certifications).
- For each IV&V check, the reviewer verifies both the security assessment plan and privacy assessment plan implementations, and concludes that the evidence is accurate and complete (or records specific gaps and required remediation).
- The reviewer receives enough information through the evidence pack and has an approved authority/escalation route to obtain any missing verification inputs.
- IV&V reports are auditable and include the plan/evidence versions reviewed, evidence identifiers/hashes, the verification approach, and the final verification decision submitted to the CISO.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Appoint an independent assessor who meets the organisation’s independence requirements, and keep the qualification evidence.
- [ ] Define entry and exit requirements for the independent verification and validation (IV&V) gate, and set lifecycle milestones for verifying the security and privacy assessment plan.
- [ ] Create and enforce a standard template for an evidence pack that covers both the security and privacy assessment plans and the proof that those plans were carried out.
- [ ] Set up a read-only evidence workspace and grant the assessor time-limited access to the required supporting systems and logs.
- [ ] Set up an approved route for escalation so the assessor can request any missing information needed to complete the verification.
- [ ] Require the assessor to produce an auditable independent verification and validation (IV&V) report. The report must include: the plans and evidence versions that were reviewed, the evidence identifiers and hashes, the verification method used, and the final conclusion. Submit the report to the chief information security officer (CISO).


---

#### SA-11.4 — Manual Code Reviews (Enhancement)

This requirement is about having a careful human check of the most sensitive parts of software before they are released. The goal is to confirm they work as intended and do not create opportunities for attackers. Without this, serious weaknesses could be missed—for example, incorrect checks for logins and permissions, mistakes in how secret keys and encryption are handled, or unsafe responses to unexpected inputs. These problems could lead to unauthorised access, data theft, or compromise of the system.

The organisation must ensure the software developer keeps a documented list of the specific critical code areas that must be reviewed by hand. The organisation must assign responsibility for completing these reviews and carry out a peer review that compares the code against the organisation’s documented expectations for threats and its permission rules.

The reviewer must also confirm that encryption and other security methods are implemented correctly, that inputs are validated, and that errors are handled safely. After the review, the reviewer must provide recorded approval for the exact scope that was checked. The organisation must keep records showing what was reviewed and what was approved.

##### Manual review gate for critical auth/access/crypto code in SDLC

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a software development life cycle (SDLC) process that requires the system developer to carry out a **manual code review** of the organisation-defined **specific code** (critical authentication, access control, cryptographic modules, and security-relevant firmware). The review must follow the organisation-defined **processes/procedures/techniques**, including peer review against the threat model and access control matrix, checking that cryptography is correct, reviewing input validation and error handling, and obtaining documented sign-off from the reviewer.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-11(4)** (enhancement) requires the developer to **perform a manual code review** of the specified critical code using the specified review processes/techniques. The goal is to find weaknesses that automated checks may miss—especially issues in **access control logic** and **cryptographic correctness**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain a critical-code inventory** at the level of modules/files/functions (the “specific code” scope). Mark these items in the code repository (for example, using security labels and CODEOWNERS/security ownership) so the workflow can reliably identify what must be reviewed.
2. **Define a manual review checklist** that matches the organisation-defined techniques:
   - Peer review against the **threat model** and **access control matrix** (link each reviewed code path to the relevant threat/model items and authorisation rules).
   - Verify **cryptographic correctness** (for example, correct algorithms/modes, key handling, nonce/initialisation vector (IV) use, authentication/tag verification, and secure behaviour when errors occur).
   - Check **input validation** and **error handling** (reject or normalise invalid inputs; do not expose secrets; ensure consistent ways of failing).
   - Require **documented reviewer sign-off** that clearly relates to the reviewed scope.
3. **Apply a CI/CD release gate**: if a change affects tagged critical code, stop the merge/release until the checklist is completed and the sign-off is recorded. Make sure this gate applies to both application code and security-relevant firmware artefacts produced by the delivery pipeline.
4. **Keep audit evidence in a system of record**: save the completed checklist, reviewer identity, date/time, identifiers for what was reviewed (commit hash/build ID/release version), and the sign-off result. Link this evidence to the change request.
5. **Set reviewer accountability**: ensure the manual review is carried out by an authorised reviewer (for example, a security engineering peer) and that the reviewer sign-off is captured as a required approval step.

###### WHO
- **System developer**: performs the manual code review for the tagged critical code.
- **Authorised security reviewer/peer reviewer**: carries out the peer review against the threat model and access control matrix, verifies cryptographic correctness and input/error handling, and provides documented sign-off.
- **Engineering manager/SDLC owner**: ensures the workflow is enforced in CI/CD and that evidence retention is configured.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every change that updates tagged **critical authentication/access control/cryptography/firmware** code, CI/CD must block release until a completed manual review checklist exists with **documented reviewer sign-off**.
- The checklist must include clear evidence of:
  - peer review against the **threat model** and **access control matrix**,
  - verification of **cryptographic correctness**,
  - checks for **input validation** and **error handling**.
- Evidence must be retained and traceable to the exact reviewed artefacts (commit/build/release identifiers) and the reviewer identity.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and keep up to date an inventory of the specific critical code that must be manually reviewed, at the module or function level, including code related to authentication, access control, cryptography, and security-relevant firmware.
- [ ] Create a manual code review checklist that clearly includes peer review of the threat model and access control matrix, checks that cryptography is correct, verifies input validation, and confirms proper error handling.
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) so that merges and releases are blocked when changes affect tagged critical code unless there is completed checklist evidence and documented approval from the reviewer.
- [ ] Require reviewer approval to be recorded with the reviewer’s identity and linked to the exact item being approved (commit hash, build identifier, or release version).
- [ ] Store and keep manual code review records in an auditable system of record, with clear traceability back to the change request.


---

#### SA-11.5 — Penetration Testing (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build your system deliberately try to “break in” in a controlled way, so weaknesses are found before criminals can exploit them. Without this, problems in parts of the system that users and administrators depend on—such as services that are reachable from the public internet, internal user and administrator functions, website features, and the ways systems communicate with each other—could allow unauthorised access, access to higher privileges than allowed, or access to sensitive information. This could include weaknesses in login and session handling.

The organisation must require the system developer to carry out thorough penetration testing using both manual checks and automated checks. Testing must cover every service that can be reached from outside the organisation and every internal user and administrator function. The testing must also attempt to see whether protections for authentication and sessions can be bypassed, and assess the potential impact, including privilege escalation and unauthorised access to data.

Testing must only take place during pre-approved test windows, must not disrupt operational technology processes, must stay within the agreed scope, and must ensure that any personal data collected during testing is properly protected, minimised, and handled in line with privacy requirements. A documented plan and results must be produced and reviewed independently.

##### Developer penetration testing contract with scope, depth, and OT-safe constraints

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Require the system developer (or a contracted testing provider acting on the developer’s behalf) to carry out penetration testing that tries to get around the security and privacy protections that are in place, with **clearly defined coverage and intensity**, covering **all services that can be reached from outside** and **all internal services that support user and administrative functions**, using **both manual and automated methods**, and testing up to the level of **privilege escalation and potential impact on access to data** (including attempts to bypass authentication and session controls). Testing must be carried out during **approved test time periods**, with **no exploitation outside the agreed scope**, **no disruption to operational technology (OT) processes**, and **proper handling of any personal data captured** during testing.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-11(5)** (Enhancement) requires the organisation to **require the developer** to carry out penetration testing. The guidance explains that penetration testing should use available documentation, be carried out by skilled professionals simulating attacker behaviour (white/gray/black box), and should identify weaknesses caused by implementation, configuration, or operational problems. The enhancement also requires explicit **coverage**, **intensity**, and **constraints**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an authoritative scope inventory** for the system/component/service that lists:
   - externally reachable services/endpoints
   - internal services that support user and administrator functions
   - web applications
   - application programming interfaces (APIs)
   - authentication and identity provider (IdP) integration points
   - session and token mechanisms
   - privilege boundary areas
2. **Update procurement documents** (solicitation/contract/statement of work (SOW)/service level agreement (SLA)) so the developer/provider must submit a **Penetration Testing Plan** before testing starts, including:
   - **Coverage**: “All externally reachable services plus internally reachable services supporting user and administrative functions” (link each inventory item to test coverage).
   - **Intensity**: clear test objectives and test cases for privilege escalation and data access impact, including attempts to bypass authentication and session controls.
   - **Methods**: a mix of automated discovery and verification, plus manual attempts and validation by skilled testers (white/gray/black box as appropriate), with evidence that any results are confirmed beyond what tools alone show.
   - **Constraints**: testing only during approved test time periods; rules to avoid disrupting OT processes; strict “no exploitation beyond agreed scope”; and privacy handling requirements for any personal data captured.
3. **Enforce traceability from scope to evidence**: require a matrix that maps each inventory item → test case(s) → evidence artefacts (screenshots/log extracts/redacted outputs) → vulnerability finding(s) → impact assessment.
4. **Require independent verification**: require a separate reviewer/verifier (the internal security team or an independent third party) to check that the plan is complete, that it covers the full agreed scope, and that the reported results match the agreed coverage/intensity and constraints.
5. **Require retesting after fixes**: include contractual requirements to retest confirmed issues after remediation, using the same agreed constraints and within the same approved time periods.
6. **Privacy and data handling controls**: require data minimisation, redaction/masking in reports, a secure storage location for any captured data, retention limits, and confirmation of deletion/return as part of the final evidence package.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: ensures the contract includes the required clauses.
- **Application/System owner or Security engineering lead**: maintains the authoritative scope inventory and approves the PenTest Plan.
- **Cybersecurity testing lead/verifier**: performs independent verification of plan completeness and evidence quality.
- **Developer/provider penetration testing team**: carries out the penetration testing and produces the evidence package.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The contract/SOW includes explicit requirements for **coverage**, **intensity**, and **constraints** that match the organisation-defined parameters.
- A Penetration Testing Plan is approved **before** testing and includes scope mapping covering **all externally reachable services** and **all internally reachable services supporting user/admin functions**.
- Testing includes explicit attempts to bypass **authentication** and **session controls**, with assessment up to **privilege escalation and data access impact**.
- The evidence package includes validated findings (manual confirmation), a traceability matrix, and independent verification sign-off.
- Testing takes place only during **approved test time periods**, with documented controls to prevent disruption to OT processes and to prevent exploitation beyond agreed scope.
- Any captured personal data is handled according to the required privacy controls (minimised, protected, redacted, retained appropriately, and deleted/returned with attestation).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep up to date an official list of all services that can be accessed from outside the organisation, and all internal user and administrator support services for the system.
- [ ] Update acquisition documents so they require a penetration testing plan that clearly states the testing coverage (breadth), level of detail (depth), and any limits (constraints), in line with sa-11.05_odp.01/02/03.
- [ ] Require end-to-end traceability from each scope item to the test case and the supporting evidence for network, web applications, application programming interfaces (APIs), authentication, and privilege boundaries.
- [ ] Require penetration testing to include attempts to bypass login and session controls, and to assess the impact up to gaining higher privileges and accessing sensitive data.
- [ ] Set contractual rules for approved testing time windows, safe operation around operational technology (OT), and a strict requirement not to use or exploit anything beyond the agreed scope.
- [ ] Require privacy controls for any captured personal data, including: minimising what is collected, protecting it, redacting it where needed, setting retention limits, and confirming deletion or return through written attestation.
- [ ] Appoint an independent reviewer to check that the plan is complete, that the supporting evidence is high quality, and that it meets the agreed scope and constraints.


---

#### SA-11.6 — Attack Surface Reviews (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that whenever a system or service is changed, the people who build it carefully check every part that is exposed or can be accessed, to see what new weaknesses those changes might create. Without this, updates and improvements can accidentally create new “open doors”, such as features that are not properly protected, unsafe functions, or gaps that were missed in the software, hardware, or underlying components—allowing criminals to exploit the system in ways the organisation did not expect.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the developer to carry out an attack surface review for every design or implementation change, analyse the changes to identify any new or increased attack risks, record those risks, and fix them by removing or deprecating unsafe functions where needed. The developer must also keep clear records showing the review was completed and what was found and corrected.

##### Change-triggered attack surface reviews with auditable remediation

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Implement a structured **attack surface review (ASR)** that is **triggered by design or implementation changes** to the system, a system component, or a system service. The ASR must produce **auditable evidence** of: (1) newly exposed or newly accessible areas, (2) new or increased ways an attacker could exploit the system, (3) identified risks, and (4) the planned mitigations—including **deprecation or removal of unsafe functions**, where applicable.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-11(6)** (Enhancement) requires the **developer** of the system, component, or service to **carry out attack surface reviews**. The guidance explains that an attack surface is the exposed area that can increase vulnerability. It also requires reviews to analyse design or implementation changes so that any new attack opportunities created by the change are addressed, including **deprecation of unsafe functions**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define an ASR trigger in the SDLC/change process**: require an ASR for any change that affects exposed or accessible areas. Examples include: new or changed public interfaces, authentication and authorisation flows, network listeners and ports, application programming interface (API) endpoints, remote access paths, data moving across trust boundaries, dependency or software development kit (SDK) upgrades, configuration changes that change the security posture, and firmware or agent updates where relevant.
2. **Create a standard ASR template** that the developer must complete, at minimum covering:
   - The scope of exposed or accessible areas affected by the change (hardware, software, firmware as applicable)
   - Threat or attack-vector analysis focused on what the change newly exposes or increases
   - Risk identification and the reasoning (what could be exploited and the likely impact)
   - Mitigation plan and how the mitigation will be checked
   - Clear actions for **unsafe functions**: deprecate or remove them where identified. If they are not removed, document the compensating controls and the justification
3. **Add the ASR into the delivery workflow as a gate**:
   - Require the ASR document to be attached to the change ticket and referenced in the release record.
   - Do not allow merge or release for in-scope changes until the ASR is completed.
4. **Automate evidence capture and protect integrity**:
   - Store the ASR document (or the structured output) in an auditable repository.
   - Record links between the ASR, the change ticket or pull request (PR), and build or release information (for example, an immutable build identifier and a document hash).
5. **Require remediation acceptance criteria**:
   - The ASR must list each identified risk and the expected mitigation outcome.
   - Findings about unsafe functions must lead to either deprecation or removal, or to an explicitly documented decision on compensating controls, approved by the relevant security reviewer.

###### WHO
- **Developer / engineering owner**: completes the ASR and documents the scope, attack-vector analysis, risks, and mitigations (including actions for unsafe functions).
- **Security reviewer (application security / cyber security)**: checks that the ASR is complete, that the mitigations are suitable, and that decisions about unsafe functions are justified and approved.
- **Release manager / change manager**: ensures the ASR artefact exists and is linked before release.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each in-scope design or implementation change, an ASR artefact exists and is linked to the change ticket or pull request (PR) and the release record.
- The ASR includes analysis of newly exposed or increased attack vectors caused by the change.
- All identified issues and risks have documented mitigations, with evidence of checks or a defined plan for how checks will be carried out.
- Any identified unsafe functions are either deprecated or removed, or a compensating-control decision is documented and approved.
- Evidence is auditable (stored immutably, with links to build and release metadata).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the criteria for when an automatic security review is required for in-scope design or implementation changes that affect areas that are exposed or accessible.
- [ ] Create and publish a standard artificial intelligence safety and risk (ASR) template that requires: the scope, an analysis of possible attack routes, identification of risks, the planned mitigations, and actions to address unsafe functions.
- [ ] Update the software development life cycle (SDLC) and change workflow so that, for in-scope changes, the change cannot be merged or released until the accessibility statement review (ASR) is completed and attached to the change ticket.
- [ ] Set up secure, auditable record-keeping and clear links between automatic speech recognition (ASR) outputs, change requests (change tickets) and pull requests (PRs), and build and release information (metadata).
- [ ] Set clear acceptance criteria for remediation that require documented fixes, and require either the explicit deprecation or removal of unsafe functions (or approved alternative compensating controls).
- [ ] Train developers and security reviewers on how to complete application security reports (ASRs) and on what to expect when unsafe functions are withdrawn


---

#### SA-11.7 — Verify Scope of Testing and Evaluation (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build or supply a system can prove that their security testing truly covers everything you need, completely and with strong confidence. Without this, important security weaknesses could be missed because some checks were not done, not done in every part of the system, or only done in a superficial way—leaving you with a false sense of safety.

The organisation must require the developer to formally link every required security measure (including any inherited measures or substitute measures) to specific test cases across all parts of the system and across how those parts connect to other systems. The developer must provide clear evidence that each test was carried out, the results of each test, and a documented review showing that the results confirm the measures are set up correctly and work as intended.

This must be supported by structured analysis, with records that can be traced back to the requirements, and it must be included in the testing plan, test results, and the relevant procurement and contract documents.

##### Formal control-to-test coverage matrix with evidence gates

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Require the system developer to **formally confirm** that the **testing and evaluation plan** covers **all required controls** with **high rigour**. This includes **controls that are inherited** and **controls that compensate for others**. Coverage must apply across **all system parts and connections**, using a **formal mapping** that links **each required control** to the **specific test cases** that prove it, with **proof that the tests were run** and **a documented review of the test results**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-11(7)** enhancement requires the developer to confirm that the testing and evaluation scope provides **complete coverage** of required controls at the organisation-defined level of rigour: **“High rigour: formal mapping of each required control to test cases with evidence of execution and results review”** (sa-11.7_prm_1). The guidance also expects assurance to increase, with the highest assurance supported by **formal modelling and analysis** and a **clear link between how the control is implemented and the test cases that verify it**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define what must be covered (inputs)**: give the developer an authoritative list of **required controls**, including **inherited and compensating controls**, and an **authoritative inventory of system components and interfaces** (for example: architecture plus data-flow map plus interface list) so “breadth” and “depth” can be assessed consistently.
2. **Require a Coverage Matrix**: the developer must submit a **control-to-test mapping matrix** showing how each required control maps to one or more **specific test cases**. For each mapping, require:
   - component/interface coverage (which parts and connections are tested)
   - test case identifiers and test type (for example: functional, configuration, integration, negative)
   - **evidence references** (continuous integration (CI) job identifiers, signed execution logs, fixed report links, artefact hashes)
   - expected versus actual results
   - explicit links to the **implementation or configuration elements** being tested
3. **Enforce breadth and depth requirements** (organisation-defined parameters):
   - **Breadth**: ensure all required controls (including inherited/compensating) are tested across **all system components and interfaces** (sa-11.07_odp.01).
   - **Depth**: ensure the testing is deep enough to confirm implementation, configuration, and effectiveness using **formal analysis** and **traceable evidence** (sa-11.07_odp.02).
4. **Require results review and proof of resolution**: for each group of controls (or for each control, depending on size), require a documented **review of test results** by an authorised reviewer. This must include any discrepancies, the root cause, remediation actions, and **evidence that the tests were repeated (retested)**.
5. **Add formal analysis correlation (highest assurance)**: require the developer to include a short, structured explanation showing how the mapped tests demonstrate control effectiveness (for example: how the control implementation/configuration links to the test checks; model-based reasoning where possible). This meets the expectation for formal modelling/analysis and correlation.
6. **Use acceptance gates (evidence completeness checks)**: before accepting the testing and evaluation outputs, confirm that:
   - no required control is left unmapped
   - every mapped test has execution proof and recorded results
   - inherited/compensating controls are included in the matrix
   - coverage includes the declared system components and interfaces
   - results review records exist, and discrepancies are either closed or accepted as risk through the organisation’s governance process.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Developer / system integrator**: produces the Coverage Matrix, test execution evidence, results, and results review documentation; performs the formal confirmation of coverage.
- **Organisation security assurance / authorising official (or delegated security governance)**: defines required controls and scope inputs; performs acceptance gate checks and reviews evidence.
- **Quality assurance (QA) / test lead (developer side)**: ensures test execution and evidence capture are complete and traceable.

###### Acceptance criteria
