- A submitted **Coverage Matrix** exists that maps **100% of required controls** (including **inherited and compensating controls**) to **specific test cases**.
- For each mapped control/test case, there is **traceable proof that the test was run** and **recorded results**.
- The matrix shows coverage across **all declared system components and interfaces**.
- A documented **results review** exists showing reviewers confirmed correct implementation/configuration/effectiveness, including how discrepancies were handled with **retest evidence**.
- The developer provides a **formal analysis/correlation rationale** consistent with the control guidance and the organisation’s required level of rigour (sa-11.7_prm_1).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the official list of required safeguards that developers must use when matching controls, including any safeguards they inherit and any alternative safeguards used to compensate.
- [ ] Create an official list of components and interfaces—covering the overall architecture, the complete interface list, and a map of how data moves—so we can clearly define the “breadth” and “depth” of coverage.
- [ ] Require submission of a “control-to-test coverage matrix” that lists: test case IDs, the components or interfaces being tested, the expected versus actual results, and references to the supporting evidence.
- [ ] Set acceptance gate checks so delivery is blocked if any required control is not mapped, or if any mapped test does not have execution evidence and recorded results.
- [ ] For each control cluster, require documented evidence that the results were reviewed, including how any discrepancies were handled and proof of any retesting.
- [ ] Require a formal analysis and explanation showing how the way each control is implemented or configured links to the mapped test assertions.


---

#### SA-11.8 — Dynamic Code Analysis (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the software is tested while it is actually running, so obvious security weaknesses are found early. If developers only review the code on paper, they can miss problems that appear during real use, such as the software behaving incorrectly, handling data in an unsafe way, or allowing people to get more access than they should.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the person or team building the system, component, or service to use tools that monitor the running software to identify common security weaknesses, including memory errors and unauthorised privilege or access problems. The developer must then record what the testing found and provide those documented results to the organisation for review, so any weaknesses can be tracked and addressed as part of the wider development and assurance process.

##### Dynamic code analysis gate with documented evidence for releases

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Add a mandatory **dynamic code analysis** step to the software development life cycle (SDLC) / continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline for the system/component/service developer. Use runtime testing tools (and **fuzz testing where appropriate**) to find common security weaknesses, and produce **documented analysis results** that can be checked during audits as release evidence.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-11(8)** (Enhancement) requires that the developer **uses dynamic code analysis tools** to identify common weaknesses and **records the results** of the analysis.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Set an organisation-approved dynamic analysis policy** that states:
   - which dynamic analysis tools are allowed
   - which runtime parts are in scope (for example: application programming interface (API) services, parsers, file upload handlers)
   - how long testing must run
   - severity thresholds that will block a release
2. **Make dynamic analysis a release “go/no-go” step** in CI/CD for each release candidate:
   - Run dynamic analysis using runtime monitoring/instrumentation where possible (for example: builds with sanitiser-based checks, memory safety checks, and checks for excessive privileges/access).
   - Run **fuzz testing** on input-handling areas based on how the application is intended to be used and its functional/design specifications (for example: request/response data, message formats, upload parsers).
   - Optionally collect **code coverage** and/or **concordance** metrics to help reviewers understand what was tested.
3. **Standardise how evidence is captured**:
   - Save the raw tool outputs (logs/reports) as build artefacts.
   - Create a **Dynamic Code Analysis Report** that includes: which components were analysed, tool versions, test configuration, execution environment, a summary of findings, severity, affected modules, steps to reproduce (where available), and whether findings were triaged and fixed.
4. **Publish results for organisational review**:
   - Share the Dynamic Code Analysis Report in the organisation’s evidence repository (or in the ticketing system) linked to the release/version.
   - Require security engineering to review findings above agreed thresholds and approve any exceptions.
5. **Apply the same approach to third-party components**:
   - For vendor/contracted development, require the same evidence package and confirmation that dynamic analysis was run on the delivered build.

###### WHO
- **Application/Platform Engineering Lead**: responsible for implementing the pipeline gate and configuring the tools.
- **Security Engineering (AppSec)**: sets severity thresholds, reviews evidence, and approves exceptions.
- **Release Manager / DevOps**: ensures pipeline artefacts are kept and linked to releases.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every release candidate, the pipeline runs dynamic analysis and (where applicable) fuzz testing for the defined input-handling areas.
- A Dynamic Code Analysis Report is produced and kept as an auditable artefact linked to the release/version.
- Promotion to production is blocked when findings exceed the defined severity thresholds unless an approved exception exists.
- Evidence includes enough tool output to reproduce or understand findings (at minimum: the affected component, severity, and reproduction guidance where available).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an organisation-approved dynamic analysis policy that sets out which tools are allowed, which targets are included, and the severity levels required to decide whether a release can proceed (release gating).
- [ ] Update the continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline templates so that each release candidate build runs dynamic analysis and fuzz testing.
- [ ] Set up runtime monitoring for the supported components (for example, builds that include sanitiser checks) and make sure tests include checks that confirm users only have the required permissions and access.
- [ ] Create a standard Dynamic Code Analysis Report, and save the raw outputs from the analysis tool as unchangeable build records linked to the specific release or version.
- [ ] Add the findings to the defect or ticket workflow, and require that the triage status is recorded in the evidence pack.
- [ ] Put in place release promotion controls to stop production deployments when security findings are above agreed thresholds, unless there is documented security approval.


---

#### SA-11.9 — Interactive Application Security Testing (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that, before software is released, the people building it actively test the real, running application to find security weaknesses—not just rely on theory or a quick review. If this is not done, weaknesses may make it into live systems, where attackers could use them to steal data, disrupt services, or gain unauthorised access.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must require system developers (including suppliers) to use tools that observe the application while it is running, identify any security weaknesses they find, and then record the results in clear, auditable reports or findings. Your procurement and project documentation should set out these testing and evidence responsibilities. Your internal procedures should explain how staff must enforce them and what proof to keep. Project security records should show that this interactive testing took place and that the documented findings were tracked through to resolution, or to a formally accepted decision.

##### Contractual interactive testing evidence with release acceptance gates

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Add a requirement into procurement and delivery governance that the system developer must carry out **interactive (instrumentation-based) application security testing** against the **live, running application**, and must **record the results** as audit-ready evidence.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-11(9)** (Enhancement) requires: *“Require the developer of the system, system component, or system service to employ interactive application security testing tools to identify flaws and document the results.”* The guidance explains that interactive testing checks the application **while it is running**, using built-in observation (instrumentation), and can be used **throughout the system development life cycle**.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Update procurement documents** (statement of work (SOW)/service level agreements (SLAs)/security addenda) to include a specific deliverable: **Interactive Application Security Testing (IAST) evidence pack**.
2. Set **minimum evidence content** the developer must submit, including:
   - What was tested (scope/targets), and where it was tested (for example: services/endpoints, user interface (UI) journeys, application programming interfaces (APIs), background jobs) and the environment used (development/testing/lab).
   - The tool type and name, plus a configuration summary (for example: instrumentation-based/IAST) showing it observed the running application.
   - The test run dates/times, and how runtime instrumentation was enabled.
   - A findings table that records: flaw title, affected component/location, what was observed/recorded from the running behaviour, severity, and outcome (for example: fixed, accepted risk, deferred with the reason).
   - Links to the developer’s defect and remediation tracking records (ticket references or exported records).
3. Add **enforceable approval gates** for release sign-off:
   - Release is blocked if **no interactive testing evidence** is provided.
   - Release is blocked if **findings are not documented**.
   - Release is conditional if findings cannot be linked to remediation tickets, or if risk acceptance decisions were not formally recorded.
4. Require **evidence submission** to an organisation-controlled repository (for example, secure document management), and require findings to be **able to be checked and traced** during governance (for example, change/release security review).
5. For software as a service (SaaS), managed services, and vendor-supplied components, require the same evidence through **security reporting SLAs** (for example: per release or periodically), plus audit and evidence-sharing rights where the contract allows.

###### WHO (responsible roles)
- **Procurement/Commercial Manager**: updates contract templates and ensures the requirement can be enforced.
- **Application Security Lead / chief information security officer (CISO) delegate**: sets evidence requirements, approval gates, and review expectations.
- **Programme/Delivery Manager**: ensures the developer submits evidence at the required release milestones.
- **Security Governance/Assurance team**: checks evidence completeness and traceability as part of release sign-off.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Contract documents clearly require **interactive (instrumentation-based) application security testing** and **documented results**.
- For each release/component, the organisation receives an **IAST evidence pack** that includes scope, runtime/instrumentation evidence, and a documented findings table.
- Evidence is **reviewable** and **traceable** to remediation tracking records or to formally accepted risk decisions.
- The release sign-off process enforces: missing interactive evidence or missing documented findings results in **failed acceptance**.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update the statement of work (SOW), service level agreements (SLAs), and security addendum templates to require interactive, instrumentation-based application security testing and documented test results.
- [ ] Define and publish a minimum interactive application security testing (IAST) evidence pack format, including: scope, evidence from the tools or instrumentation used, the time window when testing was run, a findings table, and the outcome (disposition).
- [ ] Add release acceptance gates that prevent approval if interactive testing evidence or documented findings are missing
- [ ] For every documented finding, ensure it can be traced to either a remediation ticket or a formally approved decision to accept the risk.
- [ ] Set up an evidence submission process that sends documents to a repository controlled by the organisation, and appoint reviewers to approve release security sign-off.
- [ ] For software-as-a-service (SaaS) and managed services, include in the contract a regular schedule for security reporting and the right to share evidence and carry out audits.


---

### SA-15 — Development Process, Standards, and Tools (Control)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build your software or services follow a clear, written way of working that protects sensitive information and personal data. Without it, developers may use the wrong methods or settings, make changes without proper approval, or fail to include the right safeguards for Confidential and Secret data. This could result in unauthorised access, data leaks, or failure to meet legal and regulatory requirements such as the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA) and the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR), as well as export and defence expectations.

The organisation must ensure the developer uses a documented development process that clearly covers both security and privacy needs. It must also state the standards and development tools they will use, including the exact tool options and configurations, and it must control changes to those tools and processes so only authorised updates are made and their integrity is maintained.

The organisation must also review these choices at least quarterly, and after any major tool or process change or any material incident. The purpose is to confirm they still achieve the required outcomes for secure coding, risk analysis, identifying and fixing weaknesses, limiting access, and maintaining integrity, as well as privacy measures such as data minimisation, access limits, and auditability.

##### Integrity-controlled SDLC baseline with tool config drift checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place a documented, organisation-approved Secure and Private software development life cycle (SDLC) baseline that standardises how software is developed, what security and privacy work must be done, and which approved development tools are used—**including the exact settings for those tools**—for systems that handle **Confidential/Secret** data. Keep the baseline and tool settings accurate and unchanged by using controlled updates and automated checks for unauthorised changes (“drift”). Carry out **documented reviews at least every quarter**, and also **after any major tool or process change or any significant incident**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
This requirement (SA-15) says that development processes, standards, and tools—including the **exact tool settings**—must be clearly defined. It also requires that the **integrity of changes** to tools and processes is maintained through **controlled management of settings across the whole software development life cycle**. It further requires reviews of the development process/standards/tools/settings at a defined frequency.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain a Secure and Private SDLC baseline** (a single source of truth) with stage gates (for example: requirements → design → implementation → verification → release → maintenance). The baseline must clearly state:
   - **Security requirements** for Confidential/Secret data: secure coding, threat modelling, vulnerability management, least privilege, and controls to protect integrity.
   - **Privacy requirements** for Confidential/Secret data: data minimisation aligned with HIPAA and the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR), limits on access, and auditability.
   - **Development standards** (coding standards, threat-modelling approach, expectations for fixing vulnerabilities, and integrity expectations), and how these map to the security and privacy requirements above.
2. **Define approved tool categories and exact configuration profiles** for each tool category used in the SDLC (for example: security testing, software composition analysis, secrets scanning, build, and artefact signing). For each tool profile, record the **exact settings**, including ruleset versions, severity thresholds, where logs are sent, how long logs are kept, how users authenticate, and any required integrations.
3. **Control changes to the SDLC and tool settings**:
   - Store the SDLC baseline and tool configuration profiles in a version-controlled repository.
   - Require approval from authorised roles (for example, security engineering and the application owner) before updating the baseline or tool profiles.
   - Keep an auditable change log that links each change to an approval record and the affected projects.
4. **Use automated evidence and drift detection in continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD)**:
   - Check that builds and releases use the approved tool configuration profiles (for example: confirm the ruleset version/hash, ensure required pipeline steps are present, and confirm secrets scanning is enabled).
   - Stop or flag releases when drift is detected (for example: tool settings do not match the approved profile, or required checks are missing).
5. **Carry out structured quarterly reviews** of the SDLC baseline and tool configurations, and also after **major tool or process changes** or **significant incidents**:
   - Use a maturity-model style assessment to confirm the approach is still effective.
   - Record results, gaps, and corrective actions (including updates to the baseline/tool profiles where needed).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Product Owner**: responsible for adopting the baseline and ensuring it is followed.
- **Secure SDLC/DevSecOps Lead**: maintains the baseline, tool profiles, and CI/CD enforcement.
- **Information Security / Privacy Officer (or delegated privacy lead)**: checks that security and privacy requirements for Confidential/Secret data are met and approves changes.
- **Configuration Management / Change Management function**: ensures authorised change control and auditability.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented Secure and Private SDLC baseline exists and clearly covers security and privacy requirements for Confidential/Secret data.
- Development standards and approved tools are documented, including **the specific tool options/settings**.
- Changes to the SDLC baseline and tool profiles are made only through an authorised, auditable change process.
- CI/CD enforcement shows that builds and releases use the approved tool configuration profiles; drift is detected and prevents or flags non-compliant releases.
- Evidence exists of **quarterly reviews** and additional reviews after any major tool/process change or material incident, including recorded outcomes and corrective actions.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Publish a secure and private software development life cycle (SDLC) baseline that sets out the security and privacy requirements for Confidential and Secret data.
- [ ] Document the approved software development standards and link them to secure coding, threat modelling, vulnerability management, least privilege, and integrity controls.
- [ ] Define approved tool configuration profiles with the exact settings for each tool, including rule set versions, thresholds, logging and retention periods, and authentication details.
- [ ] Put continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) checks in place to confirm that tool settings match the required profiles and to spot any changes in tools or processes over time.
- [ ] Set up an approved change process for updates to the software development lifecycle (SDLC) baseline and tool settings, with approvals that can be audited and a recorded version history.
- [ ] Schedule and complete quarterly reviews of the software development lifecycle (SDLC) baseline and the configuration of the tools. Record the results and any corrective actions taken.
- [ ] After any major change to a tool or process, or any significant incident, carry out an additional review and record the results.


---

#### SA-15.1 — Quality Metrics (Enhancement)

This requirement is about agreeing up front what “good quality” means for a new system, and then showing that it was achieved when the work is handed over. Without clear, measurable quality targets, a developer could deliver something that appears complete but still misses important requirements. This can lead to costly rework, disruption to services, or problems that only become clear after release.  

The organisation must therefore require the system developer to define measurable quality measures at the very start of development. This must include clear quality checks at each key stage (quality gates) for major milestones. The developer must then provide proof that the system meets these measures at handover, at each major development milestone, and again before release.  

Finally, the organisation must review progress against these quality measures at least every quarter through a programme review involving security and privacy representatives. The organisation must record the results and any follow-up actions.

##### Quality Gate Pack with milestone evidence for delivery acceptance

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Require the system developer to agree measurable quality targets at the start of development, including quality “go/no-go” checkpoints at each key stage. Require them to provide clear, checkable proof that the delivered system/component/service meets these targets:
- at each major development milestone, and
- before release.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-15(1)** enhancement requires the developer to set quality targets and quality checkpoints, and to provide proof that they have been met. The proof must cover **major development milestones and before release**. The organisation’s defined parameters also set how often proof is provided and when it is reviewed.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a standard “Quality Gate Pack” template** (controlled by the organisation) that the developer must complete. The pack must include:
   - A **dated quality-targets document** produced at the start of the project (aligned to **sa-15.01_odp.01: “upon delivery”** as the proof anchor, but created at kickoff/planning sign-off).
   - A **list of measurable targets** with criteria that can be checked (for example: how compiler warnings are handled, test pass requirements, vulnerability severity limits such as Common Vulnerability Scoring System (CVSS) thresholds, and any security and privacy sufficiency standards).
   - **Quality checkpoints for each major development stage** with clear pass/fail criteria (aligned to guidance that checkpoints represent satisfactory completion of specific phases).
   - A **mapping table from each target to the proof required**, stating what evidence document is needed for each target (for example: test reports, scan reports, defect closure records, and signed security/privacy sign-off records).
   - **Linking of every proof item to the exact version/build**, such as the release identifier, build ID/commit, container digest, and a Software Bill of Materials (SBOM) reference where applicable.

2. **Require proof submission at the required times**:
   - Require the developer to submit the Quality Gate Pack evidence **at each major development milestone and before release** (aligned to **sa-15.01_odp.02: “At each major development milestone and before release”**).
   - Require a final proof submission **at delivery** (aligned to **sa-15.01_odp.01: “upon delivery”**).

3. **Use automated checks in the delivery pipeline (where feasible)** to reduce missing or incomplete proof:
   - Configure Continuous Integration/Continuous Delivery (CI/CD) so builds fail when required quality checkpoint outcomes are not met (for example: vulnerability limits exceeded, required test suite not run/passed, or unresolved critical/high defects).
   - Ensure pipeline outputs are captured as proof documents and referenced in the Quality Gate Pack.

4. **Run a quarterly programme review with security and privacy representatives** using the same structured Quality Gate Pack information:
   - Carry out a **quarterly review** and record results and follow-up actions (aligned to **sa-15.01_odp.03: “Quarterly program review with security and privacy representatives”**).
   - Track any exceptions or remaining risks until they are closed, ensuring the review can be audited.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Programme/Project Manager (or Procurement/Contract Owner):** ensures contractual/acceptance requirements are applied and proof deadlines are met.
- **Developer/Engineering Lead:** defines the quality targets and quality checkpoints, and produces the proof documents.
- **Security and Privacy Representatives:** take part in the quarterly programme review and confirm that any exceptions are justified.
- **Release Manager/DevOps Lead:** ensures CI/CD checkpoint checks produce proof that can be audited.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A completed **Quality Gate Pack** exists for the system/component/service, containing measurable targets and stage-based quality checkpoints with explicit pass/fail criteria.
- Proof is provided **at delivery**, **at each major development milestone**, and **before release**, and each proof document is **linked to the relevant build/version**.
- The proof mapping is complete (no required target is missing a referenced proof document).
- A quarterly programme review is completed with security and privacy representatives, with recorded outcomes and follow-up actions.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish a standard “Quality Gate Pack” template that includes: a list of quality metrics, the quality checkpoints for each stage, and the requirements for linking each metric to the supporting evidence.
- [ ] Require developers to submit a dated quality metrics document and the definitions for the quality checks at the start of each project (at the kickoff or planning approval stage).
- [ ] Require evidence to be submitted at every major development milestone, before any release, and again at delivery. Each submission must be linked to a specific build or version identifier.
- [ ] Set the minimum measurable quality checks to match the organisation’s agreed security and privacy risk tolerance (for example, vulnerability severity thresholds, whether testing and defect levels are sufficient, and expectations for how compiler warnings are handled).
- [ ] Create an acceptance checklist to confirm that all required evidence is complete and can be traced back to each required metric.
- [ ] Run a quarterly programme review with security and privacy representatives, using the structured Quality Gate Pack information, and record the results and any follow-up actions.


---

#### SA-15.2 — Security and Privacy Tracking Tools (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building a system use the right tools to keep a clear, auditable record of both security and privacy work as it is being created. Without this, important fixes and privacy-related changes can be missed, delayed, or handled in different ways, which increases the risk that security weaknesses remain in the system or that personal information is used or protected incorrectly.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system developer to select and use suitable tracking tools throughout the development process for:  
- **Security work**, such as recording and managing security issues from when they are first identified through to completion.  
- **Privacy work**, such as recording and managing privacy tasks from identification through to completion.  

The organisation should also keep evidence of which tools were chosen and be able to show that the developer used them to assign responsibility, track progress, and record completion—including when systems are developed by third parties through procurement and contract requirements.

##### Approved security & privacy work-item tracking with pipeline evidence

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Require developers to use explicitly chosen security and privacy tracking tools during development. These tools must be used to record, assign, sort/filter, and track completed security and privacy work items.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-15(2)** (Enhancement) requires the developer of the system, system component, or system service to **choose and use security and privacy tracking tools** during development. The guidance also expects teams to use tools that support **assigning, sorting/filtering, and tracking completed work items/tasks** linked to development activities.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Select and publish approved tools** for security tracking and privacy tracking (they may be the same issue tracker, with enforced labels, but the choice must be explicit and documented). Include the tool(s) used for vulnerability/work-item tracking and the tool(s) used for privacy work items.
2. **Create standard work-item templates** in the approved tools:
   - Security template (for example, vulnerability/defect) with required fields such as affected component, severity, discovery source, remediation plan, owner, and closure evidence.
   - Privacy template with required fields such as data category, privacy impact type, lawful basis/consent impact (as applicable), retention/erasure impact, DPIA/PIA reference (if used), owner, and closure evidence.
3. **Enforce tool usage through CI/CD gates** in development and release processes:
   - Stop merge/release unless the required security and/or privacy work items exist for the change type (based on change classification rules set by the organisation).
   - Require that work items have an assigned owner and the required labels/categories (for example, `security` / `privacy`), and that they have reached at least one status update after being created.
4. **Automate evidence collection** from the tracking tools:
   - Export or search work items filtered by `security`/`privacy` labels and a date range.
   - Store exports in an evidence repository with unchangeable retention (or an append-only log) so auditors can confirm the tools were not just named, but actually used.
5. **Extend the requirement to third parties through onboarding/procurement**:
   - Add requirements to vendor onboarding and/or contract terms stating that the developer/service provider must use the organisation’s approved security and privacy tracking tools and meet the same evidence expectations.

###### WHO
- **Application Development Lead / Engineering Manager**: ensures teams follow the templates and CI/CD gates.
- **Security Governance / AppSec Lead**: sets the security work-item fields, labels, and evidence expectations.
- **Privacy Officer / Data Protection Lead**: sets the privacy work-item fields, labels, and evidence expectations.
- **Platform/DevOps Engineer**: implements CI/CD policy gates and evidence export automation.
- **Procurement / Contract Manager**: ensures third-party requirements include tool usage and evidence expectations.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The organisation has an **approved, documented list** of security and privacy tracking tools used during development.
- Security and privacy work items are created using **standard templates** with required fields and labels.
- CI/CD gates prevent merge/release when required security/privacy work items are missing or incomplete (including owner/assignee, labels, and status update requirements).
- Evidence exports from the tracking tools show **assignment, progress, and completion** of security/privacy work items for relevant development activities.
- For third-party development, onboarding/procurement documentation requires the provider to use the approved tools and produce evidence consistent with the organisation’s process.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Document and publish the organisation’s approved security and privacy monitoring tools for use during software development.
- [ ] Set up security and privacy work-item templates that include all required fields, clear labels, and proof of closure.
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) “policy gates” so that security and privacy tasks are in place—such as having an assigned owner, the correct labels, and the right progress status—before code can be merged or released.
- [ ] Automate exporting and retrieving evidence from the tracking tools, using the security and privacy labels to filter what is included, and store the results with write-once (immutable) retention.
- [ ] Update vendor onboarding and/or procurement contract requirements to require the use of the approved tracking tools and to specify what proof (evidence) must be provided.


---

#### SA-15.3 — Criticality Analysis (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that, before a system is built—and every time it is changed—those developing it carefully assess what could happen if the system were harmed. Without this, serious problems could be missed, such as sensitive information being exposed, data being changed, services becoming unavailable, or safety being put at risk—particularly when the system depends on purchased products, third-party services, or supplier components.

To meet this expectation, the system developer must carry out a full criticality analysis of the entire system, including every component and connection. This must cover confidentiality, integrity, availability, and safety, and must explicitly consider supply-chain risks. The analysis must be done at each key stage: when requirements are first defined, throughout design, before implementation, after any major change, and for every release.

The analysis must use the organisation’s documented impact ratings. It must also include realistic examples of how harm could occur, a clear description of how information moves through the system, and links showing how the results connect to the relevant security measures and the organisation’s mission impact.

##### Developer criticality analysis gates with traceable CIA+Safety

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a developer-led, evidence-ready process to assess “criticality” and produce a structured criticality model covering the whole system and every component and interface. This includes commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) and third-party services, as well as operational technology (OT) assets that support safety or operational continuity. Update the model at agreed points in the project life cycle.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-15(3)** (Enhancement) requires the **developer** to carry out a **criticality analysis**. The guidance explains that developer input is needed because organisations may not have detailed design documentation for COTS and third-party products. It also states that criticality analysis is important for high-value assets and for supply chain criticality analysis. The organisation-defined requirements include:

- **decision points**: at concept and requirements, during design, before implementation, after major changes, and at each release  
- **breadth**: all components and interfaces, including COTS/third-party services and OT assets that support safety or operational continuity  
- **depth**: use documented impact levels, supported by threat-informed scenarios, data flow mapping, and traceability to security controls and mission impact

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Create and enforce a versioned “criticality model” document that is required at each decision point, with checks for completeness and traceability.

1. **Define the criticality model format** in the developer’s software development life cycle (SDLC) tools (for example, a repository-backed template), covering:
   - Component and interface inventory (including COTS/third-party services and any OT assets supporting safety/operational continuity)
   - References for data flow mapping (what talks to what, and where data moves)
   - Confidentiality, integrity, and availability (CIA) impact ratings and **safety impact** ratings using the organisation’s documented impact levels
   - Threat-informed scenarios for each component/interface and for each relevant CIA plus safety impact area
   - Supply chain considerations for each COTS/third-party item (for example, supplier security posture and assumptions, known limitations, and incident history where available)
   - Traceability links showing how each scenario and impact maps to the specific security controls and to mission/operational impact statements

2. **Build decision points into SDLC gates** (manual approval plus automated completeness checks):
   - At **concept/requirements**, **during design**, **before implementation**, **after major changes**, and **at each release**, require the criticality model document to exist and be updated.
   - Automated checks must confirm that:
     - every component/interface in the inventory has CIA plus safety impact ratings
     - every interface has at least one threat-informed scenario
     - scenarios reference the relevant data flow elements
     - each scenario and impact maps to security controls and mission impact

3. **Require evidence from COTS/third parties** to complete the supply chain sections:
   - Record supplier-provided security information (for example, application programming interface (API) security model, configuration and use constraints, vulnerability and incident information, and supported security features).
   - Record assumptions and limitations that affect CIA plus safety.
   - Ensure the criticality model clearly states how these supply chain factors influence the CIA plus safety ratings and the scenarios.

4. **Keep versioned, audit-ready outputs**:
   - Store the criticality model in a version-controlled repository with fixed, unchangeable release tags.
   - Ensure the change history clearly shows updates after major changes and at each release.

###### WHO
- **Developer / Engineering Lead**: owns the criticality model and ensures it is produced and updated at each decision point.
- **Security Architect / Application Security (AppSec) Lead**: checks scenario quality, confirms the correct CIA plus safety impact selection, and verifies traceability to security controls.
- **Release Manager / SDLC Gatekeeper**: enforces the gate at the required decision points.
- **Supply Chain / Supplier Risk Owner (where applicable)**: provides or validates supplier evidence used for supply chain considerations.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For the system and all components/interfaces (including COTS/third-party services and any OT safety/continuity assets), a **criticality analysis document** exists and is updated at each required decision point.
- The document includes **CIA plus safety** impacts using the organisation’s documented impact levels, with **threat-informed scenarios**, **data flow mapping**, and **traceability** to security controls and mission impact.
- For each COTS/third-party service/component, the document includes **supply chain considerations** based on supplier evidence and records assumptions/limitations.
- The repository shows versioned evidence, with updates after major changes and at each release, and SDLC gates prevent release unless a complete and traceable criticality model is in place.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a version-controlled template for a criticality model that covers: a list of components and interfaces, references to how data moves through the system, impact ratings for confidentiality, integrity, availability and safety, threat-informed scenarios, supply-chain considerations, and traceability from security controls to mission impact.
- [ ] Set up software development lifecycle (SDLC) checkpoints so that the criticality model is required at the concept and requirements stage, during design, before implementation, after major changes, and for every release.
- [ ] Set up automated checks to confirm that every component and interface has security and safety ratings (confidentiality, integrity, availability plus safety). Use documented impact levels and trace each scenario through the data flow to the controls.
- [ ] Set up and enforce a supplier evidence submission process for commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) and third-party services. Use it to fill in supply-chain considerations and to record any assumptions and limitations.
- [ ] Carry out a security architecture review at each project gate to confirm that each scenario is high quality and correct, and that the selection and tracking of the “confidentiality, integrity, availability (CIA) plus Safety” impacts are accurate and traceable.
- [ ] Store and label the criticality model file for each release in an unchangeable, audit-ready repository that keeps a complete history of changes.


---

#### SA-15.5 — Attack Surface Reduction (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making a system “smaller and harder to attack” before it goes live. It does this by removing anything that is not needed and by flagging anything that is already known to be unsafe. If unused features, unnecessary access points, leftover user accounts, or unnecessary ways for other software to connect are left in place, criminals have more opportunities to find weaknesses and break in, steal data, or disrupt services.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set clear rules for what must be removed or disabled, and make sure the system developer follows these rules before release. This includes removing or turning off unused services, network ports, accounts, and application connections, and disabling unsafe functions before the release date.

The organisation must also build these expectations into procurement and development processes. It should require them in bids, contracts, and service agreements, document how the reduction will be carried out in design and security plans, and keep evidence that the changes were completed and checked before the release date.

##### CI/CD attack surface baseline gate for release candidates

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Add an automated “attack surface baseline” check (gate) to the developer continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline that (1) lists the system’s exposed and reachable attack surface (services, ports/protocols, accounts/service identities, and deployed application programming interfaces (APIs)/functions), (2) compares it against reduction limits set by the organisation, and (3) stops release candidates that do not materially reduce attack surface or that include known-unsafe or deprecated functions.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-15(5)** (Enhancement) requires developers to **reduce attack surfaces to the organisation’s defined limits**: “Reduce attack surface by removing or disabling unused services, ports, accounts, and APIs; and deprecate unsafe functions before release.” The guidance also requires layered protections, least privilege/least functionality, deprecating unsafe functions, reducing entry points, reducing code that runs, and removing vulnerable APIs.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create the official baseline inputs**: keep an organisation-controlled “allowed vs disallowed” exposure model covering (a) required vs disallowed services/ports/protocols, (b) required vs disallowed accounts/service identities (including default/test/shared where not required), and (c) required vs disallowed API routes/versions and unsafe functions.
2. **Create an attack surface inventory during build/release**: the pipeline must produce evidence of what will be deployed, including at minimum:
   - enabled/listening services and ports/protocols for the target runtime image
   - application-exposed endpoints (API route list/open application programming interface (OpenAPI)-derived routes) and enabled feature switches
   - account/service identity inventory relevant to the component (for example, container runtime users, service identities, application-level service accounts)
   - indicators of unsafe/deprecated function use (for example, dependency/function allow/deny lists, and static analysis results mapped to “unsafe functions”)
3. **Apply the policy automatically against the limits**: implement automated checks that fail the release candidate when:
   - any disallowed service/port/protocol/API is present or enabled
   - any unused exposure remains enabled beyond the defined limits
   - any unsafe function is present and not in a controlled “deprecated” state
   - any exception is missing the required risk acceptance evidence (if your organisation allows exceptions)
4. **Deprecation must be both documented and enforced in practice**: make sure unsafe functions are (a) marked as deprecated in developer documentation/release notes and (b) disabled by default or removed/guarded so they do not run in production unless they are explicitly enabled in a safe way.
5. **Produce audit-ready evidence for each release**: the pipeline must output a release evidence pack containing the inventory snapshot, policy check results, and the list of removed/disabled items (services/ports/accounts/APIs/functions) to support the system security plan and release approval.

###### WHO
- **Application security engineer / DevSecOps lead**: defines the baseline inputs, implements the automated policy checks, and ensures evidence is produced.
- **Software developer team**: fixes issues (removes/disables exposure; deprecates unsafe functions) until the gate passes.
- **Release manager / system owner**: ensures only release candidates approved by the pipeline are promoted to production.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A release candidate cannot be promoted to production unless the CI/CD gate passes and the evidence pack shows compliance with the organisation-defined limits (sa-15.05_odp).
- The evidence pack includes: (1) service/port/protocol inventory, (2) API route/endpoint list, (3) account/service identity inventory relevant to the component, (4) unsafe function/deprecation status, and (5) policy check results.
- Unsafe functions are deprecated and operationally disabled/guarded so they do not run in production by default.
- For each failed gate, the developer team can show that they removed/disabled the specific disallowed/unused exposure items before re-submitting.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set and publish the organisation-approved baseline limits for the attack surface for each service, including the network ports and protocols, user accounts and service principals, application programming interfaces (APIs), and any unsafe functions (sa-15.05_odp).
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) to collect an inventory of what is included in each release, and export: the services and ports, the application programming interface (API) route list, the enabled feature settings, and the relevant account or service identity evidence for the release package.
- [ ] Create policy-as-code rules that compare the exported inventory against the baseline and stop (fail) the promotion if any prohibited or unused exposure, or any unsafe functions, are detected.
- [ ] Make sure unsafe functions are phased out by turning them off (or blocking their use) by default in production releases, and include the deprecation information in the release files.
- [ ] For each release candidate, create and store an audit-ready set of evidence (a snapshot of what is in place, the results of policy checks, and a list of items that were removed or disabled) to support the system security plan and release approval.
- [ ] Link the gate to the release process so that only artefacts approved by the pipeline can be moved into production environments.


---

#### SA-15.6 — Continuous Improvement (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build or maintain your system do not treat development as a one-time task. Instead, they must run an ongoing, documented improvement routine that keeps improving how they build the system over time. Without this, quality can gradually decline, the same mistakes can repeat, and the system may not keep pace with new security and privacy risks as the outside world changes.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must require the developer to cover all development work used to build, change, or maintain the system. The developer must also regularly check whether their approach is meeting the agreed quality goals efficiently and effectively. In addition, they must review how well their process supports the security and privacy capabilities needed for today’s risk environment, using the results from security and privacy assessments and quality reviews as input.

The developer must set measurable improvement goals, record the actions they will take, assign responsibility, set milestone dates, and keep these updates in the relevant procurement and contract records (including service agreements where used). This ensures progress can be tracked and overseen by the appropriate internal roles.

##### Contractual continuous improvement evidence packs with measurable cadence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Require every system, component, or service developer to run a clear, documented continuous improvement process for the development work used to build, change, or maintain the system or service. The developer must provide evidence that can be checked during an audit, showing ongoing improvement against agreed quality objectives, and showing how security and privacy results are reviewed and used to make measurable improvements.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-15(6)** (Enhancement) requires: “Require the developer of the system, system component, or system service to implement an explicit process to continuously improve the development process.” The guidance also expects developers to consider whether the approach is effective and efficient for meeting quality objectives, and to address security and privacy capabilities in today’s threat environment.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Build the requirement into procurement and contract documents**: update organisational purchasing policies and procedures, and contract templates, so that continuous improvement is a named deliverable in all relevant software or application development procurements (including managed service providers).
2. **Define a standard “Continuous Improvement Evidence Pack”** as a contract schedule item. Minimum required contents for each reporting period:
   - **Effectiveness and efficiency measures** linked to agreed quality objectives (for example: defect escape rate, test coverage trends, rework percentage, lead time, build or release success rate).
   - **Inputs from security and privacy capability reviews** (for example: references or identifiers to security assessment outcomes, quality review outcomes, vulnerability findings, dependency and software bill of materials (SBOM) findings, penetration test or security testing results, and privacy risk review outcomes such as privacy impact assessments (PIAs) or privacy risk assessments (PRAs), where applicable).
   - **Improvement actions**: each action must name the owner or team, include a start date, include milestone or target dates, state the expected outcome, and show which specific assessment or review evidence triggered it.
   - **Before-and-after evidence**: show at least one measurable change that resulted from previous improvement actions (for example, a trend or difference over time).
3. **Set a measurable schedule and continuity requirement**: require a defined cadence (for example, monthly status updates with a quarterly improvement review) and require evidence across **at least two consecutive periods** to show continuity.
4. **Require governance and oversight**: specify that the developer must provide the evidence pack to the organisation’s designated contract owner or technical authority, and that the organisation will review and accept or reject improvements against the agreed quality, security, and privacy objectives.
5. **Make the process auditable**: require the evidence pack to reference the developer’s internal process records (for example: updated quality gates, changes to continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline policy, test strategy updates, security scanning rule updates) at a level sufficient for audit, without telling the developer what internal tools to use.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: updates purchasing policy, invitations to tender, and contract templates.
- **Application/Engineering governance lead (or Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) delegate)**: sets the minimum evidence pack content, the mapping to quality objectives, and the reporting cadence.
- **Contract manager / contract owner**: receives evidence packs, tracks acceptance, and escalates any failure to meet requirements.
- **Developer/vendor management contact**: prepares and submits the evidence packs, and updates actions and milestones.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For applicable procurements, contracts and service level agreements (SLAs) include a continuous improvement deliverable requiring a clear, documented process and an evidence pack.
- Evidence packs submitted by the developer include: (a) effectiveness and efficiency measures linked to agreed quality objectives, (b) security and privacy capability review inputs, (c) documented improvement actions with owners and time-bound milestones, and (d) traceability to the specific assessment or review evidence that triggered each action.
- Evidence is provided for at least two consecutive reporting periods, showing ongoing improvement (trend or change over time), not a one-off submission.
- The organisation’s designated reviewers can check completeness against the defined minimum evidence pack content and can trace actions back to the evidence that triggered them.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update the acquisition policy and procedures to require continuous improvement deliverables under SA-15(6) for relevant developer procurements.
- [ ] Update the tender (solicitation) and contract templates to add a “Continuous Improvement Evidence Pack” schedule item, including the minimum required contents.
- [ ] Agree on quality targets and the effectiveness and efficiency measures the developer must report each period.
- [ ] Set a regular reporting schedule and require proof of progress in at least two consecutive reporting periods, with time-bound improvement milestones.
- [ ] Assign internal responsibility for contract ownership and technical review for receiving, checking, and approving evidence packs.
- [ ] Implement contract acceptance criteria that confirm there is a clear link (traceability) from security, privacy, and quality assessment evidence to the documented improvement actions.


---

#### SA-15.7 — Automated Vulnerability Analysis (Enhancement)

This requirement is about regularly checking your system for known security weaknesses, and doing the same immediately after any code or configuration change. The checks should be automated and cover: reviewing the code itself, testing the running service, checking third-party components, and scanning any packaged software images. This matters because weaknesses that are not discovered or assessed can be used by criminals to break in, steal data, or disrupt services—especially after new changes are introduced.

To meet this requirement, the system developer must run these automated checks every week and after each code change or configuration update. If any issues are found, the developer must assess how easily they could be exploited and identify practical ways to reduce the risk, such as fixes or other risk-reducing measures.

The developer must then share the scan results and conclusions—including how easily the issues could be exploited and the proposed mitigations—with the system owner and the vulnerability management lead every week and after each change.

##### CI/CD automated vulnerability analysis with exploitation triage

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an automated vulnerability analysis process inside the system developer’s continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline. It must run **weekly** and also **after any code change or configuration update**. The process must use **static application security testing (SAST)**, **dynamic application security testing (DAST)**, **software composition analysis (SCA)**, and **container or image vulnerability scanning**.  

For every issue found, the system must automatically combine the results and require an **assessment of how easily it could be exploited** and **recommendations to reduce the risk**. Then it must send the complete analysis package to the **system owner** and the **vulnerability management lead**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication 800-53 **SA-15(7)** enhancement requires the system developer to:  
1. run automated vulnerability analysis at the defined **frequency** and **after code or configuration changes**;  
2. use the defined **tools**;  
3. for any vulnerabilities found, determine **exploitation potential** and **risk mitigations**; and  
4. provide the **tool outputs and results** to the defined people/roles.  

This solution meets the organisation’s defined requirements:
- **frequency (sa-15.07_odp.01):** weekly and after any code change or configuration update  
- **tools (sa-15.07_odp.02):** SAST, DAST, SCA, and container/image vulnerability scanning  
- **personnel/roles (sa-15.07_odp.03):** system owner and vulnerability management lead  

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a standard CI/CD security workflow** that runs when triggered by:  
   - a scheduled weekly pipeline job, and  
   - any merge or deployment event that relates to a **code change** or **configuration update** (for example: infrastructure-as-code changes, buildpack/base image changes, Helm chart updates).  

2. **Run the required automated tools** in the workflow against the same build/version:  
   - **SAST** on the source code (or compiled build outputs if your SAST tool supports this).  
   - **SCA** on dependencies (including indirect/transitive dependencies) and generate or attach a **software bill of materials (SBOM)** where supported.  
   - **Container/image vulnerability scanning** on the produced image(s) from the build.  
   - **DAST** against an temporary test or staging deployment of the same build/version (only where a safe test environment exists). If not, document the limitation and ensure the workflow still runs the other required tools.  

3. **Link results to traceable identifiers** by including them in the evidence package:  
   - repository/build ID, commit hash, artefact or image digest, and deployment environment,  
   - the change ticket or reference that triggered the post-change run.  

4. **Add exploitation-potential and mitigation information** for each finding:  
   - require a structured judgement of exploitation potential (for example: Low/Medium/High) with a short explanation based on exploitation indicators (for example: whether authentication is required, whether the affected component is reachable, required privileges, and exposure conditions);  
   - require a mitigation recommendation record that includes either a fix (patch/code/configuration change) or a compensating control (for example: hardening, segmentation, web application firewall (WAF) rule, or access restriction), and explain how it reduces risk.  

5. **Package and deliver evidence automatically** after each run:  
   - produce a single “vulnerability analysis package” containing the raw tool outputs plus the combined findings, exploitation-potential judgement, and mitigation recommendations;  
   - deliver it to both the **system owner** and **vulnerability management lead** (for example: as a ticket attachment, via a secure email distribution list, or through a governance portal), and keep it for audit.  

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Developer / DevSecOps engineer:** builds the CI/CD workflow, integrates the tools, prepares the evidence package, and automates delivery.  
- **System owner:** receives the analysis package and uses it for risk acceptance and prioritisation decisions.  
- **Vulnerability management lead:** receives the analysis package and manages remediation and mitigation tracking.  

###### Acceptance criteria
- Weekly pipeline runs execute the workflow and produce an evidence package containing **SAST, SCA, container/image scanning, and DAST results** (where DAST is possible through a test deployment).  
- Any code change or configuration update triggers a post-change run, and the evidence package includes the triggering change reference.  
- For each vulnerability finding, the package includes an **exploitation potential** judgement and a **risk mitigation** recommendation.  
- The evidence package is delivered to the **system owner** and **vulnerability management lead** for every weekly and post-change run.  
- Evidence can be traced to the specific build/version (commit hash and image digest/build ID).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline triggers to run on a weekly schedule and automatically after changes to code or configuration.
- [ ] Make static application security testing (SAST), software composition analysis (SCA), and container and image vulnerability scanning required steps in the workflow.
- [ ] Set up a temporary test or staging deployment and run dynamic application security testing (DAST) using the same build or version.
- [ ] Implement a consolidation step that links all findings to the build or version identifier and the reference to the change that triggered them.
- [ ] Create an exploitation risk and mitigation recommendation workflow that generates structured outputs for each finding.
- [ ] Automate the creation of one complete evidence pack and securely send it to the system owner and the lead responsible for vulnerability management.


---

#### SA-15.8 — Reuse of Threat and Vulnerability Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about learning from similar systems before you build or change your own, so you do not repeat the same mistakes. It matters because attackers often take advantage of the same types of weaknesses across comparable products. If you only consider risks from the start, you may miss well-known problem patterns, which can lead to expensive fixes, service disruption, or loss of sensitive information.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure the system developer uses “threat thinking” (identifying the most likely ways harm could happen) and “vulnerability reviews” (checking for known weaknesses) taken from similar systems, components, or services. The developer must then use the findings to make real development decisions, such as design choices, requirements, and implementation guidance.

The organisation should require this reuse to be built into the development process, documented in relevant development procedures and security plans, and included in procurement documents and contracts. This makes it enforceable when buying or commissioning services.

##### Threat/vuln reuse library with traceable design decision mapping

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Put in place a controlled process and supporting tools that require developers to reuse threat modelling and vulnerability analysis results from **similar systems, components, or services**. Developers must also record how those reused findings **influenced** the design and implementation choices being made now.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-15(8)** (Enhancement) requires developers to **use threat modelling and vulnerability analyses from similar systems, components, or services** to guide the current development work. This helps ensure known attacker paths and known weaknesses are considered early, which reduces repeated design and implementation mistakes.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Create and maintain a “similarity reference library”** that includes threat models and vulnerability analyses from comparable internal systems and relevant vendor or public sources (for example, the National Vulnerability Database (NVD)). Include audit-friendly details such as system/component/service, version, scope, and a clear explanation of **why** the sources are considered similar.
2. **Set clear similarity selection criteria** (for example, the same architecture pattern, trust boundaries, authentication and authorisation model, data classification, deployment layout, and integration points). Require developers to choose references that meet these criteria.
3. **Build reuse into the development workflow** using a controlled “gate” (for example, a design review checklist plus a continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) or release approval gate). For each development milestone, require:
   - the **reference IDs** of the threat model(s) and vulnerability analysis(es) reused,
   - the **specific reused items** (threat scenarios, weakness categories, Common Vulnerabilities and Exposures (CVE) identifiers/advisories, affected components), and
   - a **traceable link** from each reused item to the resulting design and implementation decision(s) (for example, architecture decision records (ADRs), ticket links, configuration changes, and test cases).
4. **Automate evidence collection** by building the required mapping into existing tools (issue tracker, architecture decision records, and security review documents). The gate must fail if any required mapping information is missing.
5. **Review the library and mappings regularly** to keep reused evidence up to date (for example, when dependencies change or when new advisories are issued for referenced components).

###### WHO
- **Application Security Lead / Security Architect**: sets the similarity criteria, manages the library governance, and checks the quality of the evidence.
- **Developers / Solution Architects**: select suitable references and create the traceable mapping to the design and implementation decisions.
- **DevOps/Release Manager**: sets up the workflow gate and ensures it is enforced in CI/CD or release approvals.
- **Quality/Assurance or Security Review Board** (where applicable): samples and checks mappings during design reviews.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each relevant development milestone, the workflow gate produces an **audit-ready record** showing:
  - at least one **threat modelling** reference and at least one **vulnerability analysis** reference from **similar** systems/components/services,
  - the **exact sources** (reference IDs and/or URLs/identifiers such as CVE/advisory IDs),
  - a **documented traceability** from the reused threat/vulnerability items to the specific design and implementation decisions.
- Evidence is **enforced** (it cannot be skipped without an approved exception process).
- The similarity rationale is included and matches the defined selection criteria.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the rules for choosing similar components to reuse in threat modelling and vulnerability analysis, including: architecture, trust boundaries, authentication model, data classification, and integration points.
- [ ] Create a curated library of known threats and vulnerabilities, with supporting details (metadata) and a clear explanation of why each item is similar to the others in the library.
- [ ] Set up a development workflow checkpoint that requires developers to choose reference IDs and provide clear, auditable links between those IDs and the design and implementation decisions they made.
- [ ] Add evidence capture to the tools you already use (issue tracker, architecture decision records, and design review checklists) so that evidence-to-requirement links are created and kept automatically.
- [ ] Add automated checks to confirm submissions are complete (required fields filled in, enough threat and vulnerability references included, and decision links present) to prevent releasing items without supporting evidence.
- [ ] Set up a regular review of the library to update references when dependencies change or when new security advisories are issued.


---

#### SA-15.10 — Incident Response Plan (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that, if something goes wrong, the people who build the system already have a clear, tested plan for how to respond. Without an incident response plan that is actually used and checked through testing, serious problems such as a security breach, service disruption, or misuse could be dealt with in different ways. This can lead to delays, more damage, and confusion about who should do what. To meet this expectation, the system developer must provide the organisation with an incident response plan for the specific system, component, or service, ensure it is followed in day-to-day operations, and test it so it works in real situations. The developer must also share the plan in a practical format so the organisation can include it in its own arrangements for handling incidents.

##### Developer incident response plan pack with tested implementation evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Require the system developer to provide an incident response plan (IRP) for the specific system/component/service. The plan must be in a format the organisation can use, and the developer must provide evidence that the plan is followed in everyday operations and has been tested.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-15(10)** (Enhancement) requires the developer of the system, system component, or system service to **provide, implement, and test an incident response plan**. Information provided by the developer should be usable by the organisation and can be built into the organisation’s incident response arrangements.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Set up an acquisition/contract deliverable called an **“IR Plan Pack”** for each vendor/system component, with minimum required sections and clear evidence documents.

1. **Define the minimum IR Plan Pack content** in procurement templates (for example: plan scope, roles and responsibilities, escalation routes, severity and triage approach, communications approach, containment/eradication/recovery steps, evidence and forensics handling, and post-incident reporting). Make sure the pack is written so it can be incorporated into the organisation’s incident response plan.
2. **Require evidence of day-to-day use** from the developer, such as: named incident roles (including who is on call), integration with ticketing or workflow tools (for example, Jira/ServiceNow), extracts from runbooks showing how the plan is carried out, and examples of how the plan has been used during real incidents (redacted where needed).
3. **Require evidence of testing**: tabletop exercise report(s) and/or technical validation results showing the plan works in realistic scenarios relevant to the system (for example: credential compromise, signs of data being taken out, service disruption, and exploitation of vulnerabilities). Include dates, scenario descriptions, results, and corrective actions with closure dates.
4. **Require commitments to keep the plan up to date**: state how the developer will review and update the IRP when the system changes (for example, releases, architecture changes, and new integrations), and how updates will be communicated to the organisation.
5. **Make the deliverable auditable and enforceable**: include acceptance criteria in the contract/SOW (for example: completeness against the minimum content list; evidence documents provided; test results include remediation actions). Require the developer to provide the IR Plan Pack in an organisation-approved format (for example, an editable document plus supporting evidence exports).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement / Contracting lead**: includes IR Plan Pack deliverables and acceptance criteria in SOWs/MSAs.
- **Cybersecurity/Incident Response lead**: sets the minimum IRP content, which scenarios must be relevant, and what evidence is required.
- **Solution architect / System owner**: ensures the plan pack matches the specific system/component/service boundaries and integration points.
- **Vendor management / Vendor liaison**: collects and tracks submission of the pack and evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The developer provides an IR Plan Pack that is specific to the contracted system/component/service and can be used for incorporation into the organisation’s incident response arrangements.
- The pack includes evidence that the plan is **implemented** in day-to-day operations (for example: documented roles/on-call arrangements, workflow integration, and runbook execution evidence).
- The pack includes evidence that the plan has been **tested** (exercise or validation results, including outcomes and corrective actions).
- Contract acceptance criteria are met: minimum content completeness and inclusion of both implementation and testing evidence.
- The developer commits to maintaining/updating the IRP and provides a way and timeline for communicating updates to the organisation.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an incident response plan pack template with the minimum required sections, aligned to SA-15(10). The sections should cover: responsibilities and roles, escalation steps, containment and recovery actions, how evidence is handled, communications, and reporting after an incident.
- [ ] Add incident response plan (IR plan) pack deliverables and acceptance criteria to the statement of work (SOW) and master services agreement (MSA) for each developer or vendor system component.
- [ ] Define the required proof of implementation, including: the on-call and incident responsibilities, how the process connects to ticketing and workflow tools, and extracts from the runbook that show day-to-day execution.
- [ ] Define what proof of testing is required, including tabletop and technical validation scenarios, the results, and the remediation actions needed to fix any issues, with closure dates for each action.
- [ ] Create a vendor onboarding checklist to collect, review, and approve the incident response plan pack before launch and after any major changes.
- [ ] Document an internal process for adding the approved developer incident response plan pack into the organisation’s incident response arrangements.


---

#### SA-15.11 — Archive System or Component (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that when a supplier delivers a system or component, they also provide a complete “archive pack” showing what they built and the evidence that it was checked and approved for both security and privacy. This is important because if you only receive the finished product, you may not be able to confirm what was included, fix problems later, or demonstrate to regulators and customers that the final checks were completed correctly. That can result in costly delays, weaker oversight, and avoidable risk.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require, through its purchasing and contract arrangements, that the developer prepares and archives the delivered system or component before delivery. The archive pack must be provided together with the evidence used to support the final security and privacy review, including key development materials such as hardware details, the human-readable build code, the compiled release files, and the relevant build and configuration documentation. It must be packaged so it is a clear starting point for future updates.

##### Vendor delivery archive pack with final security/privacy evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Require every developer or vendor to archive the system or component that they are releasing or delivering, and include with that archive the proof (evidence) that supports the final security and privacy review.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 Security and Privacy Controls, System and Services Acquisition (SA)-15(11) enhancement requires: “Require the developer of the system or system component to archive the system or component to be released or delivered together with the corresponding evidence supporting the final security and privacy review.” The guidance also expects key development records to be kept (for example, hardware specifications where applicable, source code, compiled code, and relevant documentation) so there is a ready-to-use baseline of what was built and configured.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a standard “Delivery Archive Manifest”** that lists what must be included in the archive and what information must be recorded (release/component identifier, version/build number, target environment, checksums/hashes, and fields that link the archive items to the final security review and final privacy review).
2. **Update procurement and contracting templates** (statement of work (SOW)/contract clauses) so the developer must: (a) create the archive for the exact item being released/delivered, (b) complete archiving **before delivery**, and (c) include the corresponding final security and privacy review evidence.
3. **Require configuration-baseline records** to be included in the archive package, aligned to the guidance: hardware specifications where applicable, source code, compiled code, and relevant development/build/configuration documentation.
