4. **Add an automated acceptance check** to the organisation’s release/vendor delivery process that verifies the submitted archive package against the manifest (that all required items are present, the version/build matches, and the evidence references match the final security/privacy review records).
5. **Carry out a retrieval test during acceptance** (for example, a read-only download from the controlled repository plus checksum/hash verification) to show the archive can be retrieved and matches the delivered release.
6. **Store the archive package and acceptance proof together** in the organisation’s controlled document/repository system, and keep the contract/SOW reference and acceptance logs as audit evidence.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: updates contract/SOW templates and enforces delivery requirements.
- **Application Security and Privacy leads**: define the evidence mapping fields and confirm what counts as “final security and privacy review” evidence.
- **Release/Configuration Manager (or DevOps/continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) release owner)**: implements the acceptance check and retrieval test.
- **Vendor/Developer**: produces the archive package and evidence bundle according to the manifest.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every delivered release/component, an archive package is submitted **before delivery** and is checked against the Delivery Archive Manifest.
- The archive includes the required configuration-baseline records (where applicable: hardware specifications, source code, compiled code, and relevant documentation).
- The package includes evidence that is clearly linked to the **final** security review and **final** privacy review for that exact release/component.
- The archive can be retrieved from the controlled repository during acceptance (the retrieval test succeeds) and the hashes/checksums match the manifest.
- The contract/SOW reference and acceptance validation logs are kept for audit.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a Delivery Archive Manifest template that sets out the required documents, the rules for how they must be named and versioned, and the fields needed to link evidence for the final security review and the final privacy review.
- [ ] Update the contract and statement of work (SOW) templates to require the developer to archive the exact component that is being released or delivered before delivery, and to include the supporting evidence from the final security and privacy reviews.
- [ ] Set the minimum archive contents for your application context, including the source code, compiled code (object code), and any relevant build and configuration documentation. Include hardware specifications where they apply.
- [ ] Set up an automated approval check that confirms the archive is complete, that the version and build match the final reviewed records, and that all evidence references are consistent with those final records.
- [ ] During acceptance testing, run a retrieval test (a read-only fetch followed by checksum verification) and record the results in the acceptance evidence pack.
- [ ] Store the archive package and acceptance logs in the organisation’s controlled repository, with access limited to authorised roles.


---

#### SA-15.12 — Minimize Personally Identifiable Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping people’s personal details out of the places where systems are built and tested, so that only the minimum necessary information is used. The real-world risk it addresses is that development and testing work often involves copying real customer or employee information into environments that are less protected. This increases the chance of accidental exposure, misuse, or privacy harm.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system or component developer to minimise the use of personal information in development and test settings. This should be done using practical steps, such as removing direct identifiers or using artificial data that does not relate to real people. The organisation must also ensure the developer documents how this minimisation is carried out, builds it into day-to-day development and testing, and has clear, approved authority for any rare cases where real personal data is used, including keeping evidence of those approvals.

Finally, the organisation must include these expectations in procurement documents and internal plans so they are enforceable from the start.

##### Enforce PII minimisation in dev/test via contractual clauses & CI/CD gates

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Require system and component developers to minimise the use of personally identifiable information (PII) in **development and test** environments by using **de-identification and/or synthetic data**, and to keep evidence that can be audited showing how this is done. Any use of real PII in dev/test must be **exceptional, authorised, and limited to a specific time period**, with recorded approvals.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SA-15(12) (Enhancement) requires: “Require the developer of the system or system component to minimize the use of personally identifiable information in development and test environments.” Guidance supports de-identification and synthetic data to reduce privacy risk.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Update procurement and governance documents** so the requirement is enforceable from the start: amend acquisition policy/procedures, wording in requests for proposals, and contract/service level agreement (SLA)/security and privacy plan templates to require:
   - Default dev/test datasets use **de-identified and/or synthetic data**.
   - A **PII minimisation plan** is submitted (which fields are treated as PII, the approach for transforming/masking/generalising data, and the approach for synthetic data).
   - **Real PII in dev/test** is allowed only through an **approved exception process** (authoriser, justification, scope, duration), with evidence kept.
2. **Require dataset manifests as evidence**: require developers to maintain a version-controlled dataset manifest for each dev/test dataset (data structure, which attributes are PII, the transformation method, and references to the approved source/exception record).
3. **Use automated “policy checks” in continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) and environment setup** so non-compliant imports cannot proceed:
   - Block test/dev dataset imports unless they reference an **approved dataset manifest**.
   - Add automated checks that stop builds when direct identifiers (for example, names, email addresses, national identifiers, and account numbers) are present in dev/test datasets **unless** the pipeline is linked to an **active approved exception**.
4. **Link to identity and access processes**: ensure only authorised roles can request real PII for dev/test, and that the approval reference is required by the pipeline (so exceptions can be traced).
5. **Make developer responsibilities operational**: require developers to explain how minimisation reduces privacy risk (for example, reduced ability to identify individuals, reduced ability to link data to real people) as part of the system security and privacy plan inputs.

###### WHO
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: updates solicitation/contract/SLA clauses.
- **Privacy Officer / Data Protection Officer (DPO)** and **cybersecurity/privacy governance**: defines acceptable minimisation methods and exception criteria.
- **Solution/Platform Architect**: defines the dataset manifest format and the CI/CD automated policy checks.
- **Engineering Manager / Developer**: implements de-identification/synthetic data, produces manifests, and follows the exception process.
- **DevOps/Platform team**: implements CI/CD and environment setup gates.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Contracts/SLAs and security/privacy plan templates include clear SA-15(12) dev/test PII minimisation requirements (de-identification/synthetic data; exception authorisation and evidence).
- For each dev/test dataset used by the application, there is a **version-controlled dataset manifest** showing PII fields and the minimisation method.
- CI/CD/environment setup fails when dev/test dataset imports include direct identifiers **without** an active approved exception reference.
- Evidence exists for any dev/test use of real PII (authoriser, justification, scope, duration), and pipeline runs reference the exception ID.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update procurement templates (request for proposals, contract, service level agreement, and security and privacy plan input requirements) to require that developers and testers minimise personal data by using de-identification and/or synthetic data.
- [ ] Define and publish an exception process for using real personal data (PII) in development and testing, including who can approve it, how long approvals last, and what proof must be kept and for how long.
- [ ] Create a standard dataset “manifest” format that requires identifying any personal data (PII) fields and stating the data minimisation method used for each development and testing (dev/test) dataset.
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) and use “policy-as-code” checks to stop development and testing environment dataset imports unless they point to an approved dataset manifest.
- [ ] Add automated checks in the software build pipeline to look for direct identifiers in development and testing data, and stop the build unless an active exception ID is provided.
- [ ] Require developers to submit a design showing how they will minimise personal data (PII) and a written explanation of the privacy risks, as part of the system security and privacy plan documentation.


---

#### SA-15.13 — Logging Syntax (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that, when software is being built and tested, the logs record security-relevant activity in a clear and consistent way, without accidentally recording personal information about people. If developers include names, email addresses, or other identifying details in test and development logs, that information can spread beyond its intended purpose and be harder to protect. It can also slow down and reduce the reliability of investigations if something goes wrong.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set clear rules for both suppliers and internal developers so that development and test logs are written in a consistent format. Each event must be recorded as one line, with:
- dates and times in a standard format
- consistent field names
- only the minimum information needed: a pseudonymised identifier, whether the event succeeded or failed, the time, where it came from, and a correlation reference to link related events

The organisation must also ensure these logging rules are included in procurement and contract documents and are reflected in the system security plan. Finally, the organisation must check that developers’ test logs follow the required structure and do not include free-text personal details for authentication, authorisation, access to sensitive data, changes to security settings, and access to audit logs.

##### CI/CD-enforced JSONL secure logging schema with PII linting

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Set up an automated logging agreement that developers must follow for **development and test environments**. It should produce **JSON Lines** (one event per line) with **ISO 8601 timestamps**, **consistent field names**, and a **minimum set of required fields**. The agreement must **reduce personally identifiable information (PII)** by using **pseudonymised identifiers** and **not including free-text PII** for the specified security event types.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) **SA-15(13)** enhancement requires developers to **reduce the use of personally identifiable information in development and test environments**. This guidance also supports incident response by producing consistent audit event records that match **AU-02 event types**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define a versioned logging format** (machine-checkable) for development/test that specifies:
   - Format: **JSON Lines** (one event per line)
   - Timestamp: **ISO 8601**
   - Field naming: **consistent field names**
   - Minimum required fields: **pseudonymised identifiers**, **event outcome**, **timestamp**, **source**, **correlationId**
   - Required event types to record: **authentication events, authorisation events, access to sensitive data, security configuration changes, audit log access**
   - PII minimisation rules: **record only the required identifiers (pseudonymised)** and **omit free-text PII** (no names/email addresses/postal addresses in free-text/message fields for these event types)
2. **Add CI/CD “policy as code” checks** that test whether application-produced logs in development/test builds meet the rules:
   - Enforce **JSON Lines** structure (exactly one JSON object per line; reject events that span multiple lines)
   - Check that timestamps can be parsed as **ISO 8601**
   - Check required keys and **consistent field names**
   - Check that all required event categories are covered
   - Check PII minimisation by rejecting disallowed patterns in any free-text/message fields and in structured fields where PII is not allowed
3. **Implement a PII-aware checker** (clear, repeatable rules) that reviews the log content produced during tests:
   - Identify common PII signs (for example, email-like strings, phone-like strings, address-like tokens) in free-text fields
   - Allow only approved non-personal identifiers (for example, opaque IDs, pseudonyms)
   - Produce an auditable CI report showing which rule failed and where
4. **Ensure correlationId is carried through** in development/test so security events can be linked back together:
   - Create and pass **correlationId** using the application’s request context or tracing method
   - Check that **correlationId** is present in the emitted security events
5. **Require evidence to be produced**:
   - Save CI validation outputs (schema version, validation results, PII checker findings) as build outputs for audit purposes.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Software Engineering Lead / Developer**: implements the logging agreement and ensures emitted events follow it.
- **DevOps / CI/CD Engineer**: implements CI/CD checks and PII checking.
- **Security Engineering / AppSec**: maintains the logging format and PII detection rules; reviews any exceptions.

###### Acceptance criteria
- In development/test, emitted security events are **JSON Lines** with **one event per line**.
- All emitted events include **ISO 8601 timestamps**, **consistent field names**, and the **minimum required field set** (pseudonymised identifiers, event outcome, timestamp, source, correlationId).
- For the required event types (authentication, authorisation, access to sensitive data, security configuration changes, audit log access), logs **omit free-text PII** and use **pseudonymised identifiers**.
- CI/CD fails builds when logs break the format rules or when PII checking finds disallowed PII patterns in free-text/message fields.
- CI/CD produces an auditable validation report that references the schema version and the specific validation failures (if any).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a versioned **JSON Lines** logging template for development and testing that uses **ISO 8601** date and time stamps, consistent field names, a defined set of required event types, and a minimum set of required fields.
- [ ] Set up continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) checks to confirm that each security event is correctly formatted, including valid JSON Lines (JSONL) structure, ISO 8601 date and time format, all required fields are present, and field names are consistent.
- [ ] Develop and integrate a personal data (PII) checker (linter) that stops users from entering free-text or message content containing prohibited personal data patterns, and that requires pseudonymised identifiers.
- [ ] Add generation and propagation of a correlation identifier (correlationId) in the application, and check that a correlationId is present in every required security event type.
- [ ] Run automated test suites that generate security event records and save continuous integration (CI) test results (including the schema version, pass/fail outcome, and any personal data (PII) checks) as audit evidence.


---

### SA-16 — Developer-provided Training (Control)

This requirement is about making sure that everyone who builds your system is trained every year to use and operate the safety and privacy features they have built in, and to write code in a way that reduces security weaknesses. Without this, even well-meaning developers can misunderstand how the built-in protections are supposed to work, set them up incorrectly by accident, or leave gaps that could lead to data being exposed, privacy promises being broken, or new security weaknesses being introduced.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must require the system or service developer—whether they are part of your organisation or a third party—to provide annual training. This training must include both online learning and practical, hands-on sessions. It must cover the security and privacy features, secure coding practices, and how to operate and check that the protections work correctly in everyday use. You must also keep training materials and records showing who attended and confirming it was completed within the last 12 months. Finally, this requirement must be included in procurement and contract documents so it can be enforced.

##### Annual developer security/privacy training as contract deliverable

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Require the system developer (internal or external) to provide **annual** training on the **correct use and operation** of the implemented **security and privacy features/controls**. This must include **secure coding** practices and **practical, hands-on** instruction on how to operate and check that the controls work as intended.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-16** requires the organisation to **require the developer** to provide training on the correct use and operation of the implemented security and privacy features/controls. The organisation-defined parameter **sa-16_odp (training)** specifies **annual developer training (web-based plus hands-on)** covering security and privacy features, secure coding, and the correct operation of the implemented controls.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Procurement/contract clause**: Add an enforceable clause to the invitation to tender, statement of work (SOW), and contract requiring **annual** developer training for the specific system/service. This must cover the implemented security and privacy features/controls.
2. **Training scope & learning objectives**: Require the developer to provide a training plan that clearly links to the system’s implemented security and privacy features (for example: how users are authenticated and authorised, how encryption is used, what logging and monitoring is expected, and how privacy/data handling controls work). It must also include **secure coding** topics relevant to the technology used.
3. **Delivery format**: Require **web-based** training modules plus **practical hands-on** sessions (for example: guided exercises in a separate test environment) that show correct operation and how to check that the controls behave as intended.
4. **Evidence pack per annual cycle**: Require the developer to submit an audit-ready evidence pack including: training materials (slides/handouts), agenda, module completion records, attendance logs (named individuals), dates, and a **mapping matrix** showing how each learning objective links to the implemented security and privacy features/controls.
5. **Internal developers**: For in-house development, apply the same annual requirement through internal governance (for example, an engineering enablement programme) and produce the same evidence pack.
6. **Operational integration**: Store evidence in a controlled repository with retention aligned to the organisation’s audit needs, and link it to the system’s security and privacy documentation for traceability.

###### WHO
- **Procurement/Commercial lead**: includes the SA-16 training requirement in contracts/SOWs.
- **System owner / Security & Privacy lead**: sets the required training scope and checks that the learning-objective mapping is correct.
- **Developer training lead (vendor or internal)**: delivers the training and provides the evidence pack.
- **Compliance/Assurance function**: checks the evidence pack is complete and keeps audit records.

###### Acceptance criteria
- An evidence pack exists for the current annual cycle showing **web-based plus hands-on** training delivery.
- Evidence includes **named attendance and completion records** dated within the last 12 months.
- Training materials and a mapping matrix show coverage of the implemented **security and privacy features/controls** and include **secure coding** and **correct operation/verification**.
- The contract/SOW includes an enforceable requirement for annual delivery and submission of evidence.
- For in-house developers, the same annual training and evidence pack process is clearly in place.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update procurement templates (statement of work (SOW), contract, and service level agreement (SLA)) to require annual training for developers on how to correctly use and operate the implemented security and privacy functions, controls, and mechanisms.
- [ ] Set clear training learning objectives and create a mapping matrix that links them to the system’s security and privacy controls and mechanisms that have been put in place.
- [ ] Specify delivery requirements for web-based modules, plus hands-on practical training in a separate test or laboratory environment.
- [ ] Provide an evidence pack for each yearly cycle, including named records of attendance and completion, the training materials used, and a clear link showing which objectives relate to which controls.
- [ ] Apply the same annual training and evidence pack process to internal developers through engineering governance.
- [ ] Create an audit-ready repository entry that links each annual training evidence pack to the relevant system security and privacy documentation.


---

### SA-17 — Developer Security and Privacy Architecture and Design (Control)

This requirement is about making sure the people building your system plan for security and privacy properly before they write any code, rather than treating it as an afterthought. If there is no clear, complete design, key safety and privacy protections can be missed, repeated, or added in ways that do not work well together. This can create gaps that may lead to unauthorised access, misuse of personal information, or inconsistent handling of data across the organisation.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system, component, or service developer to produce a detailed design document and a security and privacy blueprint. These must align with the organisation’s own security and privacy plans, which form part of the organisation’s wider business architecture.

The developer must fully explain what security and privacy functions are needed, where they apply across both the physical parts and the software parts, and how each part works together as a coordinated approach to deliver the required protection.

##### Architecture pack deliverables for security & privacy design (SA-17)

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Require each system, system component, or system service developer (especially external developers) to produce a **design specification** and a **security and privacy architecture** that are complete, consistent, and clearly show how security and privacy responsibilities are assigned and built into **physical and logical components**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-17 requires the developer to produce a design specification and security/privacy architecture that:
- is **consistent with the organisation’s security and privacy architecture** (part of the enterprise architecture),
- gives **full descriptions of security and privacy functions**,
- shows how security and privacy measures are assigned across **physical and logical components**, and
- explains how security and privacy measures work together as a **coordinated approach**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Create an “Architecture Pack” as a contractual and governance deliverable with required content, traceability, and review checkpoints.

1. **Publish authoritative reference architectures** for the organisation (enterprise security architecture and enterprise privacy architecture), covering at minimum: identity/trust boundaries, data classification handling, encryption/key management expectations, logging/monitoring expectations, and privacy requirements (for example, consent/retention/erasure where applicable).
2. **Create a standard Architecture Pack template** that developers must complete, including:
   - security/privacy use-cases and assumptions,
   - trust boundaries and data flows (logical and, where relevant, physical),
   - a **security control allocation matrix** mapping required security functions to **physical/logical components**,
   - a **privacy control allocation matrix** mapping required privacy functions to **physical/logical components**,
   - a single narrative explaining how security and privacy measures fit together as one approach,
   - component-level architecture (for example, services/modules) and deployment-level view (for example, environments, network segments, cloud accounts/virtual private clouds, regions),
   - traceability from organisation requirements → developer mechanisms → components → planned verification evidence.
3. **Add SA-17 acceptance criteria to procurement/contracting** for external development and to internal design checkpoints where applicable. Acceptance criteria must require that:
   - every required security/privacy capability is mapped to components,
   - every data category and processing purpose has matching safeguards and privacy measures,
   - the design clearly covers separation of environments (development/testing/production/disaster recovery) and administrative separation where feasible,
   - the design is consistent with the published enterprise security/privacy architectures.
4. **Run architecture review checkpoints** before build/test sign-off:
   - the security architect and privacy/data protection lead review the allocation matrices and the integration narrative,
   - the application/infrastructure architect confirms alignment at deployment level (for example, separation across tiers, separation of environments, and integration points such as identity, security information and event management (SIEM)/monitoring, and backup/recovery).
   - require a remediation plan for any gaps, and re-review after changes.
5. **Carry out automated checks** on submitted Architecture Packs (where feasible) to confirm completeness and consistency, for example: required sections are present, identifiers match across matrices, and data-flow diagrams refer to the same data categories used in the allocation matrices.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement/contract owner**: includes SA-17 deliverables and acceptance criteria in contracts.
- **Security architecture lead**: maintains the enterprise security architecture references and reviews security allocation.
- **Privacy/data protection lead**: maintains the enterprise privacy architecture references and reviews privacy allocation.
- **Solution/application architect**: checks component/deployment views and integration points.
- **Developer (external or internal)**: produces the Architecture Pack and any remediation responses.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The developer submits an Architecture Pack that includes all required sections (design specification + security/privacy architecture).
- Security and privacy allocation matrices map required capabilities to **both physical and logical components**, using consistent identifiers.
- The architecture includes a single integration narrative showing how security and privacy measures work together.
- The architecture can be shown to be consistent with the organisation’s published enterprise security and privacy architectures.
- Architecture review checkpoints are completed with documented sign-off (or documented remediation and re-review) before build/test.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Publish the organisation’s enterprise security architecture and enterprise privacy architecture reference models so developers can align their work.
- [ ] Create and keep up to date an “Architecture Pack” template that includes mandatory sections covering security and privacy design, how data moves through the system, and a single, joined-up explanation of how all integrations work.
- [ ] Update procurement and contract templates to require submission of an Architecture Pack and acceptance criteria specific to SA-17 before authorising build and testing sign-off.
- [ ] Require developers to provide security and privacy responsibility maps for both physical and logical parts of the system, with clear links to the planned proof (verification evidence) that will be used to check them.
- [ ] Set up architecture review checkpoints involving the security architect, the privacy and data protection lead, and the solution architect, and require a remediation plan to fix any identified gaps.
- [ ] Set up automated checks to confirm that Architecture Pack submissions are complete and consistent, including that all required sections are included, identifiers match correctly, and the data flow and data categories are aligned.


---

#### SA-17.1 — Formal Policy Model (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building a system can clearly and verifiably explain, before it is built, exactly how it will protect sensitive information and personal data. Without this, organisations risk ending up with software that only claims to protect data in general terms, but in practice may allow unauthorised access, fail to record important events, send or store data without proper protection, mishandle highly sensitive information, or keep and process personal data in ways that breach legal and privacy obligations.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system developer, as part of the development work, to produce a formal, documented model that clearly sets out the rules for:
- controlling who can access information,
- recording and auditing events,
- encrypting data while it is being sent and while it is stored,
- handling Confidential and Secret data,
- lawful processing of data,
- data minimisation,
- retention limits, and
- notifying the organisation of breaches.

The developer must also provide evidence that these models are internally consistent and sufficient to ensure the organisation’s security and privacy requirements are correctly followed when the system is implemented. The organisation must make this a contractual development deliverable.

##### Formal policy models for access, audit, crypto, and data handling

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Ask the system developer to provide **formal, computer-checkable policy models** for both **security** and **privacy** requirements. These models must cover only the system behaviours that matter, such as: authorisation decisions, audit event generation, encryption requirements, and handling of Confidential/Secret data; plus lawful processing, data minimisation, retention limits, and breach notification.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST Special Publication 800-53 **SA-17(1) Formal Policy Model (enhancement)** requires the developer to produce a **formal policy model** so that the correctness of the required security and privacy behaviours can be **proven formally** (where within the agreed scope), rather than relying on informal descriptions.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Define what is being modelled and the policy parts** for each system component/service (for example: API authorisation, admin actions, audit event emission, enforcement of encryption in transit/at rest, and the data lifecycle for Confidential/Secret data; plus privacy parts covering lawful processing, minimisation, retention, and breach notification).
2. **Choose a formal modelling language/approach** that has documented meaning for the behaviours being covered (for example: a typed logic or automata-based access-control model with event/data constraints, or a policy language with a verified compiler). Record the chosen language, key assumptions, and what is explicitly **in scope vs out of scope**.
3. **Create a formal security policy model** that includes, at minimum:
   - **Rules for non-discretionary access control** (role-based or attribute-based access control): roles/attributes, conditions, and “deny by default” behaviour.
   - **Audit logging policy**: which events must be generated, required fields, and when logging must happen (for example, success/failure and administrative actions).
   - **Encryption policy**: requirements for **encryption in transit** (for example, Transport Layer Security (TLS)/mutual TLS (mTLS)) and **encryption at rest** for Confidential/Secret data, including key-handling constraints where applicable.
   - **Secure configuration and data-handling constraints**: rules that prevent prohibited data flows (for example, keeping secrets in plaintext, or sending Confidential/Secret data to unauthorised destinations).
4. **Create a formal privacy policy model** that includes:
   - **Lawful processing rules** (what processing is allowed under the organisation’s privacy policy).
   - **Data minimisation rules** (which attributes/fields may be processed for each purpose).
   - **Retention limits** (time-bounded storage/processing rules).
   - **Breach notification triggers** (events that must start the notification process).
5. **Formally check (verify) both models**:
   - **Internal consistency**: look for contradictions or unclear areas (for example, access rules that both allow and deny the same non-discretionary case; audit rules that conflict with the conditions for generating events).
   - **Adequacy for enforcement** (within the defined scope): show that the model’s rules cover the required security/privacy elements and can be linked to the points where the system enforces them (authorisation middleware, audit pipeline, cryptography configuration, data lifecycle controls, and breach workflow triggers).
6. **Provide evidence as contractual deliverables**:
   - Supply the formal model source, the verification results (logs/reports), and a **traceability matrix** that links each formal rule/predicate to the corresponding design, configuration, and enforcement mechanism that can be tested.

###### WHO
- **System developer / engineering lead**: produces the formal security and privacy models and the verification evidence.
- **Security architect / privacy lead**: defines the policy parts and scope, and reviews whether the models cover what is required and whether the verification scope is correct.
- **Contracting/procurement owner**: ensures the formal model and verification evidence are included as acceptance deliverables.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The developer delivers **two formal models** (security and privacy) using an explicitly documented formal language/approach and defined scope.
- Verification evidence shows **internal consistency** for the behaviours within scope.
- Verification evidence and traceability show **adequacy**: the models cover the organisation-defined elements for **role-based access control (RBAC)/attribute-based access control (ABAC)**, audit logging, encryption in transit/at rest, Confidential/Secret handling, and **privacy lawful processing, minimisation, retention limits, and breach notification**.
- A traceability matrix links each formal rule/predicate to the system enforcement points and the corresponding test evidence (for example: authorisation checks, audit event generation, cryptography configuration, data lifecycle controls, breach workflow triggers).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the in-scope security and privacy expectations for each component or service, including: authorisation, audit records, enforcing encryption, handling Confidential and Secret information, lawful processing, data minimisation, data retention, and the triggers for responding to a breach.
- [ ] Choose and document the formal modelling language or approach and explain what it means (its semantics), including clear boundaries for what is in scope and what is out of scope.
- [ ] Create a formal security policy model that covers:

- role-based access control (RBAC) and attribute-based access control (ABAC)
- rules for recording and reviewing security audit logs
- encryption in transit and encryption at rest
- how Confidential and Secret data must be handled and protected
- [ ] Create a formal privacy policy template that covers lawful processing, collecting only the minimum necessary data, setting clear limits on how long data is kept, and defining when to notify people about a data breach.
- [ ] Carry out formal verification to prove the system is internally consistent and that the checks cover everything needed for enforcement, and keep the verification reports and logs.
- [ ] Create a traceability matrix that links each formal rule or condition to where it is enforced in the system and what proof of testing is required. Include this matrix, along with the related test evidence, as contractual acceptance deliverables.


---

#### SA-17.2 — Security-relevant Components (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building or supplying a system clearly state exactly which parts are trusted to keep the security protections working. If there is not a complete, agreed list of the hardware, software, and firmware that matter for security, important security measures could be missed. This could leave weaknesses that attackers could exploit, or it could cause security protections to stop working after changes.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the developer to provide:
- a written definition of the security-relevant hardware,
- a written definition of the security-relevant software, and
- a written definition of the security-relevant firmware.

The developer must also provide a written justification explaining why these lists are complete and cover everything the organisation considers trusted for security.

The organisation should include these requirements in procurement and contract documents, so these documents are delivered as part of delivery and handover.

##### Supplier deliverables for security-relevant hardware/software/firmware lists

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Require the system developer/supplier to provide auditable, written definitions of the **security-relevant hardware**, **security-relevant software**, and **security-relevant firmware**, plus a **written justification of completeness** explaining why the identified items cover everything the organisation considers trusted to keep the required security protections in place.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **SA-17(2)** (enhancement) requires the developer of the system/component/service to identify the part of the system that is trusted to work correctly in order to maintain the required security protections. The guidance clarifies that security-relevant hardware/software/firmware is the trusted part; therefore, the organisation must make sure the developer’s identification is clear and complete.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Apply SA-17(2) as a mandatory procurement and delivery acceptance requirement, enforced through contract terms and handover sign-off checkpoints.

1. **Create standard contract/procurement clauses** that require four deliverables at delivery/hand-over:
   - Written **List/Definition of security-relevant hardware**
   - Written **List/Definition of security-relevant software**
   - Written **List/Definition of security-relevant firmware**
   - Written **Rationale of completeness** covering all three categories

2. **Provide a template for the developer** that requires, for each listed item:
   - Name/identifier, version/build (where applicable), and deployment scope (for example, environment boundary: customer-managed versus provider-managed)
   - The **security protection/function** it supports (for example, enforcing authentication, enforcing authorisation, key management, protecting secure updates and rollbacks, secure boot/attestation, audit log integrity)
   - An **evidence pointer** linking to the relevant architecture document, bill of materials (BOM), software inventory, firmware inventory, and/or release documents (as applicable to the technology)

3. **Require completeness mapping** in the rationale:
   - Developers must explain how the lists were built from the architecture and inventories (for example, bill of materials (BOM), software/firmware inventories) and must clearly state any security protection/function that is not included (either excluded with a justification or included).
   - For multi-tier and microservices architectures, require mapping at least to the component boundaries that enforce security protections (for example, API gateway/auth service, identity integration modules, cryptography/key management libraries, secure update agents).

4. **Add acceptance criteria** to prevent handover without the required documents:
   - Do not accept delivery until all four documents are provided and they match each other internally (for example, version/build identifiers match the release documents; scope matches the deployment model).
   - For cloud/software-as-a-service (SaaS), require an **environment boundary statement** so the lists are scoped correctly.

5. **Assign ownership and review**:
   - Require the supplier to submit the deliverables to the organisation’s security/compliance review email address or portal.
   - The organisation checks the documents for completeness and consistency (not a full technical verification at this stage) and records acceptance evidence.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement/Commercial owner**: includes the SA-17(2) deliverables in the contract, service level agreement (SLA), and/or tender documents.
- **Application Security / Security Assurance lead**: maintains the template, reviews submissions for completeness/mapping, and approves acceptance.
- **Supplier/developer contract manager**: produces and signs the three lists and the completeness rationale.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The supplier provides **three written definitions/lists** (hardware, software, firmware) and a **written completeness rationale**.
- Each listed item includes a **security protection/function mapping** and an **evidence pointer** to the relevant architecture, inventories, and/or release documents.
- The rationale shows the lists were derived from the system’s trusted portion and explains any security protections/functions that are not mapped, with justification.
- Delivery is not accepted until the documents are present and internally consistent, including correct scoping for customer-managed versus provider-managed environments.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Draft and approve standard procurement and contract clauses that require SA-17(2) deliverables, including lists of required hardware, software, and firmware, plus a justification explaining why the list is complete.
- [ ] Publish a developer template that requires, for each item, a security function mapping and references to evidence stored in the architecture, bill of materials (BOM), software, and firmware inventories.
- [ ] Set acceptance gates that prevent handover until all four documents are provided and they all match each other internally, including the document version/build and which environment they apply to.
- [ ] Require suppliers to include an “environment boundary” statement for cloud software as a service (cloud/SaaS) in the completeness rationale, clearly stating whether the customer manages the environment or the provider manages it.
- [ ] Set up a review process for application security and security assurance to check that all requirements are fully covered, and to keep records showing what was accepted.


---

#### SA-17.3 — Formal Correspondence (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building your system clearly and formally explain how the security-critical parts connect to the underlying hardware and software, including what happens when something goes wrong. Without this, security protections might work differently in real use than intended, which could lead to unauthorised access, data exposure, or failures that are difficult to spot and explain.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system or service developer, as part of development, to produce and keep an up-to-date formal top-level description of every security-relevant interface. This description must state the abnormal situations (exceptions), the exact error information produced, and the resulting outcomes (effects).

The developer must also provide evidence that this description matches the organisation’s security rules to the extent that can be shown rigorously. It must fully cover all relevant interfaces and accurately reflect what is actually implemented.

Finally, the developer must document any security-relevant internal mechanisms that exist but are not covered in the formal description.

##### Formal interface correspondence pack for security-relevant APIs

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Create and maintain a **formal, top-level interface specification** for every **security-relevant** system component/service interface. This includes **exceptions**, **error information (codes/messages)**, and **effects (changes in system state/results)**. Provide evidence that the formal specification is **consistent with the formal policy model** (a formal proof where feasible; a structured explanation where not) and that it **matches the actual implementation** (a structured explanation and/or verification evidence). Also document any **strictly internal security mechanisms** (for example, internal mapping tables or direct memory input/output equivalents within the component) that are not covered by the top-level formal interface description.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-17(3) Formal Correspondence (enhancement)** requires the developer to show that the **implementation is an accurate transformation of the model**, and that any additional code/implementation details do not change the behaviours or policies described in the model. It also recognises that a full proof of consistency may not be possible, so a **mix of formal and informal methods** is acceptable.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Create a security-relevant interface inventory** (per component/service) covering all externally reachable and security-critical internal interfaces. Examples include authentication/authorisation decision points, key/secret handling endpoints, audit/log ingestion endpoints, session/token issuance and validation, admin-only operations, and any privileged workflows.
2. For each interface, produce a **formal top-level contract** that clearly models:
   - Inputs and outputs
   - **Exceptions** (for example, invalid credentials, unauthorised access, expired tokens, malformed requests, dependency failures)
   - **Error information** as a first-class output (for example, an error code plus the matching message)
   - **Effects** (for example, allowing or denying a session, emitting an audit event, updating rate-limit counters, lockout state changes, refusing to release secrets)
3. Create a **policy-model correspondence mapping**:
   - Link each policy rule (from the organisation’s formal policy model) to the specific interface operations and their effects.
   - Where feasible, run **formal proof** that interface effects preserve the policy rules.
   - Where proof is not practical, provide a **structured informal demonstration** (assumptions, reasoning steps, and targeted verification results such as tests that confirm error codes are deterministic and that forbidden side-effects do not occur).
4. Produce a **spec-to-implementation correspondence record**:
   - For each interface operation, identify the implementing modules/services and the verification evidence (for example, unit and integration tests, contract tests, static analysis evidence, and traceability from the interface contract to the code paths).
   - Show that any additional implementation details do not change the modelled behaviours/policies (for example, middleware, retries, caching, circuit breakers) by documenting their impact and demonstrating equivalence through tests or reasoned argument.
5. Maintain a **strictly-internal mechanism register** for security-relevant internal mechanisms not represented in the top-level formal interface (for example, internal mapping tables, low-level buffer handling, direct memory input/output equivalents, and token/session storage internals). Document how these mechanisms are constrained so they cannot violate the modelled interface behaviours.
6. Version the entire **Formal Correspondence Pack** (formal interface specifications, correspondence mappings, proof/informal demonstration reports, and spec-to-implementation evidence) and link it to release/change records.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Lead developer / system architect**: owns the formal interface contracts and the correspondence pack.
- **Security engineering lead**: ensures the policy-model mapping is correct and that informal demonstrations are credible.
- **Verification/QA engineer**: produces and maintains verification evidence (contract tests, property-based tests, traceability to code paths).
- **Configuration/release manager**: ensures versioning and linkage to change/release artefacts.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A **complete interface inventory** exists for all security-relevant interfaces in scope.
- For each interface, there is a **formal top-level specification** covering **exceptions, error information, and effects**.
- There is a **policy-model correspondence mapping** showing how interface effects satisfy policy rules, with **proof where feasible** and **structured informal demonstration** where not.
- There is a **spec-to-implementation correspondence record** linking each interface operation to implementation modules and verification evidence, demonstrating that additional implementation details do not change modelled behaviours/policies.
- A **strictly-internal mechanism register** exists for security-relevant internal mechanisms not covered by the top-level formal interface description, with documented constraints/evidence that they cannot violate modelled behaviours.
- All artefacts are **versioned** and traceable to the relevant release/change record.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a complete list (inventory) of all application interfaces that are important for security, including application programming interfaces (APIs), service operations, and internal calls that are critical to security.
- [ ] Create a formal, top-level interface agreement for each interface. This should include any exceptions, the error codes and messages, and what state changes each interface causes.
- [ ] Match each policy rule (in the formal policy model) to the matching interface actions and their outcomes.
- [ ] Where possible, produce evidence reports. Where proof is not practical, provide structured informal demonstrations, covering consistency with the policy.
- [ ] Create and keep a record that links every interface operation to the relevant code modules and the proof used to verify that it works.
- [ ] Document any strictly internal security measures that are not covered by the main formal interface, and record the constraints and evidence showing that these measures cannot change the modelled behaviours.
- [ ] Create a version history for the Formal Correspondence Pack and link each version to the relevant release or change records so it can be audited.


---

#### SA-17.4 — Informal Correspondence (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people building the system clearly explain, in plain language, how the system’s security-critical parts communicate with each other and what happens when something goes wrong. Without this, the system could behave in ways the organisation did not intend—allowing unauthorised actions, concealing failures, or producing confusing results that make problems harder to spot and fix.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require the system developer to produce, as part of development, an informal high-level description of every security-relevant interface. For each interface, the developer must state:
- what exceptions can occur,
- the exact error messages users or other systems will see, and
- the observable effects on the system.

The developer must also show that this description matches the organisation’s formal security rules, confirm that nothing security-relevant is missing or described incorrectly, and separately document any security-relevant mechanisms that are internal only and not covered by the interface description.

##### Informal security interface behaviour spec with policy alignment evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT must be done
Ask the system developer to produce an informal, high-level description of **security-relevant interface behaviour** for the system (or system component/service). For each interface, the description must state:
- the **types of problems (exceptions)** that can happen,
- the **exact error text and/or error codes** that the people or systems calling the interface will see,
- the **visible impact on the system** when those problems occur.

Also require the developer to provide an **informal demonstration** that this description matches the organisation’s **formal policy model** (using the organisation-defined parameter for SA-17(4)). The developer must also show **complete and accurate coverage** of all security-relevant interfaces.

Finally, require a **separate informal description** of any security-relevant mechanisms that are strictly internal (for example, internal mapping registers, direct memory input/output, or other internal-only security mechanisms). These must be documented separately because they are **not** covered by the interface behaviour description.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The SA-17(4) enhancement guidance explains that assurance is shown by demonstrating that the implementation is an accurate “translation” of the model, and that extra implementation detail does not change the behaviours or policies being modelled. Full formal proof is usually not practical, so a mix of formal and **informal methods** is expected. This enhancement specifically requires informal evidence that the security-relevant interface behaviour (exceptions, error messages, and effects) matches the model, and separate documentation for strictly internal security mechanisms.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an interface inventory** covering all security-relevant interfaces (for example: authentication and authorisation endpoints, administrative functions, privileged operations, application programming interface (API) methods, message-bus topics and handlers, device management endpoints, and any security-relevant calls between services).
2. For each interface, require the developer to complete a standard **“Security-relevant Interface Behaviour”** template including:
   - interface identifier and purpose,
   - inputs and outputs that matter for security decisions,
   - listed **exceptions** (for example: unauthorised, forbidden, invalid input, expired credentials, cryptography failure, rate-limit/lockout conditions where applicable),
   - **exact error messages and/or codes** as seen by callers (for example: HTTP status plus application error code/message, gRPC status plus error details, and user interface error text where applicable),
   - **observable effects** (for example: no change to system state, an audit event is recorded, a session is not created, access to specific data is denied, a transaction is rolled back).
3. Require **informal coverage evidence**: show that every security-relevant interface in the inventory is included in the description (for example, a traceability matrix from inventory items to the relevant sections of the specification).
4. Require **informal accuracy evidence**: provide an evidence pack showing that representative failure cases produce the same error outputs as the specification (for example: contract tests, integration tests, or staging runs capturing the exact error payloads and confirming observable effects such as audit/log entries and state changes).
5. Require **informal consistency with the formal policy model**: link each interface exception/effect to the relevant elements in the formal policy model, and provide a narrative explaining why the described behaviour aligns with the formal rules (using the organisation-defined parameter **sa-17.04_odp** as the basis for the organisation’s expected approach to matching the policy model).
6. Require a separate section titled **“Strictly internal security mechanisms (not covered by interface behaviour)”**. This section must describe internal-only security mechanisms in plain language, clearly stating that they are internal to security-relevant hardware/software/firmware (for example: mapping registers, direct memory input/output, internal secure decision logic). Because they are internal, they must not be described as caller-observable interface behaviour.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **System developer / engineering lead**: produces the interface inventory, the informal interface behaviour specification, and the evidence pack.
- **Security architect / assurance lead**: checks completeness, reviews the narratives showing alignment to the policy model, and approves the internal-mechanisms documentation.
- **QA/test lead**: ensures test evidence records the exact error messages/codes and the observable effects.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented **interface inventory** exists and is complete for all security-relevant interfaces.
- For each security-relevant interface, the informal specification includes **exceptions, exact error messages/codes, and observable effects**.
- A traceability matrix shows **complete coverage** (no security-relevant interface is omitted).
- An evidence pack shows **accuracy** by demonstrating that observed error outputs and effects match the specification for representative failure cases.
- A documented mapping/justification shows **informal consistency** between the descriptive specification and the organisation’s **formal policy model**, using **sa-17.04_odp**.
- Strictly internal security mechanisms are documented separately, with clear statements that they are internal-only and not part of caller-observable interface behaviour.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep up to date a complete list (inventory) of all system, component, or service interfaces that are relevant to security.
- [ ] Create a standard template for security-relevant interface behaviour that records any exceptions, the exact error messages and error codes, and the observable outcomes.
- [ ] Complete the template for every interface, and link each entry to the relevant parts of the formal policy model using **sa-17.04_odp**.
- [ ] Create an informal coverage traceability matrix that shows every security-relevant interface is included exactly once, with none missing or duplicated.
- [ ] Collect an evidence pack from representative failure tests and staging runs that show the error messages received and confirm they match the specification.
- [ ] Document the internal security controls in plain language, separately from any caller-facing behaviour, and make it clear that these controls are not observable or detectable by the caller.


---

#### SA-17.5 — Conceptually Simple Design (Enhancement)

This requirement is about building security in a way that is easy to understand and hard to get wrong. The system’s most important security functions should use one complete, straightforward protection approach. The rules and expected results should have a clear, unambiguous meaning, and the internal design should be set up specifically to deliver that approach.

Without this, security features can become mixed together, inconsistent, or only partly effective. This can leave weaknesses that attackers can exploit, and it can make it difficult for the organisation to show that the protection works exactly as intended.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must make sure the system developer:
- identifies which hardware, software, and firmware parts affect security
- designs and structures those parts around a single simple protection mechanism
- defines and documents exactly how that mechanism works from start to finish
- explains how each internal component maps to the mechanism it implements

The developer must also provide design and development documentation so others can check the approach and confirm there are no competing or overlapping protection mechanisms.

##### Single conceptually simple security mechanism for all security decisions

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Design and implement the system so that all security-relevant decisions are made by one clear, complete protection mechanism with exactly defined behaviour. Structure the internal parts so they use that mechanism, rather than adding separate or overlapping checks.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-17(5)** (Enhancement) requires the developer to apply the **principle of reduced complexity**: the system design should be as simple and small as possible, because simpler designs are easier to understand and assess and are less likely to contain errors. This directly supports successful security reviews and the production of evidence about the system’s overall security behaviour.

###### HOW (specific approach)
Implement a **single authoritative protection mechanism** (for example, an authorisation decision point, a policy engine, or a device posture gate) and ensure:
1. **Security-relevant parts are identified** across the whole application (user interface, application services, application programming interfaces (APIs), administrative functions, and any security-relevant firmware or agents if applicable). These parts must be required to call the mechanism for security decisions.
2. **One complete protection mechanism is used**: avoid split or partially overlapping mechanisms (for example, multiple independent authorisation implementations across services).
3. **Conceptual simplicity is achieved** by using the smallest possible set of stored information (state) and a single decision rule set (inputs → state changes → outputs), with clearly defined ways it can fail.
4. **Precise behaviour is defined**: document the exact meaning of rules, states, triggers, and outcomes (allow, deny, or step-up), and what happens when the mechanism fails (for example, fail-closed versus fail-open).
5. **Internal structure is built around the mechanism**: change security-relevant components so they only enforce the mechanism’s decision (consume the decision) rather than re-creating the policy rules.
6. **Traceability is produced**: create a record showing which internal module or component implements which part of the mechanism (for example, decision logic, input checking/normalisation, enforcement points, and audit event generation).
7. **Evidence is generated**: provide design and security architecture documentation, plus developer documentation, showing the mechanism’s rule contract, the module-to-mechanism mapping, and how the design avoids competing mechanisms.

###### Practical implementation pattern (typical enterprise application)
- Create a central **authorisation decision point** (policy engine, middleware, or application programming interface (API) gateway enforcement layer) that receives a normalised request context (user or service identity, resource details, action, and environment or device posture signals) and returns one decision outcome.
- Ensure every security-relevant enforcement path (web and API endpoints, service-to-service calls, administrative actions, and any security-sensitive background jobs) goes through the same decision point.
- Keep enforcement layers thin: they apply the returned decision and do not repeat the policy rules.
- Generate audit events only from the mechanism’s outputs, so monitoring uses consistent meaning.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **System architect / security architect**: defines the mechanism’s rule contract, behaviour, and overall design.
- **Lead developer / engineering manager**: updates components to call the mechanism and removes unnecessary repeated checks.
- **Security engineering / identity and access management (IAM) team**: verifies the decision contract is correct, including the defined failure behaviour.
- **Quality assurance (QA) / security test lead**: creates tests that prove the single-mechanism behaviour and confirm there is no competing logic.

###### Acceptance criteria
- There is exactly **one** authoritative protection mechanism for the defined security decision(s) (for example, authorisation), and all security-relevant components call it.
- The mechanism has **documented, precise behaviour** (inputs, state model, decision outcomes, and explicit failure behaviour).
- Internal components use the mechanism through **thin enforcement**; policy logic is not duplicated in multiple places.
- A **module-to-mechanism mapping** exists and covers all security-relevant components.
- Evidence includes design/developer documentation and test results showing consistent behaviour across endpoints and services (including tests for failure behaviour).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify all parts of the system and all key decision points across the application (from front end to back end) that currently enforce security checks.
- [ ] Define one protection mechanism “contract” (what information it takes in, how it behaves over time, what it produces, and the exact ways it can fail) and publish it in the system design documentation.
- [ ] Restructure security-relevant components so they all use one single decision-making mechanism for security, and remove any duplicated or conflicting policy logic.
- [ ] Implement thin enforcement layers that apply the mechanism’s decision, without re-writing or re-encoding the rules.
- [ ] Create and keep up to date a record that links each internal component to the specific mechanism elements it implements.
- [ ] Add tests (including failure scenarios) to show that the behaviour is consistent and that there are no other ways to enforce the requirements.
- [ ] Create developer documentation and architecture evidence that shows, in a clear and straightforward way, how the design works and how it can be traced for security analysis.


---

#### SA-17.6 — Structure for Testing (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the people who build your system include the important security parts in a way that can be tested properly, consistently, and in the same way each time. If the design is difficult to review, or if key details are missing, security weaknesses may not be found until it is too late—leaving the organisation exposed to data loss, fraud, or service disruption.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require—through procurement and internal planning documents—that developers clearly identify which hardware, software, and firmware parts affect security, organise the design so it can be tested thoroughly, and provide the necessary design and configuration documentation as part of delivery. The organisation should also ensure its own system design and security planning documents use this same test-friendly structure, so testers can link each security feature to the specific components and settings they need to check.

##### Test-structured security design deliverables in dev contracts

- Category: Manual
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Require developers to organise security-relevant hardware, software, and firmware so that security testing can be carried out in a complete, consistent, and thorough way. Also require the developer to provide a test-structured design package as objective evidence.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST Special Publication 800-53 **SA-17(6)** (Enhancement) requires: “Require the developer of the system, system component, or system service to **structure security-relevant hardware, software, and firmware to facilitate testing**.” The guidance explains that using security-focused design principles supports thorough testing and provides evidence for an assurance case or argument.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Add a contractual schedule** (for example, “Security Test-Structure Deliverables”) that states what the developer must structure and what must be handed over.
2. **Define the security-relevant scope** the developer must cover (at minimum: the security functions and the components, interfaces, and configuration points that implement them). Include examples relevant to the enterprise application context, such as authentication and authorisation enforcement points, cryptographic modules and key-management integration points, secure boot and attestation if applicable, audit and logging pipelines, and security-relevant configuration parameters.
3. **Require a structured design mapping** in the delivery pack: a traceable mapping of **security function → component(s) → interfaces → configuration parameters that affect security behaviour → trust boundaries**. This mapping must be detailed enough for testers to reproduce and verify security behaviour in the organisation’s test environment.
4. **Require test-enabling structure** in the design: clear component boundaries, well-defined interfaces, and explicit configuration points that affect security-relevant behaviour. This allows tests to be targeted and repeated.
5. **Require delivery of evidence**: include the structured design mapping, relevant module or component inventories (for example, a software bill of materials (SBOM) or module inventory at an appropriate level of detail), and configuration documentation sufficient to reproduce security-relevant behaviour for testing.
6. **Make delivery an acceptance criterion**: missing or incomplete evidence triggers non-acceptance or remediation before release to test or production.
7. **Implement automated checks in the delivery pipeline** (where feasible) to confirm required items are included (for example, SBOM or module inventories, interface specification documents, and configuration test-readiness notes).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement and commercial lead**: updates contract templates and acceptance criteria.
- **Application security architect / security engineering lead**: defines the required structure and the required content of the evidence package.
- **Delivery or project manager**: ensures the developer’s handover includes the evidence pack.
- **Test lead / assurance team**: checks that the delivered structure is sufficient to plan and carry out security tests.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The contract includes a non-optional clause requiring **test-facilitating structure** for security-relevant hardware, software, and firmware, and delivery of the **Security Test-Structure Evidence Pack**.
- The developer provides a **traceable mapping** (security function → components → interfaces and configuration points → trust boundaries) and **test-readiness configuration documentation**.
- The developer provides **module or component inventory** (for example, an SBOM at an appropriate level of detail) and identifies security-relevant components.
- The organisation’s test or assurance team can use the delivered materials to create and run security tests that can be repeated across environments (for example, development and test), with no critical gaps in interface or configuration identification.
- Where automated checks are used, pipeline checks confirm required evidence is present before release sign-off.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update procurement and software development contract templates to include a mandatory schedule called **“Security Test-Structure Deliverables”**.
- [ ] Define and publish the application’s security-relevant boundaries—specifically, the security functions and the application parts (including interfaces and configuration points) that carry them out.
- [ ] Require developers to provide a clear, auditable link showing which security functions are implemented in which components, including the interfaces, configuration settings, and the points where trust is established or transferred (trust boundaries).
- [ ] Require delivery of test setup design documents and configuration documents that are detailed enough to reproduce security-relevant behaviour in test environments.
- [ ] Provide evidence of a complete inventory of software modules or components (for example, a software bill of materials (SBOM) or an equivalent document) at a level of detail appropriate for security-relevant elements.
- [ ] Set clear acceptance criteria so that any missing or incomplete test setup items prevent a release to testing or production and trigger fixes.
- [ ] Set up checks in the delivery pipeline to confirm that all required items are included: the mapping, interface specifications, configuration test-readiness notes, and the inventory.


---

#### SA-17.7 — Structure for Least Privilege (Enhancement)

This requirement is about designing systems so that each part only has the minimum access it needs to do its job. The risk it addresses is that if software, firmware, or hardware components are given too much access, a mistake, misuse, or failure in one part could cause more damage than necessary. It also makes it harder to understand and prove what the system can do.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must, when commissioning or developing systems, instruct the developer to:
- identify which parts are security-relevant, and
- design and document how those parts are set up to support limited access.

The organisation should require clear descriptions of:
- the internal design,
- the different ways different roles can carry out different actions, and
- the minimum access each module has, including how audit-related functions are separated by role.

The organisation must also ensure these “least privilege” requirements are included in procurement documents, and then referenced in the system’s security plan, supported by design, architecture, and configuration evidence.

##### Least-privilege design deliverables and evidence pack for developers

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Require the system developer (including any subcontractors) to design security-relevant hardware, software, and firmware so that access is controlled using the minimum permissions needed (least privilege). The developer must provide evidence that can be reviewed, showing: (1) which parts are security-relevant, (2) how permissions are broken down and reduced for each component/module, (3) which role-appropriate interfaces are used to perform each capability, and (4) how internal modules keep their responsibilities contained and limit access methods (for example, read, write, or execute).

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-17(7)** (enhancement) requires: “Require the developer of the system, system component, or system service to structure security-relevant hardware, software, and firmware to facilitate controlling access with least privilege.” The guidance also expects internal least privilege through module containment and limiting the scope of access methods, and it highlights breaking permissions into smaller parts and using role-appropriate interfaces.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Add contractual or statement-of-work requirements** that explicitly require least-privilege design for security-relevant **hardware, software, and firmware**, including the required documents and review checkpoints.
2. Require the developer to produce a **Security-Relevant Elements & Privilege Model** document that lists the covered elements and defines the minimum permission set for each element/component.
3. Require a **Role-to-Interface/Capability Matrix** that links each capability to the authorised roles and the specific interfaces/workflows that trigger it (for example, administrator versus operator versus auditor), including how the interface enforces those permissions.
4. Require a **Module Decomposition & Encapsulation Design** (logical module boundaries) showing which internal elements each module directly works with, what it can reach indirectly through calls, and the **minimum access methods** per module (read/write/execute), with justification for any broader access.
5. Require **evidence traceability** from requirements to design to configuration (for example, configuration exports, identity and access management/service principal scopes, database permissions, and firmware configuration settings) so reviewers can confirm the implemented permission structure matches the documented model.
6. Set **acceptance criteria** so the organisation checks during design review and before release that the documents are complete, consistent, and clearly aligned to the documented permission model.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Procurement/Commercial owner**: includes the SA-17(7) least-privilege deliverables in contracts/statement of work.
- **System Security Architect / Security Engineering**: defines the required document templates, reviews the evidence pack, and confirms alignment to the permission model.
- **Developer/Integrator**: produces the required design documents and configuration evidence.
- **Configuration Management/Release Manager**: ensures the evidence pack is version-controlled and linked to the agreed release baseline.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A complete **Security-Relevant Elements & Privilege Model** exists and has been reviewed and approved.
- A **Role-to-Interface/Capability Matrix** exists showing role-appropriate interfaces and how permissions are enforced.
- A **Module Decomposition & Encapsulation Design** exists showing limited module scope and minimum access methods (read/write/execute), with justification.
- Evidence traceability exists from the documented model to **implemented configuration** (for example, exported access grants/scopes and relevant firmware/software configuration settings) for the release baseline.
