- Any departure from least privilege is documented with clear justification and approved by the organisation’s security authority.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update procurement and statement of work (SoW) templates to require least-privilege structuring deliverables under security requirement SA-17(7) for security-relevant hardware, software, and firmware.
- [ ] Define and publish the required document templates for the following items: the Security-Relevant Elements and Privilege Model, the Role-to-Interface and Capability Matrix, and the Module Decomposition and Encapsulation Design.
- [ ] Require the developer to show, with evidence, how the release baseline meets the least-privilege requirements—linking those requirements to the design and to the implemented configuration evidence.
- [ ] Set up design review “acceptance gates” to check that users are given only the minimum necessary permissions, that permissions are broken down into fine-grained parts, and that interfaces are restricted to what each role is allowed to use.
- [ ] Set up release approval checkpoints that check the configuration evidence (for example, granted access permissions and their scopes, and the relevant firmware and software settings) matches the documented privilege model.
- [ ] Document and get approval for any justified exceptions to least privilege, with sign-off from the security authority and a recorded reason.


---

#### SA-17.8 — Orchestration (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure the most important systems that control who can access your organisation and how encryption keys are managed are updated in a coordinated, predictable way, rather than one-off changes. Without this, a patch or change made on one system could be assumed to be present on another system that has not yet been updated. That can lead to inconsistent protection, security rules being applied differently across systems, or knock-on failures where one issue triggers others.

To meet this, the organisation must design its mission-critical authentication, access control, and key management services for Secret and Classified data so that security updates and changes follow a defined order across all critical systems: patches are applied and checked first, then any related configuration changes are made together, and the same protection rules are enforced everywhere. The organisation must document this coordinated approach, reflect it in its security and privacy planning, and require it through architecture and procurement documents, including service agreements and contracts, with named responsibilities for those who design, buy, and oversee the overall coordination.

##### Orchestrated patch-and-policy release gates for critical auth/KMS

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Design and implement an orchestration mechanism for the organisation’s **mission-critical authentication, access control, and key management services** (for **Secret and Classified data**) so that security updates happen with **coordinated behaviour**: **synchronised patching**, **patching before any configuration changes that depend on it**, and **consistent enforcement of security rules** across all in-scope critical systems.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-17(8) Orchestration** requires the organisation to **design** (plan/architect) coordinated behaviour to deliver capabilities for **critical systems** and **capabilities** defined by the organisation. The guidance notes that security components spread across different systems can interact incorrectly, leading to **chain-reaction failures, interference, or missing coverage**. Orchestration prevents this by, for example, ensuring **a patch is installed on all resources before a configuration change is made that assumes the patch has already taken effect everywhere**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Implement a release orchestration design and supporting automation that enforces the correct order and confirms completion across all in-scope critical systems:

1. **Define orchestration scope and dependency map**
   - Identify the in-scope critical systems (for example: identity provider, privileged access management, directory services, privileged access gateways, hardware security module/key management service endpoints, and certificate/key rotation services) and the components that can be patched.
   - For each dependent configuration or policy change, set clear prerequisites (for example: “a policy update requires the identity provider patch level to be at least X and the key management service client library to be at least Y”).

2. **Create one shared source of “policy intent”**
   - Store authentication/access/key-management policy as versioned artefacts (for example, policy-as-code or an equivalent approach) with a **unique policy version identifier** (for example, a policy intent hash/version).
   - Ensure all critical systems use the same policy version identifier when enforcing the policy.

3. **Build an orchestration pipeline with stop-go checkpoints**
   - **Stage A (patch rollout):** install the required patches on *all* in-scope critical systems in a coordinated manner.
   - **Stage B (verification):** automatically check that each system reports the required patch level (for example, through attestation, telemetry, or agent-reported versions).
   - **Stage C (dependent changes):** apply configuration changes and activate the policy version **only after Stage B succeeds for every in-scope system**.
   - If any system fails verification, **pause** the pipeline and either fix that system or **stop/cancel and roll back** the dependent configuration/policy changes.

4. **Enforce consistency and detect “policy drift”**
   - Only mark the process as complete when every in-scope system reports the expected **policy version identifier** and the expected enforcement state.
   - Continuously (or at agreed intervals) check for drift (systems enforcing a different policy version) and trigger remediation.

5. **Document the orchestration design and include it in governance documents**
   - Record the orchestration design (scope, sequencing, checkpoints, rollback/abort logic, verification evidence, and responsibilities) in system design documentation.
   - Ensure orchestration requirements are reflected in system security planning documents and, where applicable, privacy planning documents.
   - Include orchestration requirements in procurement/acquisition and contract/service level agreement (SLA) documentation, with named responsibilities for design, acquisition, and oversight.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architecture Lead / Identity and Access Management (IAM) and Key Management Architect:** owns the orchestration design, dependency map, and consistency requirements.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineering Lead:** builds the orchestration pipeline and verification mechanisms.
- **Change/Release Manager:** ensures orchestration checkpoints are built into release and change processes.
- **System Owners (identity provider, privileged access management, key management services, hardware security module services):** provide system-specific patch verification and remediation actions.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The organisation can show (through pipeline logs and supporting evidence) that **dependent configuration/policy changes are applied only after all in-scope critical systems meet the required patch levels**.
- For each orchestrated release, evidence shows **all in-scope systems report the same policy version identifier and enforcement state** after completion.
- If any system fails patch verification, the pipeline **blocks** dependent configuration/policy changes (stop-go checkpoint) and records the result (remediate/abort/rollback).
- Orchestration scope, sequencing, verification method, rollback/abort logic, and responsibilities are documented and traceable to governance and (where applicable) contractual artefacts.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define what is included in the orchestration scope for mission-critical authentication, access control, and key management services that support Secret and Classified data.
- [ ] Create a dependency map showing, for each configuration or policy change that depends on something, the required patch level and any prerequisites across all in-scope critical systems.
- [ ] Use one single source for policy intent, with a unique policy version identifier that every in-scope system uses.
- [ ] Set up an orchestration process that rolls out software updates, checks that every in-scope system has the correct updated version, and only after that applies any related configuration or policy changes that depend on the updates.
- [ ] Add stop-go checkpoints and rollback or abort actions so dependent changes cannot move forward if any system fails the patch verification check.
- [ ] Set up drift detection to check that all in-scope systems are using the correct, expected policy version identifier, and start remediation if any system does not match.
- [ ] Document the orchestration plan—what is included (scope), the order of steps (sequencing), the checkpoints (gates), the proof needed to confirm it worked (verification evidence), what happens if it must be stopped or undone (rollback/abort), and who is responsible (responsibilities)—and include the required operating flow details in the security planning and in the relevant contract and service level agreement (SLA) documents.


---

#### SA-17.9 — Design Diversity (Enhancement)

This requirement is about avoiding a single “one-size-fits-all” design for the most important parts that protect sign-ins, permissions, and the safeguarding of encryption keys. The risk it addresses is that if these critical components are built in exactly the same way, then a single design flaw, coding mistake, or unexpected weakness could affect every system at the same time. That would make a serious incident more likely and harder to contain.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must require that, for mission-critical sign-in, access control, and key management components, more than one developer creates different design options. These options must all be based on the same agreed requirements, but they can differ as long as each option provides the same level of protection.

The organisation must set this requirement through procurement and contracting processes, include it in requests for proposals and service agreements where relevant, document how the approach will work, record which design options exist and how each one meets the requirements, ensure configuration records reflect the different options, verify that the options genuinely differ, and keep evidence showing that the work was carried out.

##### Requirements-locked dual design variants for auth, access, and KMS

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement mission-critical authentication, access control, and key management using **two (or more) independently developed design options** (software and/or hardware) that meet the **same agreed requirements** (or provide **equivalent functionality**).

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-17(9) Design Diversity (Enhancement)** requires: *“Use different designs for {{ insert: param, sa-17.09_odp }} to satisfy a common set of requirements or to provide equivalent functionality.”* The organisation-defined scope for this enhancement is **mission-critical authentication, access control, and key management components**.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Create a requirements pack** for each scoped component (authentication, access control/authorisation, key management) including functional requirements, security requirements, performance targets, and a **shared set of tests** to confirm compliance.
2. **Set stable interface agreements** (for example, identity protocol agreements such as OpenID Connect (OIDC)/Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML), authorisation decision agreement rules such as policy decision point (PDP)/policy enforcement point (PEP) patterns, and key management system (KMS) integration agreements for key use/rotation/audit event formats) so the different options can vary internally while still meeting the same requirements.
3. **Develop and procure independent options**: require that Variant A and Variant B are produced by **different developers/teams/suppliers** and include documented design reasoning showing genuine differences (for example, different cryptography library choices, different authorisation decision logic, different key lifecycle workflows), not just a surface-level re-skin.
4. **Confirm equivalence and genuine design diversity** using the shared test set and security regression tests (authentication journeys, authorisation results, key lifecycle actions, and audit logging), and record the results as evidence that can be audited.
5. **Record which configuration uses which variant** in configuration records and design/security documentation (which environments use which variant(s), how routing/selection is done, and how equivalence is maintained).
6. **Include the requirement in procurement/contract documents** (statement of work (SOW)/request for proposal (RFP)/service level agreements (SLAs)) so suppliers commit to independent design options and provide evidence (design reasoning, test results, and confirmation they meet the interface agreements).

###### WHO
- **Application Security Architect**: owns the requirements packs, interface agreements, and compliance test criteria.
- **Procurement/Commercial Lead**: ensures RFP/SOW/contract wording requires independent design options and supporting evidence.
- **Engineering Leads (Authentication/Access/Key Management)**: deliver Variant A and Variant B and provide design reasoning.
- **Quality Assurance (QA)/Security Test Lead**: runs the shared test set and produces verification evidence.
- **Configuration Manager**: maintains configuration records mapping environments to variants.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each scoped component (authentication, access control, key management), there are **at least two independently developed design variants**.
- Both variants are built to the **same requirements pack** and pass the shared test set with equivalent security-relevant outcomes.
- Evidence shows the variants have **meaningfully different designs** (documented rationale and/or demonstrable architectural/design differences).
- Configuration records and design/security documentation clearly show **which variant(s)** are deployed in each environment and how selection/routing is controlled.
- Procurement/contract documents include the design diversity requirement and require supplier evidence (design rationale and test results).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define a requirements document pack and a shared set of tests to check compliance for mission-critical authentication, access control, and key management components.
- [ ] Publish stable interface contracts covering identity, authorisation, and key management system (KMS) integration rules and the format of audit events, which all variants must follow.
- [ ] For each scoped component, select and contract at least two independent development teams or suppliers, and require documented design reasons that show clear, meaningful differences between their approaches.
- [ ] Run the shared compliance checks and security regression tests for each version, and keep auditable records showing that the functionality is equivalent.
- [ ] Update the configuration management records to link each environment to the deployed version(s), and document how the system chooses and routes traffic to the correct version.
- [ ] Update the request for proposal (RFP), statement of work (SOW), and service level agreements (SLAs) to require design variety, separate independent development, and delivery of the design reasoning and proof that the design has been verified.


---

### SA-20 — Customized Development of Critical Components (Control)

This requirement is about rebuilding the most sensitive parts of your on-premises systems yourself, or commissioning their redesign, so they are harder for attackers to break into or tamper with. In particular, it covers:

- the parts that decide who can access the system  
- how secret keys are handled  
- the start-up software that helps ensure the device boots using trusted code  

Without this, weaknesses in these critical components could allow criminals to bypass access checks, steal or misuse secrets, or replace trusted start-up software with malicious versions.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use documented and controlled development processes, along with supporting measures that make common attack methods less likely to succeed. It must also keep clear written procedures and detailed design and configuration records showing how the work was carried out.

The organisation must further maintain up-to-date plans for managing supplier risk, acquisition policies, change tracking, and audit records, as well as a security plan. Named individuals must be responsible for purchasing, oversight, and the custom development work.

##### Reimplement critical auth, key management, and secure boot via secure SDLC

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Rebuild or custom-develop the organisation’s defined **critical system components**: **authentication and access control modules**, **cryptographic key management components**, and **secure boot/firmware for on-premises systems**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) **SA-20** requires that the organisation **rebuilds or custom-develops** these specified critical components using **controlled, documented development processes**. It also requires safeguards so that **common attacks are less likely to succeed**. This must be supported by **complete records** showing the design, configuration, and development work.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and document the SA-20 scope**: create a component inventory that links each critical component to system boundaries, trust zones, and interfaces (for example, an authentication gateway/privileged access management broker; a key orchestration service/hardware security module integration; a firmware signing/verification agent).
2. **Design each component using a threat-led approach**: produce a threat model and security requirements that explicitly address common attacker methods relevant to authentication, key management, and secure boot (for example, bypass or forgery, replay, privilege escalation, key extraction, and firmware rollback or tampering).
3. **Use a controlled software development life cycle (SDLC) with evidence checkpoints**: run a secure SDLC in the development pipeline with approval checkpoints for requirements, design, implementation, and verification. Require evidence such as design documents, code review records, test results, and cryptographic validation results.
4. **Strengthen the implementation to reduce common attacks**: apply security measures suited to each component type (for example, strong authorisation checks and session/token validation for authentication; key separation, hardware-backed key storage, and controlled key lifecycle for key management; signed firmware with verified boot and anti-rollback protections for secure boot/firmware).
5. **Control builds and releases, and protect integrity**: ensure only signed artefacts are released. Where possible, use reproducible builds and provenance, and apply dependency controls (approved libraries, vulnerability scanning, and documented exceptions).
6. **Keep audit-ready documentation and configuration records**: maintain configuration management records linking each release to the exact source, build configuration, cryptographic settings, and verification results. Keep change history and approvals for each SA-20 component.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: accountable for the SA-20 scope and acceptance of the rebuilt components.
- **Security Engineering Lead**: responsible for threat modelling, security requirements, and verification criteria.
- **Software/Embedded Engineering Manager**: delivers the controlled SDLC and ensures the pipeline checkpoints produce evidence.
- **Configuration Management Lead**: ensures traceability from requirements → code → build → release → verification.
- **Information Security / Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) (or delegate)**: approves the security plan and checks that evidence is complete.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The organisation can show that the **authentication/access control**, **cryptographic key management**, and **secure boot/firmware** components were **rebuilt or custom-developed** (not just configured), and that the scope matches the defined **critical system** parameter.
- For each component, there is **documented** evidence of: threat model, security requirements, design/configuration, controlled SDLC checkpoints, code review/approvals, verification/testing results, and release integrity controls.
- Released artefacts are protected so that **common attacks are less likely to succeed** (for example, verified boot with signature checks and anti-rollback; key lifecycle controls; strict token/session validation and authorisation enforcement), with evidence retained in configuration management records.
- Audit-ready traceability exists from each SA-20 component release to the exact source/build configuration and verification artefacts.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an inventory of SA-20 components for on-premises systems, covering authentication and access control, cryptographic key management, and secure boot and firmware.
- [ ] Create threat models and security requirements for each SA-20 component, and obtain security approval (sign-off).
- [ ] Set up a controlled software development life cycle (SDLC) process with approval checkpoints, mandatory code reviews, and recorded evidence for the design, build, and testing stages.
- [ ] Rebuild or custom-develop the SA-20 components using more secure methods, including stronger access checks (authentication enforcement), tighter control of encryption key creation and use (key lifecycle controls), and starting the system only from trusted software (signed and verified boot), with protection against running older, less secure versions where applicable (anti-rollback).
- [ ] Require that released software files are digitally signed, and ensure build and release integrity is controlled (including approving dependencies and scanning for security vulnerabilities, with any exceptions clearly documented).
- [ ] Set up configuration management traceability so that every **SA-20** release can be traced back to its source code, build configuration, cryptographic settings, and test and verification results.


---

### SA-21 — Developer Screening (Control)

This requirement ensures that only the correct external people are allowed to work on your on-premises classified development environment and the build process for the Department of Defense (DoD) mission system, and that they are properly checked before they are given access. Without this, the organisation risks unauthorised changes, accidental or deliberate misuse, or the introduction of unreliable work that could harm the mission or expose sensitive information.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- define which external developers are allowed to participate
- assign each developer specific responsibilities, such as developing, modifying, testing, or deploying approved code changes
- grant access only for the specific responsibilities they are approved to perform, with approval from the system owner

Before access is granted, each developer must be confirmed as a “US person” with the appropriate clearance to handle Secret/Confidential information, have passed a background check, provide evidence that they meet International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR) and Export Administration Regulations (EAR) requirements, complete a nationality review, and undergo a review of the company’s ownership and affiliations.

The organisation must also keep an up-to-date list of all authorised developers so it can verify both their access and their screening records.

##### Screening-gated access for external developers to classified dev pipeline

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Require that **external developers** who can access the organisation’s **on-premises classified development environment and associated build pipeline** are (1) **authorised for the specific development work they do** and (2) have completed the defined **personnel screening checks**. Keep an **authoritative, up-to-date list** of every developer authorised to carry out development work on the selected system/component/service.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-21 Developer Screening** requires the organisation to make sure developers are trustworthy by enforcing **authorisation and personnel screening checks** for external developers. This includes keeping a **list of all people authorised to carry out development work**, so the organisation can confirm that screening and authorisation requirements have been met.

This approach is based on the stated requirements:
- **sa-21_odp.01**: the on-premises classified development environment and associated build pipeline
- **sa-21_odp.02**: authorised duties to develop/modify/test/deploy code changes, with system owner approval
- **sa-21_odp.03**: US person status with the appropriate clearance for Secret/Confidential data; background check; ITAR/EAR compliance attestation; nationality review; vendor ownership/affiliations review

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Put in place an **identity and access process** that only allows access to the classified development environment/build pipeline after screening is completed and the system owner has approved it. Also limit permissions so each developer can only do the work they are authorised to do.

1. **Create a duty-to-permission model** for the classified development environment/build pipeline (for example, permissions for *Develop*, *Modify*, *Test*, *Deploy/Release*). Make sure each permission set matches the duties defined in **sa-21_odp.02**.
2. **Set up an authoritative “Authorised External Developer Register”** (the system of record) that contains, at minimum: developer identity, contractor/vendor, assigned duties, environment/build pipeline scope, system owner approval reference, screening status, and last-updated date/time.
3. **Implement a screening evidence verification workflow** (case management linked to identity and access management) that requires completion of every item in **sa-21_odp.03** before the register entry can be marked “Screening Complete”. Store evidence reference identifiers/dates (for example: clearance verification record ID, background check completion reference, ITAR/EAR attestation reference, nationality review record, vendor ownership/affiliations review record).
4. **Make system owner approval an explicit gate**: the identity and access workflow must require a recorded approval by the system owner for the specific duties before access is enabled for that developer.
5. **Control account enabling and enforce authorisation**: configure the identity provider/identity and access management so external developer accounts for the classified development environment/build pipeline are **disabled by default**. Enable them only when the register shows both **(a) Screening Complete** and **(b) System owner approved** for the specific duties. Use **role-based access control (RBAC)** and **attribute-based access control (ABAC)** so the developer only receives permissions that match their authorised duties.
6. **Keep audit-ready traceability**: ensure every access approval/change can be traced back to the relevant register entry and the associated approval/screening evidence reference identifiers.
7. **Run lifecycle controls in practice**: integrate offboarding (end of contract/role removal) so duties are removed and access is revoked promptly when a developer is no longer authorised (for example, a register status change triggers identity and access role removal).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **System Owner / Mission System Owner**: approves the authorised duties for each external developer (approval gate).
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) / Identity Governance and Administration (IGA) Administrator (with Security)**: implements the duty-to-permission mapping, gating logic, and enforcement in the identity layer.
- **Security/Compliance (Personnel Vetting Owner)**: checks and records completion of screening criteria and the associated evidence reference identifiers.
- **Procurement/Vendor Management**: supplies vendor/ownership/affiliations information needed for the screening criteria.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every external developer with access to the on-premises classified development environment/build pipeline, the authoritative register shows: assigned duties (per sa-21_odp.02), **system owner approval reference**, and **all screening checks completed** (per sa-21_odp.03).
- No external developer account is enabled for the classified development environment/build pipeline unless the register shows both screening completion and system owner approval for the specific duties.
- The register is current (last-updated date/time is maintained) and can be exported to show the full list of people authorised to carry out development work.
- Audit logs show traceability from access approvals to the register entry and the evidence reference identifiers.
- Offboarding removes duties/access when a developer is no longer authorised (a register status change results in identity and access role removal).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define role-based access control (RBAC) and attribute-based access control (ABAC) roles or attributes that link each authorised duty (develop, modify, test, deploy) to the permissions needed to use the classified development environment and build pipeline.
- [ ] Create and keep an official external developer register that records their identity, assigned responsibilities, the scope of their work, a reference to the system owner’s approval, and their screening status.
- [ ] Set up a screening evidence verification process that must be finished before marking **“Screening Complete”**. The process must include: confirming the person’s United States status and clearance, completing a background check, confirming compliance with International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR) and Export Administration Regulations (EAR), reviewing nationality, and checking the vendor’s ownership and affiliations.
- [ ] Set up identity and access management (IAM) and identity governance and administration (IGA) controls so that external developer accounts are turned off by default, and only turned on when the register shows both (1) screening is complete and (2) the system owner has approved the specific duties.
- [ ] Ensure that each access approval can be traced back to the relevant register entry and the associated evidence document identifiers, so it can be verified during audits.
- [ ] Set up offboarding triggers so that when a developer is removed from the register, their responsibilities and access to the classified development environment and build pipeline are immediately revoked.


---

### SA-22 — Unsupported System Components (Control)

This requirement is about making sure outdated parts of your systems do not keep running after the people who created them stop providing updates and support. If you continue using components that no longer receive security fixes, you increase the risk that known weaknesses could be used to cause harm. This could lead to service disruption, data loss, or fraud.

The organisation must replace any component as soon as the developer, supplier, or manufacturer can no longer support it—for example, by providing security updates, firmware updates, replacement parts, or maintenance.

If a component is so critical that it cannot be replaced, the organisation must document this, get approval, and provide a clear justification for keeping it in use. It must also choose how it will continue to receive support: either provide support internally or arrange support from external providers.

The organisation must keep records showing what was replaced, which support option was chosen, and the approvals and evidence that support arrangements and any measures to reduce risk are in place.

##### Automate end-of-support replacement and exception approvals

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an operational process that (1) finds system parts that are no longer supported (for example, they stop receiving security updates, firmware updates, replacement parts, or maintenance support) and (2) makes prompt replacement the default outcome. Continued use should only be allowed when there is documented approval that clearly chooses an alternative support arrangement (either in-house support or support from external providers) and records the required evidence.

###### WHY (control requirement)
SA-22 requires that unsupported system parts are either replaced when the vendor’s support ends (including security, firmware, and maintenance support). If replacement is not practical, they may only be kept under documented approval with a clear justification and an explicit alternative support arrangement (in-house support or support from external providers). The organisation must also keep evidence showing what was replaced, what support option was chosen, and the approvals and risk-reducing measures.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an authoritative support-status view of components** by enhancing the organisation’s asset/component inventory with vendor details (vendor, product/model, version, firmware level, serial number where applicable). Link each component to the dates when support ends and when patches/firmware are no longer available.
2. **Define what “unsupported” means in the workflow** in line with SA-22 guidance: treat a component as unsupported when the vendor/manufacturer stops providing critical security patches or product/firmware updates, or when maintenance contracts and replacement parts are no longer available.
3. **Automate replacement work**: when a component meets the unsupported trigger, automatically create a change request/work order to replace it (or upgrade it to a supported version). Set a target date based on how critical the business impact is.
4. **Control exceptions for continued use**: if replacement is not feasible, require a mandatory approval record that includes:
   - the justification for continued use,
   - an explicit choice of alternative support arrangement: **in-house support** or **support from external providers**,
   - the documented reason for accepting the risk and the required mitigations (for example, isolation requirements where relevant to the system design).
   Continued use must be blocked in the workflow until the approval is recorded.
5. **Automatically store audit-ready evidence**: link each replacement/exception record to inventory snapshots and deployment/change records, including component identifiers, before/after versions/firmware, work order IDs, approval IDs, and the selected support arrangement.
6. **Link to the system security plan and supply chain risk documents** by ensuring the exception record points to the relevant section of the system security plan and the supply chain risk management documentation for handling unsupported components.

###### WHO
- **Application/System Owner**: responsible for the business justification and whether replacement is feasible.
- **Configuration/Asset Management Lead**: maintains the authoritative inventory and component identifiers.
- **Change Management Lead / ITSM Owner**: implements the workflow controls and evidence capture.
- **Cybersecurity/Supply Chain Risk Team**: checks the unsupported triggers, the mitigations for exceptions, and the chosen alternative support arrangement.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Unsupported components (as defined by the trigger) generate automated replacement work orders within the agreed service level agreement (SLA).
- For any exception allowing continued use, there is a documented approval that explicitly selects **in-house support** or **support from external providers**, includes justification, and is linked to the system security plan and supply chain risk management documents.
- Evidence is kept for each case: component identifiers, replacement/upgrade actions, the approval record, and the chosen support arrangement.
- Continued use cannot proceed in the workflow without the required approval record and evidence links.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the organisation’s criteria for when SA-22 (patches, firmware, maintenance, or replacement parts) is considered “unsupported”.
- [ ] Extend the authoritative inventory and configuration management database (CMDB) to record vendor identifiers, component versions and firmware levels, and support-status information.
- [ ] Set up an automated check that identifies components that have moved past the unsupported trigger and then creates replacement work orders.
- [ ] Set up the change and exception process so that continued use is blocked unless the approval record includes a clear reason and explicitly selects either in-house support or support from external providers.
- [ ] Set up evidence capture so every replacement or exception is linked to the relevant inventory snapshot, the change record, and the approval ID, so the activity can be audited.
- [ ] Link exception records to the system security plan and provide the supply chain risk management documents to ensure governance traceability.


---

### SA-23 — Specialization (Control)

This requirement is about ensuring that when the organisation improves the systems used to verify who people are, it does so in a controlled way that genuinely makes those systems more reliable and trustworthy. Without this, changes to sign-in and identity services—along with the supporting servers and any components that share identity information—could be made in an unplanned, ad hoc manner. That could introduce weaknesses, mistakes, or unreviewed vendor add-ons that reduce confidence in who is actually accessing services.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out which types of design changes and add-on components it will allow for these mission-critical identity services. It must then use documented, step-by-step processes to plan, approve, implement, and verify each change. Every change must be intended to improve trustworthiness, applied to both the main identity services and their supporting components, and fully recorded with supporting evidence in the system security plan, the supply chain risk management plan, and other relevant records. Responsibility must be clearly assigned across procurement, information security oversight, security architecture, and configuration management.

##### Identity service hardening via controlled design augmentation

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement controlled **design changes and/or enhancements** to the organisation’s **mission-critical authentication and identity services**, including **supporting authentication servers and identity federation components**, where each change is clearly intended to **improve trustworthiness**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **SA-23 Specialization** requires the organisation to use the specified approach ({{param:sa-23_odp.01}}) on {{param:sa-23_odp.02}} to improve trustworthiness in those systems/components. The guidance also notes that improvements can be made after the initial design, and must be applied to both the main identity services and the supporting components, with supporting evidence and approvals.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define an “allowed enhancement catalogue”** for mission-critical identity services (for example, phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA) for relevant user journeys, stronger federation trust boundaries such as issuer/audience checks and limited relying parties, stronger token signing and support for non-repudiation, and key rotation controls). Each catalogue item must include: affected component(s), the reason it improves trustworthiness, measurable results, and what testing/verification is expected.
2. **Create step-by-step procedures** for enhancing identity designs, covering: intake, security architecture review, information security approval, choosing the configuration baseline, implementation plan, test plan, rollback plan, deployment execution, checks after deployment, and recording evidence.
3. **Apply the procedures to every change** to the defined mission-critical identity services and their supporting components (authentication servers and federation components). The change record must clearly state the trustworthiness goal and link to one catalogue item (or document an exception with the same level of approval).
4. **Use configuration baselines and automated evidence**: record “before” and “after” configuration snapshots (for example, identity provider (IdP)/federation metadata, signing/encryption settings, multi-factor authentication (MFA) policy links, and token lifetime/validation settings) and store them with the change record. Verification must confirm the intended settings are in place in the target environment(s).
5. **Record evidence in the required plans/records**: make sure each approved enhancement is referenced in the **system security plan** and the **supply chain risk management plan** (where vendor/supplier components or changes are involved), and in the change and configuration management records.
6. **Assign named responsibilities** across the change process (acquisition owner, information security oversight, security architecture, configuration management) and ensure approvals are enforced in the workflow (for example, through change management system approval gates).

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) architect**: maintains the enhancement catalogue and the reasons for improving trustworthiness.
- **Information Security (or Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) delegate)**: approves that each enhancement improves trustworthiness and that testing/evidence is sufficient.
- **Security Architecture review authority**: checks that the design intent and trust boundaries are correct.
- **Configuration Management / Release Manager**: ensures baselines, deployment, and evidence recording are completed.
- **Acquisition owner / procurement** (as applicable): ensures supplier/vendor identity components and changes are tracked for supply chain risk evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- There is an approved **enhancement catalogue** covering mission-critical authentication and identity services and supporting authentication/federation components.
- There is a documented **step-by-step procedure** that includes planning, approval, implementation, verification, rollback, and evidence recording.
- For at least one recent identity enhancement change, the change record includes: (a) the trustworthiness goal, (b) the link to a catalogue item (or an approved exception), (c) “before/after” configuration baseline evidence, (d) results of checks after deployment, and (e) references in the **system security plan** and **supply chain risk management plan** (where applicable).
- Approvals are enforced so changes cannot be deployed without the required security architecture and information security sign-offs.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and get approval for an “augmentation catalogue” covering mission-critical authentication and identity services, including the supporting authentication servers and identity federation components.
- [ ] Document step-by-step procedures for improving identity design, covering: intake, approvals, implementation, testing, rollback, and checks after deployment.
- [ ] Set up the change management process so that, for every identity update within the SA-23 scope, it requires approval from Information Security and the Security Architecture team.
- [ ] Set agreed “baseline” configurations and record before-and-after snapshots of the identity provider (IdP) and federation settings for each approved augmentation.
- [ ] Update the system security plan and, where supplier or vendor components are involved, the supply chain risk management plan. Include references to each update (augmentation) and the evidence that supports it.
- [ ] Assign named responsibilities (acquisition owner, information security oversight, security architecture, configuration management) and record them in the procedure.


---

### SA-24 — Design For Cyber Resiliency (Control)

This expectation is about designing your systems so they keep working, limit how far any damage can spread, and recover quickly even if something goes wrong. Without this, an attacker or a serious internal failure could shut down key services, damage important information, cause long outages, and leave the organisation unable to carry out its mission or protect its reputation.

To meet this, the organisation must set clear cyber resilience goals to keep the mission running, limit the “blast radius” of an incident, and ensure recovery happens on time. It must also set objectives to reduce the chance of systems being compromised, keep critical services running during an attack, and restore the accuracy and availability of information within agreed timeframes.

It must build in practical protections such as separating parts of the system, giving users and services only the minimum access they need, using authentication designed to resist common attacks, providing backup capacity (redundancy), and using backups that cannot be changed or tampered with.

These choices must be applied during design reviews, based on the threats most likely to occur. The organisation must also continuously check that the approach works through testing and exercises. Planning must assume a breach could happen, systems should fail in a safe way, trust should be kept to a minimum, operations should be able to run in a reduced mode if needed, and the system should be designed to recover and be rebuilt. These requirements must be carried through specification, development, implementation, and changes.

##### Resilient design blueprint with RTO/RPO, blast-radius limits, and reconstitution

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set and apply cyber resilience goals, objectives, methods, implementation approaches, and design principles to the application’s architecture and design documents, and confirm they work through testing and exercises.

###### WHY (control requirement)
**Security and resilience requirement SA-24** asks the organisation to design for cyber resilience so the system can keep delivering its mission, limit damage (the “blast radius”), and restore data integrity and system availability within agreed time limits. This must be supported by documented goals, objectives, methods, and principles, with ongoing checks to confirm they still work.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Set resilience goals and objectives for each system tier**: Write a short, system-specific resilience statement covering:  
   (a) maintaining mission capability, (b) limiting blast radius, and (c) recovering in time.  
   Then set objectives covering: (a) reducing the likelihood of compromise, (b) keeping critical services running during an attack, and (c) restoring integrity and availability within agreed timeframes.
2. **Turn these into measurable engineering targets**: For each critical service, define **recovery time objective (RTO)** and **recovery point objective (RPO)**, plus integrity expectations. For example: “restore from the last known-good snapshot, verified using checksum or signature, and confirm application-level consistency checks.”
3. **Build the methods into the architecture** (documented and checked in design reviews):
   - **Segmentation/isolation**: keep clear logical separation between user-facing, application, data, and administrative functions; isolate administrative routes and service accounts.
   - **Least privilege**: use role-based access control (RBAC) with separate administrator roles, use workload identity where applicable, and allow “just-in-time” elevation only when privileged actions are needed.
   - **Secure-by-design authentication**: require phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA) for privileged access, and apply conditional access and session controls.
   - **Redundancy**: design critical services to degrade gracefully and switch over when needed (for example, multi–availability zone (multi-AZ) setups and stateless components where feasible).
   - **Immutable backups**: use write-once and retention controls, keep backup credentials separate, and require restore testing to prove recovery is possible.
4. **Apply these approaches during design and change**: Add a mandatory “cyber resilience engineering” checkpoint to design reviews and architecture sign-off. This must include a threat-informed explanation of the architecture and a clear, explicit link from each resilience method to the stated goals and objectives.
5. **Validate continuously with evidence**: Run scheduled disaster recovery (DR) and restore tests (including immutable backup restore and integrity verification). Also run tabletop and technical exercises that show blast-radius containment and the steps to rebuild services. Store results as audit-ready evidence.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / System Owner**: accountable for setting goals and objectives and approving resilience targets.
- **Security Architect / Cyber Resilience Engineer**: owns the resilience plan, the mapping of methods to goals, and the design-review requirements.
- **Infrastructure/Cloud Architect**: implements segmentation, redundancy, and backup platform capabilities.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead**: implements secure-by-design authentication and enforces least privilege.
- **Disaster Recovery (DR)/Operations Lead**: runs restore tests, performs failover drills, and maintains the rebuild (reconstitution) procedures.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A system-specific **cyber resilience statement** exists and is approved, including goals, objectives, methods, implementation approaches, and design principles.
- For each **critical service**, documented **RTO/RPO** and the integrity verification method are in place and agreed by the system owner.
- Architecture and design documents show an explicit mapping from resilience methods to goals and objectives, and design-review sign-off includes this mapping.
- Within the defined review cycle, at least one **immutable backup restore test** (with integrity verification) and one **disaster recovery (DR)/failover exercise** are completed, with pass/fail results kept as evidence.
- Reconstitution steps (how the system returns to an operational, integrity-verified state) are documented and tested, demonstrating blast-radius containment and graceful degradation behaviour.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and get approval for a cyber resilience statement specific to the system. It should set out the goals, objectives, methods, how it will be implemented, and the design principles.
- [ ] Define the recovery time objective (RTO) and recovery point objective (RPO), and the integrity verification requirements, for each critical service. Record these in the resilience records.
- [ ] Update the architecture and design documents to clearly show how each resilience measure—network segmentation, least privilege, secure-by-design authentication, redundancy, and immutable backups—directly supports the stated goals and objectives.
- [ ] Add a mandatory cyber resilience engineering checkpoint to design reviews, and require approval of the maximum blast radius limits and the steps for restoring service after an incident.
- [ ] Carry out an unchangeable backup restore test, verify the data is correct, and keep audit-ready records showing the results.
- [ ] Run a disaster recovery (DR) and failover test, plus a tabletop and technical exercise, to confirm that the organisation can degrade service gracefully, contain the impact, and restore systems using the documented procedures.
